3573: rustling in the wind, and the pool rippling to the waving of the
3574: reeds--the rattling teacups would change to tinkling sheep-
3576: boy--and the sneeze of the baby, the shriek of the Gryphon, and
3577: all the other queer noises, would change (she knew) to the
3578: confused clamour of the busy farm-yard--while the lowing of the
3580: heavy sobs.
3581: 
3582:   Lastly, she pictured to herself how this same little sister of
3583: hers would, in the after-time, be herself a grown woman; and how
3584: she would keep, through all her riper years, the simple and
3585: loving heart of her childhood:  and how she would gather about
3586: her other little children, and make THEIR eyes bright and eager
3587: with many a strange tale, perhaps even with the dream of
3588: Wonderland of long ago:  and how she would feel with all their
3589: simple sorrows, and find a pleasure in all their simple joys,
3590: remembering her own child-life, and the happy summer days.
3591: 
3592:                              THE END
1670: Dormouse was sitting between them, fast asleep, and the other two
2515:   They very soon came upon a Gryphon, lying fast asleep in the
3195: Dormouse denied nothing, being fast asleep.
1: A TALE OF TWO CITIES A STORY OF THE FRENCH REVOLUTION
2: By Charles Dickens
3: 
4: I. The Period
5: 
6: It was the best of times,
7: it was the worst of times,
8: it was the age of wisdom,
9: it was the age of foolishness,
10: it was the epoch of belief,
11: it was the epoch of incredulity,
12: it was the season of Light,
13: it was the season of Darkness,
14: it was the spring of hope,
15: it was the winter of despair,
16: we had everything before us,
17: we had nothing before us,
18: we were all going direct to Heaven,
19: we were all going direct the other way--
20: in short, the period was so far like the present period, that some of
21: its noisiest authorities insisted on its being received, for good or for
22: evil, in the superlative degree of comparison only.
23: 
24: There were a king with a large jaw and a queen with a plain face, on the
25: throne of England; there were a king with a large jaw and a queen with
26: a fair face, on the throne of France. In both countries it was clearer
27: than crystal to the lords of the State preserves of loaves and fishes,
28: that things in general were settled for ever.
29: 
30: It was the year of Our Lord one thousand seven hundred and seventy-five.
31: Spiritual revelations were conceded to England at that favoured period,
32: as at this. Mrs. Southcott had recently attained her five-and-twentieth
33: blessed birthday, of whom a prophetic private in the Life Guards had
34: heralded the sublime appearance by announcing that arrangements were
35: made for the swallowing up of London and Westminster. Even the Cock-lane
36: ghost had been laid only a round dozen of years, after rapping out its
37: messages, as the spirits of this very year last past (supernaturally
38: deficient in originality) rapped out theirs. Mere messages in the
39: earthly order of events had lately come to the English Crown and People,
40: from a congress of British subjects in America: which, strange
41: to relate, have proved more important to the human race than any
42: communications yet received through any of the chickens of the Cock-lane
43: brood.
44: 
45: France, less favoured on the whole as to matters spiritual than her
46: sister of the shield and trident, rolled with exceeding smoothness down
47: hill, making paper money and spending it. Under the guidance of her
48: Christian pastors, she entertained herself, besides, with such humane
49: achievements as sentencing a youth to have his hands cut off, his tongue
50: torn out with pincers, and his body burned alive, because he had not
51: kneeled down in the rain to do honour to a dirty procession of monks
52: which passed within his view, at a distance of some fifty or sixty
53: yards. It is likely enough that, rooted in the woods of France and
54: Norway, there were growing trees, when that sufferer was put to death,
55: already marked by the Woodman, Fate, to come down and be sawn into
56: boards, to make a certain movable framework with a sack and a knife in
57: it, terrible in history. It is likely enough that in the rough outhouses
58: of some tillers of the heavy lands adjacent to Paris, there were
59: sheltered from the weather that very day, rude carts, bespattered with
60: rustic mire, snuffed about by pigs, and roosted in by poultry, which
61: the Farmer, Death, had already set apart to be his tumbrils of
62: the Revolution. But that Woodman and that Farmer, though they work
63: unceasingly, work silently, and no one heard them as they went about
64: with muffled tread: the rather, forasmuch as to entertain any suspicion
65: that they were awake, was to be atheistical and traitorous.
66: 
67: In England, there was scarcely an amount of order and protection to
68: justify much national boasting. Daring burglaries by armed men, and
69: highway robberies, took place in the capital itself every night;
70: families were publicly cautioned not to go out of town without removing
71: their furniture to upholsterers' warehouses for security; the highwayman
72: in the dark was a City tradesman in the light, and, being recognised and
73: challenged by his fellow-tradesman whom he stopped in his character of
74: "the Captain," gallantly shot him through the head and rode away; the
75: mail was waylaid by seven robbers, and the guard shot three dead, and
76: then got shot dead himself by the other four, "in consequence of the
77: failure of his ammunition:" after which the mail was robbed in peace;
78: that magnificent potentate, the Lord Mayor of London, was made to stand
79: and deliver on Turnham Green, by one highwayman, who despoiled the
80: illustrious creature in sight of all his retinue; prisoners in London
81: gaols fought battles with their turnkeys, and the majesty of the law
82: fired blunderbusses in among them, loaded with rounds of shot and ball;
83: thieves snipped off diamond crosses from the necks of noble lords at
84: Court drawing-rooms; musketeers went into St. Giles's, to search
85: for contraband goods, and the mob fired on the musketeers, and the
86: musketeers fired on the mob, and nobody thought any of these occurrences
87: much out of the common way. In the midst of them, the hangman, ever busy
88: and ever worse than useless, was in constant requisition; now, stringing
89: up long rows of miscellaneous criminals; now, hanging a housebreaker on
90: Saturday who had been taken on Tuesday; now, burning people in the
91: hand at Newgate by the dozen, and now burning pamphlets at the door of
92: Westminster Hall; to-day, taking the life of an atrocious murderer,
93: and to-morrow of a wretched pilferer who had robbed a farmer's boy of
94: sixpence.
95: 
96: All these things, and a thousand like them, came to pass in and close
97: upon the dear old year one thousand seven hundred and seventy-five.
98: Environed by them, while the Woodman and the Farmer worked unheeded,
99: those two of the large jaws, and those other two of the plain and the
100: fair faces, trod with stir enough, and carried their divine rights
101: with a high hand. Thus did the year one thousand seven hundred
102: and seventy-five conduct their Greatnesses, and myriads of small
103: creatures--the creatures of this chronicle among the rest--along the
104: roads that lay before them.
105: 
106: 
107: 
108: 
109: II. The Mail
110: 
111: 
112: It was the Dover road that lay, on a Friday night late in November,
113: before the first of the persons with whom this history has business.
114: The Dover road lay, as to him, beyond the Dover mail, as it lumbered up
115: Shooter's Hill. He walked up hill in the mire by the side of the mail,
116: as the rest of the passengers did; not because they had the least relish
117: for walking exercise, under the circumstances, but because the hill,
118: and the harness, and the mud, and the mail, were all so heavy, that the
119: horses had three times already come to a stop, besides once drawing the
120: coach across the road, with the mutinous intent of taking it back
121: to Blackheath. Reins and whip and coachman and guard, however, in
122: combination, had read that article of war which forbade a purpose
123: otherwise strongly in favour of the argument, that some brute animals
124: are endued with Reason; and the team had capitulated and returned to
125: their duty.
126: 
127: With drooping heads and tremulous tails, they mashed their way through
128: the thick mud, floundering and stumbling between whiles, as if they were
129: falling to pieces at the larger joints. As often as the driver rested
130: them and brought them to a stand, with a wary "Wo-ho! so-ho-then!" the
131: near leader violently shook his head and everything upon it--like an
132: unusually emphatic horse, denying that the coach could be got up the
133: hill. Whenever the leader made this rattle, the passenger started, as a
134: nervous passenger might, and was disturbed in mind.
135: 
136: There was a steaming mist in all the hollows, and it had roamed in its
137: forlornness up the hill, like an evil spirit, seeking rest and finding
138: none. A clammy and intensely cold mist, it made its slow way through the
139: air in ripples that visibly followed and overspread one another, as the
140: waves of an unwholesome sea might do. It was dense enough to shut out
141: everything from the light of the coach-lamps but these its own workings,
142: and a few yards of road; and the reek of the labouring horses steamed
143: into it, as if they had made it all.
144: 
145: Two other passengers, besides the one, were plodding up the hill by the
146: side of the mail. All three were wrapped to the cheekbones and over the
147: ears, and wore jack-boots. Not one of the three could have said, from
148: anything he saw, what either of the other two was like; and each was
149: hidden under almost as many wrappers from the eyes of the mind, as from
150: the eyes of the body, of his two companions. In those days, travellers
151: were very shy of being confidential on a short notice, for anybody on
152: the road might be a robber or in league with robbers. As to the latter,
153: when every posting-house and ale-house could produce somebody in
154: "the Captain's" pay, ranging from the landlord to the lowest stable
155: non-descript, it was the likeliest thing upon the cards. So the guard
156: of the Dover mail thought to himself, that Friday night in November, one
157: thousand seven hundred and seventy-five, lumbering up Shooter's Hill, as
158: he stood on his own particular perch behind the mail, beating his feet,
159: and keeping an eye and a hand on the arm-chest before him, where a
160: loaded blunderbuss lay at the top of six or eight loaded horse-pistols,
161: deposited on a substratum of cutlass.
162: 
163: The Dover mail was in its usual genial position that the guard suspected
164: the passengers, the passengers suspected one another and the guard, they
165: all suspected everybody else, and the coachman was sure of nothing but
166: the horses; as to which cattle he could with a clear conscience have
167: taken his oath on the two Testaments that they were not fit for the
168: journey.
169: 
170: "Wo-ho!" said the coachman. "So, then! One more pull and you're at the
171: top and be damned to you, for I have had trouble enough to get you to
172: it!--Joe!"
173: 
174: "Halloa!" the guard replied.
175: 
176: "What o'clock do you make it, Joe?"
177: 
178: "Ten minutes, good, past eleven."
179: 
180: "My blood!" ejaculated the vexed coachman, "and not atop of Shooter's
181: yet! Tst! Yah! Get on with you!"
182: 
183: The emphatic horse, cut short by the whip in a most decided negative,
184: made a decided scramble for it, and the three other horses followed
185: suit. Once more, the Dover mail struggled on, with the jack-boots of its
186: passengers squashing along by its side. They had stopped when the coach
187: stopped, and they kept close company with it. If any one of the three
188: had had the hardihood to propose to another to walk on a little ahead
189: into the mist and darkness, he would have put himself in a fair way of
190: getting shot instantly as a highwayman.
191: 
192: The last burst carried the mail to the summit of the hill. The horses
193: stopped to breathe again, and the guard got down to skid the wheel for
194: the descent, and open the coach-door to let the passengers in.
195: 
196: "Tst! Joe!" cried the coachman in a warning voice, looking down from his
197: box.
198: 
199: "What do you say, Tom?"
200: 
201: They both listened.
202: 
203: "I say a horse at a canter coming up, Joe."
204: 
205: "_I_ say a horse at a gallop, Tom," returned the guard, leaving his hold
206: of the door, and mounting nimbly to his place. "Gentlemen! In the king's
207: name, all of you!"
208: 
209: With this hurried adjuration, he cocked his blunderbuss, and stood on
210: the offensive.
211: 
212: The passenger booked by this history, was on the coach-step, getting in;
213: the two other passengers were close behind him, and about to follow. He
214: remained on the step, half in the coach and half out of; they remained
215: in the road below him. They all looked from the coachman to the guard,
216: and from the guard to the coachman, and listened. The coachman looked
217: back and the guard looked back, and even the emphatic leader pricked up
218: his ears and looked back, without contradicting.
219: 
220: The stillness consequent on the cessation of the rumbling and labouring
221: of the coach, added to the stillness of the night, made it very quiet
222: indeed. The panting of the horses communicated a tremulous motion to
223: the coach, as if it were in a state of agitation. The hearts of the
224: passengers beat loud enough perhaps to be heard; but at any rate, the
225: quiet pause was audibly expressive of people out of breath, and holding
226: the breath, and having the pulses quickened by expectation.
227: 
228: The sound of a horse at a gallop came fast and furiously up the hill.
229: 
230: "So-ho!" the guard sang out, as loud as he could roar. "Yo there! Stand!
231: I shall fire!"
232: 
233: The pace was suddenly checked, and, with much splashing and floundering,
234: a man's voice called from the mist, "Is that the Dover mail?"
235: 
236: "Never you mind what it is!" the guard retorted. "What are you?"
237: 
238: "_Is_ that the Dover mail?"
239: 
240: "Why do you want to know?"
241: 
242: "I want a passenger, if it is."
243: 
244: "What passenger?"
245: 
246: "Mr. Jarvis Lorry."
247: 
248: Our booked passenger showed in a moment that it was his name. The guard,
249: the coachman, and the two other passengers eyed him distrustfully.
250: 
251: "Keep where you are," the guard called to the voice in the mist,
252: "because, if I should make a mistake, it could never be set right in
253: your lifetime. Gentleman of the name of Lorry answer straight."
254: 
255: "What is the matter?" asked the passenger, then, with mildly quavering
256: speech. "Who wants me? Is it Jerry?"
257: 
258: ("I don't like Jerry's voice, if it is Jerry," growled the guard to
259: himself. "He's hoarser than suits me, is Jerry.")
260: 
261: "Yes, Mr. Lorry."
262: 
263: "What is the matter?"
264: 
265: "A despatch sent after you from over yonder. T. and Co."
266: 
267: "I know this messenger, guard," said Mr. Lorry, getting down into the
268: road--assisted from behind more swiftly than politely by the other two
269: passengers, who immediately scrambled into the coach, shut the door, and
270: pulled up the window. "He may come close; there's nothing wrong."
271: 
272: "I hope there ain't, but I can't make so 'Nation sure of that," said the
273: guard, in gruff soliloquy. "Hallo you!"
274: 
275: "Well! And hallo you!" said Jerry, more hoarsely than before.
276: 
277: "Come on at a footpace! d'ye mind me? And if you've got holsters to that
278: saddle o' yourn, don't let me see your hand go nigh 'em. For I'm a devil
279: at a quick mistake, and when I make one it takes the form of Lead. So
280: now let's look at you."
281: 
282: The figures of a horse and rider came slowly through the eddying mist,
283: and came to the side of the mail, where the passenger stood. The rider
284: stooped, and, casting up his eyes at the guard, handed the passenger
285: a small folded paper. The rider's horse was blown, and both horse and
286: rider were covered with mud, from the hoofs of the horse to the hat of
287: the man.
288: 
289: "Guard!" said the passenger, in a tone of quiet business confidence.
290: 
291: The watchful guard, with his right hand at the stock of his raised
292: blunderbuss, his left at the barrel, and his eye on the horseman,
293: answered curtly, "Sir."
294: 
295: "There is nothing to apprehend. I belong to Tellson's Bank. You must
296: know Tellson's Bank in London. I am going to Paris on business. A crown
297: to drink. I may read this?"
298: 
299: "If so be as you're quick, sir."
300: 
301: He opened it in the light of the coach-lamp on that side, and
302: read--first to himself and then aloud: "'Wait at Dover for Mam'selle.'
303: It's not long, you see, guard. Jerry, say that my answer was, RECALLED
304: TO LIFE."
305: 
306: Jerry started in his saddle. "That's a Blazing strange answer, too,"
307: said he, at his hoarsest.
308: 
309: "Take that message back, and they will know that I received this, as
310: well as if I wrote. Make the best of your way. Good night."
311: 
312: With those words the passenger opened the coach-door and got in; not at
313: all assisted by his fellow-passengers, who had expeditiously secreted
314: their watches and purses in their boots, and were now making a general
315: pretence of being asleep. With no more definite purpose than to escape
316: the hazard of originating any other kind of action.
317: 
318: The coach lumbered on again, with heavier wreaths of mist closing round
319: it as it began the descent. The guard soon replaced his blunderbuss
320: in his arm-chest, and, having looked to the rest of its contents, and
321: having looked to the supplementary pistols that he wore in his belt,
322: looked to a smaller chest beneath his seat, in which there were a
323: few smith's tools, a couple of torches, and a tinder-box. For he was
324: furnished with that completeness that if the coach-lamps had been blown
325: and stormed out, which did occasionally happen, he had only to shut
326: himself up inside, keep the flint and steel sparks well off the straw,
327: and get a light with tolerable safety and ease (if he were lucky) in
328: five minutes.
329: 
330: "Tom!" softly over the coach roof.
331: 
332: "Hallo, Joe."
333: 
334: "Did you hear the message?"
335: 
336: "I did, Joe."
337: 
338: "What did you make of it, Tom?"
339: 
340: "Nothing at all, Joe."
341: 
342: "That's a coincidence, too," the guard mused, "for I made the same of it
343: myself."
344: 
345: Jerry, left alone in the mist and darkness, dismounted meanwhile, not
346: only to ease his spent horse, but to wipe the mud from his face, and
347: shake the wet out of his hat-brim, which might be capable of
348: holding about half a gallon. After standing with the bridle over his
349: heavily-splashed arm, until the wheels of the mail were no longer within
350: hearing and the night was quite still again, he turned to walk down the
351: hill.
352: 
353: "After that there gallop from Temple Bar, old lady, I won't trust your
354: fore-legs till I get you on the level," said this hoarse messenger,
355: glancing at his mare. "'Recalled to life.' That's a Blazing strange
356: message. Much of that wouldn't do for you, Jerry! I say, Jerry! You'd
357: be in a Blazing bad way, if recalling to life was to come into fashion,
358: Jerry!"
359: 
360: 
361: 
362: 
363: III. The Night Shadows
364: 
365: 
366: A wonderful fact to reflect upon, that every human creature is
367: constituted to be that profound secret and mystery to every other. A
368: solemn consideration, when I enter a great city by night, that every
369: one of those darkly clustered houses encloses its own secret; that every
370: room in every one of them encloses its own secret; that every beating
371: heart in the hundreds of thousands of breasts there, is, in some of
372: its imaginings, a secret to the heart nearest it! Something of the
373: awfulness, even of Death itself, is referable to this. No more can I
374: turn the leaves of this dear book that I loved, and vainly hope in time
375: to read it all. No more can I look into the depths of this unfathomable
376: water, wherein, as momentary lights glanced into it, I have had glimpses
377: of buried treasure and other things submerged. It was appointed that the
378: book should shut with a spring, for ever and for ever, when I had read
379: but a page. It was appointed that the water should be locked in an
380: eternal frost, when the light was playing on its surface, and I stood
381: in ignorance on the shore. My friend is dead, my neighbour is dead,
382: my love, the darling of my soul, is dead; it is the inexorable
383: consolidation and perpetuation of the secret that was always in that
384: individuality, and which I shall carry in mine to my life's end. In
385: any of the burial-places of this city through which I pass, is there
386: a sleeper more inscrutable than its busy inhabitants are, in their
387: innermost personality, to me, or than I am to them?
388: 
389: As to this, his natural and not to be alienated inheritance, the
390: messenger on horseback had exactly the same possessions as the King, the
391: first Minister of State, or the richest merchant in London. So with the
392: three passengers shut up in the narrow compass of one lumbering old mail
393: coach; they were mysteries to one another, as complete as if each had
394: been in his own coach and six, or his own coach and sixty, with the
395: breadth of a county between him and the next.
396: 
397: The messenger rode back at an easy trot, stopping pretty often at
398: ale-houses by the way to drink, but evincing a tendency to keep his
399: own counsel, and to keep his hat cocked over his eyes. He had eyes that
400: assorted very well with that decoration, being of a surface black, with
401: no depth in the colour or form, and much too near together--as if they
402: were afraid of being found out in something, singly, if they kept too
403: far apart. They had a sinister expression, under an old cocked-hat like
404: a three-cornered spittoon, and over a great muffler for the chin and
405: throat, which descended nearly to the wearer's knees. When he stopped
406: for drink, he moved this muffler with his left hand, only while he
407: poured his liquor in with his right; as soon as that was done, he
408: muffled again.
409: 
410: "No, Jerry, no!" said the messenger, harping on one theme as he rode.
411: "It wouldn't do for you, Jerry. Jerry, you honest tradesman, it wouldn't
412: suit _your_ line of business! Recalled--! Bust me if I don't think he'd
413: been a drinking!"
414: 
415: His message perplexed his mind to that degree that he was fain, several
416: times, to take off his hat to scratch his head. Except on the crown,
417: which was raggedly bald, he had stiff, black hair, standing jaggedly all
418: over it, and growing down hill almost to his broad, blunt nose. It was
419: so like Smith's work, so much more like the top of a strongly spiked
420: wall than a head of hair, that the best of players at leap-frog might
421: have declined him, as the most dangerous man in the world to go over.
422: 
423: While he trotted back with the message he was to deliver to the night
424: watchman in his box at the door of Tellson's Bank, by Temple Bar, who
425: was to deliver it to greater authorities within, the shadows of the
426: night took such shapes to him as arose out of the message, and took such
427: shapes to the mare as arose out of _her_ private topics of uneasiness.
428: They seemed to be numerous, for she shied at every shadow on the road.
429: 
430: What time, the mail-coach lumbered, jolted, rattled, and bumped upon
431: its tedious way, with its three fellow-inscrutables inside. To whom,
432: likewise, the shadows of the night revealed themselves, in the forms
433: their dozing eyes and wandering thoughts suggested.
434: 
435: Tellson's Bank had a run upon it in the mail. As the bank
436: passenger--with an arm drawn through the leathern strap, which did what
437: lay in it to keep him from pounding against the next passenger,
438: and driving him into his corner, whenever the coach got a special
439: jolt--nodded in his place, with half-shut eyes, the little
440: coach-windows, and the coach-lamp dimly gleaming through them, and the
441: bulky bundle of opposite passenger, became the bank, and did a great
442: stroke of business. The rattle of the harness was the chink of money,
443: and more drafts were honoured in five minutes than even Tellson's, with
444: all its foreign and home connection, ever paid in thrice the time. Then
445: the strong-rooms underground, at Tellson's, with such of their valuable
446: stores and secrets as were known to the passenger (and it was not a
447: little that he knew about them), opened before him, and he went in among
448: them with the great keys and the feebly-burning candle, and found them
449: safe, and strong, and sound, and still, just as he had last seen them.
450: 
451: But, though the bank was almost always with him, and though the coach
452: (in a confused way, like the presence of pain under an opiate) was
453: always with him, there was another current of impression that never
454: ceased to run, all through the night. He was on his way to dig some one
455: out of a grave.
456: 
457: Now, which of the multitude of faces that showed themselves before him
458: was the true face of the buried person, the shadows of the night did
459: not indicate; but they were all the faces of a man of five-and-forty by
460: years, and they differed principally in the passions they expressed,
461: and in the ghastliness of their worn and wasted state. Pride, contempt,
462: defiance, stubbornness, submission, lamentation, succeeded one another;
463: so did varieties of sunken cheek, cadaverous colour, emaciated hands
464: and figures. But the face was in the main one face, and every head was
465: prematurely white. A hundred times the dozing passenger inquired of this
466: spectre:
467: 
468: "Buried how long?"
469: 
470: The answer was always the same: "Almost eighteen years."
471: 
472: "You had abandoned all hope of being dug out?"
473: 
474: "Long ago."
475: 
476: "You know that you are recalled to life?"
477: 
478: "They tell me so."
479: 
480: "I hope you care to live?"
481: 
482: "I can't say."
483: 
484: "Shall I show her to you? Will you come and see her?"
485: 
486: The answers to this question were various and contradictory. Sometimes
487: the broken reply was, "Wait! It would kill me if I saw her too soon."
488: Sometimes, it was given in a tender rain of tears, and then it was,
489: "Take me to her." Sometimes it was staring and bewildered, and then it
490: was, "I don't know her. I don't understand."
491: 
492: After such imaginary discourse, the passenger in his fancy would dig,
493: and dig, dig--now with a spade, now with a great key, now with his
494: hands--to dig this wretched creature out. Got out at last, with earth
495: hanging about his face and hair, he would suddenly fan away to dust. The
496: passenger would then start to himself, and lower the window, to get the
497: reality of mist and rain on his cheek.
498: 
499: Yet even when his eyes were opened on the mist and rain, on the moving
500: patch of light from the lamps, and the hedge at the roadside retreating
501: by jerks, the night shadows outside the coach would fall into the train
502: of the night shadows within. The real Banking-house by Temple Bar, the
503: real business of the past day, the real strong rooms, the real express
504: sent after him, and the real message returned, would all be there. Out
505: of the midst of them, the ghostly face would rise, and he would accost
506: it again.
507: 
508: "Buried how long?"
509: 
510: "Almost eighteen years."
511: 
512: "I hope you care to live?"
513: 
514: "I can't say."
515: 
516: Dig--dig--dig--until an impatient movement from one of the two
517: passengers would admonish him to pull up the window, draw his arm
518: securely through the leathern strap, and speculate upon the two
519: slumbering forms, until his mind lost its hold of them, and they again
520: slid away into the bank and the grave.
521: 
522: "Buried how long?"
523: 
524: "Almost eighteen years."
525: 
526: "You had abandoned all hope of being dug out?"
527: 
528: "Long ago."
529: 
530: The words were still in his hearing as just spoken--distinctly in
531: his hearing as ever spoken words had been in his life--when the weary
532: passenger started to the consciousness of daylight, and found that the
533: shadows of the night were gone.
534: 
535: He lowered the window, and looked out at the rising sun. There was a
536: ridge of ploughed land, with a plough upon it where it had been left
537: last night when the horses were unyoked; beyond, a quiet coppice-wood,
538: in which many leaves of burning red and golden yellow still remained
539: upon the trees. Though the earth was cold and wet, the sky was clear,
540: and the sun rose bright, placid, and beautiful.
541: 
542: "Eighteen years!" said the passenger, looking at the sun. "Gracious
543: Creator of day! To be buried alive for eighteen years!"
544: 
545: 
546: 
547: 
548: IV. The Preparation
549: 
550: 
551: When the mail got successfully to Dover, in the course of the forenoon,
552: the head drawer at the Royal George Hotel opened the coach-door as his
553: custom was. He did it with some flourish of ceremony, for a mail journey
554: from London in winter was an achievement to congratulate an adventurous
555: traveller upon.
556: 
557: By that time, there was only one adventurous traveller left be
558: congratulated: for the two others had been set down at their respective
559: roadside destinations. The mildewy inside of the coach, with its damp
560: and dirty straw, its disagreeable smell, and its obscurity, was rather
561: like a larger dog-kennel. Mr. Lorry, the passenger, shaking himself out
562: of it in chains of straw, a tangle of shaggy wrapper, flapping hat, and
563: muddy legs, was rather like a larger sort of dog.
564: 
565: "There will be a packet to Calais, tomorrow, drawer?"
566: 
567: "Yes, sir, if the weather holds and the wind sets tolerable fair. The
568: tide will serve pretty nicely at about two in the afternoon, sir. Bed,
569: sir?"
570: 
571: "I shall not go to bed till night; but I want a bedroom, and a barber."
572: 
573: "And then breakfast, sir? Yes, sir. That way, sir, if you please.
574: Show Concord! Gentleman's valise and hot water to Concord. Pull off
575: gentleman's boots in Concord. (You will find a fine sea-coal fire, sir.)
576: Fetch barber to Concord. Stir about there, now, for Concord!"
577: 
578: The Concord bed-chamber being always assigned to a passenger by the
579: mail, and passengers by the mail being always heavily wrapped up from
580: head to foot, the room had the odd interest for the establishment of the
581: Royal George, that although but one kind of man was seen to go into it,
582: all kinds and varieties of men came out of it. Consequently, another
583: drawer, and two porters, and several maids and the landlady, were all
584: loitering by accident at various points of the road between the Concord
585: and the coffee-room, when a gentleman of sixty, formally dressed in a
586: brown suit of clothes, pretty well worn, but very well kept, with large
587: square cuffs and large flaps to the pockets, passed along on his way to
588: his breakfast.
589: 
590: The coffee-room had no other occupant, that forenoon, than the gentleman
591: in brown. His breakfast-table was drawn before the fire, and as he sat,
592: with its light shining on him, waiting for the meal, he sat so still,
593: that he might have been sitting for his portrait.
594: 
595: Very orderly and methodical he looked, with a hand on each knee, and a
596: loud watch ticking a sonorous sermon under his flapped waist-coat,
597: as though it pitted its gravity and longevity against the levity and
598: evanescence of the brisk fire. He had a good leg, and was a little vain
599: of it, for his brown stockings fitted sleek and close, and were of a
600: fine texture; his shoes and buckles, too, though plain, were trim. He
601: wore an odd little sleek crisp flaxen wig, setting very close to his
602: head: which wig, it is to be presumed, was made of hair, but which
603: looked far more as though it were spun from filaments of silk or glass.
604: His linen, though not of a fineness in accordance with his stockings,
605: was as white as the tops of the waves that broke upon the neighbouring
606: beach, or the specks of sail that glinted in the sunlight far at sea. A
607: face habitually suppressed and quieted, was still lighted up under the
608: quaint wig by a pair of moist bright eyes that it must have cost
609: their owner, in years gone by, some pains to drill to the composed and
610: reserved expression of Tellson's Bank. He had a healthy colour in his
611: cheeks, and his face, though lined, bore few traces of anxiety.
612: But, perhaps the confidential bachelor clerks in Tellson's Bank were
613: principally occupied with the cares of other people; and perhaps
614: second-hand cares, like second-hand clothes, come easily off and on.
615: 
616: Completing his resemblance to a man who was sitting for his portrait,
617: Mr. Lorry dropped off to sleep. The arrival of his breakfast roused him,
618: and he said to the drawer, as he moved his chair to it:
619: 
620: "I wish accommodation prepared for a young lady who may come here at any
621: time to-day. She may ask for Mr. Jarvis Lorry, or she may only ask for a
622: gentleman from Tellson's Bank. Please to let me know."
623: 
624: "Yes, sir. Tellson's Bank in London, sir?"
625: 
626: "Yes."
627: 
628: "Yes, sir. We have oftentimes the honour to entertain your gentlemen in
629: their travelling backwards and forwards betwixt London and Paris, sir. A
630: vast deal of travelling, sir, in Tellson and Company's House."
631: 
632: "Yes. We are quite a French House, as well as an English one."
633: 
634: "Yes, sir. Not much in the habit of such travelling yourself, I think,
635: sir?"
636: 
637: "Not of late years. It is fifteen years since we--since I--came last
638: from France."
639: 
640: "Indeed, sir? That was before my time here, sir. Before our people's
641: time here, sir. The George was in other hands at that time, sir."
642: 
643: "I believe so."
644: 
645: "But I would hold a pretty wager, sir, that a House like Tellson and
646: Company was flourishing, a matter of fifty, not to speak of fifteen
647: years ago?"
648: 
649: "You might treble that, and say a hundred and fifty, yet not be far from
650: the truth."
651: 
652: "Indeed, sir!"
653: 
654: Rounding his mouth and both his eyes, as he stepped backward from the
655: table, the waiter shifted his napkin from his right arm to his left,
656: dropped into a comfortable attitude, and stood surveying the guest while
657: he ate and drank, as from an observatory or watchtower. According to the
658: immemorial usage of waiters in all ages.
659: 
660: When Mr. Lorry had finished his breakfast, he went out for a stroll on
661: the beach. The little narrow, crooked town of Dover hid itself away
662: from the beach, and ran its head into the chalk cliffs, like a marine
663: ostrich. The beach was a desert of heaps of sea and stones tumbling
664: wildly about, and the sea did what it liked, and what it liked was
665: destruction. It thundered at the town, and thundered at the cliffs, and
666: brought the coast down, madly. The air among the houses was of so strong
667: a piscatory flavour that one might have supposed sick fish went up to be
668: dipped in it, as sick people went down to be dipped in the sea. A little
669: fishing was done in the port, and a quantity of strolling about by
670: night, and looking seaward: particularly at those times when the tide
671: made, and was near flood. Small tradesmen, who did no business whatever,
672: sometimes unaccountably realised large fortunes, and it was remarkable
673: that nobody in the neighbourhood could endure a lamplighter.
674: 
675: As the day declined into the afternoon, and the air, which had been
676: at intervals clear enough to allow the French coast to be seen, became
677: again charged with mist and vapour, Mr. Lorry's thoughts seemed to cloud
678: too. When it was dark, and he sat before the coffee-room fire, awaiting
679: his dinner as he had awaited his breakfast, his mind was busily digging,
680: digging, digging, in the live red coals.
681: 
682: A bottle of good claret after dinner does a digger in the red coals no
683: harm, otherwise than as it has a tendency to throw him out of work.
684: Mr. Lorry had been idle a long time, and had just poured out his last
685: glassful of wine with as complete an appearance of satisfaction as is
686: ever to be found in an elderly gentleman of a fresh complexion who has
687: got to the end of a bottle, when a rattling of wheels came up the narrow
688: street, and rumbled into the inn-yard.
689: 
690: He set down his glass untouched. "This is Mam'selle!" said he.
691: 
692: In a very few minutes the waiter came in to announce that Miss Manette
693: had arrived from London, and would be happy to see the gentleman from
694: Tellson's.
695: 
696: "So soon?"
697: 
698: Miss Manette had taken some refreshment on the road, and required none
699: then, and was extremely anxious to see the gentleman from Tellson's
700: immediately, if it suited his pleasure and convenience.
701: 
702: The gentleman from Tellson's had nothing left for it but to empty his
703: glass with an air of stolid desperation, settle his odd little flaxen
704: wig at the ears, and follow the waiter to Miss Manette's apartment.
705: It was a large, dark room, furnished in a funereal manner with black
706: horsehair, and loaded with heavy dark tables. These had been oiled and
707: oiled, until the two tall candles on the table in the middle of the room
708: were gloomily reflected on every leaf; as if _they_ were buried, in deep
709: graves of black mahogany, and no light to speak of could be expected
710: from them until they were dug out.
711: 
712: The obscurity was so difficult to penetrate that Mr. Lorry, picking his
713: way over the well-worn Turkey carpet, supposed Miss Manette to be, for
714: the moment, in some adjacent room, until, having got past the two tall
715: candles, he saw standing to receive him by the table between them and
716: the fire, a young lady of not more than seventeen, in a riding-cloak,
717: and still holding her straw travelling-hat by its ribbon in her hand. As
718: his eyes rested on a short, slight, pretty figure, a quantity of golden
719: hair, a pair of blue eyes that met his own with an inquiring look, and
720: a forehead with a singular capacity (remembering how young and smooth
721: it was), of rifting and knitting itself into an expression that was
722: not quite one of perplexity, or wonder, or alarm, or merely of a bright
723: fixed attention, though it included all the four expressions--as his
724: eyes rested on these things, a sudden vivid likeness passed before him,
725: of a child whom he had held in his arms on the passage across that very
726: Channel, one cold time, when the hail drifted heavily and the sea ran
727: high. The likeness passed away, like a breath along the surface of
728: the gaunt pier-glass behind her, on the frame of which, a hospital
729: procession of negro cupids, several headless and all cripples, were
730: offering black baskets of Dead Sea fruit to black divinities of the
731: feminine gender--and he made his formal bow to Miss Manette.
732: 
733: "Pray take a seat, sir." In a very clear and pleasant young voice; a
734: little foreign in its accent, but a very little indeed.
735: 
736: "I kiss your hand, miss," said Mr. Lorry, with the manners of an earlier
737: date, as he made his formal bow again, and took his seat.
738: 
739: "I received a letter from the Bank, sir, yesterday, informing me that
740: some intelligence--or discovery--"
741: 
742: "The word is not material, miss; either word will do."
743: 
744: "--respecting the small property of my poor father, whom I never saw--so
745: long dead--"
746: 
747: Mr. Lorry moved in his chair, and cast a troubled look towards the
748: hospital procession of negro cupids. As if _they_ had any help for
749: anybody in their absurd baskets!
750: 
751: "--rendered it necessary that I should go to Paris, there to communicate
752: with a gentleman of the Bank, so good as to be despatched to Paris for
753: the purpose."
754: 
755: "Myself."
756: 
757: "As I was prepared to hear, sir."
758: 
759: She curtseyed to him (young ladies made curtseys in those days), with a
760: pretty desire to convey to him that she felt how much older and wiser he
761: was than she. He made her another bow.
762: 
763: "I replied to the Bank, sir, that as it was considered necessary, by
764: those who know, and who are so kind as to advise me, that I should go to
765: France, and that as I am an orphan and have no friend who could go with
766: me, I should esteem it highly if I might be permitted to place myself,
767: during the journey, under that worthy gentleman's protection. The
768: gentleman had left London, but I think a messenger was sent after him to
769: beg the favour of his waiting for me here."
770: 
771: "I was happy," said Mr. Lorry, "to be entrusted with the charge. I shall
772: be more happy to execute it."
773: 
774: "Sir, I thank you indeed. I thank you very gratefully. It was told me
775: by the Bank that the gentleman would explain to me the details of the
776: business, and that I must prepare myself to find them of a surprising
777: nature. I have done my best to prepare myself, and I naturally have a
778: strong and eager interest to know what they are."
779: 
780: "Naturally," said Mr. Lorry. "Yes--I--"
781: 
782: After a pause, he added, again settling the crisp flaxen wig at the
783: ears, "It is very difficult to begin."
784: 
785: He did not begin, but, in his indecision, met her glance. The young
786: forehead lifted itself into that singular expression--but it was pretty
787: and characteristic, besides being singular--and she raised her hand,
788: as if with an involuntary action she caught at, or stayed some passing
789: shadow.
790: 
791: "Are you quite a stranger to me, sir?"
792: 
793: "Am I not?" Mr. Lorry opened his hands, and extended them outwards with
794: an argumentative smile.
795: 
796: Between the eyebrows and just over the little feminine nose, the line of
797: which was as delicate and fine as it was possible to be, the expression
798: deepened itself as she took her seat thoughtfully in the chair by which
799: she had hitherto remained standing. He watched her as she mused, and the
800: moment she raised her eyes again, went on:
801: 
802: "In your adopted country, I presume, I cannot do better than address you
803: as a young English lady, Miss Manette?"
804: 
805: "If you please, sir."
806: 
807: "Miss Manette, I am a man of business. I have a business charge to
808: acquit myself of. In your reception of it, don't heed me any more than
809: if I was a speaking machine--truly, I am not much else. I will, with
810: your leave, relate to you, miss, the story of one of our customers."
811: 
812: "Story!"
813: 
814: He seemed wilfully to mistake the word she had repeated, when he added,
815: in a hurry, "Yes, customers; in the banking business we usually call
816: our connection our customers. He was a French gentleman; a scientific
817: gentleman; a man of great acquirements--a Doctor."
818: 
819: "Not of Beauvais?"
820: 
821: "Why, yes, of Beauvais. Like Monsieur Manette, your father, the
822: gentleman was of Beauvais. Like Monsieur Manette, your father, the
823: gentleman was of repute in Paris. I had the honour of knowing him there.
824: Our relations were business relations, but confidential. I was at that
825: time in our French House, and had been--oh! twenty years."
826: 
827: "At that time--I may ask, at what time, sir?"
828: 
829: "I speak, miss, of twenty years ago. He married--an English lady--and
830: I was one of the trustees. His affairs, like the affairs of many other
831: French gentlemen and French families, were entirely in Tellson's hands.
832: In a similar way I am, or I have been, trustee of one kind or other for
833: scores of our customers. These are mere business relations, miss;
834: there is no friendship in them, no particular interest, nothing like
835: sentiment. I have passed from one to another, in the course of my
836: business life, just as I pass from one of our customers to another in
837: the course of my business day; in short, I have no feelings; I am a mere
838: machine. To go on--"
839: 
840: "But this is my father's story, sir; and I begin to think"--the
841: curiously roughened forehead was very intent upon him--"that when I was
842: left an orphan through my mother's surviving my father only two years,
843: it was you who brought me to England. I am almost sure it was you."
844: 
845: Mr. Lorry took the hesitating little hand that confidingly advanced
846: to take his, and he put it with some ceremony to his lips. He then
847: conducted the young lady straightway to her chair again, and, holding
848: the chair-back with his left hand, and using his right by turns to rub
849: his chin, pull his wig at the ears, or point what he said, stood looking
850: down into her face while she sat looking up into his.
851: 
852: "Miss Manette, it _was_ I. And you will see how truly I spoke of myself
853: just now, in saying I had no feelings, and that all the relations I hold
854: with my fellow-creatures are mere business relations, when you reflect
855: that I have never seen you since. No; you have been the ward of
856: Tellson's House since, and I have been busy with the other business of
857: Tellson's House since. Feelings! I have no time for them, no chance
858: of them. I pass my whole life, miss, in turning an immense pecuniary
859: Mangle."
860: 
861: After this odd description of his daily routine of employment, Mr. Lorry
862: flattened his flaxen wig upon his head with both hands (which was most
863: unnecessary, for nothing could be flatter than its shining surface was
864: before), and resumed his former attitude.
865: 
866: "So far, miss (as you have remarked), this is the story of your
867: regretted father. Now comes the difference. If your father had not died
868: when he did--Don't be frightened! How you start!"
869: 
870: She did, indeed, start. And she caught his wrist with both her hands.
871: 
872: "Pray," said Mr. Lorry, in a soothing tone, bringing his left hand from
873: the back of the chair to lay it on the supplicatory fingers that clasped
874: him in so violent a tremble: "pray control your agitation--a matter of
875: business. As I was saying--"
876: 
877: Her look so discomposed him that he stopped, wandered, and began anew:
878: 
879: "As I was saying; if Monsieur Manette had not died; if he had suddenly
880: and silently disappeared; if he had been spirited away; if it had not
881: been difficult to guess to what dreadful place, though no art could
882: trace him; if he had an enemy in some compatriot who could exercise a
883: privilege that I in my own time have known the boldest people afraid
884: to speak of in a whisper, across the water there; for instance, the
885: privilege of filling up blank forms for the consignment of any one
886: to the oblivion of a prison for any length of time; if his wife had
887: implored the king, the queen, the court, the clergy, for any tidings of
888: him, and all quite in vain;--then the history of your father would have
889: been the history of this unfortunate gentleman, the Doctor of Beauvais."
890: 
891: "I entreat you to tell me more, sir."
892: 
893: "I will. I am going to. You can bear it?"
894: 
895: "I can bear anything but the uncertainty you leave me in at this
896: moment."
897: 
898: "You speak collectedly, and you--_are_ collected. That's good!" (Though
899: his manner was less satisfied than his words.) "A matter of business.
900: Regard it as a matter of business--business that must be done. Now
901: if this doctor's wife, though a lady of great courage and spirit,
902: had suffered so intensely from this cause before her little child was
903: born--"
904: 
905: "The little child was a daughter, sir."
906: 
907: "A daughter. A-a-matter of business--don't be distressed. Miss, if the
908: poor lady had suffered so intensely before her little child was born,
909: that she came to the determination of sparing the poor child the
910: inheritance of any part of the agony she had known the pains of, by
911: rearing her in the belief that her father was dead--No, don't kneel! In
912: Heaven's name why should you kneel to me!"
913: 
914: "For the truth. O dear, good, compassionate sir, for the truth!"
915: 
916: "A--a matter of business. You confuse me, and how can I transact
917: business if I am confused? Let us be clear-headed. If you could kindly
918: mention now, for instance, what nine times ninepence are, or how many
919: shillings in twenty guineas, it would be so encouraging. I should be so
920: much more at my ease about your state of mind."
921: 
922: Without directly answering to this appeal, she sat so still when he had
923: very gently raised her, and the hands that had not ceased to clasp
924: his wrists were so much more steady than they had been, that she
925: communicated some reassurance to Mr. Jarvis Lorry.
926: 
927: "That's right, that's right. Courage! Business! You have business before
928: you; useful business. Miss Manette, your mother took this course with
929: you. And when she died--I believe broken-hearted--having never slackened
930: her unavailing search for your father, she left you, at two years old,
931: to grow to be blooming, beautiful, and happy, without the dark cloud
932: upon you of living in uncertainty whether your father soon wore his
933: heart out in prison, or wasted there through many lingering years."
934: 
935: As he said the words he looked down, with an admiring pity, on the
936: flowing golden hair; as if he pictured to himself that it might have
937: been already tinged with grey.
938: 
939: "You know that your parents had no great possession, and that what
940: they had was secured to your mother and to you. There has been no new
941: discovery, of money, or of any other property; but--"
942: 
943: He felt his wrist held closer, and he stopped. The expression in the
944: forehead, which had so particularly attracted his notice, and which was
945: now immovable, had deepened into one of pain and horror.
946: 
947: "But he has been--been found. He is alive. Greatly changed, it is too
948: probable; almost a wreck, it is possible; though we will hope the best.
949: Still, alive. Your father has been taken to the house of an old servant
950: in Paris, and we are going there: I, to identify him if I can: you, to
951: restore him to life, love, duty, rest, comfort."
952: 
953: A shiver ran through her frame, and from it through his. She said, in a
954: low, distinct, awe-stricken voice, as if she were saying it in a dream,
955: 
956: "I am going to see his Ghost! It will be his Ghost--not him!"
957: 
958: Mr. Lorry quietly chafed the hands that held his arm. "There, there,
959: there! See now, see now! The best and the worst are known to you, now.
960: You are well on your way to the poor wronged gentleman, and, with a fair
961: sea voyage, and a fair land journey, you will be soon at his dear side."
962: 
963: She repeated in the same tone, sunk to a whisper, "I have been free, I
964: have been happy, yet his Ghost has never haunted me!"
965: 
966: "Only one thing more," said Mr. Lorry, laying stress upon it as a
967: wholesome means of enforcing her attention: "he has been found under
968: another name; his own, long forgotten or long concealed. It would be
969: worse than useless now to inquire which; worse than useless to seek to
970: know whether he has been for years overlooked, or always designedly
971: held prisoner. It would be worse than useless now to make any inquiries,
972: because it would be dangerous. Better not to mention the subject,
973: anywhere or in any way, and to remove him--for a while at all
974: events--out of France. Even I, safe as an Englishman, and even
975: Tellson's, important as they are to French credit, avoid all naming of
976: the matter. I carry about me, not a scrap of writing openly referring
977: to it. This is a secret service altogether. My credentials, entries,
978: and memoranda, are all comprehended in the one line, 'Recalled to Life;'
979: which may mean anything. But what is the matter! She doesn't notice a
980: word! Miss Manette!"
981: 
982: Perfectly still and silent, and not even fallen back in her chair, she
983: sat under his hand, utterly insensible; with her eyes open and fixed
984: upon him, and with that last expression looking as if it were carved or
985: branded into her forehead. So close was her hold upon his arm, that he
986: feared to detach himself lest he should hurt her; therefore he called
987: out loudly for assistance without moving.
988: 
989: A wild-looking woman, whom even in his agitation, Mr. Lorry observed to
990: be all of a red colour, and to have red hair, and to be dressed in some
991: extraordinary tight-fitting fashion, and to have on her head a most
992: wonderful bonnet like a Grenadier wooden measure, and good measure too,
993: or a great Stilton cheese, came running into the room in advance of the
994: inn servants, and soon settled the question of his detachment from the
995: poor young lady, by laying a brawny hand upon his chest, and sending him
996: flying back against the nearest wall.
997: 
998: ("I really think this must be a man!" was Mr. Lorry's breathless
999: reflection, simultaneously with his coming against the wall.)
1000: 
1001: "Why, look at you all!" bawled this figure, addressing the inn servants.
1002: "Why don't you go and fetch things, instead of standing there staring
1003: at me? I am not so much to look at, am I? Why don't you go and fetch
1004: things? I'll let you know, if you don't bring smelling-salts, cold
1005: water, and vinegar, quick, I will."
1006: 
1007: There was an immediate dispersal for these restoratives, and she
1008: softly laid the patient on a sofa, and tended her with great skill and
1009: gentleness: calling her "my precious!" and "my bird!" and spreading her
1010: golden hair aside over her shoulders with great pride and care.
1011: 
1012: "And you in brown!" she said, indignantly turning to Mr. Lorry;
1013: "couldn't you tell her what you had to tell her, without frightening her
1014: to death? Look at her, with her pretty pale face and her cold hands. Do
1015: you call _that_ being a Banker?"
1016: 
1017: Mr. Lorry was so exceedingly disconcerted by a question so hard to
1018: answer, that he could only look on, at a distance, with much feebler
1019: sympathy and humility, while the strong woman, having banished the inn
1020: servants under the mysterious penalty of "letting them know" something
1021: not mentioned if they stayed there, staring, recovered her charge by a
1022: regular series of gradations, and coaxed her to lay her drooping head
1023: upon her shoulder.
1024: 
1025: "I hope she will do well now," said Mr. Lorry.
1026: 
1027: "No thanks to you in brown, if she does. My darling pretty!"
1028: 
1029: "I hope," said Mr. Lorry, after another pause of feeble sympathy and
1030: humility, "that you accompany Miss Manette to France?"
1031: 
1032: "A likely thing, too!" replied the strong woman. "If it was ever
1033: intended that I should go across salt water, do you suppose Providence
1034: would have cast my lot in an island?"
1035: 
1036: This being another question hard to answer, Mr. Jarvis Lorry withdrew to
1037: consider it.
1038: 
1039: 
1040: 
1041: 
1042: V. The Wine-shop
1043: 
1044: 
1045: A large cask of wine had been dropped and broken, in the street. The
1046: accident had happened in getting it out of a cart; the cask had tumbled
1047: out with a run, the hoops had burst, and it lay on the stones just
1048: outside the door of the wine-shop, shattered like a walnut-shell.
1049: 
1050: All the people within reach had suspended their business, or their
1051: idleness, to run to the spot and drink the wine. The rough, irregular
1052: stones of the street, pointing every way, and designed, one might have
1053: thought, expressly to lame all living creatures that approached them,
1054: had dammed it into little pools; these were surrounded, each by its own
1055: jostling group or crowd, according to its size. Some men kneeled down,
1056: made scoops of their two hands joined, and sipped, or tried to help
1057: women, who bent over their shoulders, to sip, before the wine had all
1058: run out between their fingers. Others, men and women, dipped in
1059: the puddles with little mugs of mutilated earthenware, or even with
1060: handkerchiefs from women's heads, which were squeezed dry into infants'
1061: mouths; others made small mud-embankments, to stem the wine as it ran;
1062: others, directed by lookers-on up at high windows, darted here and
1063: there, to cut off little streams of wine that started away in new
1064: directions; others devoted themselves to the sodden and lee-dyed
1065: pieces of the cask, licking, and even champing the moister wine-rotted
1066: fragments with eager relish. There was no drainage to carry off the
1067: wine, and not only did it all get taken up, but so much mud got taken up
1068: along with it, that there might have been a scavenger in the street,
1069: if anybody acquainted with it could have believed in such a miraculous
1070: presence.
1071: 
1072: A shrill sound of laughter and of amused voices--voices of men, women,
1073: and children--resounded in the street while this wine game lasted. There
1074: was little roughness in the sport, and much playfulness. There was a
1075: special companionship in it, an observable inclination on the part
1076: of every one to join some other one, which led, especially among the
1077: luckier or lighter-hearted, to frolicsome embraces, drinking of healths,
1078: shaking of hands, and even joining of hands and dancing, a dozen
1079: together. When the wine was gone, and the places where it had been
1080: most abundant were raked into a gridiron-pattern by fingers, these
1081: demonstrations ceased, as suddenly as they had broken out. The man who
1082: had left his saw sticking in the firewood he was cutting, set it in
1083: motion again; the women who had left on a door-step the little pot of
1084: hot ashes, at which she had been trying to soften the pain in her own
1085: starved fingers and toes, or in those of her child, returned to it; men
1086: with bare arms, matted locks, and cadaverous faces, who had emerged into
1087: the winter light from cellars, moved away, to descend again; and a gloom
1088: gathered on the scene that appeared more natural to it than sunshine.
1089: 
1090: The wine was red wine, and had stained the ground of the narrow street
1091: in the suburb of Saint Antoine, in Paris, where it was spilled. It had
1092: stained many hands, too, and many faces, and many naked feet, and many
1093: wooden shoes. The hands of the man who sawed the wood, left red marks
1094: on the billets; and the forehead of the woman who nursed her baby, was
1095: stained with the stain of the old rag she wound about her head again.
1096: Those who had been greedy with the staves of the cask, had acquired a
1097: tigerish smear about the mouth; and one tall joker so besmirched, his
1098: head more out of a long squalid bag of a nightcap than in it, scrawled
1099: upon a wall with his finger dipped in muddy wine-lees--BLOOD.
1100: 
1101: The time was to come, when that wine too would be spilled on the
1102: street-stones, and when the stain of it would be red upon many there.
1103: 
1104: And now that the cloud settled on Saint Antoine, which a momentary
1105: gleam had driven from his sacred countenance, the darkness of it was
1106: heavy--cold, dirt, sickness, ignorance, and want, were the lords in
1107: waiting on the saintly presence--nobles of great power all of them;
1108: but, most especially the last. Samples of a people that had undergone a
1109: terrible grinding and regrinding in the mill, and certainly not in the
1110: fabulous mill which ground old people young, shivered at every corner,
1111: passed in and out at every doorway, looked from every window, fluttered
1112: in every vestige of a garment that the wind shook. The mill which
1113: had worked them down, was the mill that grinds young people old; the
1114: children had ancient faces and grave voices; and upon them, and upon the
1115: grown faces, and ploughed into every furrow of age and coming up afresh,
1116: was the sigh, Hunger. It was prevalent everywhere. Hunger was pushed out
1117: of the tall houses, in the wretched clothing that hung upon poles and
1118: lines; Hunger was patched into them with straw and rag and wood and
1119: paper; Hunger was repeated in every fragment of the small modicum of
1120: firewood that the man sawed off; Hunger stared down from the smokeless
1121: chimneys, and started up from the filthy street that had no offal,
1122: among its refuse, of anything to eat. Hunger was the inscription on the
1123: baker's shelves, written in every small loaf of his scanty stock of
1124: bad bread; at the sausage-shop, in every dead-dog preparation that
1125: was offered for sale. Hunger rattled its dry bones among the roasting
1126: chestnuts in the turned cylinder; Hunger was shred into atomics in every
1127: farthing porringer of husky chips of potato, fried with some reluctant
1128: drops of oil.
1129: 
1130: Its abiding place was in all things fitted to it. A narrow winding
1131: street, full of offence and stench, with other narrow winding streets
1132: diverging, all peopled by rags and nightcaps, and all smelling of rags
1133: and nightcaps, and all visible things with a brooding look upon them
1134: that looked ill. In the hunted air of the people there was yet some
1135: wild-beast thought of the possibility of turning at bay. Depressed and
1136: slinking though they were, eyes of fire were not wanting among them; nor
1137: compressed lips, white with what they suppressed; nor foreheads knitted
1138: into the likeness of the gallows-rope they mused about enduring, or
1139: inflicting. The trade signs (and they were almost as many as the shops)
1140: were, all, grim illustrations of Want. The butcher and the porkman
1141: painted up, only the leanest scrags of meat; the baker, the coarsest of
1142: meagre loaves. The people rudely pictured as drinking in the wine-shops,
1143: croaked over their scanty measures of thin wine and beer, and were
1144: gloweringly confidential together. Nothing was represented in a
1145: flourishing condition, save tools and weapons; but, the cutler's knives
1146: and axes were sharp and bright, the smith's hammers were heavy, and the
1147: gunmaker's stock was murderous. The crippling stones of the pavement,
1148: with their many little reservoirs of mud and water, had no footways, but
1149: broke off abruptly at the doors. The kennel, to make amends, ran down
1150: the middle of the street--when it ran at all: which was only after heavy
1151: rains, and then it ran, by many eccentric fits, into the houses. Across
1152: the streets, at wide intervals, one clumsy lamp was slung by a rope and
1153: pulley; at night, when the lamplighter had let these down, and lighted,
1154: and hoisted them again, a feeble grove of dim wicks swung in a sickly
1155: manner overhead, as if they were at sea. Indeed they were at sea, and
1156: the ship and crew were in peril of tempest.
1157: 
1158: For, the time was to come, when the gaunt scarecrows of that region
1159: should have watched the lamplighter, in their idleness and hunger, so
1160: long, as to conceive the idea of improving on his method, and hauling
1161: up men by those ropes and pulleys, to flare upon the darkness of their
1162: condition. But, the time was not come yet; and every wind that blew over
1163: France shook the rags of the scarecrows in vain, for the birds, fine of
1164: song and feather, took no warning.
1165: 
1166: The wine-shop was a corner shop, better than most others in its
1167: appearance and degree, and the master of the wine-shop had stood outside
1168: it, in a yellow waistcoat and green breeches, looking on at the struggle
1169: for the lost wine. "It's not my affair," said he, with a final shrug
1170: of the shoulders. "The people from the market did it. Let them bring
1171: another."
1172: 
1173: There, his eyes happening to catch the tall joker writing up his joke,
1174: he called to him across the way:
1175: 
1176: "Say, then, my Gaspard, what do you do there?"
1177: 
1178: The fellow pointed to his joke with immense significance, as is often
1179: the way with his tribe. It missed its mark, and completely failed, as is
1180: often the way with his tribe too.
1181: 
1182: "What now? Are you a subject for the mad hospital?" said the wine-shop
1183: keeper, crossing the road, and obliterating the jest with a handful of
1184: mud, picked up for the purpose, and smeared over it. "Why do you write
1185: in the public streets? Is there--tell me thou--is there no other place
1186: to write such words in?"
1187: 
1188: In his expostulation he dropped his cleaner hand (perhaps accidentally,
1189: perhaps not) upon the joker's heart. The joker rapped it with his
1190: own, took a nimble spring upward, and came down in a fantastic dancing
1191: attitude, with one of his stained shoes jerked off his foot into his
1192: hand, and held out. A joker of an extremely, not to say wolfishly
1193: practical character, he looked, under those circumstances.
1194: 
1195: "Put it on, put it on," said the other. "Call wine, wine; and finish
1196: there." With that advice, he wiped his soiled hand upon the joker's
1197: dress, such as it was--quite deliberately, as having dirtied the hand on
1198: his account; and then recrossed the road and entered the wine-shop.
1199: 
1200: This wine-shop keeper was a bull-necked, martial-looking man of thirty,
1201: and he should have been of a hot temperament, for, although it was a
1202: bitter day, he wore no coat, but carried one slung over his shoulder.
1203: His shirt-sleeves were rolled up, too, and his brown arms were bare to
1204: the elbows. Neither did he wear anything more on his head than his own
1205: crisply-curling short dark hair. He was a dark man altogether, with good
1206: eyes and a good bold breadth between them. Good-humoured looking on
1207: the whole, but implacable-looking, too; evidently a man of a strong
1208: resolution and a set purpose; a man not desirable to be met, rushing
1209: down a narrow pass with a gulf on either side, for nothing would turn
1210: the man.
1211: 
1212: Madame Defarge, his wife, sat in the shop behind the counter as he
1213: came in. Madame Defarge was a stout woman of about his own age, with
1214: a watchful eye that seldom seemed to look at anything, a large hand
1215: heavily ringed, a steady face, strong features, and great composure of
1216: manner. There was a character about Madame Defarge, from which one might
1217: have predicated that she did not often make mistakes against herself
1218: in any of the reckonings over which she presided. Madame Defarge being
1219: sensitive to cold, was wrapped in fur, and had a quantity of bright
1220: shawl twined about her head, though not to the concealment of her large
1221: earrings. Her knitting was before her, but she had laid it down to pick
1222: her teeth with a toothpick. Thus engaged, with her right elbow supported
1223: by her left hand, Madame Defarge said nothing when her lord came in, but
1224: coughed just one grain of cough. This, in combination with the lifting
1225: of her darkly defined eyebrows over her toothpick by the breadth of a
1226: line, suggested to her husband that he would do well to look round the
1227: shop among the customers, for any new customer who had dropped in while
1228: he stepped over the way.
1229: 
1230: The wine-shop keeper accordingly rolled his eyes about, until they
1231: rested upon an elderly gentleman and a young lady, who were seated in
1232: a corner. Other company were there: two playing cards, two playing
1233: dominoes, three standing by the counter lengthening out a short supply
1234: of wine. As he passed behind the counter, he took notice that the
1235: elderly gentleman said in a look to the young lady, "This is our man."
1236: 
1237: "What the devil do _you_ do in that galley there?" said Monsieur Defarge
1238: to himself; "I don't know you."
1239: 
1240: But, he feigned not to notice the two strangers, and fell into discourse
1241: with the triumvirate of customers who were drinking at the counter.
1242: 
1243: "How goes it, Jacques?" said one of these three to Monsieur Defarge. "Is
1244: all the spilt wine swallowed?"
1245: 
1246: "Every drop, Jacques," answered Monsieur Defarge.
1247: 
1248: When this interchange of Christian name was effected, Madame Defarge,
1249: picking her teeth with her toothpick, coughed another grain of cough,
1250: and raised her eyebrows by the breadth of another line.
1251: 
1252: "It is not often," said the second of the three, addressing Monsieur
1253: Defarge, "that many of these miserable beasts know the taste of wine, or
1254: of anything but black bread and death. Is it not so, Jacques?"
1255: 
1256: "It is so, Jacques," Monsieur Defarge returned.
1257: 
1258: At this second interchange of the Christian name, Madame Defarge, still
1259: using her toothpick with profound composure, coughed another grain of
1260: cough, and raised her eyebrows by the breadth of another line.
1261: 
1262: The last of the three now said his say, as he put down his empty
1263: drinking vessel and smacked his lips.
1264: 
1265: "Ah! So much the worse! A bitter taste it is that such poor cattle
1266: always have in their mouths, and hard lives they live, Jacques. Am I
1267: right, Jacques?"
1268: 
1269: "You are right, Jacques," was the response of Monsieur Defarge.
1270: 
1271: This third interchange of the Christian name was completed at the moment
1272: when Madame Defarge put her toothpick by, kept her eyebrows up, and
1273: slightly rustled in her seat.
1274: 
1275: "Hold then! True!" muttered her husband. "Gentlemen--my wife!"
1276: 
1277: The three customers pulled off their hats to Madame Defarge, with three
1278: flourishes. She acknowledged their homage by bending her head, and
1279: giving them a quick look. Then she glanced in a casual manner round the
1280: wine-shop, took up her knitting with great apparent calmness and repose
1281: of spirit, and became absorbed in it.
1282: 
1283: "Gentlemen," said her husband, who had kept his bright eye observantly
1284: upon her, "good day. The chamber, furnished bachelor-fashion, that you
1285: wished to see, and were inquiring for when I stepped out, is on the
1286: fifth floor. The doorway of the staircase gives on the little courtyard
1287: close to the left here," pointing with his hand, "near to the window of
1288: my establishment. But, now that I remember, one of you has already been
1289: there, and can show the way. Gentlemen, adieu!"
1290: 
1291: They paid for their wine, and left the place. The eyes of Monsieur
1292: Defarge were studying his wife at her knitting when the elderly
1293: gentleman advanced from his corner, and begged the favour of a word.
1294: 
1295: "Willingly, sir," said Monsieur Defarge, and quietly stepped with him to
1296: the door.
1297: 
1298: Their conference was very short, but very decided. Almost at the first
1299: word, Monsieur Defarge started and became deeply attentive. It had
1300: not lasted a minute, when he nodded and went out. The gentleman then
1301: beckoned to the young lady, and they, too, went out. Madame Defarge
1302: knitted with nimble fingers and steady eyebrows, and saw nothing.
1303: 
1304: Mr. Jarvis Lorry and Miss Manette, emerging from the wine-shop thus,
1305: joined Monsieur Defarge in the doorway to which he had directed his own
1306: company just before. It opened from a stinking little black courtyard,
1307: and was the general public entrance to a great pile of houses, inhabited
1308: by a great number of people. In the gloomy tile-paved entry to the
1309: gloomy tile-paved staircase, Monsieur Defarge bent down on one knee
1310: to the child of his old master, and put her hand to his lips. It was
1311: a gentle action, but not at all gently done; a very remarkable
1312: transformation had come over him in a few seconds. He had no good-humour
1313: in his face, nor any openness of aspect left, but had become a secret,
1314: angry, dangerous man.
1315: 
1316: "It is very high; it is a little difficult. Better to begin slowly."
1317: Thus, Monsieur Defarge, in a stern voice, to Mr. Lorry, as they began
1318: ascending the stairs.
1319: 
1320: "Is he alone?" the latter whispered.
1321: 
1322: "Alone! God help him, who should be with him!" said the other, in the
1323: same low voice.
1324: 
1325: "Is he always alone, then?"
1326: 
1327: "Yes."
1328: 
1329: "Of his own desire?"
1330: 
1331: "Of his own necessity. As he was, when I first saw him after they
1332: found me and demanded to know if I would take him, and, at my peril be
1333: discreet--as he was then, so he is now."
1334: 
1335: "He is greatly changed?"
1336: 
1337: "Changed!"
1338: 
1339: The keeper of the wine-shop stopped to strike the wall with his hand,
1340: and mutter a tremendous curse. No direct answer could have been half so
1341: forcible. Mr. Lorry's spirits grew heavier and heavier, as he and his
1342: two companions ascended higher and higher.
1343: 
1344: Such a staircase, with its accessories, in the older and more crowded
1345: parts of Paris, would be bad enough now; but, at that time, it was vile
1346: indeed to unaccustomed and unhardened senses. Every little habitation
1347: within the great foul nest of one high building--that is to say,
1348: the room or rooms within every door that opened on the general
1349: staircase--left its own heap of refuse on its own landing, besides
1350: flinging other refuse from its own windows. The uncontrollable and
1351: hopeless mass of decomposition so engendered, would have polluted
1352: the air, even if poverty and deprivation had not loaded it with their
1353: intangible impurities; the two bad sources combined made it almost
1354: insupportable. Through such an atmosphere, by a steep dark shaft of dirt
1355: and poison, the way lay. Yielding to his own disturbance of mind, and to
1356: his young companion's agitation, which became greater every instant, Mr.
1357: Jarvis Lorry twice stopped to rest. Each of these stoppages was made
1358: at a doleful grating, by which any languishing good airs that were left
1359: uncorrupted, seemed to escape, and all spoilt and sickly vapours seemed
1360: to crawl in. Through the rusted bars, tastes, rather than glimpses, were
1361: caught of the jumbled neighbourhood; and nothing within range, nearer
1362: or lower than the summits of the two great towers of Notre-Dame, had any
1363: promise on it of healthy life or wholesome aspirations.
1364: 
1365: At last, the top of the staircase was gained, and they stopped for the
1366: third time. There was yet an upper staircase, of a steeper inclination
1367: and of contracted dimensions, to be ascended, before the garret story
1368: was reached. The keeper of the wine-shop, always going a little in
1369: advance, and always going on the side which Mr. Lorry took, as though he
1370: dreaded to be asked any question by the young lady, turned himself about
1371: here, and, carefully feeling in the pockets of the coat he carried over
1372: his shoulder, took out a key.
1373: 
1374: "The door is locked then, my friend?" said Mr. Lorry, surprised.
1375: 
1376: "Ay. Yes," was the grim reply of Monsieur Defarge.
1377: 
1378: "You think it necessary to keep the unfortunate gentleman so retired?"
1379: 
1380: "I think it necessary to turn the key." Monsieur Defarge whispered it
1381: closer in his ear, and frowned heavily.
1382: 
1383: "Why?"
1384: 
1385: "Why! Because he has lived so long, locked up, that he would be
1386: frightened--rave--tear himself to pieces--die--come to I know not what
1387: harm--if his door was left open."
1388: 
1389: "Is it possible!" exclaimed Mr. Lorry.
1390: 
1391: "Is it possible!" repeated Defarge, bitterly. "Yes. And a beautiful
1392: world we live in, when it _is_ possible, and when many other such things
1393: are possible, and not only possible, but done--done, see you!--under
1394: that sky there, every day. Long live the Devil. Let us go on."
1395: 
1396: This dialogue had been held in so very low a whisper, that not a word
1397: of it had reached the young lady's ears. But, by this time she trembled
1398: under such strong emotion, and her face expressed such deep anxiety,
1399: and, above all, such dread and terror, that Mr. Lorry felt it incumbent
1400: on him to speak a word or two of reassurance.
1401: 
1402: "Courage, dear miss! Courage! Business! The worst will be over in a
1403: moment; it is but passing the room-door, and the worst is over. Then,
1404: all the good you bring to him, all the relief, all the happiness you
1405: bring to him, begin. Let our good friend here, assist you on that side.
1406: That's well, friend Defarge. Come, now. Business, business!"
1407: 
1408: They went up slowly and softly. The staircase was short, and they were
1409: soon at the top. There, as it had an abrupt turn in it, they came all at
1410: once in sight of three men, whose heads were bent down close together at
1411: the side of a door, and who were intently looking into the room to which
1412: the door belonged, through some chinks or holes in the wall. On hearing
1413: footsteps close at hand, these three turned, and rose, and showed
1414: themselves to be the three of one name who had been drinking in the
1415: wine-shop.
1416: 
1417: "I forgot them in the surprise of your visit," explained Monsieur
1418: Defarge. "Leave us, good boys; we have business here."
1419: 
1420: The three glided by, and went silently down.
1421: 
1422: There appearing to be no other door on that floor, and the keeper of
1423: the wine-shop going straight to this one when they were left alone, Mr.
1424: Lorry asked him in a whisper, with a little anger:
1425: 
1426: "Do you make a show of Monsieur Manette?"
1427: 
1428: "I show him, in the way you have seen, to a chosen few."
1429: 
1430: "Is that well?"
1431: 
1432: "_I_ think it is well."
1433: 
1434: "Who are the few? How do you choose them?"
1435: 
1436: "I choose them as real men, of my name--Jacques is my name--to whom the
