1437: sight is likely to do good. Enough; you are English; that is another
1438: thing. Stay there, if you please, a little moment."
1439: 
1440: With an admonitory gesture to keep them back, he stooped, and looked in
1441: through the crevice in the wall. Soon raising his head again, he struck
1442: twice or thrice upon the door--evidently with no other object than to
1443: make a noise there. With the same intention, he drew the key across it,
1444: three or four times, before he put it clumsily into the lock, and turned
1445: it as heavily as he could.
1446: 
1447: The door slowly opened inward under his hand, and he looked into the
1448: room and said something. A faint voice answered something. Little more
1449: than a single syllable could have been spoken on either side.
1450: 
1451: He looked back over his shoulder, and beckoned them to enter. Mr. Lorry
1452: got his arm securely round the daughter's waist, and held her; for he
1453: felt that she was sinking.
1454: 
1455: "A-a-a-business, business!" he urged, with a moisture that was not of
1456: business shining on his cheek. "Come in, come in!"
1457: 
1458: "I am afraid of it," she answered, shuddering.
1459: 
1460: "Of it? What?"
1461: 
1462: "I mean of him. Of my father."
1463: 
1464: Rendered in a manner desperate, by her state and by the beckoning of
1465: their conductor, he drew over his neck the arm that shook upon his
1466: shoulder, lifted her a little, and hurried her into the room. He sat her
1467: down just within the door, and held her, clinging to him.
1468: 
1469: Defarge drew out the key, closed the door, locked it on the inside,
1470: took out the key again, and held it in his hand. All this he did,
1471: methodically, and with as loud and harsh an accompaniment of noise as he
1472: could make. Finally, he walked across the room with a measured tread to
1473: where the window was. He stopped there, and faced round.
1474: 
1475: The garret, built to be a depository for firewood and the like, was dim
1476: and dark: for, the window of dormer shape, was in truth a door in the
1477: roof, with a little crane over it for the hoisting up of stores from
1478: the street: unglazed, and closing up the middle in two pieces, like any
1479: other door of French construction. To exclude the cold, one half of this
1480: door was fast closed, and the other was opened but a very little way.
1481: Such a scanty portion of light was admitted through these means, that it
1482: was difficult, on first coming in, to see anything; and long habit
1483: alone could have slowly formed in any one, the ability to do any work
1484: requiring nicety in such obscurity. Yet, work of that kind was being
1485: done in the garret; for, with his back towards the door, and his face
1486: towards the window where the keeper of the wine-shop stood looking at
1487: him, a white-haired man sat on a low bench, stooping forward and very
1488: busy, making shoes.
1489: 
1490: 
1491: 
1492: 
1493: VI. The Shoemaker
1494: 
1495: 
1496: "Good day!" said Monsieur Defarge, looking down at the white head that
1497: bent low over the shoemaking.
1498: 
1499: It was raised for a moment, and a very faint voice responded to the
1500: salutation, as if it were at a distance:
1501: 
1502: "Good day!"
1503: 
1504: "You are still hard at work, I see?"
1505: 
1506: After a long silence, the head was lifted for another moment, and the
1507: voice replied, "Yes--I am working." This time, a pair of haggard eyes
1508: had looked at the questioner, before the face had dropped again.
1509: 
1510: The faintness of the voice was pitiable and dreadful. It was not the
1511: faintness of physical weakness, though confinement and hard fare no
1512: doubt had their part in it. Its deplorable peculiarity was, that it was
1513: the faintness of solitude and disuse. It was like the last feeble echo
1514: of a sound made long and long ago. So entirely had it lost the life and
1515: resonance of the human voice, that it affected the senses like a once
1516: beautiful colour faded away into a poor weak stain. So sunken and
1517: suppressed it was, that it was like a voice underground. So expressive
1518: it was, of a hopeless and lost creature, that a famished traveller,
1519: wearied out by lonely wandering in a wilderness, would have remembered
1520: home and friends in such a tone before lying down to die.
1521: 
1522: Some minutes of silent work had passed: and the haggard eyes had looked
1523: up again: not with any interest or curiosity, but with a dull mechanical
1524: perception, beforehand, that the spot where the only visitor they were
1525: aware of had stood, was not yet empty.
1526: 
1527: "I want," said Defarge, who had not removed his gaze from the shoemaker,
1528: "to let in a little more light here. You can bear a little more?"
1529: 
1530: The shoemaker stopped his work; looked with a vacant air of listening,
1531: at the floor on one side of him; then similarly, at the floor on the
1532: other side of him; then, upward at the speaker.
1533: 
1534: "What did you say?"
1535: 
1536: "You can bear a little more light?"
1537: 
1538: "I must bear it, if you let it in." (Laying the palest shadow of a
1539: stress upon the second word.)
1540: 
1541: The opened half-door was opened a little further, and secured at that
1542: angle for the time. A broad ray of light fell into the garret, and
1543: showed the workman with an unfinished shoe upon his lap, pausing in his
1544: labour. His few common tools and various scraps of leather were at his
1545: feet and on his bench. He had a white beard, raggedly cut, but not very
1546: long, a hollow face, and exceedingly bright eyes. The hollowness and
1547: thinness of his face would have caused them to look large, under his yet
1548: dark eyebrows and his confused white hair, though they had been really
1549: otherwise; but, they were naturally large, and looked unnaturally so.
1550: His yellow rags of shirt lay open at the throat, and showed his body
1551: to be withered and worn. He, and his old canvas frock, and his loose
1552: stockings, and all his poor tatters of clothes, had, in a long seclusion
1553: from direct light and air, faded down to such a dull uniformity of
1554: parchment-yellow, that it would have been hard to say which was which.
1555: 
1556: He had put up a hand between his eyes and the light, and the very bones
1557: of it seemed transparent. So he sat, with a steadfastly vacant gaze,
1558: pausing in his work. He never looked at the figure before him, without
1559: first looking down on this side of himself, then on that, as if he had
1560: lost the habit of associating place with sound; he never spoke, without
1561: first wandering in this manner, and forgetting to speak.
1562: 
1563: "Are you going to finish that pair of shoes to-day?" asked Defarge,
1564: motioning to Mr. Lorry to come forward.
1565: 
1566: "What did you say?"
1567: 
1568: "Do you mean to finish that pair of shoes to-day?"
1569: 
1570: "I can't say that I mean to. I suppose so. I don't know."
1571: 
1572: But, the question reminded him of his work, and he bent over it again.
1573: 
1574: Mr. Lorry came silently forward, leaving the daughter by the door. When
1575: he had stood, for a minute or two, by the side of Defarge, the shoemaker
1576: looked up. He showed no surprise at seeing another figure, but the
1577: unsteady fingers of one of his hands strayed to his lips as he looked at
1578: it (his lips and his nails were of the same pale lead-colour), and then
1579: the hand dropped to his work, and he once more bent over the shoe. The
1580: look and the action had occupied but an instant.
1581: 
1582: "You have a visitor, you see," said Monsieur Defarge.
1583: 
1584: "What did you say?"
1585: 
1586: "Here is a visitor."
1587: 
1588: The shoemaker looked up as before, but without removing a hand from his
1589: work.
1590: 
1591: "Come!" said Defarge. "Here is monsieur, who knows a well-made shoe when
1592: he sees one. Show him that shoe you are working at. Take it, monsieur."
1593: 
1594: Mr. Lorry took it in his hand.
1595: 
1596: "Tell monsieur what kind of shoe it is, and the maker's name."
1597: 
1598: There was a longer pause than usual, before the shoemaker replied:
1599: 
1600: "I forget what it was you asked me. What did you say?"
1601: 
1602: "I said, couldn't you describe the kind of shoe, for monsieur's
1603: information?"
1604: 
1605: "It is a lady's shoe. It is a young lady's walking-shoe. It is in the
1606: present mode. I never saw the mode. I have had a pattern in my hand." He
1607: glanced at the shoe with some little passing touch of pride.
1608: 
1609: "And the maker's name?" said Defarge.
1610: 
1611: Now that he had no work to hold, he laid the knuckles of the right hand
1612: in the hollow of the left, and then the knuckles of the left hand in the
1613: hollow of the right, and then passed a hand across his bearded chin, and
1614: so on in regular changes, without a moment's intermission. The task of
1615: recalling him from the vagrancy into which he always sank when he
1616: had spoken, was like recalling some very weak person from a swoon, or
1617: endeavouring, in the hope of some disclosure, to stay the spirit of a
1618: fast-dying man.
1619: 
1620: "Did you ask me for my name?"
1621: 
1622: "Assuredly I did."
1623: 
1624: "One Hundred and Five, North Tower."
1625: 
1626: "Is that all?"
1627: 
1628: "One Hundred and Five, North Tower."
1629: 
1630: With a weary sound that was not a sigh, nor a groan, he bent to work
1631: again, until the silence was again broken.
1632: 
1633: "You are not a shoemaker by trade?" said Mr. Lorry, looking steadfastly
1634: at him.
1635: 
1636: His haggard eyes turned to Defarge as if he would have transferred the
1637: question to him: but as no help came from that quarter, they turned back
1638: on the questioner when they had sought the ground.
1639: 
1640: "I am not a shoemaker by trade? No, I was not a shoemaker by trade. I-I
1641: learnt it here. I taught myself. I asked leave to--"
1642: 
1643: He lapsed away, even for minutes, ringing those measured changes on his
1644: hands the whole time. His eyes came slowly back, at last, to the face
1645: from which they had wandered; when they rested on it, he started, and
1646: resumed, in the manner of a sleeper that moment awake, reverting to a
1647: subject of last night.
1648: 
1649: "I asked leave to teach myself, and I got it with much difficulty after
1650: a long while, and I have made shoes ever since."
1651: 
1652: As he held out his hand for the shoe that had been taken from him, Mr.
1653: Lorry said, still looking steadfastly in his face:
1654: 
1655: "Monsieur Manette, do you remember nothing of me?"
1656: 
1657: The shoe dropped to the ground, and he sat looking fixedly at the
1658: questioner.
1659: 
1660: "Monsieur Manette"; Mr. Lorry laid his hand upon Defarge's arm; "do you
1661: remember nothing of this man? Look at him. Look at me. Is there no old
1662: banker, no old business, no old servant, no old time, rising in your
1663: mind, Monsieur Manette?"
1664: 
1665: As the captive of many years sat looking fixedly, by turns, at Mr.
1666: Lorry and at Defarge, some long obliterated marks of an actively intent
1667: intelligence in the middle of the forehead, gradually forced themselves
1668: through the black mist that had fallen on him. They were overclouded
1669: again, they were fainter, they were gone; but they had been there. And
1670: so exactly was the expression repeated on the fair young face of her who
1671: had crept along the wall to a point where she could see him, and where
1672: she now stood looking at him, with hands which at first had been only
1673: raised in frightened compassion, if not even to keep him off and
1674: shut out the sight of him, but which were now extending towards him,
1675: trembling with eagerness to lay the spectral face upon her warm young
1676: breast, and love it back to life and hope--so exactly was the expression
1677: repeated (though in stronger characters) on her fair young face, that it
1678: looked as though it had passed like a moving light, from him to her.
1679: 
1680: Darkness had fallen on him in its place. He looked at the two, less and
1681: less attentively, and his eyes in gloomy abstraction sought the ground
1682: and looked about him in the old way. Finally, with a deep long sigh, he
1683: took the shoe up, and resumed his work.
1684: 
1685: "Have you recognised him, monsieur?" asked Defarge in a whisper.
1686: 
1687: "Yes; for a moment. At first I thought it quite hopeless, but I have
1688: unquestionably seen, for a single moment, the face that I once knew so
1689: well. Hush! Let us draw further back. Hush!"
1690: 
1691: She had moved from the wall of the garret, very near to the bench on
1692: which he sat. There was something awful in his unconsciousness of the
1693: figure that could have put out its hand and touched him as he stooped
1694: over his labour.
1695: 
1696: Not a word was spoken, not a sound was made. She stood, like a spirit,
1697: beside him, and he bent over his work.
1698: 
1699: It happened, at length, that he had occasion to change the instrument
1700: in his hand, for his shoemaker's knife. It lay on that side of him
1701: which was not the side on which she stood. He had taken it up, and was
1702: stooping to work again, when his eyes caught the skirt of her dress. He
1703: raised them, and saw her face. The two spectators started forward,
1704: but she stayed them with a motion of her hand. She had no fear of his
1705: striking at her with the knife, though they had.
1706: 
1707: He stared at her with a fearful look, and after a while his lips began
1708: to form some words, though no sound proceeded from them. By degrees, in
1709: the pauses of his quick and laboured breathing, he was heard to say:
1710: 
1711: "What is this?"
1712: 
1713: With the tears streaming down her face, she put her two hands to her
1714: lips, and kissed them to him; then clasped them on her breast, as if she
1715: laid his ruined head there.
1716: 
1717: "You are not the gaoler's daughter?"
1718: 
1719: She sighed "No."
1720: 
1721: "Who are you?"
1722: 
1723: Not yet trusting the tones of her voice, she sat down on the bench
1724: beside him. He recoiled, but she laid her hand upon his arm. A strange
1725: thrill struck him when she did so, and visibly passed over his frame; he
1726: laid the knife down softly, as he sat staring at her.
1727: 
1728: Her golden hair, which she wore in long curls, had been hurriedly pushed
1729: aside, and fell down over her neck. Advancing his hand by little and
1730: little, he took it up and looked at it. In the midst of the action
1731: he went astray, and, with another deep sigh, fell to work at his
1732: shoemaking.
1733: 
1734: But not for long. Releasing his arm, she laid her hand upon his
1735: shoulder. After looking doubtfully at it, two or three times, as if to
1736: be sure that it was really there, he laid down his work, put his hand
1737: to his neck, and took off a blackened string with a scrap of folded rag
1738: attached to it. He opened this, carefully, on his knee, and it contained
1739: a very little quantity of hair: not more than one or two long golden
1740: hairs, which he had, in some old day, wound off upon his finger.
1741: 
1742: He took her hair into his hand again, and looked closely at it. "It is
1743: the same. How can it be! When was it! How was it!"
1744: 
1745: As the concentrated expression returned to his forehead, he seemed to
1746: become conscious that it was in hers too. He turned her full to the
1747: light, and looked at her.
1748: 
1749: "She had laid her head upon my shoulder, that night when I was summoned
1750: out--she had a fear of my going, though I had none--and when I was
1751: brought to the North Tower they found these upon my sleeve. 'You will
1752: leave me them? They can never help me to escape in the body, though they
1753: may in the spirit.' Those were the words I said. I remember them very
1754: well."
1755: 
1756: He formed this speech with his lips many times before he could utter it.
1757: But when he did find spoken words for it, they came to him coherently,
1758: though slowly.
1759: 
1760: "How was this?--_Was it you_?"
1761: 
1762: Once more, the two spectators started, as he turned upon her with a
1763: frightful suddenness. But she sat perfectly still in his grasp, and only
1764: said, in a low voice, "I entreat you, good gentlemen, do not come near
1765: us, do not speak, do not move!"
1766: 
1767: "Hark!" he exclaimed. "Whose voice was that?"
1768: 
1769: His hands released her as he uttered this cry, and went up to his white
1770: hair, which they tore in a frenzy. It died out, as everything but his
1771: shoemaking did die out of him, and he refolded his little packet and
1772: tried to secure it in his breast; but he still looked at her, and
1773: gloomily shook his head.
1774: 
1775: "No, no, no; you are too young, too blooming. It can't be. See what the
1776: prisoner is. These are not the hands she knew, this is not the face
1777: she knew, this is not a voice she ever heard. No, no. She was--and He
1778: was--before the slow years of the North Tower--ages ago. What is your
1779: name, my gentle angel?"
1780: 
1781: Hailing his softened tone and manner, his daughter fell upon her knees
1782: before him, with her appealing hands upon his breast.
1783: 
1784: "O, sir, at another time you shall know my name, and who my mother was,
1785: and who my father, and how I never knew their hard, hard history. But I
1786: cannot tell you at this time, and I cannot tell you here. All that I may
1787: tell you, here and now, is, that I pray to you to touch me and to bless
1788: me. Kiss me, kiss me! O my dear, my dear!"
1789: 
1790: His cold white head mingled with her radiant hair, which warmed and
1791: lighted it as though it were the light of Freedom shining on him.
1792: 
1793: "If you hear in my voice--I don't know that it is so, but I hope it
1794: is--if you hear in my voice any resemblance to a voice that once was
1795: sweet music in your ears, weep for it, weep for it! If you touch, in
1796: touching my hair, anything that recalls a beloved head that lay on your
1797: breast when you were young and free, weep for it, weep for it! If, when
1798: I hint to you of a Home that is before us, where I will be true to you
1799: with all my duty and with all my faithful service, I bring back the
1800: remembrance of a Home long desolate, while your poor heart pined away,
1801: weep for it, weep for it!"
1802: 
1803: She held him closer round the neck, and rocked him on her breast like a
1804: child.
1805: 
1806: "If, when I tell you, dearest dear, that your agony is over, and that I
1807: have come here to take you from it, and that we go to England to be at
1808: peace and at rest, I cause you to think of your useful life laid waste,
1809: and of our native France so wicked to you, weep for it, weep for it! And
1810: if, when I shall tell you of my name, and of my father who is living,
1811: and of my mother who is dead, you learn that I have to kneel to my
1812: honoured father, and implore his pardon for having never for his sake
1813: striven all day and lain awake and wept all night, because the love of
1814: my poor mother hid his torture from me, weep for it, weep for it! Weep
1815: for her, then, and for me! Good gentlemen, thank God! I feel his sacred
1816: tears upon my face, and his sobs strike against my heart. O, see! Thank
1817: God for us, thank God!"
1818: 
1819: He had sunk in her arms, and his face dropped on her breast: a sight so
1820: touching, yet so terrible in the tremendous wrong and suffering which
1821: had gone before it, that the two beholders covered their faces.
1822: 
1823: When the quiet of the garret had been long undisturbed, and his heaving
1824: breast and shaken form had long yielded to the calm that must follow all
1825: storms--emblem to humanity, of the rest and silence into which the storm
1826: called Life must hush at last--they came forward to raise the father and
1827: daughter from the ground. He had gradually dropped to the floor, and lay
1828: there in a lethargy, worn out. She had nestled down with him, that his
1829: head might lie upon her arm; and her hair drooping over him curtained
1830: him from the light.
1831: 
1832: "If, without disturbing him," she said, raising her hand to Mr. Lorry as
1833: he stooped over them, after repeated blowings of his nose, "all could be
1834: arranged for our leaving Paris at once, so that, from the very door, he
1835: could be taken away--"
1836: 
1837: "But, consider. Is he fit for the journey?" asked Mr. Lorry.
1838: 
1839: "More fit for that, I think, than to remain in this city, so dreadful to
1840: him."
1841: 
1842: "It is true," said Defarge, who was kneeling to look on and hear. "More
1843: than that; Monsieur Manette is, for all reasons, best out of France.
1844: Say, shall I hire a carriage and post-horses?"
1845: 
1846: "That's business," said Mr. Lorry, resuming on the shortest notice his
1847: methodical manners; "and if business is to be done, I had better do it."
1848: 
1849: "Then be so kind," urged Miss Manette, "as to leave us here. You see how
1850: composed he has become, and you cannot be afraid to leave him with me
1851: now. Why should you be? If you will lock the door to secure us from
1852: interruption, I do not doubt that you will find him, when you come back,
1853: as quiet as you leave him. In any case, I will take care of him until
1854: you return, and then we will remove him straight."
1855: 
1856: Both Mr. Lorry and Defarge were rather disinclined to this course, and
1857: in favour of one of them remaining. But, as there were not only carriage
1858: and horses to be seen to, but travelling papers; and as time pressed,
1859: for the day was drawing to an end, it came at last to their hastily
1860: dividing the business that was necessary to be done, and hurrying away
1861: to do it.
1862: 
1863: Then, as the darkness closed in, the daughter laid her head down on the
1864: hard ground close at the father's side, and watched him. The darkness
1865: deepened and deepened, and they both lay quiet, until a light gleamed
1866: through the chinks in the wall.
1867: 
1868: Mr. Lorry and Monsieur Defarge had made all ready for the journey, and
1869: had brought with them, besides travelling cloaks and wrappers, bread and
1870: meat, wine, and hot coffee. Monsieur Defarge put this provender, and the
1871: lamp he carried, on the shoemaker's bench (there was nothing else in the
1872: garret but a pallet bed), and he and Mr. Lorry roused the captive, and
1873: assisted him to his feet.
1874: 
1875: No human intelligence could have read the mysteries of his mind, in
1876: the scared blank wonder of his face. Whether he knew what had happened,
1877: whether he recollected what they had said to him, whether he knew that
1878: he was free, were questions which no sagacity could have solved. They
1879: tried speaking to him; but, he was so confused, and so very slow to
1880: answer, that they took fright at his bewilderment, and agreed for
1881: the time to tamper with him no more. He had a wild, lost manner of
1882: occasionally clasping his head in his hands, that had not been seen
1883: in him before; yet, he had some pleasure in the mere sound of his
1884: daughter's voice, and invariably turned to it when she spoke.
1885: 
1886: In the submissive way of one long accustomed to obey under coercion, he
1887: ate and drank what they gave him to eat and drink, and put on the cloak
1888: and other wrappings, that they gave him to wear. He readily responded to
1889: his daughter's drawing her arm through his, and took--and kept--her hand
1890: in both his own.
1891: 
1892: They began to descend; Monsieur Defarge going first with the lamp, Mr.
1893: Lorry closing the little procession. They had not traversed many steps
1894: of the long main staircase when he stopped, and stared at the roof and
1895: round at the walls.
1896: 
1897: "You remember the place, my father? You remember coming up here?"
1898: 
1899: "What did you say?"
1900: 
1901: But, before she could repeat the question, he murmured an answer as if
1902: she had repeated it.
1903: 
1904: "Remember? No, I don't remember. It was so very long ago."
1905: 
1906: That he had no recollection whatever of his having been brought from his
1907: prison to that house, was apparent to them. They heard him mutter,
1908: "One Hundred and Five, North Tower;" and when he looked about him, it
1909: evidently was for the strong fortress-walls which had long encompassed
1910: him. On their reaching the courtyard he instinctively altered his
1911: tread, as being in expectation of a drawbridge; and when there was
1912: no drawbridge, and he saw the carriage waiting in the open street, he
1913: dropped his daughter's hand and clasped his head again.
1914: 
1915: No crowd was about the door; no people were discernible at any of the
1916: many windows; not even a chance passerby was in the street. An unnatural
1917: silence and desertion reigned there. Only one soul was to be seen, and
1918: that was Madame Defarge--who leaned against the door-post, knitting, and
1919: saw nothing.
1920: 
1921: The prisoner had got into a coach, and his daughter had followed
1922: him, when Mr. Lorry's feet were arrested on the step by his asking,
1923: miserably, for his shoemaking tools and the unfinished shoes. Madame
1924: Defarge immediately called to her husband that she would get them, and
1925: went, knitting, out of the lamplight, through the courtyard. She quickly
1926: brought them down and handed them in;--and immediately afterwards leaned
1927: against the door-post, knitting, and saw nothing.
1928: 
1929: Defarge got upon the box, and gave the word "To the Barrier!" The
1930: postilion cracked his whip, and they clattered away under the feeble
1931: over-swinging lamps.
1932: 
1933: Under the over-swinging lamps--swinging ever brighter in the better
1934: streets, and ever dimmer in the worse--and by lighted shops, gay crowds,
1935: illuminated coffee-houses, and theatre-doors, to one of the city
1936: gates. Soldiers with lanterns, at the guard-house there. "Your papers,
1937: travellers!" "See here then, Monsieur the Officer," said Defarge,
1938: getting down, and taking him gravely apart, "these are the papers of
1939: monsieur inside, with the white head. They were consigned to me, with
1940: him, at the--" He dropped his voice, there was a flutter among the
1941: military lanterns, and one of them being handed into the coach by an arm
1942: in uniform, the eyes connected with the arm looked, not an every day
1943: or an every night look, at monsieur with the white head. "It is well.
1944: Forward!" from the uniform. "Adieu!" from Defarge. And so, under a short
1945: grove of feebler and feebler over-swinging lamps, out under the great
1946: grove of stars.
1947: 
1948: Beneath that arch of unmoved and eternal lights; some, so remote from
1949: this little earth that the learned tell us it is doubtful whether their
1950: rays have even yet discovered it, as a point in space where anything
1951: is suffered or done: the shadows of the night were broad and black.
1952: All through the cold and restless interval, until dawn, they once more
1953: whispered in the ears of Mr. Jarvis Lorry--sitting opposite the buried
1954: man who had been dug out, and wondering what subtle powers were for ever
1955: lost to him, and what were capable of restoration--the old inquiry:
1956: 
1957: "I hope you care to be recalled to life?"
1958: 
1959: And the old answer:
1960: 
1961: "I can't say."
1962: 
1963: 
1964: The end of the first book.
1965: 
1966: 
1967: 
1968: 
1969: 
1970: Book the Second--the Golden Thread
1971: 
1972: 
1973: 
1974: 
1975: I. Five Years Later
1976: 
1977: 
1978: Tellson's Bank by Temple Bar was an old-fashioned place, even in the
1979: year one thousand seven hundred and eighty. It was very small, very
1980: dark, very ugly, very incommodious. It was an old-fashioned place,
1981: moreover, in the moral attribute that the partners in the House were
1982: proud of its smallness, proud of its darkness, proud of its ugliness,
1983: proud of its incommodiousness. They were even boastful of its eminence
1984: in those particulars, and were fired by an express conviction that, if
1985: it were less objectionable, it would be less respectable. This was
1986: no passive belief, but an active weapon which they flashed at more
1987: convenient places of business. Tellson's (they said) wanted
1988: no elbow-room, Tellson's wanted no light, Tellson's wanted no
1989: embellishment. Noakes and Co.'s might, or Snooks Brothers' might; but
1990: Tellson's, thank Heaven--!
1991: 
1992: Any one of these partners would have disinherited his son on the
1993: question of rebuilding Tellson's. In this respect the House was much
1994: on a par with the Country; which did very often disinherit its sons for
1995: suggesting improvements in laws and customs that had long been highly
1996: objectionable, but were only the more respectable.
1997: 
1998: Thus it had come to pass, that Tellson's was the triumphant perfection
1999: of inconvenience. After bursting open a door of idiotic obstinacy with
2000: a weak rattle in its throat, you fell into Tellson's down two steps,
2001: and came to your senses in a miserable little shop, with two little
2002: counters, where the oldest of men made your cheque shake as if the
2003: wind rustled it, while they examined the signature by the dingiest of
2004: windows, which were always under a shower-bath of mud from Fleet-street,
2005: and which were made the dingier by their own iron bars proper, and the
2006: heavy shadow of Temple Bar. If your business necessitated your seeing
2007: "the House," you were put into a species of Condemned Hold at the back,
2008: where you meditated on a misspent life, until the House came with its
2009: hands in its pockets, and you could hardly blink at it in the dismal
2010: twilight. Your money came out of, or went into, wormy old wooden
2011: drawers, particles of which flew up your nose and down your throat when
2012: they were opened and shut. Your bank-notes had a musty odour, as if they
2013: were fast decomposing into rags again. Your plate was stowed away among
2014: the neighbouring cesspools, and evil communications corrupted its good
2015: polish in a day or two. Your deeds got into extemporised strong-rooms
2016: made of kitchens and sculleries, and fretted all the fat out of their
2017: parchments into the banking-house air. Your lighter boxes of family
2018: papers went up-stairs into a Barmecide room, that always had a great
2019: dining-table in it and never had a dinner, and where, even in the year
2020: one thousand seven hundred and eighty, the first letters written to you
2021: by your old love, or by your little children, were but newly released
2022: from the horror of being ogled through the windows, by the heads
2023: exposed on Temple Bar with an insensate brutality and ferocity worthy of
2024: Abyssinia or Ashantee.
2025: 
2026: But indeed, at that time, putting to death was a recipe much in vogue
2027: with all trades and professions, and not least of all with Tellson's.
2028: Death is Nature's remedy for all things, and why not Legislation's?
2029: Accordingly, the forger was put to Death; the utterer of a bad note
2030: was put to Death; the unlawful opener of a letter was put to Death; the
2031: purloiner of forty shillings and sixpence was put to Death; the holder
2032: of a horse at Tellson's door, who made off with it, was put to
2033: Death; the coiner of a bad shilling was put to Death; the sounders of
2034: three-fourths of the notes in the whole gamut of Crime, were put to
2035: Death. Not that it did the least good in the way of prevention--it
2036: might almost have been worth remarking that the fact was exactly the
2037: reverse--but, it cleared off (as to this world) the trouble of each
2038: particular case, and left nothing else connected with it to be looked
2039: after. Thus, Tellson's, in its day, like greater places of business,
2040: its contemporaries, had taken so many lives, that, if the heads laid
2041: low before it had been ranged on Temple Bar instead of being privately
2042: disposed of, they would probably have excluded what little light the
2043: ground floor had, in a rather significant manner.
2044: 
2045: Cramped in all kinds of dim cupboards and hutches at Tellson's, the
2046: oldest of men carried on the business gravely. When they took a young
2047: man into Tellson's London house, they hid him somewhere till he was
2048: old. They kept him in a dark place, like a cheese, until he had the full
2049: Tellson flavour and blue-mould upon him. Then only was he permitted to
2050: be seen, spectacularly poring over large books, and casting his breeches
2051: and gaiters into the general weight of the establishment.
2052: 
2053: Outside Tellson's--never by any means in it, unless called in--was an
2054: odd-job-man, an occasional porter and messenger, who served as the live
2055: sign of the house. He was never absent during business hours, unless
2056: upon an errand, and then he was represented by his son: a grisly urchin
2057: of twelve, who was his express image. People understood that Tellson's,
2058: in a stately way, tolerated the odd-job-man. The house had always
2059: tolerated some person in that capacity, and time and tide had drifted
2060: this person to the post. His surname was Cruncher, and on the youthful
2061: occasion of his renouncing by proxy the works of darkness, in the
2062: easterly parish church of Hounsditch, he had received the added
2063: appellation of Jerry.
2064: 
2065: The scene was Mr. Cruncher's private lodging in Hanging-sword-alley,
2066: Whitefriars: the time, half-past seven of the clock on a windy March
2067: morning, Anno Domini seventeen hundred and eighty. (Mr. Cruncher himself
2068: always spoke of the year of our Lord as Anna Dominoes: apparently under
2069: the impression that the Christian era dated from the invention of a
2070: popular game, by a lady who had bestowed her name upon it.)
2071: 
2072: Mr. Cruncher's apartments were not in a savoury neighbourhood, and were
2073: but two in number, even if a closet with a single pane of glass in it
2074: might be counted as one. But they were very decently kept. Early as
2075: it was, on the windy March morning, the room in which he lay abed was
2076: already scrubbed throughout; and between the cups and saucers arranged
2077: for breakfast, and the lumbering deal table, a very clean white cloth
2078: was spread.
2079: 
2080: Mr. Cruncher reposed under a patchwork counterpane, like a Harlequin
2081: at home. At first, he slept heavily, but, by degrees, began to roll
2082: and surge in bed, until he rose above the surface, with his spiky hair
2083: looking as if it must tear the sheets to ribbons. At which juncture, he
2084: exclaimed, in a voice of dire exasperation:
2085: 
2086: "Bust me, if she ain't at it agin!"
2087: 
2088: A woman of orderly and industrious appearance rose from her knees in a
2089: corner, with sufficient haste and trepidation to show that she was the
2090: person referred to.
2091: 
2092: "What!" said Mr. Cruncher, looking out of bed for a boot. "You're at it
2093: agin, are you?"
2094: 
2095: After hailing the morn with this second salutation, he threw a boot at
2096: the woman as a third. It was a very muddy boot, and may introduce the
2097: odd circumstance connected with Mr. Cruncher's domestic economy, that,
2098: whereas he often came home after banking hours with clean boots, he
2099: often got up next morning to find the same boots covered with clay.
2100: 
2101: "What," said Mr. Cruncher, varying his apostrophe after missing his
2102: mark--"what are you up to, Aggerawayter?"
2103: 
2104: "I was only saying my prayers."
2105: 
2106: "Saying your prayers! You're a nice woman! What do you mean by flopping
2107: yourself down and praying agin me?"
2108: 
2109: "I was not praying against you; I was praying for you."
2110: 
2111: "You weren't. And if you were, I won't be took the liberty with. Here!
2112: your mother's a nice woman, young Jerry, going a praying agin your
2113: father's prosperity. You've got a dutiful mother, you have, my son.
2114: You've got a religious mother, you have, my boy: going and flopping
2115: herself down, and praying that the bread-and-butter may be snatched out
2116: of the mouth of her only child."
2117: 
2118: Master Cruncher (who was in his shirt) took this very ill, and, turning
2119: to his mother, strongly deprecated any praying away of his personal
2120: board.
2121: 
2122: "And what do you suppose, you conceited female," said Mr. Cruncher, with
2123: unconscious inconsistency, "that the worth of _your_ prayers may be?
2124: Name the price that you put _your_ prayers at!"
2125: 
2126: "They only come from the heart, Jerry. They are worth no more than
2127: that."
2128: 
2129: "Worth no more than that," repeated Mr. Cruncher. "They ain't worth
2130: much, then. Whether or no, I won't be prayed agin, I tell you. I can't
2131: afford it. I'm not a going to be made unlucky by _your_ sneaking. If
2132: you must go flopping yourself down, flop in favour of your husband and
2133: child, and not in opposition to 'em. If I had had any but a unnat'ral
2134: wife, and this poor boy had had any but a unnat'ral mother, I might
2135: have made some money last week instead of being counter-prayed and
2136: countermined and religiously circumwented into the worst of luck.
2137: B-u-u-ust me!" said Mr. Cruncher, who all this time had been putting
2138: on his clothes, "if I ain't, what with piety and one blowed thing and
2139: another, been choused this last week into as bad luck as ever a poor
2140: devil of a honest tradesman met with! Young Jerry, dress yourself, my
2141: boy, and while I clean my boots keep a eye upon your mother now and
2142: then, and if you see any signs of more flopping, give me a call. For, I
2143: tell you," here he addressed his wife once more, "I won't be gone agin,
2144: in this manner. I am as rickety as a hackney-coach, I'm as sleepy as
2145: laudanum, my lines is strained to that degree that I shouldn't know, if
2146: it wasn't for the pain in 'em, which was me and which somebody else, yet
2147: I'm none the better for it in pocket; and it's my suspicion that you've
2148: been at it from morning to night to prevent me from being the better for
2149: it in pocket, and I won't put up with it, Aggerawayter, and what do you
2150: say now!"
2151: 
2152: Growling, in addition, such phrases as "Ah! yes! You're religious, too.
2153: You wouldn't put yourself in opposition to the interests of your husband
2154: and child, would you? Not you!" and throwing off other sarcastic sparks
2155: from the whirling grindstone of his indignation, Mr. Cruncher betook
2156: himself to his boot-cleaning and his general preparation for business.
2157: In the meantime, his son, whose head was garnished with tenderer spikes,
2158: and whose young eyes stood close by one another, as his father's did,
2159: kept the required watch upon his mother. He greatly disturbed that poor
2160: woman at intervals, by darting out of his sleeping closet, where he made
2161: his toilet, with a suppressed cry of "You are going to flop, mother.
2162: --Halloa, father!" and, after raising this fictitious alarm, darting in
2163: again with an undutiful grin.
2164: 
2165: Mr. Cruncher's temper was not at all improved when he came to his
2166: breakfast. He resented Mrs. Cruncher's saying grace with particular
2167: animosity.
2168: 
2169: "Now, Aggerawayter! What are you up to? At it again?"
2170: 
2171: His wife explained that she had merely "asked a blessing."
2172: 
2173: "Don't do it!" said Mr. Crunches looking about, as if he rather expected
2174: to see the loaf disappear under the efficacy of his wife's petitions. "I
2175: ain't a going to be blest out of house and home. I won't have my wittles
2176: blest off my table. Keep still!"
2177: 
2178: Exceedingly red-eyed and grim, as if he had been up all night at a party
2179: which had taken anything but a convivial turn, Jerry Cruncher worried
2180: his breakfast rather than ate it, growling over it like any four-footed
2181: inmate of a menagerie. Towards nine o'clock he smoothed his ruffled
2182: aspect, and, presenting as respectable and business-like an exterior as
2183: he could overlay his natural self with, issued forth to the occupation
2184: of the day.
2185: 
2186: It could scarcely be called a trade, in spite of his favourite
2187: description of himself as "a honest tradesman." His stock consisted of
2188: a wooden stool, made out of a broken-backed chair cut down, which stool,
2189: young Jerry, walking at his father's side, carried every morning to
2190: beneath the banking-house window that was nearest Temple Bar: where,
2191: with the addition of the first handful of straw that could be gleaned
2192: from any passing vehicle to keep the cold and wet from the odd-job-man's
2193: feet, it formed the encampment for the day. On this post of his, Mr.
2194: Cruncher was as well known to Fleet-street and the Temple, as the Bar
2195: itself,--and was almost as in-looking.
2196: 
2197: Encamped at a quarter before nine, in good time to touch his
2198: three-cornered hat to the oldest of men as they passed in to Tellson's,
2199: Jerry took up his station on this windy March morning, with young Jerry
2200: standing by him, when not engaged in making forays through the Bar, to
2201: inflict bodily and mental injuries of an acute description on passing
2202: boys who were small enough for his amiable purpose. Father and son,
2203: extremely like each other, looking silently on at the morning traffic
2204: in Fleet-street, with their two heads as near to one another as the two
2205: eyes of each were, bore a considerable resemblance to a pair of monkeys.
2206: The resemblance was not lessened by the accidental circumstance, that
2207: the mature Jerry bit and spat out straw, while the twinkling eyes of the
2208: youthful Jerry were as restlessly watchful of him as of everything else
2209: in Fleet-street.
2210: 
2211: The head of one of the regular indoor messengers attached to Tellson's
2212: establishment was put through the door, and the word was given:
2213: 
2214: "Porter wanted!"
2215: 
2216: "Hooray, father! Here's an early job to begin with!"
2217: 
2218: Having thus given his parent God speed, young Jerry seated himself on
2219: the stool, entered on his reversionary interest in the straw his father
2220: had been chewing, and cogitated.
2221: 
2222: "Al-ways rusty! His fingers is al-ways rusty!" muttered young Jerry.
2223: "Where does my father get all that iron rust from? He don't get no iron
2224: rust here!"
2225: 
2226: 
2227: 
2228: 
2229: II. A Sight
2230: 
2231: 
2232: "You know the Old Bailey well, no doubt?" said one of the oldest of
2233: clerks to Jerry the messenger.
2234: 
2235: "Ye-es, sir," returned Jerry, in something of a dogged manner. "I _do_
2236: know the Bailey."
2237: 
2238: "Just so. And you know Mr. Lorry."
2239: 
2240: "I know Mr. Lorry, sir, much better than I know the Bailey. Much
2241: better," said Jerry, not unlike a reluctant witness at the establishment
2242: in question, "than I, as a honest tradesman, wish to know the Bailey."
2243: 
2244: "Very well. Find the door where the witnesses go in, and show the
2245: door-keeper this note for Mr. Lorry. He will then let you in."
2246: 
2247: "Into the court, sir?"
2248: 
2249: "Into the court."
2250: 
2251: Mr. Cruncher's eyes seemed to get a little closer to one another, and to
2252: interchange the inquiry, "What do you think of this?"
2253: 
2254: "Am I to wait in the court, sir?" he asked, as the result of that
2255: conference.
2256: 
2257: "I am going to tell you. The door-keeper will pass the note to Mr.
2258: Lorry, and do you make any gesture that will attract Mr. Lorry's
2259: attention, and show him where you stand. Then what you have to do, is,
2260: to remain there until he wants you."
2261: 
2262: "Is that all, sir?"
2263: 
2264: "That's all. He wishes to have a messenger at hand. This is to tell him
2265: you are there."
2266: 
2267: As the ancient clerk deliberately folded and superscribed the note,
2268: Mr. Cruncher, after surveying him in silence until he came to the
2269: blotting-paper stage, remarked:
2270: 
2271: "I suppose they'll be trying Forgeries this morning?"
2272: 
2273: "Treason!"
2274: 
2275: "That's quartering," said Jerry. "Barbarous!"
2276: 
2277: "It is the law," remarked the ancient clerk, turning his surprised
2278: spectacles upon him. "It is the law."
2279: 
2280: "It's hard in the law to spile a man, I think. It's hard enough to kill
2281: him, but it's wery hard to spile him, sir."
2282: 
2283: "Not at all," retained the ancient clerk. "Speak well of the law. Take
2284: care of your chest and voice, my good friend, and leave the law to take
2285: care of itself. I give you that advice."
2286: 
2287: "It's the damp, sir, what settles on my chest and voice," said Jerry. "I
2288: leave you to judge what a damp way of earning a living mine is."
2289: 
2290: "Well, well," said the old clerk; "we all have our various ways of
2291: gaining a livelihood. Some of us have damp ways, and some of us have dry
2292: ways. Here is the letter. Go along."
2293: 
2294: Jerry took the letter, and, remarking to himself with less internal
2295: deference than he made an outward show of, "You are a lean old one,
2296: too," made his bow, informed his son, in passing, of his destination,
2297: and went his way.
2298: 
2299: They hanged at Tyburn, in those days, so the street outside Newgate had
2300: not obtained one infamous notoriety that has since attached to it.
2301: But, the gaol was a vile place, in which most kinds of debauchery and
2302: villainy were practised, and where dire diseases were bred, that came
2303: into court with the prisoners, and sometimes rushed straight from the
2304: dock at my Lord Chief Justice himself, and pulled him off the bench. It
2305: had more than once happened, that the Judge in the black cap pronounced
2306: his own doom as certainly as the prisoner's, and even died before him.
2307: For the rest, the Old Bailey was famous as a kind of deadly inn-yard,
2308: from which pale travellers set out continually, in carts and coaches, on
2309: a violent passage into the other world: traversing some two miles and a
2310: half of public street and road, and shaming few good citizens, if any.
2311: So powerful is use, and so desirable to be good use in the beginning. It
2312: was famous, too, for the pillory, a wise old institution, that inflicted
2313: a punishment of which no one could foresee the extent; also, for
2314: the whipping-post, another dear old institution, very humanising and
2315: softening to behold in action; also, for extensive transactions in
2316: blood-money, another fragment of ancestral wisdom, systematically
2317: leading to the most frightful mercenary crimes that could be committed
2318: under Heaven. Altogether, the Old Bailey, at that date, was a choice
2319: illustration of the precept, that "Whatever is is right;" an aphorism
2320: that would be as final as it is lazy, did it not include the troublesome
2321: consequence, that nothing that ever was, was wrong.
2322: 
2323: Making his way through the tainted crowd, dispersed up and down this
2324: hideous scene of action, with the skill of a man accustomed to make his
2325: way quietly, the messenger found out the door he sought, and handed in
2326: his letter through a trap in it. For, people then paid to see the play
2327: at the Old Bailey, just as they paid to see the play in Bedlam--only the
2328: former entertainment was much the dearer. Therefore, all the Old Bailey
2329: doors were well guarded--except, indeed, the social doors by which the
2330: criminals got there, and those were always left wide open.
2331: 
2332: After some delay and demur, the door grudgingly turned on its hinges a
2333: very little way, and allowed Mr. Jerry Cruncher to squeeze himself into
2334: court.
2335: 
2336: "What's on?" he asked, in a whisper, of the man he found himself next
2337: to.
2338: 
2339: "Nothing yet."
2340: 
2341: "What's coming on?"
2342: 
2343: "The Treason case."
2344: 
2345: "The quartering one, eh?"
2346: 
2347: "Ah!" returned the man, with a relish; "he'll be drawn on a hurdle to
2348: be half hanged, and then he'll be taken down and sliced before his own
2349: face, and then his inside will be taken out and burnt while he looks on,
2350: and then his head will be chopped off, and he'll be cut into quarters.
2351: That's the sentence."
2352: 
2353: "If he's found Guilty, you mean to say?" Jerry added, by way of proviso.
2354: 
2355: "Oh! they'll find him guilty," said the other. "Don't you be afraid of
2356: that."
2357: 
2358: Mr. Cruncher's attention was here diverted to the door-keeper, whom he
2359: saw making his way to Mr. Lorry, with the note in his hand. Mr. Lorry
2360: sat at a table, among the gentlemen in wigs: not far from a wigged
2361: gentleman, the prisoner's counsel, who had a great bundle of papers
2362: before him: and nearly opposite another wigged gentleman with his hands
2363: in his pockets, whose whole attention, when Mr. Cruncher looked at him
2364: then or afterwards, seemed to be concentrated on the ceiling of the
2365: court. After some gruff coughing and rubbing of his chin and signing
2366: with his hand, Jerry attracted the notice of Mr. Lorry, who had stood up
2367: to look for him, and who quietly nodded and sat down again.
2368: 
2369: "What's _he_ got to do with the case?" asked the man he had spoken with.
2370: 
2371: "Blest if I know," said Jerry.
2372: 
2373: "What have _you_ got to do with it, then, if a person may inquire?"
2374: 
2375: "Blest if I know that either," said Jerry.
2376: 
2377: The entrance of the Judge, and a consequent great stir and settling
2378: down in the court, stopped the dialogue. Presently, the dock became the
2379: central point of interest. Two gaolers, who had been standing there,
2380: went out, and the prisoner was brought in, and put to the bar.
2381: 
2382: Everybody present, except the one wigged gentleman who looked at the
2383: ceiling, stared at him. All the human breath in the place, rolled
2384: at him, like a sea, or a wind, or a fire. Eager faces strained round
2385: pillars and corners, to get a sight of him; spectators in back rows
2386: stood up, not to miss a hair of him; people on the floor of the court,
2387: laid their hands on the shoulders of the people before them, to help
2388: themselves, at anybody's cost, to a view of him--stood a-tiptoe, got
2389: upon ledges, stood upon next to nothing, to see every inch of him.
2390: Conspicuous among these latter, like an animated bit of the spiked wall
2391: of Newgate, Jerry stood: aiming at the prisoner the beery breath of a
2392: whet he had taken as he came along, and discharging it to mingle with
2393: the waves of other beer, and gin, and tea, and coffee, and what not,
2394: that flowed at him, and already broke upon the great windows behind him
2395: in an impure mist and rain.
2396: 
2397: The object of all this staring and blaring, was a young man of about
2398: five-and-twenty, well-grown and well-looking, with a sunburnt cheek and
2399: a dark eye. His condition was that of a young gentleman. He was plainly
2400: dressed in black, or very dark grey, and his hair, which was long and
2401: dark, was gathered in a ribbon at the back of his neck; more to be out
2402: of his way than for ornament. As an emotion of the mind will express
2403: itself through any covering of the body, so the paleness which his
2404: situation engendered came through the brown upon his cheek, showing the
2405: soul to be stronger than the sun. He was otherwise quite self-possessed,
2406: bowed to the Judge, and stood quiet.
2407: 
2408: The sort of interest with which this man was stared and breathed at,
2409: was not a sort that elevated humanity. Had he stood in peril of a less
2410: horrible sentence--had there been a chance of any one of its savage
2411: details being spared--by just so much would he have lost in his
2412: fascination. The form that was to be doomed to be so shamefully mangled,
2413: was the sight; the immortal creature that was to be so butchered
2414: and torn asunder, yielded the sensation. Whatever gloss the various
2415: spectators put upon the interest, according to their several arts and
2416: powers of self-deceit, the interest was, at the root of it, Ogreish.
2417: 
2418: Silence in the court! Charles Darnay had yesterday pleaded Not Guilty to
2419: an indictment denouncing him (with infinite jingle and jangle) for that
2420: he was a false traitor to our serene, illustrious, excellent, and so
2421: forth, prince, our Lord the King, by reason of his having, on divers
2422: occasions, and by divers means and ways, assisted Lewis, the French
2423: King, in his wars against our said serene, illustrious, excellent, and
2424: so forth; that was to say, by coming and going, between the dominions of
2425: our said serene, illustrious, excellent, and so forth, and those of the
2426: said French Lewis, and wickedly, falsely, traitorously, and otherwise
2427: evil-adverbiously, revealing to the said French Lewis what forces our
2428: said serene, illustrious, excellent, and so forth, had in preparation
2429: to send to Canada and North America. This much, Jerry, with his head
2430: becoming more and more spiky as the law terms bristled it, made out with
2431: huge satisfaction, and so arrived circuitously at the understanding that
2432: the aforesaid, and over and over again aforesaid, Charles Darnay, stood
2433: there before him upon his trial; that the jury were swearing in; and
2434: that Mr. Attorney-General was making ready to speak.
2435: 
2436: The accused, who was (and who knew he was) being mentally hanged,
2437: beheaded, and quartered, by everybody there, neither flinched from
2438: the situation, nor assumed any theatrical air in it. He was quiet and
2439: attentive; watched the opening proceedings with a grave interest;
2440: and stood with his hands resting on the slab of wood before him, so
2441: composedly, that they had not displaced a leaf of the herbs with which
2442: it was strewn. The court was all bestrewn with herbs and sprinkled with
2443: vinegar, as a precaution against gaol air and gaol fever.
2444: 
2445: Over the prisoner's head there was a mirror, to throw the light down
2446: upon him. Crowds of the wicked and the wretched had been reflected in
2447: it, and had passed from its surface and this earth's together. Haunted
2448: in a most ghastly manner that abominable place would have been, if the
2449: glass could ever have rendered back its reflections, as the ocean is one
2450: day to give up its dead. Some passing thought of the infamy and disgrace
2451: for which it had been reserved, may have struck the prisoner's mind. Be
2452: that as it may, a change in his position making him conscious of a bar
2453: of light across his face, he looked up; and when he saw the glass his
2454: face flushed, and his right hand pushed the herbs away.
2455: 
2456: It happened, that the action turned his face to that side of the court
2457: which was on his left. About on a level with his eyes, there sat,
2458: in that corner of the Judge's bench, two persons upon whom his look
2459: immediately rested; so immediately, and so much to the changing of his
2460: aspect, that all the eyes that were turned upon him, turned to them.
2461: 
2462: The spectators saw in the two figures, a young lady of little more than
2463: twenty, and a gentleman who was evidently her father; a man of a very
2464: remarkable appearance in respect of the absolute whiteness of his hair,
2465: and a certain indescribable intensity of face: not of an active kind,
2466: but pondering and self-communing. When this expression was upon him, he
2467: looked as if he were old; but when it was stirred and broken up--as
2468: it was now, in a moment, on his speaking to his daughter--he became a
2469: handsome man, not past the prime of life.
2470: 
2471: His daughter had one of her hands drawn through his arm, as she sat by
2472: him, and the other pressed upon it. She had drawn close to him, in her
2473: dread of the scene, and in her pity for the prisoner. Her forehead had
2474: been strikingly expressive of an engrossing terror and compassion
2475: that saw nothing but the peril of the accused. This had been so very
2476: noticeable, so very powerfully and naturally shown, that starers who
2477: had had no pity for him were touched by her; and the whisper went about,
2478: "Who are they?"
2479: 
2480: Jerry, the messenger, who had made his own observations, in his own
2481: manner, and who had been sucking the rust off his fingers in his
2482: absorption, stretched his neck to hear who they were. The crowd about
2483: him had pressed and passed the inquiry on to the nearest attendant, and
2484: from him it had been more slowly pressed and passed back; at last it got
2485: to Jerry:
2486: 
2487: "Witnesses."
2488: 
2489: "For which side?"
2490: 
2491: "Against."
2492: 
2493: "Against what side?"
2494: 
2495: "The prisoner's."
2496: 
2497: The Judge, whose eyes had gone in the general direction, recalled them,
2498: leaned back in his seat, and looked steadily at the man whose life was
2499: in his hand, as Mr. Attorney-General rose to spin the rope, grind the
2500: axe, and hammer the nails into the scaffold.
2501: 
2502: 
2503: 
2504: 
2505: III. A Disappointment
2506: 
2507: 
2508: Mr. Attorney-General had to inform the jury, that the prisoner before
2509: them, though young in years, was old in the treasonable practices which
2510: claimed the forfeit of his life. That this correspondence with the
2511: public enemy was not a correspondence of to-day, or of yesterday, or
2512: even of last year, or of the year before. That, it was certain the
2513: prisoner had, for longer than that, been in the habit of passing and
2514: repassing between France and England, on secret business of which
2515: he could give no honest account. That, if it were in the nature of
2516: traitorous ways to thrive (which happily it never was), the real
2517: wickedness and guilt of his business might have remained undiscovered.
2518: That Providence, however, had put it into the heart of a person who
2519: was beyond fear and beyond reproach, to ferret out the nature of the
2520: prisoner's schemes, and, struck with horror, to disclose them to his
2521: Majesty's Chief Secretary of State and most honourable Privy Council.
2522: That, this patriot would be produced before them. That, his position and
2523: attitude were, on the whole, sublime. That, he had been the prisoner's
2524: friend, but, at once in an auspicious and an evil hour detecting his
2525: infamy, had resolved to immolate the traitor he could no longer cherish
2526: in his bosom, on the sacred altar of his country. That, if statues
2527: were decreed in Britain, as in ancient Greece and Rome, to public
2528: benefactors, this shining citizen would assuredly have had one. That, as
2529: they were not so decreed, he probably would not have one. That, Virtue,
2530: as had been observed by the poets (in many passages which he well
2531: knew the jury would have, word for word, at the tips of their tongues;
2532: whereat the jury's countenances displayed a guilty consciousness that
2533: they knew nothing about the passages), was in a manner contagious; more
2534: especially the bright virtue known as patriotism, or love of country.
2535: That, the lofty example of this immaculate and unimpeachable witness
2536: for the Crown, to refer to whom however unworthily was an honour, had
2537: communicated itself to the prisoner's servant, and had engendered in him
2538: a holy determination to examine his master's table-drawers and pockets,
2539: and secrete his papers. That, he (Mr. Attorney-General) was prepared to
2540: hear some disparagement attempted of this admirable servant; but that,
2541: in a general way, he preferred him to his (Mr. Attorney-General's)
2542: brothers and sisters, and honoured him more than his (Mr.
2543: Attorney-General's) father and mother. That, he called with confidence
2544: on the jury to come and do likewise. That, the evidence of these two
2545: witnesses, coupled with the documents of their discovering that would be
2546: produced, would show the prisoner to have been furnished with lists of
2547: his Majesty's forces, and of their disposition and preparation, both by
2548: sea and land, and would leave no doubt that he had habitually conveyed
2549: such information to a hostile power. That, these lists could not be
2550: proved to be in the prisoner's handwriting; but that it was all the
2551: same; that, indeed, it was rather the better for the prosecution, as
2552: showing the prisoner to be artful in his precautions. That, the proof
2553: would go back five years, and would show the prisoner already engaged
2554: in these pernicious missions, within a few weeks before the date of the
2555: very first action fought between the British troops and the Americans.
2556: That, for these reasons, the jury, being a loyal jury (as he knew they
2557: were), and being a responsible jury (as _they_ knew they were), must
2558: positively find the prisoner Guilty, and make an end of him, whether
2559: they liked it or not. That, they never could lay their heads upon their
2560: pillows; that, they never could tolerate the idea of their wives laying
2561: their heads upon their pillows; that, they never could endure the notion
2562: of their children laying their heads upon their pillows; in short, that
2563: there never more could be, for them or theirs, any laying of heads upon
2564: pillows at all, unless the prisoner's head was taken off. That head
2565: Mr. Attorney-General concluded by demanding of them, in the name of
2566: everything he could think of with a round turn in it, and on the faith
2567: of his solemn asseveration that he already considered the prisoner as
2568: good as dead and gone.
2569: 
2570: When the Attorney-General ceased, a buzz arose in the court as if
2571: a cloud of great blue-flies were swarming about the prisoner, in
2572: anticipation of what he was soon to become. When toned down again, the
2573: unimpeachable patriot appeared in the witness-box.
2574: 
2575: Mr. Solicitor-General then, following his leader's lead, examined the
2576: patriot: John Barsad, gentleman, by name. The story of his pure soul was
2577: exactly what Mr. Attorney-General had described it to be--perhaps, if
2578: it had a fault, a little too exactly. Having released his noble bosom
2579: of its burden, he would have modestly withdrawn himself, but that the
2580: wigged gentleman with the papers before him, sitting not far from Mr.
2581: Lorry, begged to ask him a few questions. The wigged gentleman sitting
2582: opposite, still looking at the ceiling of the court.
2583: 
2584: Had he ever been a spy himself? No, he scorned the base insinuation.
2585: What did he live upon? His property. Where was his property? He didn't
2586: precisely remember where it was. What was it? No business of anybody's.
2587: Had he inherited it? Yes, he had. From whom? Distant relation. Very
2588: distant? Rather. Ever been in prison? Certainly not. Never in a debtors'
2589: prison? Didn't see what that had to do with it. Never in a debtors'
2590: prison?--Come, once again. Never? Yes. How many times? Two or three
2591: times. Not five or six? Perhaps. Of what profession? Gentleman. Ever
2592: been kicked? Might have been. Frequently? No. Ever kicked downstairs?
2593: Decidedly not; once received a kick on the top of a staircase, and fell
2594: downstairs of his own accord. Kicked on that occasion for cheating at
2595: dice? Something to that effect was said by the intoxicated liar who
2596: committed the assault, but it was not true. Swear it was not true?
2597: Positively. Ever live by cheating at play? Never. Ever live by play? Not
2598: more than other gentlemen do. Ever borrow money of the prisoner? Yes.
2599: Ever pay him? No. Was not this intimacy with the prisoner, in reality a
2600: very slight one, forced upon the prisoner in coaches, inns, and packets?
2601: No. Sure he saw the prisoner with these lists? Certain. Knew no more
2602: about the lists? No. Had not procured them himself, for instance? No.
2603: Expect to get anything by this evidence? No. Not in regular government
2604: pay and employment, to lay traps? Oh dear no. Or to do anything? Oh dear
2605: no. Swear that? Over and over again. No motives but motives of sheer
2606: patriotism? None whatever.
2607: 
2608: The virtuous servant, Roger Cly, swore his way through the case at a
2609: great rate. He had taken service with the prisoner, in good faith and
2610: simplicity, four years ago. He had asked the prisoner, aboard the Calais
2611: packet, if he wanted a handy fellow, and the prisoner had engaged him.
2612: He had not asked the prisoner to take the handy fellow as an act of
2613: charity--never thought of such a thing. He began to have suspicions of
2614: the prisoner, and to keep an eye upon him, soon afterwards. In arranging
2615: his clothes, while travelling, he had seen similar lists to these in the
2616: prisoner's pockets, over and over again. He had taken these lists from
2617: the drawer of the prisoner's desk. He had not put them there first. He
2618: had seen the prisoner show these identical lists to French gentlemen
2619: at Calais, and similar lists to French gentlemen, both at Calais and
2620: Boulogne. He loved his country, and couldn't bear it, and had given
2621: information. He had never been suspected of stealing a silver tea-pot;
2622: he had been maligned respecting a mustard-pot, but it turned out to be
2623: only a plated one. He had known the last witness seven or eight years;
2624: that was merely a coincidence. He didn't call it a particularly curious
2625: coincidence; most coincidences were curious. Neither did he call it a
2626: curious coincidence that true patriotism was _his_ only motive too. He
2627: was a true Briton, and hoped there were many like him.
2628: 
2629: The blue-flies buzzed again, and Mr. Attorney-General called Mr. Jarvis
2630: Lorry.
2631: 
2632: "Mr. Jarvis Lorry, are you a clerk in Tellson's bank?"
2633: 
2634: "I am."
2635: 
2636: "On a certain Friday night in November one thousand seven hundred and
2637: seventy-five, did business occasion you to travel between London and
2638: Dover by the mail?"
2639: 
2640: "It did."
2641: 
2642: "Were there any other passengers in the mail?"
2643: 
2644: "Two."
2645: 
2646: "Did they alight on the road in the course of the night?"
2647: 
2648: "They did."
2649: 
2650: "Mr. Lorry, look upon the prisoner. Was he one of those two passengers?"
2651: 
2652: "I cannot undertake to say that he was."
2653: 
2654: "Does he resemble either of these two passengers?"
2655: 
2656: "Both were so wrapped up, and the night was so dark, and we were all so
2657: reserved, that I cannot undertake to say even that."
2658: 
2659: "Mr. Lorry, look again upon the prisoner. Supposing him wrapped up as
2660: those two passengers were, is there anything in his bulk and stature to
2661: render it unlikely that he was one of them?"
2662: 
2663: "No."
2664: 
2665: "You will not swear, Mr. Lorry, that he was not one of them?"
2666: 
2667: "No."
2668: 
2669: "So at least you say he may have been one of them?"
2670: 
2671: "Yes. Except that I remember them both to have been--like
2672: myself--timorous of highwaymen, and the prisoner has not a timorous
2673: air."
2674: 
2675: "Did you ever see a counterfeit of timidity, Mr. Lorry?"
2676: 
2677: "I certainly have seen that."
2678: 
2679: "Mr. Lorry, look once more upon the prisoner. Have you seen him, to your
2680: certain knowledge, before?"
2681: 
2682: "I have."
2683: 
2684: "When?"
2685: 
2686: "I was returning from France a few days afterwards, and, at Calais, the
2687: prisoner came on board the packet-ship in which I returned, and made the
2688: voyage with me."
2689: 
2690: "At what hour did he come on board?"
2691: 
2692: "At a little after midnight."
2693: 
2694: "In the dead of the night. Was he the only passenger who came on board
2695: at that untimely hour?"
2696: 
2697: "He happened to be the only one."
2698: 
2699: "Never mind about 'happening,' Mr. Lorry. He was the only passenger who
2700: came on board in the dead of the night?"
2701: 
2702: "He was."
2703: 
2704: "Were you travelling alone, Mr. Lorry, or with any companion?"
2705: 
2706: "With two companions. A gentleman and lady. They are here."
2707: 
2708: "They are here. Had you any conversation with the prisoner?"
2709: 
2710: "Hardly any. The weather was stormy, and the passage long and rough, and
2711: I lay on a sofa, almost from shore to shore."
2712: 
2713: "Miss Manette!"
2714: 
2715: The young lady, to whom all eyes had been turned before, and were now
2716: turned again, stood up where she had sat. Her father rose with her, and
2717: kept her hand drawn through his arm.
2718: 
2719: "Miss Manette, look upon the prisoner."
2720: 
2721: To be confronted with such pity, and such earnest youth and beauty, was
2722: far more trying to the accused than to be confronted with all the crowd.
2723: Standing, as it were, apart with her on the edge of his grave, not all
2724: the staring curiosity that looked on, could, for the moment, nerve him
2725: to remain quite still. His hurried right hand parcelled out the herbs
2726: before him into imaginary beds of flowers in a garden; and his efforts
2727: to control and steady his breathing shook the lips from which the colour
2728: rushed to his heart. The buzz of the great flies was loud again.
2729: 
2730: "Miss Manette, have you seen the prisoner before?"
2731: 
2732: "Yes, sir."
2733: 
2734: "Where?"
2735: 
2736: "On board of the packet-ship just now referred to, sir, and on the same
2737: occasion."
2738: 
2739: "You are the young lady just now referred to?"
2740: 
2741: "O! most unhappily, I am!"
2742: 
2743: The plaintive tone of her compassion merged into the less musical voice
2744: of the Judge, as he said something fiercely: "Answer the questions put
2745: to you, and make no remark upon them."
2746: 
2747: "Miss Manette, had you any conversation with the prisoner on that
2748: passage across the Channel?"
2749: 
2750: "Yes, sir."
2751: 
2752: "Recall it."
2753: 
2754: In the midst of a profound stillness, she faintly began: "When the
2755: gentleman came on board--"
2756: 
2757: "Do you mean the prisoner?" inquired the Judge, knitting his brows.
2758: 
2759: "Yes, my Lord."
2760: 
2761: "Then say the prisoner."
2762: 
2763: "When the prisoner came on board, he noticed that my father," turning
2764: her eyes lovingly to him as he stood beside her, "was much fatigued
2765: and in a very weak state of health. My father was so reduced that I was
2766: afraid to take him out of the air, and I had made a bed for him on the
2767: deck near the cabin steps, and I sat on the deck at his side to take
2768: care of him. There were no other passengers that night, but we four.
2769: The prisoner was so good as to beg permission to advise me how I could
2770: shelter my father from the wind and weather, better than I had done. I
2771: had not known how to do it well, not understanding how the wind would
2772: set when we were out of the harbour. He did it for me. He expressed
2773: great gentleness and kindness for my father's state, and I am sure he
2774: felt it. That was the manner of our beginning to speak together."
2775: 
2776: "Let me interrupt you for a moment. Had he come on board alone?"
2777: 
2778: "No."
2779: 
2780: "How many were with him?"
2781: 
2782: "Two French gentlemen."
2783: 
2784: "Had they conferred together?"
2785: 
2786: "They had conferred together until the last moment, when it was
2787: necessary for the French gentlemen to be landed in their boat."
2788: 
2789: "Had any papers been handed about among them, similar to these lists?"
2790: 
2791: "Some papers had been handed about among them, but I don't know what
2792: papers."
2793: 
2794: "Like these in shape and size?"
2795: 
2796: "Possibly, but indeed I don't know, although they stood whispering very
2797: near to me: because they stood at the top of the cabin steps to have the
2798: light of the lamp that was hanging there; it was a dull lamp, and they
2799: spoke very low, and I did not hear what they said, and saw only that
2800: they looked at papers."
2801: 
2802: "Now, to the prisoner's conversation, Miss Manette."
2803: 
2804: "The prisoner was as open in his confidence with me--which arose out
2805: of my helpless situation--as he was kind, and good, and useful to my
2806: father. I hope," bursting into tears, "I may not repay him by doing him
2807: harm to-day."
2808: 
2809: Buzzing from the blue-flies.
2810: 
2811: "Miss Manette, if the prisoner does not perfectly understand that
2812: you give the evidence which it is your duty to give--which you must
2813: give--and which you cannot escape from giving--with great unwillingness,
2814: he is the only person present in that condition. Please to go on."
2815: 
2816: "He told me that he was travelling on business of a delicate and
2817: difficult nature, which might get people into trouble, and that he was
2818: therefore travelling under an assumed name. He said that this business
2819: had, within a few days, taken him to France, and might, at intervals,
2820: take him backwards and forwards between France and England for a long
2821: time to come."
2822: 
2823: "Did he say anything about America, Miss Manette? Be particular."
2824: 
2825: "He tried to explain to me how that quarrel had arisen, and he said
2826: that, so far as he could judge, it was a wrong and foolish one on
2827: England's part. He added, in a jesting way, that perhaps George
2828: Washington might gain almost as great a name in history as George the
2829: Third. But there was no harm in his way of saying this: it was said
2830: laughingly, and to beguile the time."
2831: 
2832: Any strongly marked expression of face on the part of a chief actor in
2833: a scene of great interest to whom many eyes are directed, will be
2834: unconsciously imitated by the spectators. Her forehead was painfully
2835: anxious and intent as she gave this evidence, and, in the pauses when
2836: she stopped for the Judge to write it down, watched its effect upon
2837: the counsel for and against. Among the lookers-on there was the same
2838: expression in all quarters of the court; insomuch, that a great majority
2839: of the foreheads there, might have been mirrors reflecting the witness,
2840: when the Judge looked up from his notes to glare at that tremendous
2841: heresy about George Washington.
2842: 
2843: Mr. Attorney-General now signified to my Lord, that he deemed it
2844: necessary, as a matter of precaution and form, to call the young lady's
2845: father, Doctor Manette. Who was called accordingly.
2846: 
2847: "Doctor Manette, look upon the prisoner. Have you ever seen him before?"
2848: 
2849: "Once. When he called at my lodgings in London. Some three years, or
2850: three years and a half ago."
2851: 
2852: "Can you identify him as your fellow-passenger on board the packet, or
2853: speak to his conversation with your daughter?"
2854: 
2855: "Sir, I can do neither."
2856: 
2857: "Is there any particular and special reason for your being unable to do
2858: either?"
2859: 
2860: He answered, in a low voice, "There is."
2861: 
2862: "Has it been your misfortune to undergo a long imprisonment, without
2863: trial, or even accusation, in your native country, Doctor Manette?"
2864: 
2865: He answered, in a tone that went to every heart, "A long imprisonment."
2866: 
2867: "Were you newly released on the occasion in question?"
2868: 
2869: "They tell me so."
2870: 
2871: "Have you no remembrance of the occasion?"
2872: 
2873: "None. My mind is a blank, from some time--I cannot even say what
2874: time--when I employed myself, in my captivity, in making shoes, to the
2875: time when I found myself living in London with my dear daughter
2876: here. She had become familiar to me, when a gracious God restored
2877: my faculties; but, I am quite unable even to say how she had become
2878: familiar. I have no remembrance of the process."
2879: 
2880: Mr. Attorney-General sat down, and the father and daughter sat down
2881: together.
2882: 
2883: A singular circumstance then arose in the case. The object in hand being
2884: to show that the prisoner went down, with some fellow-plotter untracked,
2885: in the Dover mail on that Friday night in November five years ago, and
2886: got out of the mail in the night, as a blind, at a place where he did
2887: not remain, but from which he travelled back some dozen miles or more,
2888: to a garrison and dockyard, and there collected information; a witness
2889: was called to identify him as having been at the precise time required,
2890: in the coffee-room of an hotel in that garrison-and-dockyard town,
2891: waiting for another person. The prisoner's counsel was cross-examining
2892: this witness with no result, except that he had never seen the prisoner
2893: on any other occasion, when the wigged gentleman who had all this time
2894: been looking at the ceiling of the court, wrote a word or two on a
2895: little piece of paper, screwed it up, and tossed it to him. Opening
2896: this piece of paper in the next pause, the counsel looked with great
2897: attention and curiosity at the prisoner.
2898: 
2899: "You say again you are quite sure that it was the prisoner?"
2900: 
2901: The witness was quite sure.
2902: 
2903: "Did you ever see anybody very like the prisoner?"
2904: 
2905: Not so like (the witness said) as that he could be mistaken.
2906: 
2907: "Look well upon that gentleman, my learned friend there," pointing
2908: to him who had tossed the paper over, "and then look well upon the
2909: prisoner. How say you? Are they very like each other?"
2910: 
2911: Allowing for my learned friend's appearance being careless and slovenly
2912: if not debauched, they were sufficiently like each other to surprise,
2913: not only the witness, but everybody present, when they were thus brought
2914: into comparison. My Lord being prayed to bid my learned friend lay aside
2915: his wig, and giving no very gracious consent, the likeness became
2916: much more remarkable. My Lord inquired of Mr. Stryver (the prisoner's
2917: counsel), whether they were next to try Mr. Carton (name of my learned
2918: friend) for treason? But, Mr. Stryver replied to my Lord, no; but he
2919: would ask the witness to tell him whether what happened once, might
2920: happen twice; whether he would have been so confident if he had seen
2921: this illustration of his rashness sooner, whether he would be so
2922: confident, having seen it; and more. The upshot of which, was, to smash
2923: this witness like a crockery vessel, and shiver his part of the case to
2924: useless lumber.
2925: 
2926: Mr. Cruncher had by this time taken quite a lunch of rust off his
