2927: fingers in his following of the evidence. He had now to attend while Mr.
2928: Stryver fitted the prisoner's case on the jury, like a compact suit
2929: of clothes; showing them how the patriot, Barsad, was a hired spy and
2930: traitor, an unblushing trafficker in blood, and one of the greatest
2931: scoundrels upon earth since accursed Judas--which he certainly did look
2932: rather like. How the virtuous servant, Cly, was his friend and partner,
2933: and was worthy to be; how the watchful eyes of those forgers and false
2934: swearers had rested on the prisoner as a victim, because some family
2935: affairs in France, he being of French extraction, did require his making
2936: those passages across the Channel--though what those affairs were, a
2937: consideration for others who were near and dear to him, forbade him,
2938: even for his life, to disclose. How the evidence that had been warped
2939: and wrested from the young lady, whose anguish in giving it they
2940: had witnessed, came to nothing, involving the mere little innocent
2941: gallantries and politenesses likely to pass between any young gentleman
2942: and young lady so thrown together;--with the exception of that
2943: reference to George Washington, which was altogether too extravagant and
2944: impossible to be regarded in any other light than as a monstrous joke.
2945: How it would be a weakness in the government to break down in this
2946: attempt to practise for popularity on the lowest national antipathies
2947: and fears, and therefore Mr. Attorney-General had made the most of it;
2948: how, nevertheless, it rested upon nothing, save that vile and infamous
2949: character of evidence too often disfiguring such cases, and of which the
2950: State Trials of this country were full. But, there my Lord interposed
2951: (with as grave a face as if it had not been true), saying that he could
2952: not sit upon that Bench and suffer those allusions.
2953: 
2954: Mr. Stryver then called his few witnesses, and Mr. Cruncher had next to
2955: attend while Mr. Attorney-General turned the whole suit of clothes Mr.
2956: Stryver had fitted on the jury, inside out; showing how Barsad and
2957: Cly were even a hundred times better than he had thought them, and the
2958: prisoner a hundred times worse. Lastly, came my Lord himself, turning
2959: the suit of clothes, now inside out, now outside in, but on the whole
2960: decidedly trimming and shaping them into grave-clothes for the prisoner.
2961: 
2962: And now, the jury turned to consider, and the great flies swarmed again.
2963: 
2964: Mr. Carton, who had so long sat looking at the ceiling of the court,
2965: changed neither his place nor his attitude, even in this excitement.
2966: While his teamed friend, Mr. Stryver, massing his papers before him,
2967: whispered with those who sat near, and from time to time glanced
2968: anxiously at the jury; while all the spectators moved more or less, and
2969: grouped themselves anew; while even my Lord himself arose from his seat,
2970: and slowly paced up and down his platform, not unattended by a suspicion
2971: in the minds of the audience that his state was feverish; this one man
2972: sat leaning back, with his torn gown half off him, his untidy wig put
2973: on just as it had happened to light on his head after its removal, his
2974: hands in his pockets, and his eyes on the ceiling as they had been all
2975: day. Something especially reckless in his demeanour, not only gave him
2976: a disreputable look, but so diminished the strong resemblance he
2977: undoubtedly bore to the prisoner (which his momentary earnestness,
2978: when they were compared together, had strengthened), that many of the
2979: lookers-on, taking note of him now, said to one another they would
2980: hardly have thought the two were so alike. Mr. Cruncher made the
2981: observation to his next neighbour, and added, "I'd hold half a guinea
2982: that _he_ don't get no law-work to do. Don't look like the sort of one
2983: to get any, do he?"
2984: 
2985: Yet, this Mr. Carton took in more of the details of the scene than he
2986: appeared to take in; for now, when Miss Manette's head dropped upon
2987: her father's breast, he was the first to see it, and to say audibly:
2988: "Officer! look to that young lady. Help the gentleman to take her out.
2989: Don't you see she will fall!"
2990: 
2991: There was much commiseration for her as she was removed, and much
2992: sympathy with her father. It had evidently been a great distress to
2993: him, to have the days of his imprisonment recalled. He had shown
2994: strong internal agitation when he was questioned, and that pondering or
2995: brooding look which made him old, had been upon him, like a heavy cloud,
2996: ever since. As he passed out, the jury, who had turned back and paused a
2997: moment, spoke, through their foreman.
2998: 
2999: They were not agreed, and wished to retire. My Lord (perhaps with George
3000: Washington on his mind) showed some surprise that they were not agreed,
3001: but signified his pleasure that they should retire under watch and ward,
3002: and retired himself. The trial had lasted all day, and the lamps in
3003: the court were now being lighted. It began to be rumoured that the
3004: jury would be out a long while. The spectators dropped off to get
3005: refreshment, and the prisoner withdrew to the back of the dock, and sat
3006: down.
3007: 
3008: Mr. Lorry, who had gone out when the young lady and her father went out,
3009: now reappeared, and beckoned to Jerry: who, in the slackened interest,
3010: could easily get near him.
3011: 
3012: "Jerry, if you wish to take something to eat, you can. But, keep in the
3013: way. You will be sure to hear when the jury come in. Don't be a moment
3014: behind them, for I want you to take the verdict back to the bank. You
3015: are the quickest messenger I know, and will get to Temple Bar long
3016: before I can."
3017: 
3018: Jerry had just enough forehead to knuckle, and he knuckled it in
3019: acknowledgment of this communication and a shilling. Mr. Carton came up
3020: at the moment, and touched Mr. Lorry on the arm.
3021: 
3022: "How is the young lady?"
3023: 
3024: "She is greatly distressed; but her father is comforting her, and she
3025: feels the better for being out of court."
3026: 
3027: "I'll tell the prisoner so. It won't do for a respectable bank gentleman
3028: like you, to be seen speaking to him publicly, you know."
3029: 
3030: Mr. Lorry reddened as if he were conscious of having debated the point
3031: in his mind, and Mr. Carton made his way to the outside of the bar.
3032: The way out of court lay in that direction, and Jerry followed him, all
3033: eyes, ears, and spikes.
3034: 
3035: "Mr. Darnay!"
3036: 
3037: The prisoner came forward directly.
3038: 
3039: "You will naturally be anxious to hear of the witness, Miss Manette. She
3040: will do very well. You have seen the worst of her agitation."
3041: 
3042: "I am deeply sorry to have been the cause of it. Could you tell her so
3043: for me, with my fervent acknowledgments?"
3044: 
3045: "Yes, I could. I will, if you ask it."
3046: 
3047: Mr. Carton's manner was so careless as to be almost insolent. He stood,
3048: half turned from the prisoner, lounging with his elbow against the bar.
3049: 
3050: "I do ask it. Accept my cordial thanks."
3051: 
3052: "What," said Carton, still only half turned towards him, "do you expect,
3053: Mr. Darnay?"
3054: 
3055: "The worst."
3056: 
3057: "It's the wisest thing to expect, and the likeliest. But I think their
3058: withdrawing is in your favour."
3059: 
3060: Loitering on the way out of court not being allowed, Jerry heard no
3061: more: but left them--so like each other in feature, so unlike each other
3062: in manner--standing side by side, both reflected in the glass above
3063: them.
3064: 
3065: An hour and a half limped heavily away in the thief-and-rascal crowded
3066: passages below, even though assisted off with mutton pies and ale.
3067: The hoarse messenger, uncomfortably seated on a form after taking that
3068: refection, had dropped into a doze, when a loud murmur and a rapid tide
3069: of people setting up the stairs that led to the court, carried him along
3070: with them.
3071: 
3072: "Jerry! Jerry!" Mr. Lorry was already calling at the door when he got
3073: there.
3074: 
3075: "Here, sir! It's a fight to get back again. Here I am, sir!"
3076: 
3077: Mr. Lorry handed him a paper through the throng. "Quick! Have you got
3078: it?"
3079: 
3080: "Yes, sir."
3081: 
3082: Hastily written on the paper was the word "ACQUITTED."
3083: 
3084: "If you had sent the message, 'Recalled to Life,' again," muttered
3085: Jerry, as he turned, "I should have known what you meant, this time."
3086: 
3087: He had no opportunity of saying, or so much as thinking, anything else,
3088: until he was clear of the Old Bailey; for, the crowd came pouring out
3089: with a vehemence that nearly took him off his legs, and a loud buzz
3090: swept into the street as if the baffled blue-flies were dispersing in
3091: search of other carrion.
3092: 
3093: 
3094: 
3095: 
3096: IV. Congratulatory
3097: 
3098: 
3099: From the dimly-lighted passages of the court, the last sediment of the
3100: human stew that had been boiling there all day, was straining off, when
3101: Doctor Manette, Lucie Manette, his daughter, Mr. Lorry, the solicitor
3102: for the defence, and its counsel, Mr. Stryver, stood gathered round Mr.
3103: Charles Darnay--just released--congratulating him on his escape from
3104: death.
3105: 
3106: It would have been difficult by a far brighter light, to recognise
3107: in Doctor Manette, intellectual of face and upright of bearing, the
3108: shoemaker of the garret in Paris. Yet, no one could have looked at him
3109: twice, without looking again: even though the opportunity of observation
3110: had not extended to the mournful cadence of his low grave voice, and
3111: to the abstraction that overclouded him fitfully, without any apparent
3112: reason. While one external cause, and that a reference to his long
3113: lingering agony, would always--as on the trial--evoke this condition
3114: from the depths of his soul, it was also in its nature to arise of
3115: itself, and to draw a gloom over him, as incomprehensible to those
3116: unacquainted with his story as if they had seen the shadow of the actual
3117: Bastille thrown upon him by a summer sun, when the substance was three
3118: hundred miles away.
3119: 
3120: Only his daughter had the power of charming this black brooding from
3121: his mind. She was the golden thread that united him to a Past beyond his
3122: misery, and to a Present beyond his misery: and the sound of her voice,
3123: the light of her face, the touch of her hand, had a strong beneficial
3124: influence with him almost always. Not absolutely always, for she could
3125: recall some occasions on which her power had failed; but they were few
3126: and slight, and she believed them over.
3127: 
3128: Mr. Darnay had kissed her hand fervently and gratefully, and had turned
3129: to Mr. Stryver, whom he warmly thanked. Mr. Stryver, a man of little
3130: more than thirty, but looking twenty years older than he was, stout,
3131: loud, red, bluff, and free from any drawback of delicacy, had a pushing
3132: way of shouldering himself (morally and physically) into companies and
3133: conversations, that argued well for his shouldering his way up in life.
3134: 
3135: He still had his wig and gown on, and he said, squaring himself at his
3136: late client to that degree that he squeezed the innocent Mr. Lorry clean
3137: out of the group: "I am glad to have brought you off with honour, Mr.
3138: Darnay. It was an infamous prosecution, grossly infamous; but not the
3139: less likely to succeed on that account."
3140: 
3141: "You have laid me under an obligation to you for life--in two senses,"
3142: said his late client, taking his hand.
3143: 
3144: "I have done my best for you, Mr. Darnay; and my best is as good as
3145: another man's, I believe."
3146: 
3147: It clearly being incumbent on some one to say, "Much better," Mr. Lorry
3148: said it; perhaps not quite disinterestedly, but with the interested
3149: object of squeezing himself back again.
3150: 
3151: "You think so?" said Mr. Stryver. "Well! you have been present all day,
3152: and you ought to know. You are a man of business, too."
3153: 
3154: "And as such," quoth Mr. Lorry, whom the counsel learned in the law had
3155: now shouldered back into the group, just as he had previously shouldered
3156: him out of it--"as such I will appeal to Doctor Manette, to break up
3157: this conference and order us all to our homes. Miss Lucie looks ill, Mr.
3158: Darnay has had a terrible day, we are worn out."
3159: 
3160: "Speak for yourself, Mr. Lorry," said Stryver; "I have a night's work to
3161: do yet. Speak for yourself."
3162: 
3163: "I speak for myself," answered Mr. Lorry, "and for Mr. Darnay, and for
3164: Miss Lucie, and--Miss Lucie, do you not think I may speak for us all?"
3165: He asked her the question pointedly, and with a glance at her father.
3166: 
3167: His face had become frozen, as it were, in a very curious look at
3168: Darnay: an intent look, deepening into a frown of dislike and distrust,
3169: not even unmixed with fear. With this strange expression on him his
3170: thoughts had wandered away.
3171: 
3172: "My father," said Lucie, softly laying her hand on his.
3173: 
3174: He slowly shook the shadow off, and turned to her.
3175: 
3176: "Shall we go home, my father?"
3177: 
3178: With a long breath, he answered "Yes."
3179: 
3180: The friends of the acquitted prisoner had dispersed, under the
3181: impression--which he himself had originated--that he would not be
3182: released that night. The lights were nearly all extinguished in the
3183: passages, the iron gates were being closed with a jar and a rattle,
3184: and the dismal place was deserted until to-morrow morning's interest of
3185: gallows, pillory, whipping-post, and branding-iron, should repeople it.
3186: Walking between her father and Mr. Darnay, Lucie Manette passed into
3187: the open air. A hackney-coach was called, and the father and daughter
3188: departed in it.
3189: 
3190: Mr. Stryver had left them in the passages, to shoulder his way back
3191: to the robing-room. Another person, who had not joined the group, or
3192: interchanged a word with any one of them, but who had been leaning
3193: against the wall where its shadow was darkest, had silently strolled
3194: out after the rest, and had looked on until the coach drove away. He now
3195: stepped up to where Mr. Lorry and Mr. Darnay stood upon the pavement.
3196: 
3197: "So, Mr. Lorry! Men of business may speak to Mr. Darnay now?"
3198: 
3199: Nobody had made any acknowledgment of Mr. Carton's part in the day's
3200: proceedings; nobody had known of it. He was unrobed, and was none the
3201: better for it in appearance.
3202: 
3203: "If you knew what a conflict goes on in the business mind, when the
3204: business mind is divided between good-natured impulse and business
3205: appearances, you would be amused, Mr. Darnay."
3206: 
3207: Mr. Lorry reddened, and said, warmly, "You have mentioned that before,
3208: sir. We men of business, who serve a House, are not our own masters. We
3209: have to think of the House more than ourselves."
3210: 
3211: "_I_ know, _I_ know," rejoined Mr. Carton, carelessly. "Don't be
3212: nettled, Mr. Lorry. You are as good as another, I have no doubt: better,
3213: I dare say."
3214: 
3215: "And indeed, sir," pursued Mr. Lorry, not minding him, "I really don't
3216: know what you have to do with the matter. If you'll excuse me, as very
3217: much your elder, for saying so, I really don't know that it is your
3218: business."
3219: 
3220: "Business! Bless you, _I_ have no business," said Mr. Carton.
3221: 
3222: "It is a pity you have not, sir."
3223: 
3224: "I think so, too."
3225: 
3226: "If you had," pursued Mr. Lorry, "perhaps you would attend to it."
3227: 
3228: "Lord love you, no!--I shouldn't," said Mr. Carton.
3229: 
3230: "Well, sir!" cried Mr. Lorry, thoroughly heated by his indifference,
3231: "business is a very good thing, and a very respectable thing. And, sir,
3232: if business imposes its restraints and its silences and impediments, Mr.
3233: Darnay as a young gentleman of generosity knows how to make allowance
3234: for that circumstance. Mr. Darnay, good night, God bless you, sir!
3235: I hope you have been this day preserved for a prosperous and happy
3236: life.--Chair there!"
3237: 
3238: Perhaps a little angry with himself, as well as with the barrister, Mr.
3239: Lorry bustled into the chair, and was carried off to Tellson's. Carton,
3240: who smelt of port wine, and did not appear to be quite sober, laughed
3241: then, and turned to Darnay:
3242: 
3243: "This is a strange chance that throws you and me together. This must
3244: be a strange night to you, standing alone here with your counterpart on
3245: these street stones?"
3246: 
3247: "I hardly seem yet," returned Charles Darnay, "to belong to this world
3248: again."
3249: 
3250: "I don't wonder at it; it's not so long since you were pretty far
3251: advanced on your way to another. You speak faintly."
3252: 
3253: "I begin to think I _am_ faint."
3254: 
3255: "Then why the devil don't you dine? I dined, myself, while those
3256: numskulls were deliberating which world you should belong to--this, or
3257: some other. Let me show you the nearest tavern to dine well at."
3258: 
3259: Drawing his arm through his own, he took him down Ludgate-hill to
3260: Fleet-street, and so, up a covered way, into a tavern. Here, they were
3261: shown into a little room, where Charles Darnay was soon recruiting
3262: his strength with a good plain dinner and good wine: while Carton sat
3263: opposite to him at the same table, with his separate bottle of port
3264: before him, and his fully half-insolent manner upon him.
3265: 
3266: "Do you feel, yet, that you belong to this terrestrial scheme again, Mr.
3267: Darnay?"
3268: 
3269: "I am frightfully confused regarding time and place; but I am so far
3270: mended as to feel that."
3271: 
3272: "It must be an immense satisfaction!"
3273: 
3274: He said it bitterly, and filled up his glass again: which was a large
3275: one.
3276: 
3277: "As to me, the greatest desire I have, is to forget that I belong to it.
3278: It has no good in it for me--except wine like this--nor I for it. So we
3279: are not much alike in that particular. Indeed, I begin to think we are
3280: not much alike in any particular, you and I."
3281: 
3282: Confused by the emotion of the day, and feeling his being there with
3283: this Double of coarse deportment, to be like a dream, Charles Darnay was
3284: at a loss how to answer; finally, answered not at all.
3285: 
3286: "Now your dinner is done," Carton presently said, "why don't you call a
3287: health, Mr. Darnay; why don't you give your toast?"
3288: 
3289: "What health? What toast?"
3290: 
3291: "Why, it's on the tip of your tongue. It ought to be, it must be, I'll
3292: swear it's there."
3293: 
3294: "Miss Manette, then!"
3295: 
3296: "Miss Manette, then!"
3297: 
3298: Looking his companion full in the face while he drank the toast, Carton
3299: flung his glass over his shoulder against the wall, where it shivered to
3300: pieces; then, rang the bell, and ordered in another.
3301: 
3302: "That's a fair young lady to hand to a coach in the dark, Mr. Darnay!"
3303: he said, filling his new goblet.
3304: 
3305: A slight frown and a laconic "Yes," were the answer.
3306: 
3307: "That's a fair young lady to be pitied by and wept for by! How does it
3308: feel? Is it worth being tried for one's life, to be the object of such
3309: sympathy and compassion, Mr. Darnay?"
3310: 
3311: Again Darnay answered not a word.
3312: 
3313: "She was mightily pleased to have your message, when I gave it her. Not
3314: that she showed she was pleased, but I suppose she was."
3315: 
3316: The allusion served as a timely reminder to Darnay that this
3317: disagreeable companion had, of his own free will, assisted him in the
3318: strait of the day. He turned the dialogue to that point, and thanked him
3319: for it.
3320: 
3321: "I neither want any thanks, nor merit any," was the careless rejoinder.
3322: "It was nothing to do, in the first place; and I don't know why I did
3323: it, in the second. Mr. Darnay, let me ask you a question."
3324: 
3325: "Willingly, and a small return for your good offices."
3326: 
3327: "Do you think I particularly like you?"
3328: 
3329: "Really, Mr. Carton," returned the other, oddly disconcerted, "I have
3330: not asked myself the question."
3331: 
3332: "But ask yourself the question now."
3333: 
3334: "You have acted as if you do; but I don't think you do."
3335: 
3336: "_I_ don't think I do," said Carton. "I begin to have a very good
3337: opinion of your understanding."
3338: 
3339: "Nevertheless," pursued Darnay, rising to ring the bell, "there is
3340: nothing in that, I hope, to prevent my calling the reckoning, and our
3341: parting without ill-blood on either side."
3342: 
3343: Carton rejoining, "Nothing in life!" Darnay rang. "Do you call the whole
3344: reckoning?" said Carton. On his answering in the affirmative, "Then
3345: bring me another pint of this same wine, drawer, and come and wake me at
3346: ten."
3347: 
3348: The bill being paid, Charles Darnay rose and wished him good night.
3349: Without returning the wish, Carton rose too, with something of a threat
3350: of defiance in his manner, and said, "A last word, Mr. Darnay: you think
3351: I am drunk?"
3352: 
3353: "I think you have been drinking, Mr. Carton."
3354: 
3355: "Think? You know I have been drinking."
3356: 
3357: "Since I must say so, I know it."
3358: 
3359: "Then you shall likewise know why. I am a disappointed drudge, sir. I
3360: care for no man on earth, and no man on earth cares for me."
3361: 
3362: "Much to be regretted. You might have used your talents better."
3363: 
3364: "May be so, Mr. Darnay; may be not. Don't let your sober face elate you,
3365: however; you don't know what it may come to. Good night!"
3366: 
3367: When he was left alone, this strange being took up a candle, went to a
3368: glass that hung against the wall, and surveyed himself minutely in it.
3369: 
3370: "Do you particularly like the man?" he muttered, at his own image; "why
3371: should you particularly like a man who resembles you? There is nothing
3372: in you to like; you know that. Ah, confound you! What a change you have
3373: made in yourself! A good reason for taking to a man, that he shows you
3374: what you have fallen away from, and what you might have been! Change
3375: places with him, and would you have been looked at by those blue eyes as
3376: he was, and commiserated by that agitated face as he was? Come on, and
3377: have it out in plain words! You hate the fellow."
3378: 
3379: He resorted to his pint of wine for consolation, drank it all in a few
3380: minutes, and fell asleep on his arms, with his hair straggling over the
3381: table, and a long winding-sheet in the candle dripping down upon him.
3382: 
3383: 
3384: 
3385: 
3386: V. The Jackal
3387: 
3388: 
3389: Those were drinking days, and most men drank hard. So very great is
3390: the improvement Time has brought about in such habits, that a moderate
3391: statement of the quantity of wine and punch which one man would swallow
3392: in the course of a night, without any detriment to his reputation as a
3393: perfect gentleman, would seem, in these days, a ridiculous exaggeration.
3394: The learned profession of the law was certainly not behind any other
3395: learned profession in its Bacchanalian propensities; neither was Mr.
3396: Stryver, already fast shouldering his way to a large and lucrative
3397: practice, behind his compeers in this particular, any more than in the
3398: drier parts of the legal race.
3399: 
3400: A favourite at the Old Bailey, and eke at the Sessions, Mr. Stryver had
3401: begun cautiously to hew away the lower staves of the ladder on which
3402: he mounted. Sessions and Old Bailey had now to summon their favourite,
3403: specially, to their longing arms; and shouldering itself towards the
3404: visage of the Lord Chief Justice in the Court of King's Bench, the
3405: florid countenance of Mr. Stryver might be daily seen, bursting out of
3406: the bed of wigs, like a great sunflower pushing its way at the sun from
3407: among a rank garden-full of flaring companions.
3408: 
3409: It had once been noted at the Bar, that while Mr. Stryver was a glib
3410: man, and an unscrupulous, and a ready, and a bold, he had not that
3411: faculty of extracting the essence from a heap of statements, which is
3412: among the most striking and necessary of the advocate's accomplishments.
3413: But, a remarkable improvement came upon him as to this. The more
3414: business he got, the greater his power seemed to grow of getting at its
3415: pith and marrow; and however late at night he sat carousing with Sydney
3416: Carton, he always had his points at his fingers' ends in the morning.
3417: 
3418: Sydney Carton, idlest and most unpromising of men, was Stryver's great
3419: ally. What the two drank together, between Hilary Term and Michaelmas,
3420: might have floated a king's ship. Stryver never had a case in hand,
3421: anywhere, but Carton was there, with his hands in his pockets, staring
3422: at the ceiling of the court; they went the same Circuit, and even there
3423: they prolonged their usual orgies late into the night, and Carton was
3424: rumoured to be seen at broad day, going home stealthily and unsteadily
3425: to his lodgings, like a dissipated cat. At last, it began to get about,
3427: would never be a lion, he was an amazingly good jackal, and that he
3428: rendered suit and service to Stryver in that humble capacity.
3429: 
3430: "Ten o'clock, sir," said the man at the tavern, whom he had charged to
3431: wake him--"ten o'clock, sir."
3432: 
3433: "_What's_ the matter?"
3434: 
3435: "Ten o'clock, sir."
3436: 
3437: "What do you mean? Ten o'clock at night?"
3438: 
3439: "Yes, sir. Your honour told me to call you."
3440: 
3441: "Oh! I remember. Very well, very well."
3442: 
3443: After a few dull efforts to get to sleep again, which the man
3444: dexterously combated by stirring the fire continuously for five minutes,
3445: he got up, tossed his hat on, and walked out. He turned into the Temple,
3446: and, having revived himself by twice pacing the pavements of King's
3447: Bench-walk and Paper-buildings, turned into the Stryver chambers.
3448: 
3449: The Stryver clerk, who never assisted at these conferences, had gone
3450: home, and the Stryver principal opened the door. He had his slippers on,
3451: and a loose bed-gown, and his throat was bare for his greater ease. He
3452: had that rather wild, strained, seared marking about the eyes, which
3453: may be observed in all free livers of his class, from the portrait of
3454: Jeffries downward, and which can be traced, under various disguises of
3455: Art, through the portraits of every Drinking Age.
3456: 
3457: "You are a little late, Memory," said Stryver.
3458: 
3459: "About the usual time; it may be a quarter of an hour later."
3460: 
3461: They went into a dingy room lined with books and littered with papers,
3462: where there was a blazing fire. A kettle steamed upon the hob, and in
3463: the midst of the wreck of papers a table shone, with plenty of wine upon
3464: it, and brandy, and rum, and sugar, and lemons.
3465: 
3466: "You have had your bottle, I perceive, Sydney."
3467: 
3468: "Two to-night, I think. I have been dining with the day's client; or
3469: seeing him dine--it's all one!"
3470: 
3471: "That was a rare point, Sydney, that you brought to bear upon the
3472: identification. How did you come by it? When did it strike you?"
3473: 
3474: "I thought he was rather a handsome fellow, and I thought I should have
3475: been much the same sort of fellow, if I had had any luck."
3476: 
3477: Mr. Stryver laughed till he shook his precocious paunch.
3478: 
3479: "You and your luck, Sydney! Get to work, get to work."
3480: 
3481: Sullenly enough, the jackal loosened his dress, went into an adjoining
3482: room, and came back with a large jug of cold water, a basin, and a towel
3483: or two. Steeping the towels in the water, and partially wringing them
3484: out, he folded them on his head in a manner hideous to behold, sat down
3485: at the table, and said, "Now I am ready!"
3486: 
3487: "Not much boiling down to be done to-night, Memory," said Mr. Stryver,
3488: gaily, as he looked among his papers.
3489: 
3490: "How much?"
3491: 
3492: "Only two sets of them."
3493: 
3494: "Give me the worst first."
3495: 
3496: "There they are, Sydney. Fire away!"
3497: 
3498: The lion then composed himself on his back on a sofa on one side of the
3499: drinking-table, while the jackal sat at his own paper-bestrewn table
3500: proper, on the other side of it, with the bottles and glasses ready to
3501: his hand. Both resorted to the drinking-table without stint, but each in
3502: a different way; the lion for the most part reclining with his hands in
3503: his waistband, looking at the fire, or occasionally flirting with some
3504: lighter document; the jackal, with knitted brows and intent face,
3505: so deep in his task, that his eyes did not even follow the hand he
3506: stretched out for his glass--which often groped about, for a minute or
3507: more, before it found the glass for his lips. Two or three times, the
3508: matter in hand became so knotty, that the jackal found it imperative on
3509: him to get up, and steep his towels anew. From these pilgrimages to the
3510: jug and basin, he returned with such eccentricities of damp headgear as
3511: no words can describe; which were made the more ludicrous by his anxious
3512: gravity.
3513: 
3514: At length the jackal had got together a compact repast for the lion, and
3515: proceeded to offer it to him. The lion took it with care and caution,
3516: made his selections from it, and his remarks upon it, and the jackal
3517: assisted both. When the repast was fully discussed, the lion put his
3518: hands in his waistband again, and lay down to meditate. The jackal then
3519: invigorated himself with a bumper for his throttle, and a fresh application
3520: to his head, and applied himself to the collection of a second meal;
3521: this was administered to the lion in the same manner, and was not
3522: disposed of until the clocks struck three in the morning.
3523: 
3524: "And now we have done, Sydney, fill a bumper of punch," said Mr.
3525: Stryver.
3526: 
3527: The jackal removed the towels from his head, which had been steaming
3528: again, shook himself, yawned, shivered, and complied.
3529: 
3530: "You were very sound, Sydney, in the matter of those crown witnesses
3531: to-day. Every question told."
3532: 
3533: "I always am sound; am I not?"
3534: 
3535: "I don't gainsay it. What has roughened your temper? Put some punch to
3536: it and smooth it again."
3537: 
3538: With a deprecatory grunt, the jackal again complied.
3539: 
3541: his head over him as he reviewed him in the present and the past, "the
3542: old seesaw Sydney. Up one minute and down the next; now in spirits and
3543: now in despondency!"
3544: 
3545: "Ah!" returned the other, sighing: "yes! The same Sydney, with the same
3546: luck. Even then, I did exercises for other boys, and seldom did my own."
3547: 
3548: "And why not?"
3549: 
3550: "God knows. It was my way, I suppose."
3551: 
3552: He sat, with his hands in his pockets and his legs stretched out before
3553: him, looking at the fire.
3554: 
3555: "Carton," said his friend, squaring himself at him with a bullying air,
3556: as if the fire-grate had been the furnace in which sustained endeavour
3557: was forged, and the one delicate thing to be done for the old Sydney
3558: Carton of old Shrewsbury School was to shoulder him into it, "your way
3559: is, and always was, a lame way. You summon no energy and purpose. Look
3560: at me."
3561: 
3562: "Oh, botheration!" returned Sydney, with a lighter and more
3563: good-humoured laugh, "don't _you_ be moral!"
3564: 
3565: "How have I done what I have done?" said Stryver; "how do I do what I
3566: do?"
3567: 
3568: "Partly through paying me to help you, I suppose. But it's not worth
3569: your while to apostrophise me, or the air, about it; what you want to
3570: do, you do. You were always in the front rank, and I was always behind."
3571: 
3572: "I had to get into the front rank; I was not born there, was I?"
3573: 
3574: "I was not present at the ceremony; but my opinion is you were," said
3575: Carton. At this, he laughed again, and they both laughed.
3576: 
3577: "Before Shrewsbury, and at Shrewsbury, and ever since Shrewsbury,"
3578: pursued Carton, "you have fallen into your rank, and I have fallen into
3579: mine. Even when we were fellow-students in the Student-Quarter of Paris,
3580: picking up French, and French law, and other French crumbs that we
3581: didn't get much good of, you were always somewhere, and I was always
3582: nowhere."
3583: 
3584: "And whose fault was that?"
3585: 
3586: "Upon my soul, I am not sure that it was not yours. You were always
3587: driving and riving and shouldering and passing, to that restless degree
3588: that I had no chance for my life but in rust and repose. It's a gloomy
3589: thing, however, to talk about one's own past, with the day breaking.
3590: Turn me in some other direction before I go."
3591: 
3592: "Well then! Pledge me to the pretty witness," said Stryver, holding up
3593: his glass. "Are you turned in a pleasant direction?"
3594: 
3595: Apparently not, for he became gloomy again.
3596: 
3597: "Pretty witness," he muttered, looking down into his glass. "I have had
3598: enough of witnesses to-day and to-night; who's your pretty witness?"
3599: 
3600: "The picturesque doctor's daughter, Miss Manette."
3601: 
3602: "_She_ pretty?"
3603: 
3604: "Is she not?"
3605: 
3606: "No."
3607: 
3608: "Why, man alive, she was the admiration of the whole Court!"
3609: 
3610: "Rot the admiration of the whole Court! Who made the Old Bailey a judge
3611: of beauty? She was a golden-haired doll!"
3612: 
3613: "Do you know, Sydney," said Mr. Stryver, looking at him with sharp eyes,
3614: and slowly drawing a hand across his florid face: "do you know, I rather
3615: thought, at the time, that you sympathised with the golden-haired doll,
3616: and were quick to see what happened to the golden-haired doll?"
3617: 
3618: "Quick to see what happened! If a girl, doll or no doll, swoons within a
3619: yard or two of a man's nose, he can see it without a perspective-glass.
3620: I pledge you, but I deny the beauty. And now I'll have no more drink;
3621: I'll get to bed."
3622: 
3623: When his host followed him out on the staircase with a candle, to light
3624: him down the stairs, the day was coldly looking in through its grimy
3625: windows. When he got out of the house, the air was cold and sad, the
3626: dull sky overcast, the river dark and dim, the whole scene like a
3627: lifeless desert. And wreaths of dust were spinning round and round
3628: before the morning blast, as if the desert-sand had risen far away, and
3629: the first spray of it in its advance had begun to overwhelm the city.
3630: 
3631: Waste forces within him, and a desert all around, this man stood still
3632: on his way across a silent terrace, and saw for a moment, lying in the
3633: wilderness before him, a mirage of honourable ambition, self-denial, and
3634: perseverance. In the fair city of this vision, there were airy galleries
3635: from which the loves and graces looked upon him, gardens in which the
3636: fruits of life hung ripening, waters of Hope that sparkled in his sight.
3637: A moment, and it was gone. Climbing to a high chamber in a well of
3638: houses, he threw himself down in his clothes on a neglected bed, and its
3639: pillow was wet with wasted tears.
3640: 
3641: Sadly, sadly, the sun rose; it rose upon no sadder sight than the man of
3642: good abilities and good emotions, incapable of their directed exercise,
3643: incapable of his own help and his own happiness, sensible of the blight
3644: on him, and resigning himself to let it eat him away.
3645: 
3646: 
3647: 
3648: 
3649: VI. Hundreds of People
3650: 
3651: 
3652: The quiet lodgings of Doctor Manette were in a quiet street-corner not
3653: far from Soho-square. On the afternoon of a certain fine Sunday when the
3654: waves of four months had rolled over the trial for treason, and carried
3655: it, as to the public interest and memory, far out to sea, Mr. Jarvis
3656: Lorry walked along the sunny streets from Clerkenwell where he lived,
3657: on his way to dine with the Doctor. After several relapses into
3658: business-absorption, Mr. Lorry had become the Doctor's friend, and the
3659: quiet street-corner was the sunny part of his life.
3660: 
3661: On this certain fine Sunday, Mr. Lorry walked towards Soho, early in
3662: the afternoon, for three reasons of habit. Firstly, because, on fine
3663: Sundays, he often walked out, before dinner, with the Doctor and Lucie;
3664: secondly, because, on unfavourable Sundays, he was accustomed to be with
3665: them as the family friend, talking, reading, looking out of window, and
3666: generally getting through the day; thirdly, because he happened to have
3667: his own little shrewd doubts to solve, and knew how the ways of the
3668: Doctor's household pointed to that time as a likely time for solving
3669: them.
3670: 
3671: A quainter corner than the corner where the Doctor lived, was not to be
3672: found in London. There was no way through it, and the front windows of
3673: the Doctor's lodgings commanded a pleasant little vista of street that
3674: had a congenial air of retirement on it. There were few buildings then,
3675: north of the Oxford-road, and forest-trees flourished, and wild flowers
3676: grew, and the hawthorn blossomed, in the now vanished fields. As a
3677: consequence, country airs circulated in Soho with vigorous freedom,
3678: instead of languishing into the parish like stray paupers without a
3679: settlement; and there was many a good south wall, not far off, on which
3680: the peaches ripened in their season.
3681: 
3682: The summer light struck into the corner brilliantly in the earlier part
3683: of the day; but, when the streets grew hot, the corner was in shadow,
3684: though not in shadow so remote but that you could see beyond it into a
3685: glare of brightness. It was a cool spot, staid but cheerful, a wonderful
3686: place for echoes, and a very harbour from the raging streets.
3687: 
3688: There ought to have been a tranquil bark in such an anchorage, and
3689: there was. The Doctor occupied two floors of a large stiff house, where
3690: several callings purported to be pursued by day, but whereof little was
3691: audible any day, and which was shunned by all of them at night. In
3692: a building at the back, attainable by a courtyard where a plane-tree
3693: rustled its green leaves, church-organs claimed to be made, and silver
3694: to be chased, and likewise gold to be beaten by some mysterious giant
3695: who had a golden arm starting out of the wall of the front hall--as if
3696: he had beaten himself precious, and menaced a similar conversion of all
3697: visitors. Very little of these trades, or of a lonely lodger rumoured
3698: to live up-stairs, or of a dim coach-trimming maker asserted to have
3699: a counting-house below, was ever heard or seen. Occasionally, a stray
3700: workman putting his coat on, traversed the hall, or a stranger peered
3701: about there, or a distant clink was heard across the courtyard, or a
3702: thump from the golden giant. These, however, were only the exceptions
3703: required to prove the rule that the sparrows in the plane-tree behind
3704: the house, and the echoes in the corner before it, had their own way
3705: from Sunday morning unto Saturday night.
3706: 
3707: Doctor Manette received such patients here as his old reputation, and
3708: its revival in the floating whispers of his story, brought him.
3709: His scientific knowledge, and his vigilance and skill in conducting
3710: ingenious experiments, brought him otherwise into moderate request, and
3711: he earned as much as he wanted.
3712: 
3713: These things were within Mr. Jarvis Lorry's knowledge, thoughts, and
3714: notice, when he rang the door-bell of the tranquil house in the corner,
3715: on the fine Sunday afternoon.
3716: 
3717: "Doctor Manette at home?"
3718: 
3719: Expected home.
3720: 
3721: "Miss Lucie at home?"
3722: 
3723: Expected home.
3724: 
3725: "Miss Pross at home?"
3726: 
3727: Possibly at home, but of a certainty impossible for handmaid to
3728: anticipate intentions of Miss Pross, as to admission or denial of the
3729: fact.
3730: 
3731: "As I am at home myself," said Mr. Lorry, "I'll go upstairs."
3732: 
3733: Although the Doctor's daughter had known nothing of the country of her
3734: birth, she appeared to have innately derived from it that ability to
3735: make much of little means, which is one of its most useful and most
3736: agreeable characteristics. Simple as the furniture was, it was set off
3737: by so many little adornments, of no value but for their taste and fancy,
3738: that its effect was delightful. The disposition of everything in the
3739: rooms, from the largest object to the least; the arrangement of colours,
3740: the elegant variety and contrast obtained by thrift in trifles, by
3741: delicate hands, clear eyes, and good sense; were at once so pleasant in
3742: themselves, and so expressive of their originator, that, as Mr. Lorry
3743: stood looking about him, the very chairs and tables seemed to ask him,
3744: with something of that peculiar expression which he knew so well by this
3745: time, whether he approved?
3746: 
3747: There were three rooms on a floor, and, the doors by which they
3748: communicated being put open that the air might pass freely through them
3749: all, Mr. Lorry, smilingly observant of that fanciful resemblance which
3750: he detected all around him, walked from one to another. The first was
3751: the best room, and in it were Lucie's birds, and flowers, and books,
3752: and desk, and work-table, and box of water-colours; the second was
3753: the Doctor's consulting-room, used also as the dining-room; the third,
3754: changingly speckled by the rustle of the plane-tree in the yard, was the
3755: Doctor's bedroom, and there, in a corner, stood the disused shoemaker's
3756: bench and tray of tools, much as it had stood on the fifth floor of the
3757: dismal house by the wine-shop, in the suburb of Saint Antoine in Paris.
3758: 
3759: "I wonder," said Mr. Lorry, pausing in his looking about, "that he keeps
3760: that reminder of his sufferings about him!"
3761: 
3762: "And why wonder at that?" was the abrupt inquiry that made him start.
3763: 
3764: It proceeded from Miss Pross, the wild red woman, strong of hand, whose
3765: acquaintance he had first made at the Royal George Hotel at Dover, and
3766: had since improved.
3767: 
3768: "I should have thought--" Mr. Lorry began.
3769: 
3770: "Pooh! You'd have thought!" said Miss Pross; and Mr. Lorry left off.
3771: 
3772: "How do you do?" inquired that lady then--sharply, and yet as if to
3773: express that she bore him no malice.
3774: 
3775: "I am pretty well, I thank you," answered Mr. Lorry, with meekness; "how
3776: are you?"
3777: 
3778: "Nothing to boast of," said Miss Pross.
3779: 
3780: "Indeed?"
3781: 
3782: "Ah! indeed!" said Miss Pross. "I am very much put out about my
3783: Ladybird."
3784: 
3785: "Indeed?"
3786: 
3787: "For gracious sake say something else besides 'indeed,' or you'll
3788: fidget me to death," said Miss Pross: whose character (dissociated from
3789: stature) was shortness.
3790: 
3791: "Really, then?" said Mr. Lorry, as an amendment.
3792: 
3793: "Really, is bad enough," returned Miss Pross, "but better. Yes, I am
3794: very much put out."
3795: 
3796: "May I ask the cause?"
3797: 
3798: "I don't want dozens of people who are not at all worthy of Ladybird, to
3799: come here looking after her," said Miss Pross.
3800: 
3801: "_Do_ dozens come for that purpose?"
3802: 
3803: "Hundreds," said Miss Pross.
3804: 
3805: It was characteristic of this lady (as of some other people before her
3806: time and since) that whenever her original proposition was questioned,
3807: she exaggerated it.
3808: 
3809: "Dear me!" said Mr. Lorry, as the safest remark he could think of.
3810: 
3811: "I have lived with the darling--or the darling has lived with me, and
3812: paid me for it; which she certainly should never have done, you may take
3813: your affidavit, if I could have afforded to keep either myself or her
3814: for nothing--since she was ten years old. And it's really very hard,"
3815: said Miss Pross.
3816: 
3817: Not seeing with precision what was very hard, Mr. Lorry shook his head;
3818: using that important part of himself as a sort of fairy cloak that would
3819: fit anything.
3820: 
3821: "All sorts of people who are not in the least degree worthy of the pet,
3822: are always turning up," said Miss Pross. "When you began it--"
3823: 
3824: "_I_ began it, Miss Pross?"
3825: 
3826: "Didn't you? Who brought her father to life?"
3827: 
3828: "Oh! If _that_ was beginning it--" said Mr. Lorry.
3829: 
3830: "It wasn't ending it, I suppose? I say, when you began it, it was hard
3831: enough; not that I have any fault to find with Doctor Manette, except
3832: that he is not worthy of such a daughter, which is no imputation on
3833: him, for it was not to be expected that anybody should be, under any
3834: circumstances. But it really is doubly and trebly hard to have crowds
3835: and multitudes of people turning up after him (I could have forgiven
3836: him), to take Ladybird's affections away from me."
3837: 
3838: Mr. Lorry knew Miss Pross to be very jealous, but he also knew her by
3839: this time to be, beneath the service of her eccentricity, one of those
3840: unselfish creatures--found only among women--who will, for pure love and
3841: admiration, bind themselves willing slaves, to youth when they have lost
3842: it, to beauty that they never had, to accomplishments that they were
3843: never fortunate enough to gain, to bright hopes that never shone upon
3844: their own sombre lives. He knew enough of the world to know that there
3845: is nothing in it better than the faithful service of the heart; so
3846: rendered and so free from any mercenary taint, he had such an exalted
3847: respect for it, that in the retributive arrangements made by his own
3848: mind--we all make such arrangements, more or less--he stationed Miss
3849: Pross much nearer to the lower Angels than many ladies immeasurably
3850: better got up both by Nature and Art, who had balances at Tellson's.
3851: 
3852: "There never was, nor will be, but one man worthy of Ladybird," said
3853: Miss Pross; "and that was my brother Solomon, if he hadn't made a
3854: mistake in life."
3855: 
3856: Here again: Mr. Lorry's inquiries into Miss Pross's personal history had
3857: established the fact that her brother Solomon was a heartless scoundrel
3858: who had stripped her of everything she possessed, as a stake to
3859: speculate with, and had abandoned her in her poverty for evermore, with
3860: no touch of compunction. Miss Pross's fidelity of belief in Solomon
3861: (deducting a mere trifle for this slight mistake) was quite a serious
3862: matter with Mr. Lorry, and had its weight in his good opinion of her.
3863: 
3864: "As we happen to be alone for the moment, and are both people of
3865: business," he said, when they had got back to the drawing-room and had
3866: sat down there in friendly relations, "let me ask you--does the Doctor,
3867: in talking with Lucie, never refer to the shoemaking time, yet?"
3868: 
3869: "Never."
3870: 
3871: "And yet keeps that bench and those tools beside him?"
3872: 
3873: "Ah!" returned Miss Pross, shaking her head. "But I don't say he don't
3874: refer to it within himself."
3875: 
3876: "Do you believe that he thinks of it much?"
3877: 
3878: "I do," said Miss Pross.
3879: 
3880: "Do you imagine--" Mr. Lorry had begun, when Miss Pross took him up
3881: short with:
3882: 
3883: "Never imagine anything. Have no imagination at all."
3884: 
3885: "I stand corrected; do you suppose--you go so far as to suppose,
3886: sometimes?"
3887: 
3888: "Now and then," said Miss Pross.
3889: 
3890: "Do you suppose," Mr. Lorry went on, with a laughing twinkle in his
3891: bright eye, as it looked kindly at her, "that Doctor Manette has any
3892: theory of his own, preserved through all those years, relative to
3893: the cause of his being so oppressed; perhaps, even to the name of his
3894: oppressor?"
3895: 
3896: "I don't suppose anything about it but what Ladybird tells me."
3897: 
3898: "And that is--?"
3899: 
3900: "That she thinks he has."
3901: 
3902: "Now don't be angry at my asking all these questions; because I am a
3903: mere dull man of business, and you are a woman of business."
3904: 
3905: "Dull?" Miss Pross inquired, with placidity.
3906: 
3907: Rather wishing his modest adjective away, Mr. Lorry replied, "No, no,
3908: no. Surely not. To return to business:--Is it not remarkable that Doctor
3909: Manette, unquestionably innocent of any crime as we are all well assured
3910: he is, should never touch upon that question? I will not say with me,
3911: though he had business relations with me many years ago, and we are now
3912: intimate; I will say with the fair daughter to whom he is so devotedly
3913: attached, and who is so devotedly attached to him? Believe me, Miss
3914: Pross, I don't approach the topic with you, out of curiosity, but out of
3915: zealous interest."
3916: 
3917: "Well! To the best of my understanding, and bad's the best, you'll tell
3918: me," said Miss Pross, softened by the tone of the apology, "he is afraid
3919: of the whole subject."
3920: 
3921: "Afraid?"
3922: 
3923: "It's plain enough, I should think, why he may be. It's a dreadful
3924: remembrance. Besides that, his loss of himself grew out of it. Not
3925: knowing how he lost himself, or how he recovered himself, he may never
3926: feel certain of not losing himself again. That alone wouldn't make the
3927: subject pleasant, I should think."
3928: 
3929: It was a profounder remark than Mr. Lorry had looked for. "True," said
3930: he, "and fearful to reflect upon. Yet, a doubt lurks in my mind, Miss
3931: Pross, whether it is good for Doctor Manette to have that suppression
3932: always shut up within him. Indeed, it is this doubt and the uneasiness
3933: it sometimes causes me that has led me to our present confidence."
3934: 
3935: "Can't be helped," said Miss Pross, shaking her head. "Touch that
3936: string, and he instantly changes for the worse. Better leave it alone.
3937: In short, must leave it alone, like or no like. Sometimes, he gets up in
3938: the dead of the night, and will be heard, by us overhead there, walking
3939: up and down, walking up and down, in his room. Ladybird has learnt to
3940: know then that his mind is walking up and down, walking up and down, in
3941: his old prison. She hurries to him, and they go on together, walking up
3942: and down, walking up and down, until he is composed. But he never says
3943: a word of the true reason of his restlessness, to her, and she finds it
3944: best not to hint at it to him. In silence they go walking up and down
3945: together, walking up and down together, till her love and company have
3946: brought him to himself."
3947: 
3948: Notwithstanding Miss Pross's denial of her own imagination, there was a
3949: perception of the pain of being monotonously haunted by one sad idea,
3950: in her repetition of the phrase, walking up and down, which testified to
3951: her possessing such a thing.
3952: 
3953: The corner has been mentioned as a wonderful corner for echoes; it
3954: had begun to echo so resoundingly to the tread of coming feet, that it
3955: seemed as though the very mention of that weary pacing to and fro had
3956: set it going.
3957: 
3958: "Here they are!" said Miss Pross, rising to break up the conference;
3959: "and now we shall have hundreds of people pretty soon!"
3960: 
3961: It was such a curious corner in its acoustical properties, such a
3962: peculiar Ear of a place, that as Mr. Lorry stood at the open window,
3963: looking for the father and daughter whose steps he heard, he fancied
3964: they would never approach. Not only would the echoes die away, as though
3965: the steps had gone; but, echoes of other steps that never came would be
3966: heard in their stead, and would die away for good when they seemed close
3967: at hand. However, father and daughter did at last appear, and Miss Pross
3968: was ready at the street door to receive them.
3969: 
3970: Miss Pross was a pleasant sight, albeit wild, and red, and grim, taking
3971: off her darling's bonnet when she came up-stairs, and touching it up
3972: with the ends of her handkerchief, and blowing the dust off it, and
3973: folding her mantle ready for laying by, and smoothing her rich hair with
3974: as much pride as she could possibly have taken in her own hair if she
3975: had been the vainest and handsomest of women. Her darling was a pleasant
3976: sight too, embracing her and thanking her, and protesting against
3977: her taking so much trouble for her--which last she only dared to do
3978: playfully, or Miss Pross, sorely hurt, would have retired to her own
3979: chamber and cried. The Doctor was a pleasant sight too, looking on at
3980: them, and telling Miss Pross how she spoilt Lucie, in accents and with
3981: eyes that had as much spoiling in them as Miss Pross had, and would
3982: have had more if it were possible. Mr. Lorry was a pleasant sight too,
3983: beaming at all this in his little wig, and thanking his bachelor
3984: stars for having lighted him in his declining years to a Home. But, no
3985: Hundreds of people came to see the sights, and Mr. Lorry looked in vain
3986: for the fulfilment of Miss Pross's prediction.
3987: 
3988: Dinner-time, and still no Hundreds of people. In the arrangements of
3989: the little household, Miss Pross took charge of the lower regions, and
3990: always acquitted herself marvellously. Her dinners, of a very modest
3991: quality, were so well cooked and so well served, and so neat in their
3992: contrivances, half English and half French, that nothing could be
3993: better. Miss Pross's friendship being of the thoroughly practical
3994: kind, she had ravaged Soho and the adjacent provinces, in search of
3995: impoverished French, who, tempted by shillings and half-crowns, would
3996: impart culinary mysteries to her. From these decayed sons and daughters
3997: of Gaul, she had acquired such wonderful arts, that the woman and girl
3998: who formed the staff of domestics regarded her as quite a Sorceress,
3999: or Cinderella's Godmother: who would send out for a fowl, a rabbit,
4000: a vegetable or two from the garden, and change them into anything she
4001: pleased.
4002: 
4003: On Sundays, Miss Pross dined at the Doctor's table, but on other days
4004: persisted in taking her meals at unknown periods, either in the lower
4005: regions, or in her own room on the second floor--a blue chamber, to
4006: which no one but her Ladybird ever gained admittance. On this occasion,
4007: Miss Pross, responding to Ladybird's pleasant face and pleasant efforts
4008: to please her, unbent exceedingly; so the dinner was very pleasant, too.
4009: 
4010: It was an oppressive day, and, after dinner, Lucie proposed that the
4011: wine should be carried out under the plane-tree, and they should sit
4012: there in the air. As everything turned upon her, and revolved about her,
4013: they went out under the plane-tree, and she carried the wine down for
4014: the special benefit of Mr. Lorry. She had installed herself, some
4015: time before, as Mr. Lorry's cup-bearer; and while they sat under the
4016: plane-tree, talking, she kept his glass replenished. Mysterious backs
4017: and ends of houses peeped at them as they talked, and the plane-tree
4018: whispered to them in its own way above their heads.
4019: 
4020: Still, the Hundreds of people did not present themselves. Mr. Darnay
4021: presented himself while they were sitting under the plane-tree, but he
4022: was only One.
4023: 
4024: Doctor Manette received him kindly, and so did Lucie. But, Miss Pross
4025: suddenly became afflicted with a twitching in the head and body, and
4026: retired into the house. She was not unfrequently the victim of this
4027: disorder, and she called it, in familiar conversation, "a fit of the
4028: jerks."
4029: 
4030: The Doctor was in his best condition, and looked specially young. The
4031: resemblance between him and Lucie was very strong at such times, and as
4032: they sat side by side, she leaning on his shoulder, and he resting
4033: his arm on the back of her chair, it was very agreeable to trace the
4034: likeness.
4035: 
4036: He had been talking all day, on many subjects, and with unusual
4037: vivacity. "Pray, Doctor Manette," said Mr. Darnay, as they sat under the
4038: plane-tree--and he said it in the natural pursuit of the topic in hand,
4039: which happened to be the old buildings of London--"have you seen much of
4040: the Tower?"
4041: 
4042: "Lucie and I have been there; but only casually. We have seen enough of
4043: it, to know that it teems with interest; little more."
4044: 
4045: "_I_ have been there, as you remember," said Darnay, with a smile,
4046: though reddening a little angrily, "in another character, and not in a
4047: character that gives facilities for seeing much of it. They told me a
4048: curious thing when I was there."
4049: 
4050: "What was that?" Lucie asked.
4051: 
4052: "In making some alterations, the workmen came upon an old dungeon, which
4053: had been, for many years, built up and forgotten. Every stone of
4054: its inner wall was covered by inscriptions which had been carved by
4055: prisoners--dates, names, complaints, and prayers. Upon a corner stone
4056: in an angle of the wall, one prisoner, who seemed to have gone to
4057: execution, had cut as his last work, three letters. They were done with
4058: some very poor instrument, and hurriedly, with an unsteady hand.
4059: At first, they were read as D. I. C.; but, on being more carefully
4060: examined, the last letter was found to be G. There was no record or
4061: legend of any prisoner with those initials, and many fruitless guesses
4062: were made what the name could have been. At length, it was suggested
4063: that the letters were not initials, but the complete word, DIG. The
4064: floor was examined very carefully under the inscription, and, in the
4065: earth beneath a stone, or tile, or some fragment of paving, were found
4066: the ashes of a paper, mingled with the ashes of a small leathern case
4067: or bag. What the unknown prisoner had written will never be read, but he
4068: had written something, and hidden it away to keep it from the gaoler."
4069: 
4070: "My father," exclaimed Lucie, "you are ill!"
4071: 
4072: He had suddenly started up, with his hand to his head. His manner and
4073: his look quite terrified them all.
4074: 
4075: "No, my dear, not ill. There are large drops of rain falling, and they
4076: made me start. We had better go in."
4077: 
4078: He recovered himself almost instantly. Rain was really falling in large
4079: drops, and he showed the back of his hand with rain-drops on it. But, he
4080: said not a single word in reference to the discovery that had been told
4081: of, and, as they went into the house, the business eye of Mr. Lorry
4082: either detected, or fancied it detected, on his face, as it turned
4083: towards Charles Darnay, the same singular look that had been upon it
4084: when it turned towards him in the passages of the Court House.
4085: 
4086: He recovered himself so quickly, however, that Mr. Lorry had doubts of
4087: his business eye. The arm of the golden giant in the hall was not more
4088: steady than he was, when he stopped under it to remark to them that he
4089: was not yet proof against slight surprises (if he ever would be), and
4090: that the rain had startled him.
4091: 
4092: Tea-time, and Miss Pross making tea, with another fit of the jerks upon
4093: her, and yet no Hundreds of people. Mr. Carton had lounged in, but he
4094: made only Two.
4095: 
4096: The night was so very sultry, that although they sat with doors and
4097: windows open, they were overpowered by heat. When the tea-table was
4098: done with, they all moved to one of the windows, and looked out into the
4099: heavy twilight. Lucie sat by her father; Darnay sat beside her; Carton
4100: leaned against a window. The curtains were long and white, and some of
4101: the thunder-gusts that whirled into the corner, caught them up to the
4102: ceiling, and waved them like spectral wings.
4103: 
4104: "The rain-drops are still falling, large, heavy, and few," said Doctor
4105: Manette. "It comes slowly."
4106: 
4107: "It comes surely," said Carton.
4108: 
4109: They spoke low, as people watching and waiting mostly do; as people in a
4110: dark room, watching and waiting for Lightning, always do.
4111: 
4112: There was a great hurry in the streets of people speeding away to
4113: get shelter before the storm broke; the wonderful corner for echoes
4114: resounded with the echoes of footsteps coming and going, yet not a
4115: footstep was there.
4116: 
4117: "A multitude of people, and yet a solitude!" said Darnay, when they had
4118: listened for a while.
4119: 
4120: "Is it not impressive, Mr. Darnay?" asked Lucie. "Sometimes, I have
4121: sat here of an evening, until I have fancied--but even the shade of
4122: a foolish fancy makes me shudder to-night, when all is so black and
4123: solemn--"
4124: 
4125: "Let us shudder too. We may know what it is."
4126: 
4127: "It will seem nothing to you. Such whims are only impressive as we
4128: originate them, I think; they are not to be communicated. I have
4129: sometimes sat alone here of an evening, listening, until I have made
4130: the echoes out to be the echoes of all the footsteps that are coming
4131: by-and-bye into our lives."
4132: 
4133: "There is a great crowd coming one day into our lives, if that be so,"
4134: Sydney Carton struck in, in his moody way.
4135: 
4136: The footsteps were incessant, and the hurry of them became more and more
4137: rapid. The corner echoed and re-echoed with the tread of feet; some,
4138: as it seemed, under the windows; some, as it seemed, in the room; some
4139: coming, some going, some breaking off, some stopping altogether; all in
4140: the distant streets, and not one within sight.
4141: 
4142: "Are all these footsteps destined to come to all of us, Miss Manette, or
4143: are we to divide them among us?"
4144: 
4145: "I don't know, Mr. Darnay; I told you it was a foolish fancy, but you
4146: asked for it. When I have yielded myself to it, I have been alone, and
4147: then I have imagined them the footsteps of the people who are to come
4148: into my life, and my father's."
4149: 
4150: "I take them into mine!" said Carton. "_I_ ask no questions and make no
4151: stipulations. There is a great crowd bearing down upon us, Miss Manette,
4152: and I see them--by the Lightning." He added the last words, after there
4153: had been a vivid flash which had shown him lounging in the window.
4154: 
4155: "And I hear them!" he added again, after a peal of thunder. "Here they
4156: come, fast, fierce, and furious!"
4157: 
4158: It was the rush and roar of rain that he typified, and it stopped him,
4159: for no voice could be heard in it. A memorable storm of thunder and
4160: lightning broke with that sweep of water, and there was not a moment's
4161: interval in crash, and fire, and rain, until after the moon rose at
4162: midnight.
4163: 
4164: The great bell of Saint Paul's was striking one in the cleared air, when
4165: Mr. Lorry, escorted by Jerry, high-booted and bearing a lantern, set
4166: forth on his return-passage to Clerkenwell. There were solitary patches
4167: of road on the way between Soho and Clerkenwell, and Mr. Lorry, mindful
4168: of foot-pads, always retained Jerry for this service: though it was
4169: usually performed a good two hours earlier.
4170: 
4171: "What a night it has been! Almost a night, Jerry," said Mr. Lorry, "to
4172: bring the dead out of their graves."
4173: 
4174: "I never see the night myself, master--nor yet I don't expect to--what
4175: would do that," answered Jerry.
4176: 
4177: "Good night, Mr. Carton," said the man of business. "Good night, Mr.
4178: Darnay. Shall we ever see such a night again, together!"
4179: 
4180: Perhaps. Perhaps, see the great crowd of people with its rush and roar,
4181: bearing down upon them, too.
4182: 
4183: 
4184: 
4185: 
4186: VII. Monseigneur in Town
4187: 
4188: 
4189: Monseigneur, one of the great lords in power at the Court, held his
4190: fortnightly reception in his grand hotel in Paris. Monseigneur was in
4191: his inner room, his sanctuary of sanctuaries, the Holiest of Holiests to
4192: the crowd of worshippers in the suite of rooms without. Monseigneur
4193: was about to take his chocolate. Monseigneur could swallow a great many
4194: things with ease, and was by some few sullen minds supposed to be rather
4195: rapidly swallowing France; but, his morning's chocolate could not so
4196: much as get into the throat of Monseigneur, without the aid of four
4197: strong men besides the Cook.
4198: 
4199: Yes. It took four men, all four ablaze with gorgeous decoration, and the
4200: Chief of them unable to exist with fewer than two gold watches in his
4201: pocket, emulative of the noble and chaste fashion set by Monseigneur, to
4202: conduct the happy chocolate to Monseigneur's lips. One lacquey carried
4203: the chocolate-pot into the sacred presence; a second, milled and frothed
4204: the chocolate with the little instrument he bore for that function;
4205: a third, presented the favoured napkin; a fourth (he of the two gold
4206: watches), poured the chocolate out. It was impossible for Monseigneur to
4207: dispense with one of these attendants on the chocolate and hold his high
4208: place under the admiring Heavens. Deep would have been the blot upon
4209: his escutcheon if his chocolate had been ignobly waited on by only three
4210: men; he must have died of two.
4211: 
4212: Monseigneur had been out at a little supper last night, where the Comedy
4213: and the Grand Opera were charmingly represented. Monseigneur was out at
4214: a little supper most nights, with fascinating company. So polite and so
4215: impressible was Monseigneur, that the Comedy and the Grand Opera had far
4216: more influence with him in the tiresome articles of state affairs and
4217: state secrets, than the needs of all France. A happy circumstance
4218: for France, as the like always is for all countries similarly
4219: favoured!--always was for England (by way of example), in the regretted
4220: days of the merry Stuart who sold it.
4221: 
4222: Monseigneur had one truly noble idea of general public business, which
4223: was, to let everything go on in its own way; of particular public
4224: business, Monseigneur had the other truly noble idea that it must all go
4225: his way--tend to his own power and pocket. Of his pleasures, general and
4226: particular, Monseigneur had the other truly noble idea, that the world
4227: was made for them. The text of his order (altered from the original
4228: by only a pronoun, which is not much) ran: "The earth and the fulness
4229: thereof are mine, saith Monseigneur."
4230: 
4231: Yet, Monseigneur had slowly found that vulgar embarrassments crept into
4232: his affairs, both private and public; and he had, as to both classes of
4233: affairs, allied himself perforce with a Farmer-General. As to finances
4234: public, because Monseigneur could not make anything at all of them, and
4235: must consequently let them out to somebody who could; as to finances
4236: private, because Farmer-Generals were rich, and Monseigneur, after
4237: generations of great luxury and expense, was growing poor. Hence
4238: Monseigneur had taken his sister from a convent, while there was yet
4239: time to ward off the impending veil, the cheapest garment she could
4240: wear, and had bestowed her as a prize upon a very rich Farmer-General,
4241: poor in family. Which Farmer-General, carrying an appropriate cane with
4242: a golden apple on the top of it, was now among the company in the outer
4243: rooms, much prostrated before by mankind--always excepting superior
4244: mankind of the blood of Monseigneur, who, his own wife included, looked
4245: down upon him with the loftiest contempt.
4246: 
4247: A sumptuous man was the Farmer-General. Thirty horses stood in his
4248: stables, twenty-four male domestics sat in his halls, six body-women
4249: waited on his wife. As one who pretended to do nothing but plunder and
4250: forage where he could, the Farmer-General--howsoever his matrimonial
4251: relations conduced to social morality--was at least the greatest reality
4252: among the personages who attended at the hotel of Monseigneur that day.
4253: 
4254: For, the rooms, though a beautiful scene to look at, and adorned with
4255: every device of decoration that the taste and skill of the time could
4256: achieve, were, in truth, not a sound business; considered with any
4257: reference to the scarecrows in the rags and nightcaps elsewhere (and not
4258: so far off, either, but that the watching towers of Notre Dame, almost
4259: equidistant from the two extremes, could see them both), they would
4260: have been an exceedingly uncomfortable business--if that could have
4261: been anybody's business, at the house of Monseigneur. Military officers
4262: destitute of military knowledge; naval officers with no idea of a ship;
4263: civil officers without a notion of affairs; brazen ecclesiastics, of the
4264: worst world worldly, with sensual eyes, loose tongues, and looser lives;
4265: all totally unfit for their several callings, all lying horribly in
4266: pretending to belong to them, but all nearly or remotely of the order of
4267: Monseigneur, and therefore foisted on all public employments from which
4268: anything was to be got; these were to be told off by the score and the
4269: score. People not immediately connected with Monseigneur or the State,
4270: yet equally unconnected with anything that was real, or with lives
4271: passed in travelling by any straight road to any true earthly end, were
4272: no less abundant. Doctors who made great fortunes out of dainty remedies
4273: for imaginary disorders that never existed, smiled upon their courtly
4274: patients in the ante-chambers of Monseigneur. Projectors who had
4275: discovered every kind of remedy for the little evils with which the
4276: State was touched, except the remedy of setting to work in earnest to
4277: root out a single sin, poured their distracting babble into any ears
4278: they could lay hold of, at the reception of Monseigneur. Unbelieving
4279: Philosophers who were remodelling the world with words, and making
4280: card-towers of Babel to scale the skies with, talked with Unbelieving
4281: Chemists who had an eye on the transmutation of metals, at this
4282: wonderful gathering accumulated by Monseigneur. Exquisite gentlemen of
4283: the finest breeding, which was at that remarkable time--and has been
4284: since--to be known by its fruits of indifference to every natural
4285: subject of human interest, were in the most exemplary state of
4286: exhaustion, at the hotel of Monseigneur. Such homes had these various
4287: notabilities left behind them in the fine world of Paris, that the spies
4288: among the assembled devotees of Monseigneur--forming a goodly half
4289: of the polite company--would have found it hard to discover among
4290: the angels of that sphere one solitary wife, who, in her manners and
4291: appearance, owned to being a Mother. Indeed, except for the mere act of
4292: bringing a troublesome creature into this world--which does not go far
4293: towards the realisation of the name of mother--there was no such thing
4294: known to the fashion. Peasant women kept the unfashionable babies close,
4295: and brought them up, and charming grandmammas of sixty dressed and
4296: supped as at twenty.
4297: 
4298: The leprosy of unreality disfigured every human creature in attendance
4299: upon Monseigneur. In the outermost room were half a dozen exceptional
4300: people who had had, for a few years, some vague misgiving in them that
4301: things in general were going rather wrong. As a promising way of setting
4302: them right, half of the half-dozen had become members of a fantastic
4303: sect of Convulsionists, and were even then considering within themselves
4304: whether they should foam, rage, roar, and turn cataleptic on the
4305: spot--thereby setting up a highly intelligible finger-post to the
4306: Future, for Monseigneur's guidance. Besides these Dervishes, were other
4307: three who had rushed into another sect, which mended matters with a
4308: jargon about "the Centre of Truth:" holding that Man had got out of the
4309: Centre of Truth--which did not need much demonstration--but had not got
4310: out of the Circumference, and that he was to be kept from flying out of
4311: the Circumference, and was even to be shoved back into the Centre,
4312: by fasting and seeing of spirits. Among these, accordingly, much
4313: discoursing with spirits went on--and it did a world of good which never
4314: became manifest.
4315: 
4316: But, the comfort was, that all the company at the grand hotel of
4317: Monseigneur were perfectly dressed. If the Day of Judgment had only been
4318: ascertained to be a dress day, everybody there would have been eternally
4319: correct. Such frizzling and powdering and sticking up of hair, such
4320: delicate complexions artificially preserved and mended, such gallant
4321: swords to look at, and such delicate honour to the sense of smell, would
4322: surely keep anything going, for ever and ever. The exquisite gentlemen
4323: of the finest breeding wore little pendent trinkets that chinked as they
4324: languidly moved; these golden fetters rang like precious little bells;
4325: and what with that ringing, and with the rustle of silk and brocade and
4326: fine linen, there was a flutter in the air that fanned Saint Antoine and
4327: his devouring hunger far away.
4328: 
4329: Dress was the one unfailing talisman and charm used for keeping all
4330: things in their places. Everybody was dressed for a Fancy Ball that
4331: was never to leave off. From the Palace of the Tuileries, through
4332: Monseigneur and the whole Court, through the Chambers, the Tribunals
4333: of Justice, and all society (except the scarecrows), the Fancy Ball
4334: descended to the Common Executioner: who, in pursuance of the charm, was
4335: required to officiate "frizzled, powdered, in a gold-laced coat, pumps,
4336: and white silk stockings." At the gallows and the wheel--the axe was a
4337: rarity--Monsieur Paris, as it was the episcopal mode among his brother
4338: Professors of the provinces, Monsieur Orleans, and the rest, to call
4339: him, presided in this dainty dress. And who among the company at
4340: Monseigneur's reception in that seventeen hundred and eightieth year
4341: of our Lord, could possibly doubt, that a system rooted in a frizzled
4342: hangman, powdered, gold-laced, pumped, and white-silk stockinged, would
4343: see the very stars out!
4344: 
4345: Monseigneur having eased his four men of their burdens and taken his
4346: chocolate, caused the doors of the Holiest of Holiests to be thrown
4347: open, and issued forth. Then, what submission, what cringing and
4348: fawning, what servility, what abject humiliation! As to bowing down in
4349: body and spirit, nothing in that way was left for Heaven--which may have
4350: been one among other reasons why the worshippers of Monseigneur never
4351: troubled it.
4352: 
4353: Bestowing a word of promise here and a smile there, a whisper on one
4354: happy slave and a wave of the hand on another, Monseigneur affably
4355: passed through his rooms to the remote region of the Circumference of
4356: Truth. There, Monseigneur turned, and came back again, and so in due
4357: course of time got himself shut up in his sanctuary by the chocolate
4358: sprites, and was seen no more.
4359: 
4360: The show being over, the flutter in the air became quite a little storm,
4361: and the precious little bells went ringing downstairs. There was soon
4362: but one person left of all the crowd, and he, with his hat under his arm
4363: and his snuff-box in his hand, slowly passed among the mirrors on his
4364: way out.
4365: 
4366: "I devote you," said this person, stopping at the last door on his way,
4367: and turning in the direction of the sanctuary, "to the Devil!"
4368: 
4369: With that, he shook the snuff from his fingers as if he had shaken the
4370: dust from his feet, and quietly walked downstairs.
4371: 
4372: He was a man of about sixty, handsomely dressed, haughty in manner, and
4373: with a face like a fine mask. A face of a transparent paleness; every
4374: feature in it clearly defined; one set expression on it. The nose,
4375: beautifully formed otherwise, was very slightly pinched at the top
4376: of each nostril. In those two compressions, or dints, the only little
4377: change that the face ever showed, resided. They persisted in changing
4378: colour sometimes, and they would be occasionally dilated and contracted
4379: by something like a faint pulsation; then, they gave a look of
4380: treachery, and cruelty, to the whole countenance. Examined with
4381: attention, its capacity of helping such a look was to be found in the
4382: line of the mouth, and the lines of the orbits of the eyes, being much
4383: too horizontal and thin; still, in the effect of the face made, it was a
4384: handsome face, and a remarkable one.
4385: 
4386: Its owner went downstairs into the courtyard, got into his carriage, and
4387: drove away. Not many people had talked with him at the reception; he had
4388: stood in a little space apart, and Monseigneur might have been warmer
4389: in his manner. It appeared, under the circumstances, rather agreeable
4390: to him to see the common people dispersed before his horses, and
4391: often barely escaping from being run down. His man drove as if he were
4392: charging an enemy, and the furious recklessness of the man brought no
4393: check into the face, or to the lips, of the master. The complaint had
4394: sometimes made itself audible, even in that deaf city and dumb age,
4395: that, in the narrow streets without footways, the fierce patrician
4396: custom of hard driving endangered and maimed the mere vulgar in a
4397: barbarous manner. But, few cared enough for that to think of it a second
4398: time, and, in this matter, as in all others, the common wretches were
4399: left to get out of their difficulties as they could.
4400:
