4401: With a wild rattle and clatter, and an inhuman abandonment of
4402: consideration not easy to be understood in these days, the carriage
4403: dashed through streets and swept round corners, with women screaming
4404: before it, and men clutching each other and clutching children out of
4405: its way. At last, swooping at a street corner by a fountain, one of its
4406: wheels came to a sickening little jolt, and there was a loud cry from a
4407: number of voices, and the horses reared and plunged.
4408: 
4409: But for the latter inconvenience, the carriage probably would not have
4410: stopped; carriages were often known to drive on, and leave their wounded
4411: behind, and why not? But the frightened valet had got down in a hurry,
4412: and there were twenty hands at the horses' bridles.
4413: 
4414: "What has gone wrong?" said Monsieur, calmly looking out.
4415: 
4416: A tall man in a nightcap had caught up a bundle from among the feet of
4417: the horses, and had laid it on the basement of the fountain, and was
4418: down in the mud and wet, howling over it like a wild animal.
4419: 
4420: "Pardon, Monsieur the Marquis!" said a ragged and submissive man, "it is
4421: a child."
4422: 
4423: "Why does he make that abominable noise? Is it his child?"
4424: 
4425: "Excuse me, Monsieur the Marquis--it is a pity--yes."
4426: 
4427: The fountain was a little removed; for the street opened, where it was,
4428: into a space some ten or twelve yards square. As the tall man suddenly
4429: got up from the ground, and came running at the carriage, Monsieur the
4430: Marquis clapped his hand for an instant on his sword-hilt.
4431: 
4432: "Killed!" shrieked the man, in wild desperation, extending both arms at
4433: their length above his head, and staring at him. "Dead!"
4434: 
4435: The people closed round, and looked at Monsieur the Marquis. There was
4436: nothing revealed by the many eyes that looked at him but watchfulness
4437: and eagerness; there was no visible menacing or anger. Neither did the
4438: people say anything; after the first cry, they had been silent, and they
4439: remained so. The voice of the submissive man who had spoken, was flat
4440: and tame in its extreme submission. Monsieur the Marquis ran his eyes
4441: over them all, as if they had been mere rats come out of their holes.
4442: 
4443: He took out his purse.
4444: 
4445: "It is extraordinary to me," said he, "that you people cannot take care
4446: of yourselves and your children. One or the other of you is for ever in
4447: the way. How do I know what injury you have done my horses. See! Give
4448: him that."
4449: 
4450: He threw out a gold coin for the valet to pick up, and all the heads
4451: craned forward that all the eyes might look down at it as it fell. The
4452: tall man called out again with a most unearthly cry, "Dead!"
4453: 
4454: He was arrested by the quick arrival of another man, for whom the rest
4455: made way. On seeing him, the miserable creature fell upon his shoulder,
4456: sobbing and crying, and pointing to the fountain, where some women were
4457: stooping over the motionless bundle, and moving gently about it. They
4458: were as silent, however, as the men.
4459: 
4460: "I know all, I know all," said the last comer. "Be a brave man, my
4461: Gaspard! It is better for the poor little plaything to die so, than to
4462: live. It has died in a moment without pain. Could it have lived an hour
4463: as happily?"
4464: 
4465: "You are a philosopher, you there," said the Marquis, smiling. "How do
4466: they call you?"
4467: 
4468: "They call me Defarge."
4469: 
4470: "Of what trade?"
4471: 
4472: "Monsieur the Marquis, vendor of wine."
4473: 
4474: "Pick up that, philosopher and vendor of wine," said the Marquis,
4475: throwing him another gold coin, "and spend it as you will. The horses
4476: there; are they right?"
4477: 
4478: Without deigning to look at the assemblage a second time, Monsieur the
4479: Marquis leaned back in his seat, and was just being driven away with the
4480: air of a gentleman who had accidentally broke some common thing, and had
4481: paid for it, and could afford to pay for it; when his ease was suddenly
4482: disturbed by a coin flying into his carriage, and ringing on its floor.
4483: 
4484: "Hold!" said Monsieur the Marquis. "Hold the horses! Who threw that?"
4485: 
4486: He looked to the spot where Defarge the vendor of wine had stood, a
4487: moment before; but the wretched father was grovelling on his face on
4488: the pavement in that spot, and the figure that stood beside him was the
4489: figure of a dark stout woman, knitting.
4490: 
4491: "You dogs!" said the Marquis, but smoothly, and with an unchanged front,
4492: except as to the spots on his nose: "I would ride over any of you very
4493: willingly, and exterminate you from the earth. If I knew which rascal
4494: threw at the carriage, and if that brigand were sufficiently near it, he
4495: should be crushed under the wheels."
4496: 
4497: So cowed was their condition, and so long and hard their experience of
4498: what such a man could do to them, within the law and beyond it, that not
4499: a voice, or a hand, or even an eye was raised. Among the men, not one.
4500: But the woman who stood knitting looked up steadily, and looked the
4501: Marquis in the face. It was not for his dignity to notice it; his
4502: contemptuous eyes passed over her, and over all the other rats; and he
4503: leaned back in his seat again, and gave the word "Go on!"
4504: 
4505: He was driven on, and other carriages came whirling by in quick
4506: succession; the Minister, the State-Projector, the Farmer-General, the
4507: Doctor, the Lawyer, the Ecclesiastic, the Grand Opera, the Comedy, the
4508: whole Fancy Ball in a bright continuous flow, came whirling by. The rats
4509: had crept out of their holes to look on, and they remained looking
4510: on for hours; soldiers and police often passing between them and the
4511: spectacle, and making a barrier behind which they slunk, and through
4512: which they peeped. The father had long ago taken up his bundle and
4513: bidden himself away with it, when the women who had tended the bundle
4514: while it lay on the base of the fountain, sat there watching the running
4515: of the water and the rolling of the Fancy Ball--when the one woman who
4516: had stood conspicuous, knitting, still knitted on with the steadfastness
4517: of Fate. The water of the fountain ran, the swift river ran, the day ran
4518: into evening, so much life in the city ran into death according to rule,
4519: time and tide waited for no man, the rats were sleeping close together
4520: in their dark holes again, the Fancy Ball was lighted up at supper, all
4521: things ran their course.
4522: 
4523: 
4524: 
4525: 
4526: VIII. Monseigneur in the Country
4527: 
4528: 
4529: A beautiful landscape, with the corn bright in it, but not abundant.
4530: Patches of poor rye where corn should have been, patches of poor peas
4531: and beans, patches of most coarse vegetable substitutes for wheat. On
4532: inanimate nature, as on the men and women who cultivated it, a prevalent
4533: tendency towards an appearance of vegetating unwillingly--a dejected
4534: disposition to give up, and wither away.
4535: 
4536: Monsieur the Marquis in his travelling carriage (which might have been
4537: lighter), conducted by four post-horses and two postilions, fagged up
4538: a steep hill. A blush on the countenance of Monsieur the Marquis was
4539: no impeachment of his high breeding; it was not from within; it was
4540: occasioned by an external circumstance beyond his control--the setting
4541: sun.
4542: 
4543: The sunset struck so brilliantly into the travelling carriage when it
4544: gained the hill-top, that its occupant was steeped in crimson. "It will
4545: die out," said Monsieur the Marquis, glancing at his hands, "directly."
4546: 
4547: In effect, the sun was so low that it dipped at the moment. When the
4548: heavy drag had been adjusted to the wheel, and the carriage slid down
4549: hill, with a cinderous smell, in a cloud of dust, the red glow departed
4550: quickly; the sun and the Marquis going down together, there was no glow
4551: left when the drag was taken off.
4552: 
4553: But, there remained a broken country, bold and open, a little village
4554: at the bottom of the hill, a broad sweep and rise beyond it, a
4555: church-tower, a windmill, a forest for the chase, and a crag with a
4556: fortress on it used as a prison. Round upon all these darkening objects
4557: as the night drew on, the Marquis looked, with the air of one who was
4558: coming near home.
4559: 
4560: The village had its one poor street, with its poor brewery, poor
4561: tannery, poor tavern, poor stable-yard for relays of post-horses, poor
4562: fountain, all usual poor appointments. It had its poor people too. All
4563: its people were poor, and many of them were sitting at their doors,
4564: shredding spare onions and the like for supper, while many were at the
4565: fountain, washing leaves, and grasses, and any such small yieldings of
4566: the earth that could be eaten. Expressive signs of what made them poor,
4567: were not wanting; the tax for the state, the tax for the church, the tax
4568: for the lord, tax local and tax general, were to be paid here and to be
4569: paid there, according to solemn inscription in the little village, until
4570: the wonder was, that there was any village left unswallowed.
4571: 
4572: Few children were to be seen, and no dogs. As to the men and women,
4573: their choice on earth was stated in the prospect--Life on the lowest
4574: terms that could sustain it, down in the little village under the mill;
4575: or captivity and Death in the dominant prison on the crag.
4576: 
4577: Heralded by a courier in advance, and by the cracking of his postilions'
4578: whips, which twined snake-like about their heads in the evening air, as
4579: if he came attended by the Furies, Monsieur the Marquis drew up in
4580: his travelling carriage at the posting-house gate. It was hard by the
4581: fountain, and the peasants suspended their operations to look at him.
4582: He looked at them, and saw in them, without knowing it, the slow
4583: sure filing down of misery-worn face and figure, that was to make the
4584: meagreness of Frenchmen an English superstition which should survive the
4585: truth through the best part of a hundred years.
4586: 
4587: Monsieur the Marquis cast his eyes over the submissive faces that
4588: drooped before him, as the like of himself had drooped before
4589: Monseigneur of the Court--only the difference was, that these faces
4590: drooped merely to suffer and not to propitiate--when a grizzled mender
4591: of the roads joined the group.
4592: 
4593: "Bring me hither that fellow!" said the Marquis to the courier.
4594: 
4595: The fellow was brought, cap in hand, and the other fellows closed round
4596: to look and listen, in the manner of the people at the Paris fountain.
4597: 
4598: "I passed you on the road?"
4599: 
4600: "Monseigneur, it is true. I had the honour of being passed on the road."
4601: 
4602: "Coming up the hill, and at the top of the hill, both?"
4603: 
4604: "Monseigneur, it is true."
4605: 
4606: "What did you look at, so fixedly?"
4607: 
4608: "Monseigneur, I looked at the man."
4609: 
4610: He stooped a little, and with his tattered blue cap pointed under the
4611: carriage. All his fellows stooped to look under the carriage.
4612: 
4613: "What man, pig? And why look there?"
4614: 
4615: "Pardon, Monseigneur; he swung by the chain of the shoe--the drag."
4616: 
4617: "Who?" demanded the traveller.
4618: 
4619: "Monseigneur, the man."
4620: 
4621: "May the Devil carry away these idiots! How do you call the man? You
4622: know all the men of this part of the country. Who was he?"
4623: 
4624: "Your clemency, Monseigneur! He was not of this part of the country. Of
4625: all the days of my life, I never saw him."
4626: 
4627: "Swinging by the chain? To be suffocated?"
4628: 
4629: "With your gracious permission, that was the wonder of it, Monseigneur.
4630: His head hanging over--like this!"
4631: 
4632: He turned himself sideways to the carriage, and leaned back, with his
4633: face thrown up to the sky, and his head hanging down; then recovered
4634: himself, fumbled with his cap, and made a bow.
4635: 
4636: "What was he like?"
4637: 
4638: "Monseigneur, he was whiter than the miller. All covered with dust,
4639: white as a spectre, tall as a spectre!"
4640: 
4641: The picture produced an immense sensation in the little crowd; but all
4642: eyes, without comparing notes with other eyes, looked at Monsieur
4643: the Marquis. Perhaps, to observe whether he had any spectre on his
4644: conscience.
4645: 
4646: "Truly, you did well," said the Marquis, felicitously sensible that such
4647: vermin were not to ruffle him, "to see a thief accompanying my carriage,
4648: and not open that great mouth of yours. Bah! Put him aside, Monsieur
4649: Gabelle!"
4650: 
4651: Monsieur Gabelle was the Postmaster, and some other taxing functionary
4652: united; he had come out with great obsequiousness to assist at this
4653: examination, and had held the examined by the drapery of his arm in an
4654: official manner.
4655: 
4656: "Bah! Go aside!" said Monsieur Gabelle.
4657: 
4658: "Lay hands on this stranger if he seeks to lodge in your village
4659: to-night, and be sure that his business is honest, Gabelle."
4660: 
4661: "Monseigneur, I am flattered to devote myself to your orders."
4662: 
4663: "Did he run away, fellow?--where is that Accursed?"
4664: 
4665: The accursed was already under the carriage with some half-dozen
4666: particular friends, pointing out the chain with his blue cap. Some
4667: half-dozen other particular friends promptly hauled him out, and
4668: presented him breathless to Monsieur the Marquis.
4669: 
4670: "Did the man run away, Dolt, when we stopped for the drag?"
4671: 
4672: "Monseigneur, he precipitated himself over the hill-side, head first, as
4673: a person plunges into the river."
4674: 
4675: "See to it, Gabelle. Go on!"
4676: 
4677: The half-dozen who were peering at the chain were still among the
4678: wheels, like sheep; the wheels turned so suddenly that they were lucky
4679: to save their skins and bones; they had very little else to save, or
4680: they might not have been so fortunate.
4681: 
4682: The burst with which the carriage started out of the village and up the
4683: rise beyond, was soon checked by the steepness of the hill. Gradually,
4684: it subsided to a foot pace, swinging and lumbering upward among the many
4685: sweet scents of a summer night. The postilions, with a thousand gossamer
4686: gnats circling about them in lieu of the Furies, quietly mended the
4687: points to the lashes of their whips; the valet walked by the horses; the
4688: courier was audible, trotting on ahead into the dull distance.
4689: 
4690: At the steepest point of the hill there was a little burial-ground,
4691: with a Cross and a new large figure of Our Saviour on it; it was a poor
4692: figure in wood, done by some inexperienced rustic carver, but he had
4693: studied the figure from the life--his own life, maybe--for it was
4694: dreadfully spare and thin.
4695: 
4696: To this distressful emblem of a great distress that had long been
4697: growing worse, and was not at its worst, a woman was kneeling. She
4698: turned her head as the carriage came up to her, rose quickly, and
4699: presented herself at the carriage-door.
4700: 
4701: "It is you, Monseigneur! Monseigneur, a petition."
4702: 
4703: With an exclamation of impatience, but with his unchangeable face,
4704: Monseigneur looked out.
4705: 
4706: "How, then! What is it? Always petitions!"
4707: 
4708: "Monseigneur. For the love of the great God! My husband, the forester."
4709: 
4710: "What of your husband, the forester? Always the same with you people. He
4711: cannot pay something?"
4712: 
4713: "He has paid all, Monseigneur. He is dead."
4714: 
4715: "Well! He is quiet. Can I restore him to you?"
4716: 
4717: "Alas, no, Monseigneur! But he lies yonder, under a little heap of poor
4718: grass."
4719: 
4720: "Well?"
4721: 
4722: "Monseigneur, there are so many little heaps of poor grass?"
4723: 
4724: "Again, well?"
4725: 
4726: She looked an old woman, but was young. Her manner was one of passionate
4727: grief; by turns she clasped her veinous and knotted hands together
4728: with wild energy, and laid one of them on the carriage-door--tenderly,
4729: caressingly, as if it had been a human breast, and could be expected to
4730: feel the appealing touch.
4731: 
4732: "Monseigneur, hear me! Monseigneur, hear my petition! My husband died of
4733: want; so many die of want; so many more will die of want."
4734: 
4735: "Again, well? Can I feed them?"
4736: 
4737: "Monseigneur, the good God knows; but I don't ask it. My petition is,
4738: that a morsel of stone or wood, with my husband's name, may be placed
4739: over him to show where he lies. Otherwise, the place will be quickly
4740: forgotten, it will never be found when I am dead of the same malady, I
4741: shall be laid under some other heap of poor grass. Monseigneur, they
4742: are so many, they increase so fast, there is so much want. Monseigneur!
4743: Monseigneur!"
4744: 
4745: The valet had put her away from the door, the carriage had broken into
4746: a brisk trot, the postilions had quickened the pace, she was left far
4747: behind, and Monseigneur, again escorted by the Furies, was rapidly
4748: diminishing the league or two of distance that remained between him and
4749: his chateau.
4750: 
4751: The sweet scents of the summer night rose all around him, and rose, as
4752: the rain falls, impartially, on the dusty, ragged, and toil-worn group
4753: at the fountain not far away; to whom the mender of roads, with the aid
4754: of the blue cap without which he was nothing, still enlarged upon his
4755: man like a spectre, as long as they could bear it. By degrees, as they
4756: could bear no more, they dropped off one by one, and lights twinkled
4757: in little casements; which lights, as the casements darkened, and more
4758: stars came out, seemed to have shot up into the sky instead of having
4759: been extinguished.
4760: 
4761: The shadow of a large high-roofed house, and of many over-hanging trees,
4762: was upon Monsieur the Marquis by that time; and the shadow was exchanged
4763: for the light of a flambeau, as his carriage stopped, and the great door
4764: of his chateau was opened to him.
4765: 
4766: "Monsieur Charles, whom I expect; is he arrived from England?"
4767: 
4768: "Monseigneur, not yet."
4769: 
4770: 
4771: 
4772: 
4773: IX. The Gorgon's Head
4774: 
4775: 
4776: It was a heavy mass of building, that chateau of Monsieur the Marquis,
4777: with a large stone courtyard before it, and two stone sweeps of
4778: staircase meeting in a stone terrace before the principal door. A stony
4779: business altogether, with heavy stone balustrades, and stone urns, and
4780: stone flowers, and stone faces of men, and stone heads of lions, in
4781: all directions. As if the Gorgon's head had surveyed it, when it was
4782: finished, two centuries ago.
4783: 
4784: Up the broad flight of shallow steps, Monsieur the Marquis, flambeau
4785: preceded, went from his carriage, sufficiently disturbing the darkness
4786: to elicit loud remonstrance from an owl in the roof of the great pile
4787: of stable building away among the trees. All else was so quiet, that the
4788: flambeau carried up the steps, and the other flambeau held at the great
4789: door, burnt as if they were in a close room of state, instead of being
4790: in the open night-air. Other sound than the owl's voice there was none,
4791: save the falling of a fountain into its stone basin; for, it was one of
4792: those dark nights that hold their breath by the hour together, and then
4793: heave a long low sigh, and hold their breath again.
4794: 
4795: The great door clanged behind him, and Monsieur the Marquis crossed a
4796: hall grim with certain old boar-spears, swords, and knives of the chase;
4797: grimmer with certain heavy riding-rods and riding-whips, of which many a
4798: peasant, gone to his benefactor Death, had felt the weight when his lord
4799: was angry.
4800: 
4801: Avoiding the larger rooms, which were dark and made fast for the night,
4802: Monsieur the Marquis, with his flambeau-bearer going on before, went up
4803: the staircase to a door in a corridor. This thrown open, admitted him
4804: to his own private apartment of three rooms: his bed-chamber and two
4805: others. High vaulted rooms with cool uncarpeted floors, great dogs upon
4806: the hearths for the burning of wood in winter time, and all luxuries
4807: befitting the state of a marquis in a luxurious age and country.
4808: The fashion of the last Louis but one, of the line that was never to
4809: break--the fourteenth Louis--was conspicuous in their rich furniture;
4810: but, it was diversified by many objects that were illustrations of old
4811: pages in the history of France.
4812: 
4813: A supper-table was laid for two, in the third of the rooms; a round
4814: room, in one of the chateau's four extinguisher-topped towers. A small
4815: lofty room, with its window wide open, and the wooden jalousie-blinds
4816: closed, so that the dark night only showed in slight horizontal lines of
4817: black, alternating with their broad lines of stone colour.
4818: 
4819: "My nephew," said the Marquis, glancing at the supper preparation; "they
4820: said he was not arrived."
4821: 
4822: Nor was he; but, he had been expected with Monseigneur.
4823: 
4824: "Ah! It is not probable he will arrive to-night; nevertheless, leave the
4825: table as it is. I shall be ready in a quarter of an hour."
4826: 
4827: In a quarter of an hour Monseigneur was ready, and sat down alone to his
4828: sumptuous and choice supper. His chair was opposite to the window, and
4829: he had taken his soup, and was raising his glass of Bordeaux to his
4830: lips, when he put it down.
4831: 
4832: "What is that?" he calmly asked, looking with attention at the
4833: horizontal lines of black and stone colour.
4834: 
4835: "Monseigneur? That?"
4836: 
4837: "Outside the blinds. Open the blinds."
4838: 
4839: It was done.
4840: 
4841: "Well?"
4842: 
4843: "Monseigneur, it is nothing. The trees and the night are all that are
4844: here."
4845: 
4846: The servant who spoke, had thrown the blinds wide, had looked out into
4847: the vacant darkness, and stood with that blank behind him, looking round
4848: for instructions.
4849: 
4850: "Good," said the imperturbable master. "Close them again."
4851: 
4852: That was done too, and the Marquis went on with his supper. He was
4853: half way through it, when he again stopped with his glass in his hand,
4854: hearing the sound of wheels. It came on briskly, and came up to the
4855: front of the chateau.
4856: 
4857: "Ask who is arrived."
4858: 
4859: It was the nephew of Monseigneur. He had been some few leagues behind
4860: Monseigneur, early in the afternoon. He had diminished the distance
4861: rapidly, but not so rapidly as to come up with Monseigneur on the road.
4862: He had heard of Monseigneur, at the posting-houses, as being before him.
4863: 
4864: He was to be told (said Monseigneur) that supper awaited him then and
4865: there, and that he was prayed to come to it. In a little while he came.
4866: He had been known in England as Charles Darnay.
4867: 
4868: Monseigneur received him in a courtly manner, but they did not shake
4869: hands.
4870: 
4871: "You left Paris yesterday, sir?" he said to Monseigneur, as he took his
4872: seat at table.
4873: 
4874: "Yesterday. And you?"
4875: 
4876: "I come direct."
4877: 
4878: "From London?"
4879: 
4880: "Yes."
4881: 
4882: "You have been a long time coming," said the Marquis, with a smile.
4883: 
4884: "On the contrary; I come direct."
4885: 
4886: "Pardon me! I mean, not a long time on the journey; a long time
4887: intending the journey."
4888: 
4889: "I have been detained by"--the nephew stopped a moment in his
4890: answer--"various business."
4891: 
4892: "Without doubt," said the polished uncle.
4893: 
4894: So long as a servant was present, no other words passed between them.
4895: When coffee had been served and they were alone together, the nephew,
4896: looking at the uncle and meeting the eyes of the face that was like a
4897: fine mask, opened a conversation.
4898: 
4899: "I have come back, sir, as you anticipate, pursuing the object that
4900: took me away. It carried me into great and unexpected peril; but it is
4901: a sacred object, and if it had carried me to death I hope it would have
4902: sustained me."
4903: 
4904: "Not to death," said the uncle; "it is not necessary to say, to death."
4905: 
4906: "I doubt, sir," returned the nephew, "whether, if it had carried me to
4907: the utmost brink of death, you would have cared to stop me there."
4908: 
4909: The deepened marks in the nose, and the lengthening of the fine straight
4910: lines in the cruel face, looked ominous as to that; the uncle made a
4911: graceful gesture of protest, which was so clearly a slight form of good
4912: breeding that it was not reassuring.
4913: 
4914: "Indeed, sir," pursued the nephew, "for anything I know, you may have
4915: expressly worked to give a more suspicious appearance to the suspicious
4916: circumstances that surrounded me."
4917: 
4918: "No, no, no," said the uncle, pleasantly.
4919: 
4920: "But, however that may be," resumed the nephew, glancing at him with
4921: deep distrust, "I know that your diplomacy would stop me by any means,
4922: and would know no scruple as to means."
4923: 
4924: "My friend, I told you so," said the uncle, with a fine pulsation in the
4925: two marks. "Do me the favour to recall that I told you so, long ago."
4926: 
4927: "I recall it."
4928: 
4929: "Thank you," said the Marquis--very sweetly indeed.
4930: 
4931: His tone lingered in the air, almost like the tone of a musical
4932: instrument.
4933: 
4934: "In effect, sir," pursued the nephew, "I believe it to be at once your
4935: bad fortune, and my good fortune, that has kept me out of a prison in
4936: France here."
4937: 
4938: "I do not quite understand," returned the uncle, sipping his coffee.
4939: "Dare I ask you to explain?"
4940: 
4941: "I believe that if you were not in disgrace with the Court, and had not
4942: been overshadowed by that cloud for years past, a letter de cachet would
4943: have sent me to some fortress indefinitely."
4944: 
4945: "It is possible," said the uncle, with great calmness. "For the honour
4946: of the family, I could even resolve to incommode you to that extent.
4947: Pray excuse me!"
4948: 
4949: "I perceive that, happily for me, the Reception of the day before
4950: yesterday was, as usual, a cold one," observed the nephew.
4951: 
4952: "I would not say happily, my friend," returned the uncle, with refined
4953: politeness; "I would not be sure of that. A good opportunity for
4954: consideration, surrounded by the advantages of solitude, might influence
4955: your destiny to far greater advantage than you influence it for
4956: yourself. But it is useless to discuss the question. I am, as you say,
4957: at a disadvantage. These little instruments of correction, these gentle
4958: aids to the power and honour of families, these slight favours that
4959: might so incommode you, are only to be obtained now by interest
4960: and importunity. They are sought by so many, and they are granted
4961: (comparatively) to so few! It used not to be so, but France in all such
4962: things is changed for the worse. Our not remote ancestors held the right
4963: of life and death over the surrounding vulgar. From this room, many such
4964: dogs have been taken out to be hanged; in the next room (my bedroom),
4965: one fellow, to our knowledge, was poniarded on the spot for professing
4966: some insolent delicacy respecting his daughter--_his_ daughter? We have
4967: lost many privileges; a new philosophy has become the mode; and the
4968: assertion of our station, in these days, might (I do not go so far as
4969: to say would, but might) cause us real inconvenience. All very bad, very
4970: bad!"
4971: 
4972: The Marquis took a gentle little pinch of snuff, and shook his head;
4973: as elegantly despondent as he could becomingly be of a country still
4974: containing himself, that great means of regeneration.
4975: 
4976: "We have so asserted our station, both in the old time and in the modern
4977: time also," said the nephew, gloomily, "that I believe our name to be
4978: more detested than any name in France."
4979: 
4980: "Let us hope so," said the uncle. "Detestation of the high is the
4981: involuntary homage of the low."
4982: 
4983: "There is not," pursued the nephew, in his former tone, "a face I can
4984: look at, in all this country round about us, which looks at me with any
4985: deference on it but the dark deference of fear and slavery."
4986: 
4987: "A compliment," said the Marquis, "to the grandeur of the family,
4988: merited by the manner in which the family has sustained its grandeur.
4989: Hah!" And he took another gentle little pinch of snuff, and lightly
4990: crossed his legs.
4991: 
4992: But, when his nephew, leaning an elbow on the table, covered his eyes
4993: thoughtfully and dejectedly with his hand, the fine mask looked at
4994: him sideways with a stronger concentration of keenness, closeness,
4995: and dislike, than was comportable with its wearer's assumption of
4996: indifference.
4997: 
4998: "Repression is the only lasting philosophy. The dark deference of fear
4999: and slavery, my friend," observed the Marquis, "will keep the dogs
5000: obedient to the whip, as long as this roof," looking up to it, "shuts
5001: out the sky."
5002: 
5003: That might not be so long as the Marquis supposed. If a picture of the
5004: chateau as it was to be a very few years hence, and of fifty like it as
5005: they too were to be a very few years hence, could have been shown to
5006: him that night, he might have been at a loss to claim his own from
5007: the ghastly, fire-charred, plunder-wrecked rains. As for the roof
5008: he vaunted, he might have found _that_ shutting out the sky in a new
5009: way--to wit, for ever, from the eyes of the bodies into which its lead
5010: was fired, out of the barrels of a hundred thousand muskets.
5011: 
5012: "Meanwhile," said the Marquis, "I will preserve the honour and repose
5013: of the family, if you will not. But you must be fatigued. Shall we
5014: terminate our conference for the night?"
5015: 
5016: "A moment more."
5017: 
5018: "An hour, if you please."
5019: 
5020: "Sir," said the nephew, "we have done wrong, and are reaping the fruits
5021: of wrong."
5022: 
5023: "_We_ have done wrong?" repeated the Marquis, with an inquiring smile,
5024: and delicately pointing, first to his nephew, then to himself.
5025: 
5026: "Our family; our honourable family, whose honour is of so much account
5027: to both of us, in such different ways. Even in my father's time, we did
5028: a world of wrong, injuring every human creature who came between us and
5029: our pleasure, whatever it was. Why need I speak of my father's time,
5030: when it is equally yours? Can I separate my father's twin-brother, joint
5031: inheritor, and next successor, from himself?"
5032: 
5033: "Death has done that!" said the Marquis.
5034: 
5035: "And has left me," answered the nephew, "bound to a system that is
5036: frightful to me, responsible for it, but powerless in it; seeking to
5037: execute the last request of my dear mother's lips, and obey the last
5038: look of my dear mother's eyes, which implored me to have mercy and to
5039: redress; and tortured by seeking assistance and power in vain."
5040: 
5041: "Seeking them from me, my nephew," said the Marquis, touching him on the
5042: breast with his forefinger--they were now standing by the hearth--"you
5043: will for ever seek them in vain, be assured."
5044: 
5045: Every fine straight line in the clear whiteness of his face, was
5046: cruelly, craftily, and closely compressed, while he stood looking
5047: quietly at his nephew, with his snuff-box in his hand. Once again he
5048: touched him on the breast, as though his finger were the fine point of
5049: a small sword, with which, in delicate finesse, he ran him through the
5050: body, and said,
5051: 
5052: "My friend, I will die, perpetuating the system under which I have
5053: lived."
5054: 
5055: When he had said it, he took a culminating pinch of snuff, and put his
5056: box in his pocket.
5057: 
5058: "Better to be a rational creature," he added then, after ringing a small
5059: bell on the table, "and accept your natural destiny. But you are lost,
5060: Monsieur Charles, I see."
5061: 
5062: "This property and France are lost to me," said the nephew, sadly; "I
5063: renounce them."
5064: 
5065: "Are they both yours to renounce? France may be, but is the property? It
5066: is scarcely worth mentioning; but, is it yet?"
5067: 
5068: "I had no intention, in the words I used, to claim it yet. If it passed
5069: to me from you, to-morrow--"
5070: 
5071: "Which I have the vanity to hope is not probable."
5072: 
5073: "--or twenty years hence--"
5074: 
5075: "You do me too much honour," said the Marquis; "still, I prefer that
5076: supposition."
5077: 
5078: "--I would abandon it, and live otherwise and elsewhere. It is little to
5079: relinquish. What is it but a wilderness of misery and ruin!"
5080: 
5081: "Hah!" said the Marquis, glancing round the luxurious room.
5082: 
5083: "To the eye it is fair enough, here; but seen in its integrity,
5084: under the sky, and by the daylight, it is a crumbling tower of waste,
5085: mismanagement, extortion, debt, mortgage, oppression, hunger, nakedness,
5086: and suffering."
5087: 
5088: "Hah!" said the Marquis again, in a well-satisfied manner.
5089: 
5090: "If it ever becomes mine, it shall be put into some hands better
5091: qualified to free it slowly (if such a thing is possible) from the
5092: weight that drags it down, so that the miserable people who cannot leave
5093: it and who have been long wrung to the last point of endurance, may, in
5094: another generation, suffer less; but it is not for me. There is a curse
5095: on it, and on all this land."
5096: 
5097: "And you?" said the uncle. "Forgive my curiosity; do you, under your new
5098: philosophy, graciously intend to live?"
5099: 
5100: "I must do, to live, what others of my countrymen, even with nobility at
5101: their backs, may have to do some day--work."
5102: 
5103: "In England, for example?"
5104: 
5105: "Yes. The family honour, sir, is safe from me in this country. The
5106: family name can suffer from me in no other, for I bear it in no other."
5107: 
5108: The ringing of the bell had caused the adjoining bed-chamber to be
5109: lighted. It now shone brightly, through the door of communication. The
5110: Marquis looked that way, and listened for the retreating step of his
5111: valet.
5112: 
5113: "England is very attractive to you, seeing how indifferently you have
5114: prospered there," he observed then, turning his calm face to his nephew
5115: with a smile.
5116: 
5117: "I have already said, that for my prospering there, I am sensible I may
5118: be indebted to you, sir. For the rest, it is my Refuge."
5119: 
5120: "They say, those boastful English, that it is the Refuge of many. You
5121: know a compatriot who has found a Refuge there? A Doctor?"
5122: 
5123: "Yes."
5124: 
5125: "With a daughter?"
5126: 
5127: "Yes."
5128: 
5129: "Yes," said the Marquis. "You are fatigued. Good night!"
5130: 
5131: As he bent his head in his most courtly manner, there was a secrecy
5132: in his smiling face, and he conveyed an air of mystery to those words,
5133: which struck the eyes and ears of his nephew forcibly. At the same
5134: time, the thin straight lines of the setting of the eyes, and the thin
5135: straight lips, and the markings in the nose, curved with a sarcasm that
5136: looked handsomely diabolic.
5137: 
5138: "Yes," repeated the Marquis. "A Doctor with a daughter. Yes. So
5139: commences the new philosophy! You are fatigued. Good night!"
5140: 
5141: It would have been of as much avail to interrogate any stone face
5142: outside the chateau as to interrogate that face of his. The nephew
5143: looked at him, in vain, in passing on to the door.
5144: 
5145: "Good night!" said the uncle. "I look to the pleasure of seeing you
5146: again in the morning. Good repose! Light Monsieur my nephew to his
5147: chamber there!--And burn Monsieur my nephew in his bed, if you will," he
5148: added to himself, before he rang his little bell again, and summoned his
5149: valet to his own bedroom.
5150: 
5151: The valet come and gone, Monsieur the Marquis walked to and fro in his
5152: loose chamber-robe, to prepare himself gently for sleep, that hot still
5153: night. Rustling about the room, his softly-slippered feet making no
5154: noise on the floor, he moved like a refined tiger:--looked like some
5155: enchanted marquis of the impenitently wicked sort, in story, whose
5156: periodical change into tiger form was either just going off, or just
5157: coming on.
5158: 
5159: He moved from end to end of his voluptuous bedroom, looking again at the
5160: scraps of the day's journey that came unbidden into his mind; the slow
5161: toil up the hill at sunset, the setting sun, the descent, the mill, the
5162: prison on the crag, the little village in the hollow, the peasants at
5163: the fountain, and the mender of roads with his blue cap pointing out the
5164: chain under the carriage. That fountain suggested the Paris fountain,
5165: the little bundle lying on the step, the women bending over it, and the
5166: tall man with his arms up, crying, "Dead!"
5167: 
5168: "I am cool now," said Monsieur the Marquis, "and may go to bed."
5169: 
5170: So, leaving only one light burning on the large hearth, he let his thin
5171: gauze curtains fall around him, and heard the night break its silence
5172: with a long sigh as he composed himself to sleep.
5173: 
5174: The stone faces on the outer walls stared blindly at the black night
5175: for three heavy hours; for three heavy hours, the horses in the stables
5176: rattled at their racks, the dogs barked, and the owl made a noise with
5177: very little resemblance in it to the noise conventionally assigned to
5178: the owl by men-poets. But it is the obstinate custom of such creatures
5179: hardly ever to say what is set down for them.
5180: 
5181: For three heavy hours, the stone faces of the chateau, lion and human,
5182: stared blindly at the night. Dead darkness lay on all the landscape,
5183: dead darkness added its own hush to the hushing dust on all the roads.
5184: The burial-place had got to the pass that its little heaps of poor grass
5185: were undistinguishable from one another; the figure on the Cross might
5186: have come down, for anything that could be seen of it. In the village,
5187: taxers and taxed were fast asleep. Dreaming, perhaps, of banquets, as
5188: the starved usually do, and of ease and rest, as the driven slave and
5189: the yoked ox may, its lean inhabitants slept soundly, and were fed and
5190: freed.
5191: 
5192: The fountain in the village flowed unseen and unheard, and the fountain
5193: at the chateau dropped unseen and unheard--both melting away, like the
5194: minutes that were falling from the spring of Time--through three dark
5195: hours. Then, the grey water of both began to be ghostly in the light,
5196: and the eyes of the stone faces of the chateau were opened.
5197: 
5198: Lighter and lighter, until at last the sun touched the tops of the still
5199: trees, and poured its radiance over the hill. In the glow, the water
5200: of the chateau fountain seemed to turn to blood, and the stone faces
5201: crimsoned. The carol of the birds was loud and high, and, on the
5202: weather-beaten sill of the great window of the bed-chamber of Monsieur
5203: the Marquis, one little bird sang its sweetest song with all its might.
5204: At this, the nearest stone face seemed to stare amazed, and, with open
5205: mouth and dropped under-jaw, looked awe-stricken.
5206: 
5207: Now, the sun was full up, and movement began in the village. Casement
5208: windows opened, crazy doors were unbarred, and people came forth
5209: shivering--chilled, as yet, by the new sweet air. Then began the rarely
5210: lightened toil of the day among the village population. Some, to the
5211: fountain; some, to the fields; men and women here, to dig and delve; men
5212: and women there, to see to the poor live stock, and lead the bony cows
5213: out, to such pasture as could be found by the roadside. In the church
5214: and at the Cross, a kneeling figure or two; attendant on the latter
5215: prayers, the led cow, trying for a breakfast among the weeds at its
5216: foot.
5217: 
5218: The chateau awoke later, as became its quality, but awoke gradually and
5219: surely. First, the lonely boar-spears and knives of the chase had been
5220: reddened as of old; then, had gleamed trenchant in the morning sunshine;
5221: now, doors and windows were thrown open, horses in their stables looked
5222: round over their shoulders at the light and freshness pouring in at
5223: doorways, leaves sparkled and rustled at iron-grated windows, dogs
5224: pulled hard at their chains, and reared impatient to be loosed.
5225: 
5226: All these trivial incidents belonged to the routine of life, and the
5227: return of morning. Surely, not so the ringing of the great bell of the
5228: chateau, nor the running up and down the stairs; nor the hurried
5229: figures on the terrace; nor the booting and tramping here and there and
5230: everywhere, nor the quick saddling of horses and riding away?
5231: 
5232: What winds conveyed this hurry to the grizzled mender of roads, already
5233: at work on the hill-top beyond the village, with his day's dinner (not
5234: much to carry) lying in a bundle that it was worth no crow's while to
5235: peck at, on a heap of stones? Had the birds, carrying some grains of it
5236: to a distance, dropped one over him as they sow chance seeds? Whether or
5237: no, the mender of roads ran, on the sultry morning, as if for his life,
5238: down the hill, knee-high in dust, and never stopped till he got to the
5239: fountain.
5240: 
5241: All the people of the village were at the fountain, standing about
5242: in their depressed manner, and whispering low, but showing no other
5243: emotions than grim curiosity and surprise. The led cows, hastily brought
5244: in and tethered to anything that would hold them, were looking stupidly
5245: on, or lying down chewing the cud of nothing particularly repaying their
5246: trouble, which they had picked up in their interrupted saunter. Some of
5247: the people of the chateau, and some of those of the posting-house, and
5248: all the taxing authorities, were armed more or less, and were crowded
5249: on the other side of the little street in a purposeless way, that was
5250: highly fraught with nothing. Already, the mender of roads had penetrated
5251: into the midst of a group of fifty particular friends, and was smiting
5252: himself in the breast with his blue cap. What did all this portend,
5253: and what portended the swift hoisting-up of Monsieur Gabelle behind
5254: a servant on horseback, and the conveying away of the said Gabelle
5255: (double-laden though the horse was), at a gallop, like a new version of
5256: the German ballad of Leonora?
5257: 
5258: It portended that there was one stone face too many, up at the chateau.
5259: 
5260: The Gorgon had surveyed the building again in the night, and had added
5261: the one stone face wanting; the stone face for which it had waited
5262: through about two hundred years.
5263: 
5264: It lay back on the pillow of Monsieur the Marquis. It was like a fine
5265: mask, suddenly startled, made angry, and petrified. Driven home into the
5266: heart of the stone figure attached to it, was a knife. Round its hilt
5267: was a frill of paper, on which was scrawled:
5268: 
5269: "Drive him fast to his tomb. This, from Jacques."
5270: 
5271: 
5272: 
5273: 
5274: X. Two Promises
5275: 
5276: 
5277: More months, to the number of twelve, had come and gone, and Mr. Charles
5278: Darnay was established in England as a higher teacher of the French
5279: language who was conversant with French literature. In this age, he
5280: would have been a Professor; in that age, he was a Tutor. He read with
5281: young men who could find any leisure and interest for the study of a
5282: living tongue spoken all over the world, and he cultivated a taste for
5283: its stores of knowledge and fancy. He could write of them, besides, in
5284: sound English, and render them into sound English. Such masters were not
5285: at that time easily found; Princes that had been, and Kings that were
5286: to be, were not yet of the Teacher class, and no ruined nobility had
5287: dropped out of Tellson's ledgers, to turn cooks and carpenters. As a
5288: tutor, whose attainments made the student's way unusually pleasant and
5289: profitable, and as an elegant translator who brought something to his
5290: work besides mere dictionary knowledge, young Mr. Darnay soon became
5291: known and encouraged. He was well acquainted, more-over, with the
5292: circumstances of his country, and those were of ever-growing interest.
5293: So, with great perseverance and untiring industry, he prospered.
5294: 
5295: In London, he had expected neither to walk on pavements of gold, nor
5296: to lie on beds of roses; if he had had any such exalted expectation, he
5297: would not have prospered. He had expected labour, and he found it, and
5298: did it and made the best of it. In this, his prosperity consisted.
5299: 
5300: A certain portion of his time was passed at Cambridge, where he
5301: read with undergraduates as a sort of tolerated smuggler who drove a
5302: contraband trade in European languages, instead of conveying Greek
5303: and Latin through the Custom-house. The rest of his time he passed in
5304: London.
5305: 
5306: Now, from the days when it was always summer in Eden, to these days
5307: when it is mostly winter in fallen latitudes, the world of a man has
5308: invariably gone one way--Charles Darnay's way--the way of the love of a
5309: woman.
5310: 
5311: He had loved Lucie Manette from the hour of his danger. He had never
5312: heard a sound so sweet and dear as the sound of her compassionate voice;
5313: he had never seen a face so tenderly beautiful, as hers when it was
5314: confronted with his own on the edge of the grave that had been dug for
5315: him. But, he had not yet spoken to her on the subject; the assassination
5316: at the deserted chateau far away beyond the heaving water and the long,
5317: long, dusty roads--the solid stone chateau which had itself become the
5318: mere mist of a dream--had been done a year, and he had never yet, by so
5319: much as a single spoken word, disclosed to her the state of his heart.
5320: 
5321: That he had his reasons for this, he knew full well. It was again a
5322: summer day when, lately arrived in London from his college occupation,
5323: he turned into the quiet corner in Soho, bent on seeking an opportunity
5324: of opening his mind to Doctor Manette. It was the close of the summer
5325: day, and he knew Lucie to be out with Miss Pross.
5326: 
5327: He found the Doctor reading in his arm-chair at a window. The energy
5328: which had at once supported him under his old sufferings and aggravated
5329: their sharpness, had been gradually restored to him. He was now a
5330: very energetic man indeed, with great firmness of purpose, strength
5331: of resolution, and vigour of action. In his recovered energy he was
5332: sometimes a little fitful and sudden, as he had at first been in the
5333: exercise of his other recovered faculties; but, this had never been
5334: frequently observable, and had grown more and more rare.
5335: 
5336: He studied much, slept little, sustained a great deal of fatigue with
5337: ease, and was equably cheerful. To him, now entered Charles Darnay, at
5338: sight of whom he laid aside his book and held out his hand.
5339: 
5340: "Charles Darnay! I rejoice to see you. We have been counting on your
5342: both here yesterday, and both made you out to be more than due."
5343: 
5344: "I am obliged to them for their interest in the matter," he answered,
5345: a little coldly as to them, though very warmly as to the Doctor. "Miss
5346: Manette--"
5347: 
5348: "Is well," said the Doctor, as he stopped short, "and your return will
5349: delight us all. She has gone out on some household matters, but will
5350: soon be home."
5351: 
5352: "Doctor Manette, I knew she was from home. I took the opportunity of her
5353: being from home, to beg to speak to you."
5354: 
5355: There was a blank silence.
5356: 
5357: "Yes?" said the Doctor, with evident constraint. "Bring your chair here,
5358: and speak on."
5359: 
5360: He complied as to the chair, but appeared to find the speaking on less
5361: easy.
5362: 
5363: "I have had the happiness, Doctor Manette, of being so intimate here,"
5364: so he at length began, "for some year and a half, that I hope the topic
5365: on which I am about to touch may not--"
5366: 
5367: He was stayed by the Doctor's putting out his hand to stop him. When he
5368: had kept it so a little while, he said, drawing it back:
5369: 
5370: "Is Lucie the topic?"
5371: 
5372: "She is."
5373: 
5374: "It is hard for me to speak of her at any time. It is very hard for me
5375: to hear her spoken of in that tone of yours, Charles Darnay."
5376: 
5377: "It is a tone of fervent admiration, true homage, and deep love, Doctor
5378: Manette!" he said deferentially.
5379: 
5380: There was another blank silence before her father rejoined:
5381: 
5382: "I believe it. I do you justice; I believe it."
5383: 
5384: His constraint was so manifest, and it was so manifest, too, that it
5385: originated in an unwillingness to approach the subject, that Charles
5386: Darnay hesitated.
5387: 
5388: "Shall I go on, sir?"
5389: 
5390: Another blank.
5391: 
5392: "Yes, go on."
5393: 
5394: "You anticipate what I would say, though you cannot know how earnestly
5395: I say it, how earnestly I feel it, without knowing my secret heart, and
5396: the hopes and fears and anxieties with which it has long been
5397: laden. Dear Doctor Manette, I love your daughter fondly, dearly,
5398: disinterestedly, devotedly. If ever there were love in the world, I love
5399: her. You have loved yourself; let your old love speak for me!"
5400: 
5401: The Doctor sat with his face turned away, and his eyes bent on the
5402: ground. At the last words, he stretched out his hand again, hurriedly,
5403: and cried:
5404: 
5405: "Not that, sir! Let that be! I adjure you, do not recall that!"
5406: 
5407: His cry was so like a cry of actual pain, that it rang in Charles
5408: Darnay's ears long after he had ceased. He motioned with the hand he had
5409: extended, and it seemed to be an appeal to Darnay to pause. The latter
5410: so received it, and remained silent.
5411: 
5412: "I ask your pardon," said the Doctor, in a subdued tone, after some
5413: moments. "I do not doubt your loving Lucie; you may be satisfied of it."
5414: 
5415: He turned towards him in his chair, but did not look at him, or
5416: raise his eyes. His chin dropped upon his hand, and his white hair
5417: overshadowed his face:
5418: 
5419: "Have you spoken to Lucie?"
5420: 
5421: "No."
5422: 
5423: "Nor written?"
5424: 
5425: "Never."
5426: 
5427: "It would be ungenerous to affect not to know that your self-denial is
5428: to be referred to your consideration for her father. Her father thanks
5429: you."
5430: 
5431: He offered his hand; but his eyes did not go with it.
5432: 
5433: "I know," said Darnay, respectfully, "how can I fail to know, Doctor
5434: Manette, I who have seen you together from day to day, that between
5435: you and Miss Manette there is an affection so unusual, so touching, so
5436: belonging to the circumstances in which it has been nurtured, that it
5437: can have few parallels, even in the tenderness between a father and
5438: child. I know, Doctor Manette--how can I fail to know--that, mingled
5439: with the affection and duty of a daughter who has become a woman, there
5440: is, in her heart, towards you, all the love and reliance of infancy
5441: itself. I know that, as in her childhood she had no parent, so she is
5442: now devoted to you with all the constancy and fervour of her present
5443: years and character, united to the trustfulness and attachment of the
5444: early days in which you were lost to her. I know perfectly well that if
5445: you had been restored to her from the world beyond this life, you could
5446: hardly be invested, in her sight, with a more sacred character than that
5447: in which you are always with her. I know that when she is clinging to
5448: you, the hands of baby, girl, and woman, all in one, are round your
5449: neck. I know that in loving you she sees and loves her mother at her
5450: own age, sees and loves you at my age, loves her mother broken-hearted,
5451: loves you through your dreadful trial and in your blessed restoration. I
5452: have known this, night and day, since I have known you in your home."
5453: 
5454: Her father sat silent, with his face bent down. His breathing was a
5455: little quickened; but he repressed all other signs of agitation.
5456: 
5457: "Dear Doctor Manette, always knowing this, always seeing her and you
5458: with this hallowed light about you, I have forborne, and forborne, as
5459: long as it was in the nature of man to do it. I have felt, and do even
5460: now feel, that to bring my love--even mine--between you, is to touch
5461: your history with something not quite so good as itself. But I love her.
5462: Heaven is my witness that I love her!"
5463: 
5464: "I believe it," answered her father, mournfully. "I have thought so
5465: before now. I believe it."
5466: 
5467: "But, do not believe," said Darnay, upon whose ear the mournful voice
5468: struck with a reproachful sound, "that if my fortune were so cast as
5469: that, being one day so happy as to make her my wife, I must at any time
5470: put any separation between her and you, I could or would breathe a
5471: word of what I now say. Besides that I should know it to be hopeless, I
5472: should know it to be a baseness. If I had any such possibility, even at
5473: a remote distance of years, harboured in my thoughts, and hidden in my
5474: heart--if it ever had been there--if it ever could be there--I could not
5475: now touch this honoured hand."
5476: 
5477: He laid his own upon it as he spoke.
5478: 
5479: "No, dear Doctor Manette. Like you, a voluntary exile from France; like
5480: you, driven from it by its distractions, oppressions, and miseries; like
5481: you, striving to live away from it by my own exertions, and trusting
5482: in a happier future; I look only to sharing your fortunes, sharing your
5483: life and home, and being faithful to you to the death. Not to divide
5484: with Lucie her privilege as your child, companion, and friend; but to
5485: come in aid of it, and bind her closer to you, if such a thing can be."
5486: 
5487: His touch still lingered on her father's hand. Answering the touch for a
5488: moment, but not coldly, her father rested his hands upon the arms of
5489: his chair, and looked up for the first time since the beginning of the
5490: conference. A struggle was evidently in his face; a struggle with that
5491: occasional look which had a tendency in it to dark doubt and dread.
5492: 
5493: "You speak so feelingly and so manfully, Charles Darnay, that I thank
5494: you with all my heart, and will open all my heart--or nearly so. Have
5495: you any reason to believe that Lucie loves you?"
5496: 
5497: "None. As yet, none."
5498: 
5499: "Is it the immediate object of this confidence, that you may at once
5500: ascertain that, with my knowledge?"
5501: 
5502: "Not even so. I might not have the hopefulness to do it for weeks; I
5503: might (mistaken or not mistaken) have that hopefulness to-morrow."
5504: 
5505: "Do you seek any guidance from me?"
5506: 
5507: "I ask none, sir. But I have thought it possible that you might have it
5508: in your power, if you should deem it right, to give me some."
5509: 
5510: "Do you seek any promise from me?"
5511: 
5512: "I do seek that."
5513: 
5514: "What is it?"
5515: 
5516: "I well understand that, without you, I could have no hope. I well
5517: understand that, even if Miss Manette held me at this moment in her
5518: innocent heart--do not think I have the presumption to assume so much--I
5519: could retain no place in it against her love for her father."
5520: 
5521: "If that be so, do you see what, on the other hand, is involved in it?"
5522: 
5523: "I understand equally well, that a word from her father in any suitor's
5524: favour, would outweigh herself and all the world. For which reason,
5525: Doctor Manette," said Darnay, modestly but firmly, "I would not ask that
5526: word, to save my life."
5527: 
5528: "I am sure of it. Charles Darnay, mysteries arise out of close love, as
5529: well as out of wide division; in the former case, they are subtle and
5530: delicate, and difficult to penetrate. My daughter Lucie is, in this one
5531: respect, such a mystery to me; I can make no guess at the state of her
5532: heart."
5533: 
5534: "May I ask, sir, if you think she is--" As he hesitated, her father
5535: supplied the rest.
5536: 
5537: "Is sought by any other suitor?"
5538: 
5539: "It is what I meant to say."
5540: 
5541: Her father considered a little before he answered:
5542: 
5543: "You have seen Mr. Carton here, yourself. Mr. Stryver is here too,
5544: occasionally. If it be at all, it can only be by one of these."
5545: 
5546: "Or both," said Darnay.
5547: 
5548: "I had not thought of both; I should not think either, likely. You want
5549: a promise from me. Tell me what it is."
5550: 
5551: "It is, that if Miss Manette should bring to you at any time, on her own
5552: part, such a confidence as I have ventured to lay before you, you will
5553: bear testimony to what I have said, and to your belief in it. I hope you
5554: may be able to think so well of me, as to urge no influence against
5555: me. I say nothing more of my stake in this; this is what I ask. The
5556: condition on which I ask it, and which you have an undoubted right to
5557: require, I will observe immediately."
5558: 
5559: "I give the promise," said the Doctor, "without any condition. I believe
5560: your object to be, purely and truthfully, as you have stated it. I
5561: believe your intention is to perpetuate, and not to weaken, the ties
5562: between me and my other and far dearer self. If she should ever tell me
5563: that you are essential to her perfect happiness, I will give her to you.
5564: If there were--Charles Darnay, if there were--"
5565: 
5566: The young man had taken his hand gratefully; their hands were joined as
5567: the Doctor spoke:
5568: 
5569: "--any fancies, any reasons, any apprehensions, anything whatsoever,
5570: new or old, against the man she really loved--the direct responsibility
5571: thereof not lying on his head--they should all be obliterated for her
5572: sake. She is everything to me; more to me than suffering, more to me
5573: than wrong, more to me--Well! This is idle talk."
5574: 
5575: So strange was the way in which he faded into silence, and so strange
5576: his fixed look when he had ceased to speak, that Darnay felt his own
5577: hand turn cold in the hand that slowly released and dropped it.
5578: 
5579: "You said something to me," said Doctor Manette, breaking into a smile.
5580: "What was it you said to me?"
5581: 
5582: He was at a loss how to answer, until he remembered having spoken of a
5583: condition. Relieved as his mind reverted to that, he answered:
5584: 
5585: "Your confidence in me ought to be returned with full confidence on my
5586: part. My present name, though but slightly changed from my mother's, is
5587: not, as you will remember, my own. I wish to tell you what that is, and
5588: why I am in England."
5589: 
5590: "Stop!" said the Doctor of Beauvais.
5591: 
5592: "I wish it, that I may the better deserve your confidence, and have no
5593: secret from you."
5594: 
5595: "Stop!"
5596: 
5597: For an instant, the Doctor even had his two hands at his ears; for
5598: another instant, even had his two hands laid on Darnay's lips.
5599: 
5600: "Tell me when I ask you, not now. If your suit should prosper, if Lucie
5601: should love you, you shall tell me on your marriage morning. Do you
5602: promise?"
5603: 
5604: "Willingly.
5605: 
5606: "Give me your hand. She will be home directly, and it is better she
5607: should not see us together to-night. Go! God bless you!"
5608: 
5609: It was dark when Charles Darnay left him, and it was an hour later and
5610: darker when Lucie came home; she hurried into the room alone--for
5611: Miss Pross had gone straight up-stairs--and was surprised to find his
5612: reading-chair empty.
5613: 
5614: "My father!" she called to him. "Father dear!"
5615: 
5616: Nothing was said in answer, but she heard a low hammering sound in his
5617: bedroom. Passing lightly across the intermediate room, she looked in at
5618: his door and came running back frightened, crying to herself, with her
5619: blood all chilled, "What shall I do! What shall I do!"
5620: 
5621: Her uncertainty lasted but a moment; she hurried back, and tapped at
5622: his door, and softly called to him. The noise ceased at the sound of
5623: her voice, and he presently came out to her, and they walked up and down
5624: together for a long time.
5625: 
5626: She came down from her bed, to look at him in his sleep that night. He
5627: slept heavily, and his tray of shoemaking tools, and his old unfinished
5628: work, were all as usual.
5629: 
5630: 
5631: 
5632: 
5633: XI. A Companion Picture
5634: 
5635: 
5636: "Sydney," said Mr. Stryver, on that self-same night, or morning, to his
5637: jackal; "mix another bowl of punch; I have something to say to you."
5638: 
5639: Sydney had been working double tides that night, and the night before,
5640: and the night before that, and a good many nights in succession, making
5641: a grand clearance among Mr. Stryver's papers before the setting in
5642: of the long vacation. The clearance was effected at last; the Stryver
5643: arrears were handsomely fetched up; everything was got rid of until
5644: November should come with its fogs atmospheric, and fogs legal, and
5645: bring grist to the mill again.
5646: 
5647: Sydney was none the livelier and none the soberer for so much
5648: application. It had taken a deal of extra wet-towelling to pull him
5649: through the night; a correspondingly extra quantity of wine had preceded
5650: the towelling; and he was in a very damaged condition, as he now pulled
5651: his turban off and threw it into the basin in which he had steeped it at
5652: intervals for the last six hours.
5653: 
5654: "Are you mixing that other bowl of punch?" said Stryver the portly, with
5655: his hands in his waistband, glancing round from the sofa where he lay on
5656: his back.
5657: 
5658: "I am."
5659: 
5660: "Now, look here! I am going to tell you something that will rather
5661: surprise you, and that perhaps will make you think me not quite as
5662: shrewd as you usually do think me. I intend to marry."
5663: 
5664: "_Do_ you?"
5665: 
5666: "Yes. And not for money. What do you say now?"
5667: 
5668: "I don't feel disposed to say much. Who is she?"
5669: 
5670: "Guess."
5671: 
5672: "Do I know her?"
5673: 
5674: "Guess."
5675: 
5676: "I am not going to guess, at five o'clock in the morning, with my brains
5677: frying and sputtering in my head. If you want me to guess, you must ask
5678: me to dinner."
5679: 
5680: "Well then, I'll tell you," said Stryver, coming slowly into a sitting
5681: posture. "Sydney, I rather despair of making myself intelligible to you,
5682: because you are such an insensible dog."
5683: 
5684: "And you," returned Sydney, busy concocting the punch, "are such a
5685: sensitive and poetical spirit--"
5686: 
5687: "Come!" rejoined Stryver, laughing boastfully, "though I don't prefer
5688: any claim to being the soul of Romance (for I hope I know better), still
5689: I am a tenderer sort of fellow than _you_."
5690: 
5691: "You are a luckier, if you mean that."
5692: 
5693: "I don't mean that. I mean I am a man of more--more--"
5694: 
5695: "Say gallantry, while you are about it," suggested Carton.
5696: 
5697: "Well! I'll say gallantry. My meaning is that I am a man," said Stryver,
5698: inflating himself at his friend as he made the punch, "who cares more to
5699: be agreeable, who takes more pains to be agreeable, who knows better how
5700: to be agreeable, in a woman's society, than you do."
5701: 
5703: 
5704: "No; but before I go on," said Stryver, shaking his head in his bullying
5705: way, "I'll have this out with you. You've been at Doctor Manette's house
5706: as much as I have, or more than I have. Why, I have been ashamed of your
5707: moroseness there! Your manners have been of that silent and sullen and
5708: hangdog kind, that, upon my life and soul, I have been ashamed of you,
5709: Sydney!"
5710: 
5711: "It should be very beneficial to a man in your practice at the bar, to
5712: be ashamed of anything," returned Sydney; "you ought to be much obliged
5713: to me."
5714: 
5715: "You shall not get off in that way," rejoined Stryver, shouldering the
5716: rejoinder at him; "no, Sydney, it's my duty to tell you--and I tell you
5717: to your face to do you good--that you are a devilish ill-conditioned
5718: fellow in that sort of society. You are a disagreeable fellow."
5719: 
5720: Sydney drank a bumper of the punch he had made, and laughed.
5721: 
5722: "Look at me!" said Stryver, squaring himself; "I have less need to make
5723: myself agreeable than you have, being more independent in circumstances.
5724: Why do I do it?"
5725: 
5726: "I never saw you do it yet," muttered Carton.
5727: 
5728: "I do it because it's politic; I do it on principle. And look at me! I
5729: get on."
5730: 
5731: "You don't get on with your account of your matrimonial intentions,"
5732: answered Carton, with a careless air; "I wish you would keep to that. As
5733: to me--will you never understand that I am incorrigible?"
5734: 
5735: He asked the question with some appearance of scorn.
5736: 
5737: "You have no business to be incorrigible," was his friend's answer,
5738: delivered in no very soothing tone.
5739: 
5741: "Who is the lady?"
5742: 
5743: "Now, don't let my announcement of the name make you uncomfortable,
5744: Sydney," said Mr. Stryver, preparing him with ostentatious friendliness
5745: for the disclosure he was about to make, "because I know you don't mean
5746: half you say; and if you meant it all, it would be of no importance. I
5747: make this little preface, because you once mentioned the young lady to
5748: me in slighting terms."
5749: 
5750: "I did?"
5751: 
5752: "Certainly; and in these chambers."
5753: 
5754: Sydney Carton looked at his punch and looked at his complacent friend;
5755: drank his punch and looked at his complacent friend.
5756: 
5757: "You made mention of the young lady as a golden-haired doll. The young
5758: lady is Miss Manette. If you had been a fellow of any sensitiveness or
5759: delicacy of feeling in that kind of way, Sydney, I might have been a
5760: little resentful of your employing such a designation; but you are not.
5761: You want that sense altogether; therefore I am no more annoyed when I
5762: think of the expression, than I should be annoyed by a man's opinion of
5763: a picture of mine, who had no eye for pictures: or of a piece of music
5764: of mine, who had no ear for music."
5765: 
5766: Sydney Carton drank the punch at a great rate; drank it by bumpers,
5767: looking at his friend.
5768: 
5769: "Now you know all about it, Syd," said Mr. Stryver. "I don't care about
5770: fortune: she is a charming creature, and I have made up my mind to
5771: please myself: on the whole, I think I can afford to please myself. She
5772: will have in me a man already pretty well off, and a rapidly rising man,
5773: and a man of some distinction: it is a piece of good fortune for her,
5774: but she is worthy of good fortune. Are you astonished?"
5775: 
5776: Carton, still drinking the punch, rejoined, "Why should I be
5777: astonished?"
5778: 
5779: "You approve?"
5780: 
5781: Carton, still drinking the punch, rejoined, "Why should I not approve?"
5782: 
5783: "Well!" said his friend Stryver, "you take it more easily than I fancied
5784: you would, and are less mercenary on my behalf than I thought you would
5785: be; though, to be sure, you know well enough by this time that your
5786: ancient chum is a man of a pretty strong will. Yes, Sydney, I have had
5787: enough of this style of life, with no other as a change from it; I
5788: feel that it is a pleasant thing for a man to have a home when he feels
5789: inclined to go to it (when he doesn't, he can stay away), and I feel
5790: that Miss Manette will tell well in any station, and will always do me
5791: credit. So I have made up my mind. And now, Sydney, old boy, I want to
5792: say a word to _you_ about _your_ prospects. You are in a bad way, you
5793: know; you really are in a bad way. You don't know the value of money,
5794: you live hard, you'll knock up one of these days, and be ill and poor;
5795: you really ought to think about a nurse."
5796: 
5797: The prosperous patronage with which he said it, made him look twice as
5798: big as he was, and four times as offensive.
5799: 
5800: "Now, let me recommend you," pursued Stryver, "to look it in the face.
5801: I have looked it in the face, in my different way; look it in the face,
5802: you, in your different way. Marry. Provide somebody to take care of
5803: you. Never mind your having no enjoyment of women's society, nor
5804: understanding of it, nor tact for it. Find out somebody. Find out some
5805: respectable woman with a little property--somebody in the landlady way,
5806: or lodging-letting way--and marry her, against a rainy day. That's the
5807: kind of thing for _you_. Now think of it, Sydney."
5808: 
5809: "I'll think of it," said Sydney.
5810: 
5811: 
5812: 
5813: 
5814: XII. The Fellow of Delicacy
5815: 
5816: 
5817: Mr. Stryver having made up his mind to that magnanimous bestowal of good
5818: fortune on the Doctor's daughter, resolved to make her happiness known
5819: to her before he left town for the Long Vacation. After some mental
5820: debating of the point, he came to the conclusion that it would be as
5821: well to get all the preliminaries done with, and they could then arrange
5822: at their leisure whether he should give her his hand a week or two
5823: before Michaelmas Term, or in the little Christmas vacation between it
5824: and Hilary.
5825: 
5826: As to the strength of his case, he had not a doubt about it, but clearly
5827: saw his way to the verdict. Argued with the jury on substantial worldly
5828: grounds--the only grounds ever worth taking into account--it was a
5829: plain case, and had not a weak spot in it. He called himself for the
5830: plaintiff, there was no getting over his evidence, the counsel for
5831: the defendant threw up his brief, and the jury did not even turn to
5832: consider. After trying it, Stryver, C. J., was satisfied that no plainer
5833: case could be.
5834: 
5835: Accordingly, Mr. Stryver inaugurated the Long Vacation with a formal
5836: proposal to take Miss Manette to Vauxhall Gardens; that failing, to
5837: Ranelagh; that unaccountably failing too, it behoved him to present
5838: himself in Soho, and there declare his noble mind.
5839: 
5840: Towards Soho, therefore, Mr. Stryver shouldered his way from the Temple,
5841: while the bloom of the Long Vacation's infancy was still upon it.
5842: Anybody who had seen him projecting himself into Soho while he was yet
5843: on Saint Dunstan's side of Temple Bar, bursting in his full-blown way
5844: along the pavement, to the jostlement of all weaker people, might have
5845: seen how safe and strong he was.
5846: 
5847: His way taking him past Tellson's, and he both banking at Tellson's and
5848: knowing Mr. Lorry as the intimate friend of the Manettes, it entered Mr.
5849: Stryver's mind to enter the bank, and reveal to Mr. Lorry the brightness
5850: of the Soho horizon. So, he pushed open the door with the weak rattle
5851: in its throat, stumbled down the two steps, got past the two ancient
5852: cashiers, and shouldered himself into the musty back closet where Mr.
5853: Lorry sat at great books ruled for figures, with perpendicular iron
5854: bars to his window as if that were ruled for figures too, and everything
5855: under the clouds were a sum.
5856: 
5857: "Halloa!" said Mr. Stryver. "How do you do? I hope you are well!"
5858: 
5859: It was Stryver's grand peculiarity that he always seemed too big for any
5860: place, or space. He was so much too big for Tellson's, that old clerks
5861: in distant corners looked up with looks of remonstrance, as though he
5862: squeezed them against the wall. The House itself, magnificently reading
5863: the paper quite in the far-off perspective, lowered displeased, as if
5864: the Stryver head had been butted into its responsible waistcoat.
5865: 
5866: The discreet Mr. Lorry said, in a sample tone of the voice he would
5867: recommend under the circumstances, "How do you do, Mr. Stryver? How do
5868: you do, sir?" and shook hands. There was a peculiarity in his manner
5869: of shaking hands, always to be seen in any clerk at Tellson's who shook
5870: hands with a customer when the House pervaded the air. He shook in a
5871: self-abnegating way, as one who shook for Tellson and Co.
5872: 
5873: "Can I do anything for you, Mr. Stryver?" asked Mr. Lorry, in his
5874: business character.
5875: 
5876: "Why, no, thank you; this is a private visit to yourself, Mr. Lorry; I
5877: have come for a private word."
5878: 
5879: "Oh indeed!" said Mr. Lorry, bending down his ear, while his eye strayed
5880: to the House afar off.
5881: 
5882: "I am going," said Mr. Stryver, leaning his arms confidentially on the
5883: desk: whereupon, although it was a large double one, there appeared to
5884: be not half desk enough for him: "I am going to make an offer of myself
5885: in marriage to your agreeable little friend, Miss Manette, Mr. Lorry."
5886: 
5887: "Oh dear me!" cried Mr. Lorry, rubbing his chin, and looking at his
5888: visitor dubiously.
5889: 
5890: "Oh dear me, sir?" repeated Stryver, drawing back. "Oh dear you, sir?
5891: What may your meaning be, Mr. Lorry?"
5892: 
5893: "My meaning," answered the man of business, "is, of course, friendly and
5894: appreciative, and that it does you the greatest credit, and--in short,
5895: my meaning is everything you could desire. But--really, you know, Mr.
5896: Stryver--" Mr. Lorry paused, and shook his head at him in the oddest
5897: manner, as if he were compelled against his will to add, internally,
5898: "you know there really is so much too much of you!"
5899: 
5900: "Well!" said Stryver, slapping the desk with his contentious hand,
5901: opening his eyes wider, and taking a long breath, "if I understand you,
5902: Mr. Lorry, I'll be hanged!"
5903: 
5904: Mr. Lorry adjusted his little wig at both ears as a means towards that
5905: end, and bit the feather of a pen.
5906: 
5907: "D--n it all, sir!" said Stryver, staring at him, "am I not eligible?"
5908: 
5909: "Oh dear yes! Yes. Oh yes, you're eligible!" said Mr. Lorry. "If you say
5910: eligible, you are eligible."
5911: 
5912: "Am I not prosperous?" asked Stryver.
5913: 
5914: "Oh! if you come to prosperous, you are prosperous," said Mr. Lorry.
5915: 
5916: "And advancing?"
5917: 
5918: "If you come to advancing you know," said Mr. Lorry, delighted to be
5919: able to make another admission, "nobody can doubt that."
5920: 
5921: "Then what on earth is your meaning, Mr. Lorry?" demanded Stryver,
5922: perceptibly crestfallen.
5923: 
5924: "Well! I--Were you going there now?" asked Mr. Lorry.
5925: 
5926: "Straight!" said Stryver, with a plump of his fist on the desk.
5927: 
5928: "Then I think I wouldn't, if I was you."
5929: 
5930: "Why?" said Stryver. "Now, I'll put you in a corner," forensically
5931: shaking a forefinger at him. "You are a man of business and bound to
5932: have a reason. State your reason. Why wouldn't you go?"
5933: 
5934: "Because," said Mr. Lorry, "I wouldn't go on such an object without
5935: having some cause to believe that I should succeed."
5936: 
5937: "D--n _me_!" cried Stryver, "but this beats everything."
5938: 
5939: Mr. Lorry glanced at the distant House, and glanced at the angry
5940: Stryver.
5941: 
5942: "Here's a man of business--a man of years--a man of experience--_in_
5943: a Bank," said Stryver; "and having summed up three leading reasons for
5944: complete success, he says there's no reason at all! Says it with his
5945: head on!" Mr. Stryver remarked upon the peculiarity as if it would have
5946: been infinitely less remarkable if he had said it with his head off.
5947: 
5948: "When I speak of success, I speak of success with the young lady; and
5949: when I speak of causes and reasons to make success probable, I speak of
5950: causes and reasons that will tell as such with the young lady. The young
5951: lady, my good sir," said Mr. Lorry, mildly tapping the Stryver arm, "the
5952: young lady. The young lady goes before all."
5953: 
5954: "Then you mean to tell me, Mr. Lorry," said Stryver, squaring his
5955: elbows, "that it is your deliberate opinion that the young lady at
5956: present in question is a mincing Fool?"
5957: 
5958: "Not exactly so. I mean to tell you, Mr. Stryver," said Mr. Lorry,
5959: reddening, "that I will hear no disrespectful word of that young lady
5960: from any lips; and that if I knew any man--which I hope I do not--whose
5961: taste was so coarse, and whose temper was so overbearing, that he could
5962: not restrain himself from speaking disrespectfully of that young lady at
5963: this desk, not even Tellson's should prevent my giving him a piece of my
5964: mind."
5965: 
5966: The necessity of being angry in a suppressed tone had put Mr. Stryver's
5967: blood-vessels into a dangerous state when it was his turn to be angry;
5968: Mr. Lorry's veins, methodical as their courses could usually be, were in
5969: no better state now it was his turn.
5970: 
5971: "That is what I mean to tell you, sir," said Mr. Lorry. "Pray let there
5972: be no mistake about it."
5973: 
5974: Mr. Stryver sucked the end of a ruler for a little while, and then stood
5975: hitting a tune out of his teeth with it, which probably gave him the
5976: toothache. He broke the awkward silence by saying:
5977: 
5978: "This is something new to me, Mr. Lorry. You deliberately advise me not
5979: to go up to Soho and offer myself--_my_self, Stryver of the King's Bench
5980: bar?"
5981: 
5982: "Do you ask me for my advice, Mr. Stryver?"
5983: 
5984: "Yes, I do."
5985:
