5986: "Very good. Then I give it, and you have repeated it correctly."
5987: 
5988: "And all I can say of it is," laughed Stryver with a vexed laugh, "that
5989: this--ha, ha!--beats everything past, present, and to come."
5990: 
5991: "Now understand me," pursued Mr. Lorry. "As a man of business, I am
5992: not justified in saying anything about this matter, for, as a man of
5993: business, I know nothing of it. But, as an old fellow, who has carried
5994: Miss Manette in his arms, who is the trusted friend of Miss Manette and
5995: of her father too, and who has a great affection for them both, I have
5996: spoken. The confidence is not of my seeking, recollect. Now, you think I
5997: may not be right?"
5998: 
5999: "Not I!" said Stryver, whistling. "I can't undertake to find third
6000: parties in common sense; I can only find it for myself. I suppose sense
6001: in certain quarters; you suppose mincing bread-and-butter nonsense. It's
6002: new to me, but you are right, I dare say."
6003: 
6004: "What I suppose, Mr. Stryver, I claim to characterise for myself--And
6005: understand me, sir," said Mr. Lorry, quickly flushing again, "I
6006: will not--not even at Tellson's--have it characterised for me by any
6007: gentleman breathing."
6008: 
6009: "There! I beg your pardon!" said Stryver.
6010: 
6011: "Granted. Thank you. Well, Mr. Stryver, I was about to say:--it might be
6012: painful to you to find yourself mistaken, it might be painful to Doctor
6013: Manette to have the task of being explicit with you, it might be very
6014: painful to Miss Manette to have the task of being explicit with you. You
6015: know the terms upon which I have the honour and happiness to stand with
6016: the family. If you please, committing you in no way, representing you
6017: in no way, I will undertake to correct my advice by the exercise of a
6018: little new observation and judgment expressly brought to bear upon
6019: it. If you should then be dissatisfied with it, you can but test its
6020: soundness for yourself; if, on the other hand, you should be satisfied
6021: with it, and it should be what it now is, it may spare all sides what is
6022: best spared. What do you say?"
6023: 
6024: "How long would you keep me in town?"
6025: 
6026: "Oh! It is only a question of a few hours. I could go to Soho in the
6027: evening, and come to your chambers afterwards."
6028: 
6029: "Then I say yes," said Stryver: "I won't go up there now, I am not so
6030: hot upon it as that comes to; I say yes, and I shall expect you to look
6031: in to-night. Good morning."
6032: 
6033: Then Mr. Stryver turned and burst out of the Bank, causing such a
6034: concussion of air on his passage through, that to stand up against it
6035: bowing behind the two counters, required the utmost remaining strength
6036: of the two ancient clerks. Those venerable and feeble persons were
6037: always seen by the public in the act of bowing, and were popularly
6038: believed, when they had bowed a customer out, still to keep on bowing in
6039: the empty office until they bowed another customer in.
6040: 
6041: The barrister was keen enough to divine that the banker would not have
6042: gone so far in his expression of opinion on any less solid ground than
6043: moral certainty. Unprepared as he was for the large pill he had to
6044: swallow, he got it down. "And now," said Mr. Stryver, shaking his
6045: forensic forefinger at the Temple in general, when it was down, "my way
6046: out of this, is, to put you all in the wrong."
6047: 
6048: It was a bit of the art of an Old Bailey tactician, in which he found
6049: great relief. "You shall not put me in the wrong, young lady," said Mr.
6050: Stryver; "I'll do that for you."
6051: 
6052: Accordingly, when Mr. Lorry called that night as late as ten o'clock,
6053: Mr. Stryver, among a quantity of books and papers littered out for the
6054: purpose, seemed to have nothing less on his mind than the subject of
6055: the morning. He even showed surprise when he saw Mr. Lorry, and was
6056: altogether in an absent and preoccupied state.
6057: 
6058: "Well!" said that good-natured emissary, after a full half-hour of
6059: bootless attempts to bring him round to the question. "I have been to
6060: Soho."
6061: 
6062: "To Soho?" repeated Mr. Stryver, coldly. "Oh, to be sure! What am I
6063: thinking of!"
6064: 
6065: "And I have no doubt," said Mr. Lorry, "that I was right in the
6066: conversation we had. My opinion is confirmed, and I reiterate my
6067: advice."
6068: 
6069: "I assure you," returned Mr. Stryver, in the friendliest way, "that I
6070: am sorry for it on your account, and sorry for it on the poor father's
6071: account. I know this must always be a sore subject with the family; let
6072: us say no more about it."
6073: 
6074: "I don't understand you," said Mr. Lorry.
6075: 
6076: "I dare say not," rejoined Stryver, nodding his head in a smoothing and
6077: final way; "no matter, no matter."
6078: 
6079: "But it does matter," Mr. Lorry urged.
6080: 
6081: "No it doesn't; I assure you it doesn't. Having supposed that there was
6082: sense where there is no sense, and a laudable ambition where there is
6083: not a laudable ambition, I am well out of my mistake, and no harm is
6084: done. Young women have committed similar follies often before, and have
6085: repented them in poverty and obscurity often before. In an unselfish
6086: aspect, I am sorry that the thing is dropped, because it would have been
6087: a bad thing for me in a worldly point of view; in a selfish aspect, I am
6088: glad that the thing has dropped, because it would have been a bad thing
6089: for me in a worldly point of view--it is hardly necessary to say I could
6090: have gained nothing by it. There is no harm at all done. I have not
6091: proposed to the young lady, and, between ourselves, I am by no means
6092: certain, on reflection, that I ever should have committed myself to
6093: that extent. Mr. Lorry, you cannot control the mincing vanities and
6094: giddinesses of empty-headed girls; you must not expect to do it, or you
6095: will always be disappointed. Now, pray say no more about it. I tell you,
6096: I regret it on account of others, but I am satisfied on my own account.
6097: And I am really very much obliged to you for allowing me to sound you,
6098: and for giving me your advice; you know the young lady better than I do;
6099: you were right, it never would have done."
6100: 
6101: Mr. Lorry was so taken aback, that he looked quite stupidly at Mr.
6102: Stryver shouldering him towards the door, with an appearance of
6103: showering generosity, forbearance, and goodwill, on his erring head.
6104: "Make the best of it, my dear sir," said Stryver; "say no more about it;
6105: thank you again for allowing me to sound you; good night!"
6106: 
6107: Mr. Lorry was out in the night, before he knew where he was. Mr. Stryver
6108: was lying back on his sofa, winking at his ceiling.
6109: 
6110: 
6111: 
6112: 
6113: XIII. The Fellow of No Delicacy
6114: 
6115: 
6117: house of Doctor Manette. He had been there often, during a whole year,
6118: and had always been the same moody and morose lounger there. When he
6119: cared to talk, he talked well; but, the cloud of caring for nothing,
6120: which overshadowed him with such a fatal darkness, was very rarely
6121: pierced by the light within him.
6122: 
6123: And yet he did care something for the streets that environed that house,
6124: and for the senseless stones that made their pavements. Many a night
6125: he vaguely and unhappily wandered there, when wine had brought no
6126: transitory gladness to him; many a dreary daybreak revealed his solitary
6127: figure lingering there, and still lingering there when the first beams
6128: of the sun brought into strong relief, removed beauties of architecture
6129: in spires of churches and lofty buildings, as perhaps the quiet time
6130: brought some sense of better things, else forgotten and unattainable,
6131: into his mind. Of late, the neglected bed in the Temple Court had known
6132: him more scantily than ever; and often when he had thrown himself upon
6133: it no longer than a few minutes, he had got up again, and haunted that
6134: neighbourhood.
6135: 
6136: On a day in August, when Mr. Stryver (after notifying to his jackal
6137: that "he had thought better of that marrying matter") had carried his
6138: delicacy into Devonshire, and when the sight and scent of flowers in the
6139: City streets had some waifs of goodness in them for the worst, of health
6140: for the sickliest, and of youth for the oldest, Sydney's feet still trod
6141: those stones. From being irresolute and purposeless, his feet became
6142: animated by an intention, and, in the working out of that intention,
6143: they took him to the Doctor's door.
6144: 
6145: He was shown up-stairs, and found Lucie at her work, alone. She had
6146: never been quite at her ease with him, and received him with some little
6147: embarrassment as he seated himself near her table. But, looking up at
6148: his face in the interchange of the first few common-places, she observed
6149: a change in it.
6150: 
6151: "I fear you are not well, Mr. Carton!"
6152: 
6153: "No. But the life I lead, Miss Manette, is not conducive to health. What
6154: is to be expected of, or by, such profligates?"
6155: 
6156: "Is it not--forgive me; I have begun the question on my lips--a pity to
6157: live no better life?"
6158: 
6159: "God knows it is a shame!"
6160: 
6161: "Then why not change it?"
6162: 
6163: Looking gently at him again, she was surprised and saddened to see that
6164: there were tears in his eyes. There were tears in his voice too, as he
6165: answered:
6166: 
6167: "It is too late for that. I shall never be better than I am. I shall
6168: sink lower, and be worse."
6169: 
6170: He leaned an elbow on her table, and covered his eyes with his hand. The
6171: table trembled in the silence that followed.
6172: 
6173: She had never seen him softened, and was much distressed. He knew her to
6174: be so, without looking at her, and said:
6175: 
6176: "Pray forgive me, Miss Manette. I break down before the knowledge of
6177: what I want to say to you. Will you hear me?"
6178: 
6179: "If it will do you any good, Mr. Carton, if it would make you happier,
6180: it would make me very glad!"
6181: 
6182: "God bless you for your sweet compassion!"
6183: 
6184: He unshaded his face after a little while, and spoke steadily.
6185: 
6186: "Don't be afraid to hear me. Don't shrink from anything I say. I am like
6187: one who died young. All my life might have been."
6188: 
6189: "No, Mr. Carton. I am sure that the best part of it might still be; I am
6190: sure that you might be much, much worthier of yourself."
6191: 
6192: "Say of you, Miss Manette, and although I know better--although in the
6193: mystery of my own wretched heart I know better--I shall never forget
6194: it!"
6195: 
6196: She was pale and trembling. He came to her relief with a fixed despair
6197: of himself which made the interview unlike any other that could have
6198: been holden.
6199: 
6200: "If it had been possible, Miss Manette, that you could have returned the
6201: love of the man you see before yourself--flung away, wasted, drunken,
6202: poor creature of misuse as you know him to be--he would have been
6203: conscious this day and hour, in spite of his happiness, that he would
6204: bring you to misery, bring you to sorrow and repentance, blight you,
6205: disgrace you, pull you down with him. I know very well that you can have
6206: no tenderness for me; I ask for none; I am even thankful that it cannot
6207: be."
6208: 
6209: "Without it, can I not save you, Mr. Carton? Can I not recall
6210: you--forgive me again!--to a better course? Can I in no way repay your
6211: confidence? I know this is a confidence," she modestly said, after a
6212: little hesitation, and in earnest tears, "I know you would say this to
6213: no one else. Can I turn it to no good account for yourself, Mr. Carton?"
6214: 
6215: He shook his head.
6216: 
6217: "To none. No, Miss Manette, to none. If you will hear me through a very
6218: little more, all you can ever do for me is done. I wish you to know that
6219: you have been the last dream of my soul. In my degradation I have not
6220: been so degraded but that the sight of you with your father, and of this
6221: home made such a home by you, has stirred old shadows that I thought had
6222: died out of me. Since I knew you, I have been troubled by a remorse that
6223: I thought would never reproach me again, and have heard whispers from
6224: old voices impelling me upward, that I thought were silent for ever. I
6225: have had unformed ideas of striving afresh, beginning anew, shaking off
6226: sloth and sensuality, and fighting out the abandoned fight. A dream, all
6227: a dream, that ends in nothing, and leaves the sleeper where he lay down,
6228: but I wish you to know that you inspired it."
6229: 
6230: "Will nothing of it remain? O Mr. Carton, think again! Try again!"
6231: 
6232: "No, Miss Manette; all through it, I have known myself to be quite
6233: undeserving. And yet I have had the weakness, and have still the
6234: weakness, to wish you to know with what a sudden mastery you kindled me,
6235: heap of ashes that I am, into fire--a fire, however, inseparable in
6236: its nature from myself, quickening nothing, lighting nothing, doing no
6237: service, idly burning away."
6238: 
6239: "Since it is my misfortune, Mr. Carton, to have made you more unhappy
6240: than you were before you knew me--"
6241: 
6242: "Don't say that, Miss Manette, for you would have reclaimed me, if
6243: anything could. You will not be the cause of my becoming worse."
6244: 
6245: "Since the state of your mind that you describe, is, at all events,
6246: attributable to some influence of mine--this is what I mean, if I can
6247: make it plain--can I use no influence to serve you? Have I no power for
6248: good, with you, at all?"
6249: 
6250: "The utmost good that I am capable of now, Miss Manette, I have come
6251: here to realise. Let me carry through the rest of my misdirected life,
6252: the remembrance that I opened my heart to you, last of all the world;
6253: and that there was something left in me at this time which you could
6254: deplore and pity."
6255: 
6256: "Which I entreated you to believe, again and again, most fervently, with
6257: all my heart, was capable of better things, Mr. Carton!"
6258: 
6259: "Entreat me to believe it no more, Miss Manette. I have proved myself,
6260: and I know better. I distress you; I draw fast to an end. Will you let
6261: me believe, when I recall this day, that the last confidence of my life
6262: was reposed in your pure and innocent breast, and that it lies there
6263: alone, and will be shared by no one?"
6264: 
6265: "If that will be a consolation to you, yes."
6266: 
6267: "Not even by the dearest one ever to be known to you?"
6268: 
6269: "Mr. Carton," she answered, after an agitated pause, "the secret is
6270: yours, not mine; and I promise to respect it."
6271: 
6272: "Thank you. And again, God bless you."
6273: 
6274: He put her hand to his lips, and moved towards the door.
6275: 
6276: "Be under no apprehension, Miss Manette, of my ever resuming this
6277: conversation by so much as a passing word. I will never refer to it
6278: again. If I were dead, that could not be surer than it is henceforth. In
6279: the hour of my death, I shall hold sacred the one good remembrance--and
6280: shall thank and bless you for it--that my last avowal of myself was made
6281: to you, and that my name, and faults, and miseries were gently carried
6282: in your heart. May it otherwise be light and happy!"
6283: 
6284: He was so unlike what he had ever shown himself to be, and it was so
6285: sad to think how much he had thrown away, and how much he every day kept
6286: down and perverted, that Lucie Manette wept mournfully for him as he
6287: stood looking back at her.
6288: 
6289: "Be comforted!" he said, "I am not worth such feeling, Miss Manette. An
6290: hour or two hence, and the low companions and low habits that I scorn
6291: but yield to, will render me less worth such tears as those, than any
6292: wretch who creeps along the streets. Be comforted! But, within myself, I
6293: shall always be, towards you, what I am now, though outwardly I shall be
6294: what you have heretofore seen me. The last supplication but one I make
6295: to you, is, that you will believe this of me."
6296: 
6297: "I will, Mr. Carton."
6298: 
6299: "My last supplication of all, is this; and with it, I will relieve
6300: you of a visitor with whom I well know you have nothing in unison, and
6301: between whom and you there is an impassable space. It is useless to say
6302: it, I know, but it rises out of my soul. For you, and for any dear to
6303: you, I would do anything. If my career were of that better kind that
6304: there was any opportunity or capacity of sacrifice in it, I would
6305: embrace any sacrifice for you and for those dear to you. Try to hold
6306: me in your mind, at some quiet times, as ardent and sincere in this one
6307: thing. The time will come, the time will not be long in coming, when new
6308: ties will be formed about you--ties that will bind you yet more tenderly
6309: and strongly to the home you so adorn--the dearest ties that will ever
6310: grace and gladden you. O Miss Manette, when the little picture of a
6311: happy father's face looks up in yours, when you see your own bright
6312: beauty springing up anew at your feet, think now and then that there is
6313: a man who would give his life, to keep a life you love beside you!"
6314: 
6315: He said, "Farewell!" said a last "God bless you!" and left her.
6316: 
6317: 
6318: 
6319: 
6320: XIV. The Honest Tradesman
6321: 
6322: 
6323: To the eyes of Mr. Jeremiah Cruncher, sitting on his stool in
6324: Fleet-street with his grisly urchin beside him, a vast number and
6325: variety of objects in movement were every day presented. Who could sit
6326: upon anything in Fleet-street during the busy hours of the day, and
6327: not be dazed and deafened by two immense processions, one ever tending
6328: westward with the sun, the other ever tending eastward from the sun,
6329: both ever tending to the plains beyond the range of red and purple where
6330: the sun goes down!
6331: 
6332: With his straw in his mouth, Mr. Cruncher sat watching the two streams,
6333: like the heathen rustic who has for several centuries been on duty
6334: watching one stream--saving that Jerry had no expectation of their ever
6335: running dry. Nor would it have been an expectation of a hopeful kind,
6336: since a small part of his income was derived from the pilotage of timid
6337: women (mostly of a full habit and past the middle term of life) from
6338: Tellson's side of the tides to the opposite shore. Brief as such
6339: companionship was in every separate instance, Mr. Cruncher never failed
6340: to become so interested in the lady as to express a strong desire to
6341: have the honour of drinking her very good health. And it was from
6342: the gifts bestowed upon him towards the execution of this benevolent
6343: purpose, that he recruited his finances, as just now observed.
6344: 
6345: Time was, when a poet sat upon a stool in a public place, and mused in
6346: the sight of men. Mr. Cruncher, sitting on a stool in a public place,
6347: but not being a poet, mused as little as possible, and looked about him.
6348: 
6349: It fell out that he was thus engaged in a season when crowds were
6350: few, and belated women few, and when his affairs in general were so
6351: unprosperous as to awaken a strong suspicion in his breast that Mrs.
6352: Cruncher must have been "flopping" in some pointed manner, when an
6353: unusual concourse pouring down Fleet-street westward, attracted his
6354: attention. Looking that way, Mr. Cruncher made out that some kind of
6355: funeral was coming along, and that there was popular objection to this
6356: funeral, which engendered uproar.
6357: 
6358: "Young Jerry," said Mr. Cruncher, turning to his offspring, "it's a
6359: buryin'."
6360: 
6361: "Hooroar, father!" cried Young Jerry.
6362: 
6363: The young gentleman uttered this exultant sound with mysterious
6364: significance. The elder gentleman took the cry so ill, that he watched
6365: his opportunity, and smote the young gentleman on the ear.
6366: 
6367: "What d'ye mean? What are you hooroaring at? What do you want to conwey
6368: to your own father, you young Rip? This boy is a getting too many for
6369: _me_!" said Mr. Cruncher, surveying him. "Him and his hooroars! Don't
6370: let me hear no more of you, or you shall feel some more of me. D'ye
6371: hear?"
6372: 
6373: "I warn't doing no harm," Young Jerry protested, rubbing his cheek.
6374: 
6375: "Drop it then," said Mr. Cruncher; "I won't have none of _your_ no
6376: harms. Get a top of that there seat, and look at the crowd."
6377: 
6378: His son obeyed, and the crowd approached; they were bawling and hissing
6379: round a dingy hearse and dingy mourning coach, in which mourning coach
6380: there was only one mourner, dressed in the dingy trappings that were
6381: considered essential to the dignity of the position. The position
6382: appeared by no means to please him, however, with an increasing rabble
6383: surrounding the coach, deriding him, making grimaces at him, and
6384: incessantly groaning and calling out: "Yah! Spies! Tst! Yaha! Spies!"
6385: with many compliments too numerous and forcible to repeat.
6386: 
6387: Funerals had at all times a remarkable attraction for Mr. Cruncher; he
6388: always pricked up his senses, and became excited, when a funeral passed
6389: Tellson's. Naturally, therefore, a funeral with this uncommon attendance
6390: excited him greatly, and he asked of the first man who ran against him:
6391: 
6392: "What is it, brother? What's it about?"
6393: 
6394: "_I_ don't know," said the man. "Spies! Yaha! Tst! Spies!"
6395: 
6396: He asked another man. "Who is it?"
6397: 
6398: "_I_ don't know," returned the man, clapping his hands to his mouth
6399: nevertheless, and vociferating in a surprising heat and with the
6400: greatest ardour, "Spies! Yaha! Tst, tst! Spi--ies!"
6401: 
6402: At length, a person better informed on the merits of the case, tumbled
6403: against him, and from this person he learned that the funeral was the
6404: funeral of one Roger Cly.
6405: 
6406: "Was he a spy?" asked Mr. Cruncher.
6407: 
6408: "Old Bailey spy," returned his informant. "Yaha! Tst! Yah! Old Bailey
6409: Spi--i--ies!"
6410: 
6411: "Why, to be sure!" exclaimed Jerry, recalling the Trial at which he had
6412: assisted. "I've seen him. Dead, is he?"
6413: 
6414: "Dead as mutton," returned the other, "and can't be too dead. Have 'em
6415: out, there! Spies! Pull 'em out, there! Spies!"
6416: 
6417: The idea was so acceptable in the prevalent absence of any idea,
6418: that the crowd caught it up with eagerness, and loudly repeating the
6419: suggestion to have 'em out, and to pull 'em out, mobbed the two vehicles
6420: so closely that they came to a stop. On the crowd's opening the coach
6421: doors, the one mourner scuffled out by himself and was in their hands
6422: for a moment; but he was so alert, and made such good use of his time,
6423: that in another moment he was scouring away up a bye-street, after
6424: shedding his cloak, hat, long hatband, white pocket-handkerchief, and
6425: other symbolical tears.
6426: 
6427: These, the people tore to pieces and scattered far and wide with great
6428: enjoyment, while the tradesmen hurriedly shut up their shops; for a
6429: crowd in those times stopped at nothing, and was a monster much dreaded.
6430: They had already got the length of opening the hearse to take the coffin
6431: out, when some brighter genius proposed instead, its being escorted to
6432: its destination amidst general rejoicing. Practical suggestions being
6433: much needed, this suggestion, too, was received with acclamation, and
6434: the coach was immediately filled with eight inside and a dozen out,
6435: while as many people got on the roof of the hearse as could by any
6436: exercise of ingenuity stick upon it. Among the first of these volunteers
6437: was Jerry Cruncher himself, who modestly concealed his spiky head from
6438: the observation of Tellson's, in the further corner of the mourning
6439: coach.
6440: 
6441: The officiating undertakers made some protest against these changes in
6442: the ceremonies; but, the river being alarmingly near, and several voices
6443: remarking on the efficacy of cold immersion in bringing refractory
6444: members of the profession to reason, the protest was faint and brief.
6445: The remodelled procession started, with a chimney-sweep driving the
6446: hearse--advised by the regular driver, who was perched beside him, under
6447: close inspection, for the purpose--and with a pieman, also attended
6448: by his cabinet minister, driving the mourning coach. A bear-leader, a
6449: popular street character of the time, was impressed as an additional
6450: ornament, before the cavalcade had gone far down the Strand; and his
6451: bear, who was black and very mangy, gave quite an Undertaking air to
6452: that part of the procession in which he walked.
6453: 
6454: Thus, with beer-drinking, pipe-smoking, song-roaring, and infinite
6455: caricaturing of woe, the disorderly procession went its way, recruiting
6456: at every step, and all the shops shutting up before it. Its destination
6457: was the old church of Saint Pancras, far off in the fields. It got there
6458: in course of time; insisted on pouring into the burial-ground; finally,
6459: accomplished the interment of the deceased Roger Cly in its own way, and
6460: highly to its own satisfaction.
6461: 
6462: The dead man disposed of, and the crowd being under the necessity of
6463: providing some other entertainment for itself, another brighter
6464: genius (or perhaps the same) conceived the humour of impeaching casual
6465: passers-by, as Old Bailey spies, and wreaking vengeance on them. Chase
6466: was given to some scores of inoffensive persons who had never been near
6467: the Old Bailey in their lives, in the realisation of this fancy, and
6468: they were roughly hustled and maltreated. The transition to the sport of
6469: window-breaking, and thence to the plundering of public-houses, was easy
6470: and natural. At last, after several hours, when sundry summer-houses had
6471: been pulled down, and some area-railings had been torn up, to arm
6472: the more belligerent spirits, a rumour got about that the Guards were
6473: coming. Before this rumour, the crowd gradually melted away, and perhaps
6474: the Guards came, and perhaps they never came, and this was the usual
6475: progress of a mob.
6476: 
6477: Mr. Cruncher did not assist at the closing sports, but had remained
6478: behind in the churchyard, to confer and condole with the undertakers.
6479: The place had a soothing influence on him. He procured a pipe from a
6480: neighbouring public-house, and smoked it, looking in at the railings and
6481: maturely considering the spot.
6482: 
6483: "Jerry," said Mr. Cruncher, apostrophising himself in his usual way,
6484: "you see that there Cly that day, and you see with your own eyes that he
6485: was a young 'un and a straight made 'un."
6486: 
6487: Having smoked his pipe out, and ruminated a little longer, he turned
6488: himself about, that he might appear, before the hour of closing, on his
6489: station at Tellson's. Whether his meditations on mortality had touched
6490: his liver, or whether his general health had been previously at all
6491: amiss, or whether he desired to show a little attention to an eminent
6492: man, is not so much to the purpose, as that he made a short call upon
6493: his medical adviser--a distinguished surgeon--on his way back.
6494: 
6495: Young Jerry relieved his father with dutiful interest, and reported No
6496: job in his absence. The bank closed, the ancient clerks came out, the
6497: usual watch was set, and Mr. Cruncher and his son went home to tea.
6498: 
6499: "Now, I tell you where it is!" said Mr. Cruncher to his wife, on
6500: entering. "If, as a honest tradesman, my wenturs goes wrong to-night, I
6501: shall make sure that you've been praying again me, and I shall work you
6502: for it just the same as if I seen you do it."
6503: 
6504: The dejected Mrs. Cruncher shook her head.
6505: 
6506: "Why, you're at it afore my face!" said Mr. Cruncher, with signs of
6507: angry apprehension.
6508: 
6509: "I am saying nothing."
6510: 
6511: "Well, then; don't meditate nothing. You might as well flop as meditate.
6512: You may as well go again me one way as another. Drop it altogether."
6513: 
6514: "Yes, Jerry."
6515: 
6516: "Yes, Jerry," repeated Mr. Cruncher sitting down to tea. "Ah! It _is_
6517: yes, Jerry. That's about it. You may say yes, Jerry."
6518: 
6519: Mr. Cruncher had no particular meaning in these sulky corroborations,
6520: but made use of them, as people not unfrequently do, to express general
6521: ironical dissatisfaction.
6522: 
6523: "You and your yes, Jerry," said Mr. Cruncher, taking a bite out of his
6524: bread-and-butter, and seeming to help it down with a large invisible
6525: oyster out of his saucer. "Ah! I think so. I believe you."
6526: 
6527: "You are going out to-night?" asked his decent wife, when he took
6528: another bite.
6529: 
6530: "Yes, I am."
6531: 
6532: "May I go with you, father?" asked his son, briskly.
6533: 
6534: "No, you mayn't. I'm a going--as your mother knows--a fishing. That's
6535: where I'm going to. Going a fishing."
6536: 
6537: "Your fishing-rod gets rayther rusty; don't it, father?"
6538: 
6539: "Never you mind."
6540: 
6541: "Shall you bring any fish home, father?"
6542: 
6543: "If I don't, you'll have short commons, to-morrow," returned that
6544: gentleman, shaking his head; "that's questions enough for you; I ain't a
6545: going out, till you've been long abed."
6546: 
6547: He devoted himself during the remainder of the evening to keeping a
6548: most vigilant watch on Mrs. Cruncher, and sullenly holding her in
6549: conversation that she might be prevented from meditating any petitions
6550: to his disadvantage. With this view, he urged his son to hold her in
6551: conversation also, and led the unfortunate woman a hard life by dwelling
6552: on any causes of complaint he could bring against her, rather than
6553: he would leave her for a moment to her own reflections. The devoutest
6554: person could have rendered no greater homage to the efficacy of an
6555: honest prayer than he did in this distrust of his wife. It was as if a
6556: professed unbeliever in ghosts should be frightened by a ghost story.
6557: 
6558: "And mind you!" said Mr. Cruncher. "No games to-morrow! If I, as a
6559: honest tradesman, succeed in providing a jinte of meat or two, none
6560: of your not touching of it, and sticking to bread. If I, as a honest
6561: tradesman, am able to provide a little beer, none of your declaring
6562: on water. When you go to Rome, do as Rome does. Rome will be a ugly
6563: customer to you, if you don't. _I_'m your Rome, you know."
6564: 
6565: Then he began grumbling again:
6566: 
6567: "With your flying into the face of your own wittles and drink! I don't
6568: know how scarce you mayn't make the wittles and drink here, by your
6569: flopping tricks and your unfeeling conduct. Look at your boy: he _is_
6570: your'n, ain't he? He's as thin as a lath. Do you call yourself a mother,
6571: and not know that a mother's first duty is to blow her boy out?"
6572: 
6573: This touched Young Jerry on a tender place; who adjured his mother to
6574: perform her first duty, and, whatever else she did or neglected, above
6575: all things to lay especial stress on the discharge of that maternal
6576: function so affectingly and delicately indicated by his other parent.
6577: 
6578: Thus the evening wore away with the Cruncher family, until Young Jerry
6579: was ordered to bed, and his mother, laid under similar injunctions,
6580: obeyed them. Mr. Cruncher beguiled the earlier watches of the night with
6581: solitary pipes, and did not start upon his excursion until nearly one
6582: o'clock. Towards that small and ghostly hour, he rose up from his chair,
6583: took a key out of his pocket, opened a locked cupboard, and brought
6584: forth a sack, a crowbar of convenient size, a rope and chain, and other
6585: fishing tackle of that nature. Disposing these articles about him
6586: in skilful manner, he bestowed a parting defiance on Mrs. Cruncher,
6587: extinguished the light, and went out.
6588: 
6589: Young Jerry, who had only made a feint of undressing when he went to
6590: bed, was not long after his father. Under cover of the darkness he
6591: followed out of the room, followed down the stairs, followed down the
6592: court, followed out into the streets. He was in no uneasiness concerning
6593: his getting into the house again, for it was full of lodgers, and the
6594: door stood ajar all night.
6595: 
6596: Impelled by a laudable ambition to study the art and mystery of his
6597: father's honest calling, Young Jerry, keeping as close to house fronts,
6598: walls, and doorways, as his eyes were close to one another, held his
6599: honoured parent in view. The honoured parent steering Northward, had not
6600: gone far, when he was joined by another disciple of Izaak Walton, and
6601: the two trudged on together.
6602: 
6603: Within half an hour from the first starting, they were beyond the
6604: winking lamps, and the more than winking watchmen, and were out upon a
6605: lonely road. Another fisherman was picked up here--and that so silently,
6606: that if Young Jerry had been superstitious, he might have supposed the
6607: second follower of the gentle craft to have, all of a sudden, split
6608: himself into two.
6609: 
6610: The three went on, and Young Jerry went on, until the three stopped
6611: under a bank overhanging the road. Upon the top of the bank was a low
6612: brick wall, surmounted by an iron railing. In the shadow of bank and
6613: wall the three turned out of the road, and up a blind lane, of which
6614: the wall--there, risen to some eight or ten feet high--formed one side.
6615: Crouching down in a corner, peeping up the lane, the next object that
6616: Young Jerry saw, was the form of his honoured parent, pretty well
6617: defined against a watery and clouded moon, nimbly scaling an iron gate.
6618: He was soon over, and then the second fisherman got over, and then the
6619: third. They all dropped softly on the ground within the gate, and lay
6620: there a little--listening perhaps. Then, they moved away on their hands
6621: and knees.
6622: 
6623: It was now Young Jerry's turn to approach the gate: which he did,
6624: holding his breath. Crouching down again in a corner there, and looking
6625: in, he made out the three fishermen creeping through some rank grass!
6626: and all the gravestones in the churchyard--it was a large churchyard
6627: that they were in--looking on like ghosts in white, while the church
6628: tower itself looked on like the ghost of a monstrous giant. They did not
6629: creep far, before they stopped and stood upright. And then they began to
6630: fish.
6631: 
6632: They fished with a spade, at first. Presently the honoured parent
6633: appeared to be adjusting some instrument like a great corkscrew.
6634: Whatever tools they worked with, they worked hard, until the awful
6635: striking of the church clock so terrified Young Jerry, that he made off,
6636: with his hair as stiff as his father's.
6637: 
6638: But, his long-cherished desire to know more about these matters, not
6639: only stopped him in his running away, but lured him back again. They
6640: were still fishing perseveringly, when he peeped in at the gate for
6641: the second time; but, now they seemed to have got a bite. There was a
6642: screwing and complaining sound down below, and their bent figures were
6643: strained, as if by a weight. By slow degrees the weight broke away the
6644: earth upon it, and came to the surface. Young Jerry very well knew what
6645: it would be; but, when he saw it, and saw his honoured parent about to
6646: wrench it open, he was so frightened, being new to the sight, that he
6647: made off again, and never stopped until he had run a mile or more.
6648: 
6649: He would not have stopped then, for anything less necessary than breath,
6650: it being a spectral sort of race that he ran, and one highly desirable
6651: to get to the end of. He had a strong idea that the coffin he had seen
6652: was running after him; and, pictured as hopping on behind him, bolt
6653: upright, upon its narrow end, always on the point of overtaking him
6654: and hopping on at his side--perhaps taking his arm--it was a pursuer to
6655: shun. It was an inconsistent and ubiquitous fiend too, for, while it
6656: was making the whole night behind him dreadful, he darted out into the
6657: roadway to avoid dark alleys, fearful of its coming hopping out of them
6658: like a dropsical boy's kite without tail and wings. It hid in doorways
6659: too, rubbing its horrible shoulders against doors, and drawing them up
6660: to its ears, as if it were laughing. It got into shadows on the road,
6661: and lay cunningly on its back to trip him up. All this time it was
6662: incessantly hopping on behind and gaining on him, so that when the boy
6663: got to his own door he had reason for being half dead. And even then
6664: it would not leave him, but followed him upstairs with a bump on every
6665: stair, scrambled into bed with him, and bumped down, dead and heavy, on
6666: his breast when he fell asleep.
6667: 
6668: From his oppressed slumber, Young Jerry in his closet was awakened after
6669: daybreak and before sunrise, by the presence of his father in the
6670: family room. Something had gone wrong with him; at least, so Young Jerry
6671: inferred, from the circumstance of his holding Mrs. Cruncher by the
6672: ears, and knocking the back of her head against the head-board of the
6673: bed.
6674: 
6675: "I told you I would," said Mr. Cruncher, "and I did."
6676: 
6677: "Jerry, Jerry, Jerry!" his wife implored.
6678: 
6679: "You oppose yourself to the profit of the business," said Jerry, "and me
6680: and my partners suffer. You was to honour and obey; why the devil don't
6681: you?"
6682: 
6683: "I try to be a good wife, Jerry," the poor woman protested, with tears.
6684: 
6685: "Is it being a good wife to oppose your husband's business? Is it
6686: honouring your husband to dishonour his business? Is it obeying your
6687: husband to disobey him on the wital subject of his business?"
6688: 
6689: "You hadn't taken to the dreadful business then, Jerry."
6690: 
6691: "It's enough for you," retorted Mr. Cruncher, "to be the wife of a
6692: honest tradesman, and not to occupy your female mind with calculations
6693: when he took to his trade or when he didn't. A honouring and obeying
6694: wife would let his trade alone altogether. Call yourself a religious
6695: woman? If you're a religious woman, give me a irreligious one! You have
6696: no more nat'ral sense of duty than the bed of this here Thames river has
6697: of a pile, and similarly it must be knocked into you."
6698: 
6699: The altercation was conducted in a low tone of voice, and terminated in
6700: the honest tradesman's kicking off his clay-soiled boots, and lying down
6701: at his length on the floor. After taking a timid peep at him lying on
6702: his back, with his rusty hands under his head for a pillow, his son lay
6703: down too, and fell asleep again.
6704: 
6705: There was no fish for breakfast, and not much of anything else. Mr.
6706: Cruncher was out of spirits, and out of temper, and kept an iron pot-lid
6707: by him as a projectile for the correction of Mrs. Cruncher, in case
6708: he should observe any symptoms of her saying Grace. He was brushed
6709: and washed at the usual hour, and set off with his son to pursue his
6710: ostensible calling.
6711: 
6712: Young Jerry, walking with the stool under his arm at his father's side
6713: along sunny and crowded Fleet-street, was a very different Young Jerry
6714: from him of the previous night, running home through darkness and
6715: solitude from his grim pursuer. His cunning was fresh with the day,
6716: and his qualms were gone with the night--in which particulars it is not
6717: improbable that he had compeers in Fleet-street and the City of London,
6718: that fine morning.
6719: 
6720: "Father," said Young Jerry, as they walked along: taking care to keep
6721: at arm's length and to have the stool well between them: "what's a
6722: Resurrection-Man?"
6723: 
6724: Mr. Cruncher came to a stop on the pavement before he answered, "How
6725: should I know?"
6726: 
6727: "I thought you knowed everything, father," said the artless boy.
6728: 
6729: "Hem! Well," returned Mr. Cruncher, going on again, and lifting off his
6730: hat to give his spikes free play, "he's a tradesman."
6731: 
6732: "What's his goods, father?" asked the brisk Young Jerry.
6733: 
6734: "His goods," said Mr. Cruncher, after turning it over in his mind, "is a
6735: branch of Scientific goods."
6736: 
6737: "Persons' bodies, ain't it, father?" asked the lively boy.
6738: 
6739: "I believe it is something of that sort," said Mr. Cruncher.
6740: 
6741: "Oh, father, I should so like to be a Resurrection-Man when I'm quite
6742: growed up!"
6743: 
6744: Mr. Cruncher was soothed, but shook his head in a dubious and moral way.
6745: "It depends upon how you dewelop your talents. Be careful to dewelop
6746: your talents, and never to say no more than you can help to nobody, and
6747: there's no telling at the present time what you may not come to be fit
6748: for." As Young Jerry, thus encouraged, went on a few yards in advance,
6749: to plant the stool in the shadow of the Bar, Mr. Cruncher added to
6750: himself: "Jerry, you honest tradesman, there's hopes wot that boy will
6751: yet be a blessing to you, and a recompense to you for his mother!"
6752: 
6753: 
6754: 
6755: 
6756: XV. Knitting
6757: 
6758: 
6759: There had been earlier drinking than usual in the wine-shop of Monsieur
6760: Defarge. As early as six o'clock in the morning, sallow faces peeping
6761: through its barred windows had descried other faces within, bending over
6762: measures of wine. Monsieur Defarge sold a very thin wine at the best
6763: of times, but it would seem to have been an unusually thin wine that
6764: he sold at this time. A sour wine, moreover, or a souring, for its
6765: influence on the mood of those who drank it was to make them gloomy. No
6766: vivacious Bacchanalian flame leaped out of the pressed grape of Monsieur
6767: Defarge: but, a smouldering fire that burnt in the dark, lay hidden in
6768: the dregs of it.
6769: 
6770: This had been the third morning in succession, on which there had been
6771: early drinking at the wine-shop of Monsieur Defarge. It had begun
6772: on Monday, and here was Wednesday come. There had been more of early
6773: brooding than drinking; for, many men had listened and whispered and
6774: slunk about there from the time of the opening of the door, who could
6775: not have laid a piece of money on the counter to save their souls. These
6776: were to the full as interested in the place, however, as if they could
6777: have commanded whole barrels of wine; and they glided from seat to seat,
6778: and from corner to corner, swallowing talk in lieu of drink, with greedy
6779: looks.
6780: 
6781: Notwithstanding an unusual flow of company, the master of the wine-shop
6782: was not visible. He was not missed; for, nobody who crossed the
6783: threshold looked for him, nobody asked for him, nobody wondered to see
6784: only Madame Defarge in her seat, presiding over the distribution of
6785: wine, with a bowl of battered small coins before her, as much defaced
6786: and beaten out of their original impress as the small coinage of
6787: humanity from whose ragged pockets they had come.
6788: 
6789: A suspended interest and a prevalent absence of mind, were perhaps
6790: observed by the spies who looked in at the wine-shop, as they looked in
6791: at every place, high and low, from the king's palace to the criminal's
6792: gaol. Games at cards languished, players at dominoes musingly built
6793: towers with them, drinkers drew figures on the tables with spilt drops
6794: of wine, Madame Defarge herself picked out the pattern on her sleeve
6795: with her toothpick, and saw and heard something inaudible and invisible
6796: a long way off.
6797: 
6798: Thus, Saint Antoine in this vinous feature of his, until midday. It was
6799: high noontide, when two dusty men passed through his streets and under
6800: his swinging lamps: of whom, one was Monsieur Defarge: the other a
6801: mender of roads in a blue cap. All adust and athirst, the two entered
6802: the wine-shop. Their arrival had lighted a kind of fire in the breast
6803: of Saint Antoine, fast spreading as they came along, which stirred and
6804: flickered in flames of faces at most doors and windows. Yet, no one had
6805: followed them, and no man spoke when they entered the wine-shop, though
6806: the eyes of every man there were turned upon them.
6807: 
6808: "Good day, gentlemen!" said Monsieur Defarge.
6809: 
6810: It may have been a signal for loosening the general tongue. It elicited
6811: an answering chorus of "Good day!"
6812: 
6813: "It is bad weather, gentlemen," said Defarge, shaking his head.
6814: 
6815: Upon which, every man looked at his neighbour, and then all cast down
6816: their eyes and sat silent. Except one man, who got up and went out.
6817: 
6818: "My wife," said Defarge aloud, addressing Madame Defarge: "I have
6819: travelled certain leagues with this good mender of roads, called
6820: Jacques. I met him--by accident--a day and half's journey out of Paris.
6821: He is a good child, this mender of roads, called Jacques. Give him to
6822: drink, my wife!"
6823: 
6824: A second man got up and went out. Madame Defarge set wine before the
6825: mender of roads called Jacques, who doffed his blue cap to the company,
6826: and drank. In the breast of his blouse he carried some coarse dark
6827: bread; he ate of this between whiles, and sat munching and drinking near
6828: Madame Defarge's counter. A third man got up and went out.
6829: 
6830: Defarge refreshed himself with a draught of wine--but, he took less
6831: than was given to the stranger, as being himself a man to whom it was no
6832: rarity--and stood waiting until the countryman had made his breakfast.
6833: He looked at no one present, and no one now looked at him; not even
6834: Madame Defarge, who had taken up her knitting, and was at work.
6835: 
6836: "Have you finished your repast, friend?" he asked, in due season.
6837: 
6838: "Yes, thank you."
6839: 
6840: "Come, then! You shall see the apartment that I told you you could
6841: occupy. It will suit you to a marvel."
6842: 
6843: Out of the wine-shop into the street, out of the street into a
6844: courtyard, out of the courtyard up a steep staircase, out of the
6845: staircase into a garret--formerly the garret where a white-haired man
6846: sat on a low bench, stooping forward and very busy, making shoes.
6847: 
6848: No white-haired man was there now; but, the three men were there who had
6849: gone out of the wine-shop singly. And between them and the white-haired
6850: man afar off, was the one small link, that they had once looked in at
6851: him through the chinks in the wall.
6852: 
6853: Defarge closed the door carefully, and spoke in a subdued voice:
6854: 
6855: "Jacques One, Jacques Two, Jacques Three! This is the witness
6856: encountered by appointment, by me, Jacques Four. He will tell you all.
6857: Speak, Jacques Five!"
6858: 
6859: The mender of roads, blue cap in hand, wiped his swarthy forehead with
6860: it, and said, "Where shall I commence, monsieur?"
6861: 
6862: "Commence," was Monsieur Defarge's not unreasonable reply, "at the
6863: commencement."
6864: 
6865: "I saw him then, messieurs," began the mender of roads, "a year ago this
6866: running summer, underneath the carriage of the Marquis, hanging by the
6867: chain. Behold the manner of it. I leaving my work on the road, the sun
6868: going to bed, the carriage of the Marquis slowly ascending the hill, he
6869: hanging by the chain--like this."
6870: 
6871: Again the mender of roads went through the whole performance; in which
6872: he ought to have been perfect by that time, seeing that it had been
6873: the infallible resource and indispensable entertainment of his village
6874: during a whole year.
6875: 
6876: Jacques One struck in, and asked if he had ever seen the man before?
6877: 
6878: "Never," answered the mender of roads, recovering his perpendicular.
6879: 
6880: Jacques Three demanded how he afterwards recognised him then?
6881: 
6882: "By his tall figure," said the mender of roads, softly, and with his
6883: finger at his nose. "When Monsieur the Marquis demands that evening,
6884: 'Say, what is he like?' I make response, 'Tall as a spectre.'"
6885: 
6886: "You should have said, short as a dwarf," returned Jacques Two.
6887: 
6888: "But what did I know? The deed was not then accomplished, neither did he
6889: confide in me. Observe! Under those circumstances even, I do not
6890: offer my testimony. Monsieur the Marquis indicates me with his finger,
6891: standing near our little fountain, and says, 'To me! Bring that rascal!'
6892: My faith, messieurs, I offer nothing."
6893: 
6894: "He is right there, Jacques," murmured Defarge, to him who had
6895: interrupted. "Go on!"
6896: 
6897: "Good!" said the mender of roads, with an air of mystery. "The tall man
6898: is lost, and he is sought--how many months? Nine, ten, eleven?"
6899: 
6900: "No matter, the number," said Defarge. "He is well hidden, but at last
6901: he is unluckily found. Go on!"
6902: 
6903: "I am again at work upon the hill-side, and the sun is again about to
6904: go to bed. I am collecting my tools to descend to my cottage down in the
6905: village below, where it is already dark, when I raise my eyes, and see
6906: coming over the hill six soldiers. In the midst of them is a tall man
6907: with his arms bound--tied to his sides--like this!"
6908: 
6909: With the aid of his indispensable cap, he represented a man with his
6910: elbows bound fast at his hips, with cords that were knotted behind him.
6911: 
6912: "I stand aside, messieurs, by my heap of stones, to see the soldiers
6913: and their prisoner pass (for it is a solitary road, that, where any
6914: spectacle is well worth looking at), and at first, as they approach, I
6915: see no more than that they are six soldiers with a tall man bound, and
6916: that they are almost black to my sight--except on the side of the sun
6917: going to bed, where they have a red edge, messieurs. Also, I see that
6918: their long shadows are on the hollow ridge on the opposite side of the
6919: road, and are on the hill above it, and are like the shadows of giants.
6920: Also, I see that they are covered with dust, and that the dust moves
6921: with them as they come, tramp, tramp! But when they advance quite near
6922: to me, I recognise the tall man, and he recognises me. Ah, but he would
6923: be well content to precipitate himself over the hill-side once again, as
6924: on the evening when he and I first encountered, close to the same spot!"
6925: 
6926: He described it as if he were there, and it was evident that he saw it
6927: vividly; perhaps he had not seen much in his life.
6928: 
6929: "I do not show the soldiers that I recognise the tall man; he does not
6930: show the soldiers that he recognises me; we do it, and we know it, with
6931: our eyes. 'Come on!' says the chief of that company, pointing to the
6932: village, 'bring him fast to his tomb!' and they bring him faster. I
6933: follow. His arms are swelled because of being bound so tight, his wooden
6934: shoes are large and clumsy, and he is lame. Because he is lame, and
6935: consequently slow, they drive him with their guns--like this!"
6936: 
6937: He imitated the action of a man's being impelled forward by the
6938: butt-ends of muskets.
6939: 
6940: "As they descend the hill like madmen running a race, he falls. They
6941: laugh and pick him up again. His face is bleeding and covered with dust,
6942: but he cannot touch it; thereupon they laugh again. They bring him into
6943: the village; all the village runs to look; they take him past the mill,
6944: and up to the prison; all the village sees the prison gate open in the
6945: darkness of the night, and swallow him--like this!"
6946: 
6947: He opened his mouth as wide as he could, and shut it with a sounding
6948: snap of his teeth. Observant of his unwillingness to mar the effect by
6949: opening it again, Defarge said, "Go on, Jacques."
6950: 
6951: "All the village," pursued the mender of roads, on tiptoe and in a low
6952: voice, "withdraws; all the village whispers by the fountain; all the
6953: village sleeps; all the village dreams of that unhappy one, within the
6954: locks and bars of the prison on the crag, and never to come out of it,
6955: except to perish. In the morning, with my tools upon my shoulder, eating
6956: my morsel of black bread as I go, I make a circuit by the prison, on
6957: my way to my work. There I see him, high up, behind the bars of a lofty
6958: iron cage, bloody and dusty as last night, looking through. He has no
6959: hand free, to wave to me; I dare not call to him; he regards me like a
6960: dead man."
6961: 
6962: Defarge and the three glanced darkly at one another. The looks of all
6963: of them were dark, repressed, and revengeful, as they listened to the
6964: countryman's story; the manner of all of them, while it was secret, was
6965: authoritative too. They had the air of a rough tribunal; Jacques One
6966: and Two sitting on the old pallet-bed, each with his chin resting on
6967: his hand, and his eyes intent on the road-mender; Jacques Three, equally
6968: intent, on one knee behind them, with his agitated hand always gliding
6969: over the network of fine nerves about his mouth and nose; Defarge
6970: standing between them and the narrator, whom he had stationed in the
6971: light of the window, by turns looking from him to them, and from them to
6972: him.
6973: 
6974: "Go on, Jacques," said Defarge.
6975: 
6976: "He remains up there in his iron cage some days. The village looks
6977: at him by stealth, for it is afraid. But it always looks up, from a
6978: distance, at the prison on the crag; and in the evening, when the work
6979: of the day is achieved and it assembles to gossip at the fountain, all
6980: faces are turned towards the prison. Formerly, they were turned towards
6981: the posting-house; now, they are turned towards the prison. They
6982: whisper at the fountain, that although condemned to death he will not be
6983: executed; they say that petitions have been presented in Paris, showing
6984: that he was enraged and made mad by the death of his child; they say
6985: that a petition has been presented to the King himself. What do I know?
6986: It is possible. Perhaps yes, perhaps no."
6987: 
6988: "Listen then, Jacques," Number One of that name sternly interposed.
6989: "Know that a petition was presented to the King and Queen. All here,
6990: yourself excepted, saw the King take it, in his carriage in the street,
6991: sitting beside the Queen. It is Defarge whom you see here, who, at the
6992: hazard of his life, darted out before the horses, with the petition in
6993: his hand."
6994: 
6995: "And once again listen, Jacques!" said the kneeling Number Three:
6996: his fingers ever wandering over and over those fine nerves, with a
6997: strikingly greedy air, as if he hungered for something--that was neither
6998: food nor drink; "the guard, horse and foot, surrounded the petitioner,
6999: and struck him blows. You hear?"
7000: 
7001: "I hear, messieurs."
7002: 
7003: "Go on then," said Defarge.
7004: 
7005: "Again; on the other hand, they whisper at the fountain," resumed the
7006: countryman, "that he is brought down into our country to be executed on
7007: the spot, and that he will very certainly be executed. They even whisper
7008: that because he has slain Monseigneur, and because Monseigneur was the
7009: father of his tenants--serfs--what you will--he will be executed as a
7010: parricide. One old man says at the fountain, that his right hand, armed
7011: with the knife, will be burnt off before his face; that, into wounds
7012: which will be made in his arms, his breast, and his legs, there will be
7013: poured boiling oil, melted lead, hot resin, wax, and sulphur; finally,
7014: that he will be torn limb from limb by four strong horses. That old man
7015: says, all this was actually done to a prisoner who made an attempt on
7016: the life of the late King, Louis Fifteen. But how do I know if he lies?
7017: I am not a scholar."
7018: 
7019: "Listen once again then, Jacques!" said the man with the restless hand
7020: and the craving air. "The name of that prisoner was Damiens, and it was
7021: all done in open day, in the open streets of this city of Paris; and
7022: nothing was more noticed in the vast concourse that saw it done, than
7023: the crowd of ladies of quality and fashion, who were full of eager
7024: attention to the last--to the last, Jacques, prolonged until nightfall,
7025: when he had lost two legs and an arm, and still breathed! And it was
7026: done--why, how old are you?"
7027: 
7028: "Thirty-five," said the mender of roads, who looked sixty.
7029: 
7030: "It was done when you were more than ten years old; you might have seen
7031: it."
7032: 
7033: "Enough!" said Defarge, with grim impatience. "Long live the Devil! Go
7034: on."
7035: 
7036: "Well! Some whisper this, some whisper that; they speak of nothing else;
7037: even the fountain appears to fall to that tune. At length, on Sunday
7038: night when all the village is asleep, come soldiers, winding down from
7039: the prison, and their guns ring on the stones of the little street.
7040: Workmen dig, workmen hammer, soldiers laugh and sing; in the morning, by
7041: the fountain, there is raised a gallows forty feet high, poisoning the
7042: water."
7043: 
7044: The mender of roads looked _through_ rather than _at_ the low ceiling,
7045: and pointed as if he saw the gallows somewhere in the sky.
7046: 
7047: "All work is stopped, all assemble there, nobody leads the cows out,
7048: the cows are there with the rest. At midday, the roll of drums. Soldiers
7049: have marched into the prison in the night, and he is in the midst
7050: of many soldiers. He is bound as before, and in his mouth there is
7051: a gag--tied so, with a tight string, making him look almost as if he
7052: laughed." He suggested it, by creasing his face with his two thumbs,
7053: from the corners of his mouth to his ears. "On the top of the gallows is
7054: fixed the knife, blade upwards, with its point in the air. He is hanged
7055: there forty feet high--and is left hanging, poisoning the water."
7056: 
7057: They looked at one another, as he used his blue cap to wipe his face,
7058: on which the perspiration had started afresh while he recalled the
7059: spectacle.
7060: 
7061: "It is frightful, messieurs. How can the women and the children draw
7062: water! Who can gossip of an evening, under that shadow! Under it, have
7063: I said? When I left the village, Monday evening as the sun was going to
7064: bed, and looked back from the hill, the shadow struck across the church,
7065: across the mill, across the prison--seemed to strike across the earth,
7066: messieurs, to where the sky rests upon it!"
7067: 
7068: The hungry man gnawed one of his fingers as he looked at the other
7069: three, and his finger quivered with the craving that was on him.
7070: 
7071: "That's all, messieurs. I left at sunset (as I had been warned to do),
7072: and I walked on, that night and half next day, until I met (as I was
7073: warned I should) this comrade. With him, I came on, now riding and now
7074: walking, through the rest of yesterday and through last night. And here
7075: you see me!"
7076: 
7077: After a gloomy silence, the first Jacques said, "Good! You have acted
7078: and recounted faithfully. Will you wait for us a little, outside the
7079: door?"
7080: 
7081: "Very willingly," said the mender of roads. Whom Defarge escorted to the
7082: top of the stairs, and, leaving seated there, returned.
7083: 
7084: The three had risen, and their heads were together when he came back to
7085: the garret.
7086: 
7087: "How say you, Jacques?" demanded Number One. "To be registered?"
7088: 
7089: "To be registered, as doomed to destruction," returned Defarge.
7090: 
7091: "Magnificent!" croaked the man with the craving.
7092: 
7093: "The chateau, and all the race?" inquired the first.
7094: 
7095: "The chateau and all the race," returned Defarge. "Extermination."
7096: 
7097: The hungry man repeated, in a rapturous croak, "Magnificent!" and began
7098: gnawing another finger.
7099: 
7100: "Are you sure," asked Jacques Two, of Defarge, "that no embarrassment
7101: can arise from our manner of keeping the register? Without doubt it is
7102: safe, for no one beyond ourselves can decipher it; but shall we always
7103: be able to decipher it--or, I ought to say, will she?"
7104: 
7105: "Jacques," returned Defarge, drawing himself up, "if madame my wife
7106: undertook to keep the register in her memory alone, she would not lose
7107: a word of it--not a syllable of it. Knitted, in her own stitches and her
7108: own symbols, it will always be as plain to her as the sun. Confide in
7109: Madame Defarge. It would be easier for the weakest poltroon that lives,
7110: to erase himself from existence, than to erase one letter of his name or
7111: crimes from the knitted register of Madame Defarge."
7112: 
7113: There was a murmur of confidence and approval, and then the man who
7114: hungered, asked: "Is this rustic to be sent back soon? I hope so. He is
7115: very simple; is he not a little dangerous?"
7116: 
7117: "He knows nothing," said Defarge; "at least nothing more than would
7118: easily elevate himself to a gallows of the same height. I charge myself
7119: with him; let him remain with me; I will take care of him, and set him
7120: on his road. He wishes to see the fine world--the King, the Queen, and
7121: Court; let him see them on Sunday."
7122: 
7123: "What?" exclaimed the hungry man, staring. "Is it a good sign, that he
7124: wishes to see Royalty and Nobility?"
7125: 
7126: "Jacques," said Defarge; "judiciously show a cat milk, if you wish her
7127: to thirst for it. Judiciously show a dog his natural prey, if you wish
7128: him to bring it down one day."
7129: 
7130: Nothing more was said, and the mender of roads, being found already
7131: dozing on the topmost stair, was advised to lay himself down on the
7132: pallet-bed and take some rest. He needed no persuasion, and was soon
7133: asleep.
7134: 
7135: Worse quarters than Defarge's wine-shop, could easily have been found
7136: in Paris for a provincial slave of that degree. Saving for a mysterious
7137: dread of madame by which he was constantly haunted, his life was very
7138: new and agreeable. But, madame sat all day at her counter, so expressly
7139: unconscious of him, and so particularly determined not to perceive that
7140: his being there had any connection with anything below the surface, that
7141: he shook in his wooden shoes whenever his eye lighted on her. For, he
7142: contended with himself that it was impossible to foresee what that lady
7143: might pretend next; and he felt assured that if she should take it
7144: into her brightly ornamented head to pretend that she had seen him do a
7145: murder and afterwards flay the victim, she would infallibly go through
7146: with it until the play was played out.
7147: 
7148: Therefore, when Sunday came, the mender of roads was not enchanted
7149: (though he said he was) to find that madame was to accompany monsieur
7150: and himself to Versailles. It was additionally disconcerting to have
7151: madame knitting all the way there, in a public conveyance; it was
7152: additionally disconcerting yet, to have madame in the crowd in the
7153: afternoon, still with her knitting in her hands as the crowd waited to
7154: see the carriage of the King and Queen.
7155: 
7156: "You work hard, madame," said a man near her.
7157: 
7158: "Yes," answered Madame Defarge; "I have a good deal to do."
7159: 
7160: "What do you make, madame?"
7161: 
7162: "Many things."
7163: 
7164: "For instance--"
7165: 
7166: "For instance," returned Madame Defarge, composedly, "shrouds."
7167: 
7168: The man moved a little further away, as soon as he could, and the mender
7169: of roads fanned himself with his blue cap: feeling it mightily close
7170: and oppressive. If he needed a King and Queen to restore him, he was
7171: fortunate in having his remedy at hand; for, soon the large-faced King
7172: and the fair-faced Queen came in their golden coach, attended by the
7173: shining Bull's Eye of their Court, a glittering multitude of laughing
7174: ladies and fine lords; and in jewels and silks and powder and splendour
7175: and elegantly spurning figures and handsomely disdainful faces of both
7176: sexes, the mender of roads bathed himself, so much to his temporary
7177: intoxication, that he cried Long live the King, Long live the Queen,
7178: Long live everybody and everything! as if he had never heard of
7179: ubiquitous Jacques in his time. Then, there were gardens, courtyards,
7180: terraces, fountains, green banks, more King and Queen, more Bull's Eye,
7181: more lords and ladies, more Long live they all! until he absolutely wept
7182: with sentiment. During the whole of this scene, which lasted some three
7183: hours, he had plenty of shouting and weeping and sentimental company,
7184: and throughout Defarge held him by the collar, as if to restrain him
7185: from flying at the objects of his brief devotion and tearing them to
7186: pieces.
7187: 
7188: "Bravo!" said Defarge, clapping him on the back when it was over, like a
7189: patron; "you are a good boy!"
7190: 
7191: The mender of roads was now coming to himself, and was mistrustful of
7192: having made a mistake in his late demonstrations; but no.
7193: 
7194: "You are the fellow we want," said Defarge, in his ear; "you make
7195: these fools believe that it will last for ever. Then, they are the more
7196: insolent, and it is the nearer ended."
7197: 
7198: "Hey!" cried the mender of roads, reflectively; "that's true."
7199: 
7200: "These fools know nothing. While they despise your breath, and would
7201: stop it for ever and ever, in you or in a hundred like you rather than
7202: in one of their own horses or dogs, they only know what your breath
7203: tells them. Let it deceive them, then, a little longer; it cannot
7204: deceive them too much."
7205: 
7206: Madame Defarge looked superciliously at the client, and nodded in
7207: confirmation.
7208: 
7209: "As to you," said she, "you would shout and shed tears for anything, if
7210: it made a show and a noise. Say! Would you not?"
7211: 
7212: "Truly, madame, I think so. For the moment."
7213: 
7214: "If you were shown a great heap of dolls, and were set upon them to
7215: pluck them to pieces and despoil them for your own advantage, you would
7216: pick out the richest and gayest. Say! Would you not?"
7217: 
7218: "Truly yes, madame."
7219: 
7220: "Yes. And if you were shown a flock of birds, unable to fly, and were
7221: set upon them to strip them of their feathers for your own advantage,
7222: you would set upon the birds of the finest feathers; would you not?"
7223: 
7224: "It is true, madame."
7225: 
7226: "You have seen both dolls and birds to-day," said Madame Defarge, with
7227: a wave of her hand towards the place where they had last been apparent;
7228: "now, go home!"
7229: 
7230: 
7231: 
7232: 
7233: XVI. Still Knitting
7234: 
7235: 
7236: Madame Defarge and monsieur her husband returned amicably to the
7237: bosom of Saint Antoine, while a speck in a blue cap toiled through the
7238: darkness, and through the dust, and down the weary miles of avenue by
7239: the wayside, slowly tending towards that point of the compass where
7240: the chateau of Monsieur the Marquis, now in his grave, listened to
7241: the whispering trees. Such ample leisure had the stone faces, now,
7242: for listening to the trees and to the fountain, that the few village
7243: scarecrows who, in their quest for herbs to eat and fragments of dead
7244: stick to burn, strayed within sight of the great stone courtyard and
7245: terrace staircase, had it borne in upon their starved fancy that
7246: the expression of the faces was altered. A rumour just lived in the
7247: village--had a faint and bare existence there, as its people had--that
7248: when the knife struck home, the faces changed, from faces of pride to
7249: faces of anger and pain; also, that when that dangling figure was hauled
7250: up forty feet above the fountain, they changed again, and bore a cruel
7251: look of being avenged, which they would henceforth bear for ever. In the
7252: stone face over the great window of the bed-chamber where the murder
7253: was done, two fine dints were pointed out in the sculptured nose, which
7254: everybody recognised, and which nobody had seen of old; and on the
7255: scarce occasions when two or three ragged peasants emerged from the
7256: crowd to take a hurried peep at Monsieur the Marquis petrified, a
7257: skinny finger would not have pointed to it for a minute, before they all
7258: started away among the moss and leaves, like the more fortunate hares
7259: who could find a living there.
7260: 
7261: Chateau and hut, stone face and dangling figure, the red stain on the
7262: stone floor, and the pure water in the village well--thousands of acres
7263: of land--a whole province of France--all France itself--lay under the
7264: night sky, concentrated into a faint hair-breadth line. So does a whole
7265: world, with all its greatnesses and littlenesses, lie in a twinkling
7266: star. And as mere human knowledge can split a ray of light and analyse
7267: the manner of its composition, so, sublimer intelligences may read in
7268: the feeble shining of this earth of ours, every thought and act, every
7269: vice and virtue, of every responsible creature on it.
7270: 
7271: The Defarges, husband and wife, came lumbering under the starlight,
7272: in their public vehicle, to that gate of Paris whereunto their
7273: journey naturally tended. There was the usual stoppage at the barrier
7274: guardhouse, and the usual lanterns came glancing forth for the usual
7275: examination and inquiry. Monsieur Defarge alighted; knowing one or two
7276: of the soldiery there, and one of the police. The latter he was intimate
7277: with, and affectionately embraced.
7278: 
7279: When Saint Antoine had again enfolded the Defarges in his dusky wings,
7280: and they, having finally alighted near the Saint's boundaries, were
7281: picking their way on foot through the black mud and offal of his
7282: streets, Madame Defarge spoke to her husband:
7283: 
7284: "Say then, my friend; what did Jacques of the police tell thee?"
7285: 
7286: "Very little to-night, but all he knows. There is another spy
7287: commissioned for our quarter. There may be many more, for all that he
7288: can say, but he knows of one."
7289: 
7290: "Eh well!" said Madame Defarge, raising her eyebrows with a cool
7291: business air. "It is necessary to register him. How do they call that
7292: man?"
7293: 
7294: "He is English."
7295: 
7296: "So much the better. His name?"
7297: 
7298: "Barsad," said Defarge, making it French by pronunciation. But, he had
7299: been so careful to get it accurately, that he then spelt it with perfect
7300: correctness.
7301: 
7302: "Barsad," repeated madame. "Good. Christian name?"
7303: 
7304: "John."
7305: 
7306: "John Barsad," repeated madame, after murmuring it once to herself.
7307: "Good. His appearance; is it known?"
7308: 
7309: "Age, about forty years; height, about five feet nine; black hair;
7310: complexion dark; generally, rather handsome visage; eyes dark, face
7311: thin, long, and sallow; nose aquiline, but not straight, having a
7312: peculiar inclination towards the left cheek; expression, therefore,
7313: sinister."
7314: 
7315: "Eh my faith. It is a portrait!" said madame, laughing. "He shall be
7316: registered to-morrow."
7317: 
7318: They turned into the wine-shop, which was closed (for it was midnight),
7319: and where Madame Defarge immediately took her post at her desk, counted
7320: the small moneys that had been taken during her absence, examined the
7321: stock, went through the entries in the book, made other entries of
7322: her own, checked the serving man in every possible way, and finally
7323: dismissed him to bed. Then she turned out the contents of the bowl
7324: of money for the second time, and began knotting them up in her
7325: handkerchief, in a chain of separate knots, for safe keeping through the
7326: night. All this while, Defarge, with his pipe in his mouth, walked
7327: up and down, complacently admiring, but never interfering; in which
7328: condition, indeed, as to the business and his domestic affairs, he
7329: walked up and down through life.
7330: 
7331: The night was hot, and the shop, close shut and surrounded by so foul a
7332: neighbourhood, was ill-smelling. Monsieur Defarge's olfactory sense was
7333: by no means delicate, but the stock of wine smelt much stronger than
7334: it ever tasted, and so did the stock of rum and brandy and aniseed. He
7335: whiffed the compound of scents away, as he put down his smoked-out pipe.
7336: 
7337: "You are fatigued," said madame, raising her glance as she knotted the
7338: money. "There are only the usual odours."
7339: 
7340: "I am a little tired," her husband acknowledged.
7341: 
7342: "You are a little depressed, too," said madame, whose quick eyes had
7343: never been so intent on the accounts, but they had had a ray or two for
7344: him. "Oh, the men, the men!"
7345: 
7346: "But my dear!" began Defarge.
7347: 
7348: "But my dear!" repeated madame, nodding firmly; "but my dear! You are
7349: faint of heart to-night, my dear!"
7350: 
7351: "Well, then," said Defarge, as if a thought were wrung out of his
7352: breast, "it _is_ a long time."
7353: 
7354: "It is a long time," repeated his wife; "and when is it not a long time?
7355: Vengeance and retribution require a long time; it is the rule."
7356: 
7357: "It does not take a long time to strike a man with Lightning," said
7358: Defarge.
7359: 
7360: "How long," demanded madame, composedly, "does it take to make and store
7361: the lightning? Tell me."
7362: 
7363: Defarge raised his head thoughtfully, as if there were something in that
7364: too.
7365: 
7366: "It does not take a long time," said madame, "for an earthquake to
7367: swallow a town. Eh well! Tell me how long it takes to prepare the
7368: earthquake?"
7369: 
7370: "A long time, I suppose," said Defarge.
7371: 
7372: "But when it is ready, it takes place, and grinds to pieces everything
7373: before it. In the meantime, it is always preparing, though it is not
7374: seen or heard. That is your consolation. Keep it."
7375: 
7376: She tied a knot with flashing eyes, as if it throttled a foe.
7377: 
7378: "I tell thee," said madame, extending her right hand, for emphasis,
7379: "that although it is a long time on the road, it is on the road and
7380: coming. I tell thee it never retreats, and never stops. I tell thee it
7381: is always advancing. Look around and consider the lives of all the world
7382: that we know, consider the faces of all the world that we know, consider
7383: the rage and discontent to which the Jacquerie addresses itself with
7384: more and more of certainty every hour. Can such things last? Bah! I mock
7385: you."
7386: 
7387: "My brave wife," returned Defarge, standing before her with his head
7388: a little bent, and his hands clasped at his back, like a docile and
7389: attentive pupil before his catechist, "I do not question all this. But
7390: it has lasted a long time, and it is possible--you know well, my wife,
7391: it is possible--that it may not come, during our lives."
7392: 
7393: "Eh well! How then?" demanded madame, tying another knot, as if there
7394: were another enemy strangled.
7395: 
7396: "Well!" said Defarge, with a half complaining and half apologetic shrug.
7397: "We shall not see the triumph."
7398: 
7399: "We shall have helped it," returned madame, with her extended hand in
7400: strong action. "Nothing that we do, is done in vain. I believe, with all
7401: my soul, that we shall see the triumph. But even if not, even if I knew
7402: certainly not, show me the neck of an aristocrat and tyrant, and still I
7403: would--"
7404: 
7405: Then madame, with her teeth set, tied a very terrible knot indeed.
7406: 
7407: "Hold!" cried Defarge, reddening a little as if he felt charged with
7408: cowardice; "I too, my dear, will stop at nothing."
7409: 
7410: "Yes! But it is your weakness that you sometimes need to see your victim
7411: and your opportunity, to sustain you. Sustain yourself without that.
7412: When the time comes, let loose a tiger and a devil; but wait for the
7413: time with the tiger and the devil chained--not shown--yet always ready."
7414: 
7415: Madame enforced the conclusion of this piece of advice by striking her
7416: little counter with her chain of money as if she knocked its brains
7417: out, and then gathering the heavy handkerchief under her arm in a serene
7418: manner, and observing that it was time to go to bed.
7419: 
7420: Next noontide saw the admirable woman in her usual place in the
7421: wine-shop, knitting away assiduously. A rose lay beside her, and if she
7422: now and then glanced at the flower, it was with no infraction of her
7423: usual preoccupied air. There were a few customers, drinking or not
7424: drinking, standing or seated, sprinkled about. The day was very hot,
7425: and heaps of flies, who were extending their inquisitive and adventurous
7426: perquisitions into all the glutinous little glasses near madame, fell
7427: dead at the bottom. Their decease made no impression on the other flies
7428: out promenading, who looked at them in the coolest manner (as if they
7429: themselves were elephants, or something as far removed), until they met
7430: the same fate. Curious to consider how heedless flies are!--perhaps they
7431: thought as much at Court that sunny summer day.
7432: 
7433: A figure entering at the door threw a shadow on Madame Defarge which she
7434: felt to be a new one. She laid down her knitting, and began to pin her
7435: rose in her head-dress, before she looked at the figure.
7436: 
7437: It was curious. The moment Madame Defarge took up the rose, the
7438: customers ceased talking, and began gradually to drop out of the
7439: wine-shop.
7440: 
7441: "Good day, madame," said the new-comer.
7442: 
7443: "Good day, monsieur."
7444:
