7445: She said it aloud, but added to herself, as she resumed her knitting:
7446: "Hah! Good day, age about forty, height about five feet nine, black
7447: hair, generally rather handsome visage, complexion dark, eyes dark,
7448: thin, long and sallow face, aquiline nose but not straight, having a
7449: peculiar inclination towards the left cheek which imparts a sinister
7450: expression! Good day, one and all!"
7451: 
7452: "Have the goodness to give me a little glass of old cognac, and a
7453: mouthful of cool fresh water, madame."
7454: 
7455: Madame complied with a polite air.
7456: 
7457: "Marvellous cognac this, madame!"
7458: 
7459: It was the first time it had ever been so complimented, and Madame
7460: Defarge knew enough of its antecedents to know better. She said,
7461: however, that the cognac was flattered, and took up her knitting. The
7462: visitor watched her fingers for a few moments, and took the opportunity
7463: of observing the place in general.
7464: 
7465: "You knit with great skill, madame."
7466: 
7467: "I am accustomed to it."
7468: 
7469: "A pretty pattern too!"
7470: 
7471: "_You_ think so?" said madame, looking at him with a smile.
7472: 
7473: "Decidedly. May one ask what it is for?"
7474: 
7475: "Pastime," said madame, still looking at him with a smile while her
7476: fingers moved nimbly.
7477: 
7478: "Not for use?"
7479: 
7480: "That depends. I may find a use for it one day. If I do--Well," said
7481: madame, drawing a breath and nodding her head with a stern kind of
7482: coquetry, "I'll use it!"
7483: 
7484: It was remarkable; but, the taste of Saint Antoine seemed to be
7485: decidedly opposed to a rose on the head-dress of Madame Defarge. Two
7486: men had entered separately, and had been about to order drink, when,
7487: catching sight of that novelty, they faltered, made a pretence of
7488: looking about as if for some friend who was not there, and went away.
7489: Nor, of those who had been there when this visitor entered, was there
7490: one left. They had all dropped off. The spy had kept his eyes open,
7491: but had been able to detect no sign. They had lounged away in a
7492: poverty-stricken, purposeless, accidental manner, quite natural and
7493: unimpeachable.
7494: 
7495: "_John_," thought madame, checking off her work as her fingers knitted,
7496: and her eyes looked at the stranger. "Stay long enough, and I shall knit
7497: 'BARSAD' before you go."
7498: 
7499: "You have a husband, madame?"
7500: 
7501: "I have."
7502: 
7503: "Children?"
7504: 
7505: "No children."
7506: 
7507: "Business seems bad?"
7508: 
7509: "Business is very bad; the people are so poor."
7510: 
7511: "Ah, the unfortunate, miserable people! So oppressed, too--as you say."
7512: 
7513: "As _you_ say," madame retorted, correcting him, and deftly knitting an
7514: extra something into his name that boded him no good.
7515: 
7516: "Pardon me; certainly it was I who said so, but you naturally think so.
7517: Of course."
7518: 
7519: "_I_ think?" returned madame, in a high voice. "I and my husband have
7520: enough to do to keep this wine-shop open, without thinking. All we
7521: think, here, is how to live. That is the subject _we_ think of, and
7522: it gives us, from morning to night, enough to think about, without
7523: embarrassing our heads concerning others. _I_ think for others? No, no."
7524: 
7525: The spy, who was there to pick up any crumbs he could find or make, did
7526: not allow his baffled state to express itself in his sinister face; but,
7527: stood with an air of gossiping gallantry, leaning his elbow on Madame
7528: Defarge's little counter, and occasionally sipping his cognac.
7529: 
7530: "A bad business this, madame, of Gaspard's execution. Ah! the poor
7531: Gaspard!" With a sigh of great compassion.
7532: 
7533: "My faith!" returned madame, coolly and lightly, "if people use knives
7534: for such purposes, they have to pay for it. He knew beforehand what the
7535: price of his luxury was; he has paid the price."
7536: 
7537: "I believe," said the spy, dropping his soft voice to a tone
7538: that invited confidence, and expressing an injured revolutionary
7539: susceptibility in every muscle of his wicked face: "I believe there
7540: is much compassion and anger in this neighbourhood, touching the poor
7541: fellow? Between ourselves."
7542: 
7543: "Is there?" asked madame, vacantly.
7544: 
7545: "Is there not?"
7546: 
7547: "--Here is my husband!" said Madame Defarge.
7548: 
7549: As the keeper of the wine-shop entered at the door, the spy saluted
7550: him by touching his hat, and saying, with an engaging smile, "Good day,
7551: Jacques!" Defarge stopped short, and stared at him.
7552: 
7553: "Good day, Jacques!" the spy repeated; with not quite so much
7554: confidence, or quite so easy a smile under the stare.
7555: 
7556: "You deceive yourself, monsieur," returned the keeper of the wine-shop.
7557: "You mistake me for another. That is not my name. I am Ernest Defarge."
7558: 
7559: "It is all the same," said the spy, airily, but discomfited too: "good
7560: day!"
7561: 
7562: "Good day!" answered Defarge, drily.
7563: 
7564: "I was saying to madame, with whom I had the pleasure of chatting when
7565: you entered, that they tell me there is--and no wonder!--much sympathy
7566: and anger in Saint Antoine, touching the unhappy fate of poor Gaspard."
7567: 
7568: "No one has told me so," said Defarge, shaking his head. "I know nothing
7569: of it."
7570: 
7571: Having said it, he passed behind the little counter, and stood with his
7572: hand on the back of his wife's chair, looking over that barrier at the
7573: person to whom they were both opposed, and whom either of them would
7574: have shot with the greatest satisfaction.
7575: 
7576: The spy, well used to his business, did not change his unconscious
7577: attitude, but drained his little glass of cognac, took a sip of fresh
7578: water, and asked for another glass of cognac. Madame Defarge poured it
7579: out for him, took to her knitting again, and hummed a little song over
7580: it.
7581: 
7582: "You seem to know this quarter well; that is to say, better than I do?"
7583: observed Defarge.
7584: 
7585: "Not at all, but I hope to know it better. I am so profoundly interested
7586: in its miserable inhabitants."
7587: 
7588: "Hah!" muttered Defarge.
7589: 
7590: "The pleasure of conversing with you, Monsieur Defarge, recalls to me,"
7591: pursued the spy, "that I have the honour of cherishing some interesting
7592: associations with your name."
7593: 
7594: "Indeed!" said Defarge, with much indifference.
7595: 
7596: "Yes, indeed. When Doctor Manette was released, you, his old domestic,
7597: had the charge of him, I know. He was delivered to you. You see I am
7598: informed of the circumstances?"
7599: 
7600: "Such is the fact, certainly," said Defarge. He had had it conveyed
7601: to him, in an accidental touch of his wife's elbow as she knitted and
7602: warbled, that he would do best to answer, but always with brevity.
7603: 
7604: "It was to you," said the spy, "that his daughter came; and it was
7605: from your care that his daughter took him, accompanied by a neat brown
7606: monsieur; how is he called?--in a little wig--Lorry--of the bank of
7607: Tellson and Company--over to England."
7608: 
7609: "Such is the fact," repeated Defarge.
7610: 
7611: "Very interesting remembrances!" said the spy. "I have known Doctor
7612: Manette and his daughter, in England."
7613: 
7614: "Yes?" said Defarge.
7615: 
7616: "You don't hear much about them now?" said the spy.
7617: 
7618: "No," said Defarge.
7619: 
7620: "In effect," madame struck in, looking up from her work and her little
7621: song, "we never hear about them. We received the news of their safe
7622: arrival, and perhaps another letter, or perhaps two; but, since then,
7623: they have gradually taken their road in life--we, ours--and we have held
7624: no correspondence."
7625: 
7626: "Perfectly so, madame," replied the spy. "She is going to be married."
7627: 
7628: "Going?" echoed madame. "She was pretty enough to have been married long
7629: ago. You English are cold, it seems to me."
7630: 
7631: "Oh! You know I am English."
7632: 
7633: "I perceive your tongue is," returned madame; "and what the tongue is, I
7634: suppose the man is."
7635: 
7636: He did not take the identification as a compliment; but he made the best
7637: of it, and turned it off with a laugh. After sipping his cognac to the
7638: end, he added:
7639: 
7640: "Yes, Miss Manette is going to be married. But not to an Englishman; to
7641: one who, like herself, is French by birth. And speaking of Gaspard (ah,
7642: poor Gaspard! It was cruel, cruel!), it is a curious thing that she is
7643: going to marry the nephew of Monsieur the Marquis, for whom Gaspard
7644: was exalted to that height of so many feet; in other words, the present
7645: Marquis. But he lives unknown in England, he is no Marquis there; he is
7646: Mr. Charles Darnay. D'Aulnais is the name of his mother's family."
7647: 
7648: Madame Defarge knitted steadily, but the intelligence had a palpable
7649: effect upon her husband. Do what he would, behind the little counter,
7650: as to the striking of a light and the lighting of his pipe, he was
7651: troubled, and his hand was not trustworthy. The spy would have been no
7652: spy if he had failed to see it, or to record it in his mind.
7653: 
7654: Having made, at least, this one hit, whatever it might prove to be
7655: worth, and no customers coming in to help him to any other, Mr. Barsad
7656: paid for what he had drunk, and took his leave: taking occasion to say,
7657: in a genteel manner, before he departed, that he looked forward to the
7658: pleasure of seeing Monsieur and Madame Defarge again. For some minutes
7659: after he had emerged into the outer presence of Saint Antoine, the
7660: husband and wife remained exactly as he had left them, lest he should
7661: come back.
7662: 
7663: "Can it be true," said Defarge, in a low voice, looking down at his wife
7664: as he stood smoking with his hand on the back of her chair: "what he has
7665: said of Ma'amselle Manette?"
7666: 
7667: "As he has said it," returned madame, lifting her eyebrows a little, "it
7668: is probably false. But it may be true."
7669: 
7670: "If it is--" Defarge began, and stopped.
7671: 
7672: "If it is?" repeated his wife.
7673: 
7674: "--And if it does come, while we live to see it triumph--I hope, for her
7675: sake, Destiny will keep her husband out of France."
7676: 
7677: "Her husband's destiny," said Madame Defarge, with her usual composure,
7678: "will take him where he is to go, and will lead him to the end that is
7679: to end him. That is all I know."
7680: 
7681: "But it is very strange--now, at least, is it not very strange"--said
7682: Defarge, rather pleading with his wife to induce her to admit it,
7683: "that, after all our sympathy for Monsieur her father, and herself, her
7684: husband's name should be proscribed under your hand at this moment, by
7685: the side of that infernal dog's who has just left us?"
7686: 
7687: "Stranger things than that will happen when it does come," answered
7688: madame. "I have them both here, of a certainty; and they are both here
7689: for their merits; that is enough."
7690: 
7691: She rolled up her knitting when she had said those words, and presently
7692: took the rose out of the handkerchief that was wound about her head.
7693: Either Saint Antoine had an instinctive sense that the objectionable
7694: decoration was gone, or Saint Antoine was on the watch for its
7695: disappearance; howbeit, the Saint took courage to lounge in, very
7696: shortly afterwards, and the wine-shop recovered its habitual aspect.
7697: 
7698: In the evening, at which season of all others Saint Antoine turned
7699: himself inside out, and sat on door-steps and window-ledges, and came
7700: to the corners of vile streets and courts, for a breath of air, Madame
7701: Defarge with her work in her hand was accustomed to pass from place
7702: to place and from group to group: a Missionary--there were many like
7703: her--such as the world will do well never to breed again. All the women
7704: knitted. They knitted worthless things; but, the mechanical work was a
7705: mechanical substitute for eating and drinking; the hands moved for the
7706: jaws and the digestive apparatus: if the bony fingers had been still,
7707: the stomachs would have been more famine-pinched.
7708: 
7709: But, as the fingers went, the eyes went, and the thoughts. And as Madame
7710: Defarge moved on from group to group, all three went quicker and fiercer
7711: among every little knot of women that she had spoken with, and left
7712: behind.
7713: 
7714: Her husband smoked at his door, looking after her with admiration. "A
7715: great woman," said he, "a strong woman, a grand woman, a frightfully
7716: grand woman!"
7717: 
7718: Darkness closed around, and then came the ringing of church bells and
7719: the distant beating of the military drums in the Palace Courtyard, as
7720: the women sat knitting, knitting. Darkness encompassed them. Another
7721: darkness was closing in as surely, when the church bells, then ringing
7722: pleasantly in many an airy steeple over France, should be melted into
7723: thundering cannon; when the military drums should be beating to drown a
7724: wretched voice, that night all potent as the voice of Power and Plenty,
7725: Freedom and Life. So much was closing in about the women who sat
7726: knitting, knitting, that they their very selves were closing in around
7727: a structure yet unbuilt, where they were to sit knitting, knitting,
7728: counting dropping heads.
7729: 
7730: 
7731: 
7732: 
7733: XVII. One Night
7734: 
7735: 
7736: Never did the sun go down with a brighter glory on the quiet corner in
7737: Soho, than one memorable evening when the Doctor and his daughter sat
7738: under the plane-tree together. Never did the moon rise with a milder
7739: radiance over great London, than on that night when it found them still
7740: seated under the tree, and shone upon their faces through its leaves.
7741: 
7742: Lucie was to be married to-morrow. She had reserved this last evening
7743: for her father, and they sat alone under the plane-tree.
7744: 
7745: "You are happy, my dear father?"
7746: 
7747: "Quite, my child."
7748: 
7749: They had said little, though they had been there a long time. When it
7750: was yet light enough to work and read, she had neither engaged herself
7751: in her usual work, nor had she read to him. She had employed herself in
7752: both ways, at his side under the tree, many and many a time; but, this
7753: time was not quite like any other, and nothing could make it so.
7754: 
7755: "And I am very happy to-night, dear father. I am deeply happy in the
7756: love that Heaven has so blessed--my love for Charles, and Charles's love
7757: for me. But, if my life were not to be still consecrated to you, or
7758: if my marriage were so arranged as that it would part us, even by
7759: the length of a few of these streets, I should be more unhappy and
7760: self-reproachful now than I can tell you. Even as it is--"
7761: 
7762: Even as it was, she could not command her voice.
7763: 
7764: In the sad moonlight, she clasped him by the neck, and laid her face
7765: upon his breast. In the moonlight which is always sad, as the light of
7766: the sun itself is--as the light called human life is--at its coming and
7767: its going.
7768: 
7769: "Dearest dear! Can you tell me, this last time, that you feel quite,
7770: quite sure, no new affections of mine, and no new duties of mine, will
7771: ever interpose between us? _I_ know it well, but do you know it? In your
7772: own heart, do you feel quite certain?"
7773: 
7774: Her father answered, with a cheerful firmness of conviction he could
7775: scarcely have assumed, "Quite sure, my darling! More than that," he
7776: added, as he tenderly kissed her: "my future is far brighter, Lucie,
7777: seen through your marriage, than it could have been--nay, than it ever
7778: was--without it."
7779: 
7780: "If I could hope _that_, my father!--"
7781: 
7782: "Believe it, love! Indeed it is so. Consider how natural and how plain
7783: it is, my dear, that it should be so. You, devoted and young, cannot
7784: fully appreciate the anxiety I have felt that your life should not be
7785: wasted--"
7786: 
7787: She moved her hand towards his lips, but he took it in his, and repeated
7788: the word.
7789: 
7790: "--wasted, my child--should not be wasted, struck aside from the
7791: natural order of things--for my sake. Your unselfishness cannot entirely
7792: comprehend how much my mind has gone on this; but, only ask yourself,
7793: how could my happiness be perfect, while yours was incomplete?"
7794: 
7795: "If I had never seen Charles, my father, I should have been quite happy
7796: with you."
7797: 
7798: He smiled at her unconscious admission that she would have been unhappy
7799: without Charles, having seen him; and replied:
7800: 
7801: "My child, you did see him, and it is Charles. If it had not been
7802: Charles, it would have been another. Or, if it had been no other, I
7803: should have been the cause, and then the dark part of my life would have
7804: cast its shadow beyond myself, and would have fallen on you."
7805: 
7806: It was the first time, except at the trial, of her ever hearing him
7807: refer to the period of his suffering. It gave her a strange and new
7808: sensation while his words were in her ears; and she remembered it long
7809: afterwards.
7810: 
7811: "See!" said the Doctor of Beauvais, raising his hand towards the moon.
7812: "I have looked at her from my prison-window, when I could not bear her
7813: light. I have looked at her when it has been such torture to me to think
7814: of her shining upon what I had lost, that I have beaten my head against
7815: my prison-walls. I have looked at her, in a state so dull and lethargic,
7816: that I have thought of nothing but the number of horizontal lines I
7817: could draw across her at the full, and the number of perpendicular lines
7818: with which I could intersect them." He added in his inward and pondering
7819: manner, as he looked at the moon, "It was twenty either way, I remember,
7820: and the twentieth was difficult to squeeze in."
7821: 
7822: The strange thrill with which she heard him go back to that time,
7823: deepened as he dwelt upon it; but, there was nothing to shock her in
7824: the manner of his reference. He only seemed to contrast his present
7825: cheerfulness and felicity with the dire endurance that was over.
7826: 
7827: "I have looked at her, speculating thousands of times upon the unborn
7828: child from whom I had been rent. Whether it was alive. Whether it had
7829: been born alive, or the poor mother's shock had killed it. Whether it
7830: was a son who would some day avenge his father. (There was a time in my
7831: imprisonment, when my desire for vengeance was unbearable.) Whether it
7832: was a son who would never know his father's story; who might even live
7833: to weigh the possibility of his father's having disappeared of his own
7834: will and act. Whether it was a daughter who would grow to be a woman."
7835: 
7836: She drew closer to him, and kissed his cheek and his hand.
7837: 
7838: "I have pictured my daughter, to myself, as perfectly forgetful of
7839: me--rather, altogether ignorant of me, and unconscious of me. I have
7840: cast up the years of her age, year after year. I have seen her married
7841: to a man who knew nothing of my fate. I have altogether perished from
7842: the remembrance of the living, and in the next generation my place was a
7843: blank."
7844: 
7845: "My father! Even to hear that you had such thoughts of a daughter who
7846: never existed, strikes to my heart as if I had been that child."
7847: 
7848: "You, Lucie? It is out of the Consolation and restoration you have
7849: brought to me, that these remembrances arise, and pass between us and
7850: the moon on this last night.--What did I say just now?"
7851: 
7852: "She knew nothing of you. She cared nothing for you."
7853: 
7854: "So! But on other moonlight nights, when the sadness and the silence
7855: have touched me in a different way--have affected me with something as
7856: like a sorrowful sense of peace, as any emotion that had pain for its
7857: foundations could--I have imagined her as coming to me in my cell, and
7858: leading me out into the freedom beyond the fortress. I have seen her
7859: image in the moonlight often, as I now see you; except that I never held
7860: her in my arms; it stood between the little grated window and the door.
7861: But, you understand that that was not the child I am speaking of?"
7862: 
7863: "The figure was not; the--the--image; the fancy?"
7864: 
7865: "No. That was another thing. It stood before my disturbed sense of
7866: sight, but it never moved. The phantom that my mind pursued, was another
7867: and more real child. Of her outward appearance I know no more than
7868: that she was like her mother. The other had that likeness too--as you
7869: have--but was not the same. Can you follow me, Lucie? Hardly, I think?
7870: I doubt you must have been a solitary prisoner to understand these
7871: perplexed distinctions."
7872: 
7873: His collected and calm manner could not prevent her blood from running
7874: cold, as he thus tried to anatomise his old condition.
7875: 
7876: "In that more peaceful state, I have imagined her, in the moonlight,
7877: coming to me and taking me out to show me that the home of her married
7878: life was full of her loving remembrance of her lost father. My picture
7879: was in her room, and I was in her prayers. Her life was active,
7880: cheerful, useful; but my poor history pervaded it all."
7881: 
7882: "I was that child, my father, I was not half so good, but in my love
7883: that was I."
7884: 
7885: "And she showed me her children," said the Doctor of Beauvais, "and
7886: they had heard of me, and had been taught to pity me. When they passed
7887: a prison of the State, they kept far from its frowning walls, and looked
7888: up at its bars, and spoke in whispers. She could never deliver me; I
7889: imagined that she always brought me back after showing me such things.
7890: But then, blessed with the relief of tears, I fell upon my knees, and
7891: blessed her."
7892: 
7893: "I am that child, I hope, my father. O my dear, my dear, will you bless
7894: me as fervently to-morrow?"
7895: 
7896: "Lucie, I recall these old troubles in the reason that I have to-night
7897: for loving you better than words can tell, and thanking God for my great
7898: happiness. My thoughts, when they were wildest, never rose near the
7899: happiness that I have known with you, and that we have before us."
7900: 
7901: He embraced her, solemnly commended her to Heaven, and humbly thanked
7902: Heaven for having bestowed her on him. By-and-bye, they went into the
7903: house.
7904: 
7905: There was no one bidden to the marriage but Mr. Lorry; there was even to
7906: be no bridesmaid but the gaunt Miss Pross. The marriage was to make no
7907: change in their place of residence; they had been able to extend it,
7908: by taking to themselves the upper rooms formerly belonging to the
7909: apocryphal invisible lodger, and they desired nothing more.
7910: 
7911: Doctor Manette was very cheerful at the little supper. They were only
7912: three at table, and Miss Pross made the third. He regretted that Charles
7913: was not there; was more than half disposed to object to the loving
7914: little plot that kept him away; and drank to him affectionately.
7915: 
7916: So, the time came for him to bid Lucie good night, and they separated.
7917: But, in the stillness of the third hour of the morning, Lucie came
7918: downstairs again, and stole into his room; not free from unshaped fears,
7919: beforehand.
7920: 
7921: All things, however, were in their places; all was quiet; and he lay
7922: asleep, his white hair picturesque on the untroubled pillow, and his
7923: hands lying quiet on the coverlet. She put her needless candle in the
7924: shadow at a distance, crept up to his bed, and put her lips to his;
7925: then, leaned over him, and looked at him.
7926: 
7927: Into his handsome face, the bitter waters of captivity had worn; but, he
7928: covered up their tracks with a determination so strong, that he held the
7929: mastery of them even in his sleep. A more remarkable face in its quiet,
7930: resolute, and guarded struggle with an unseen assailant, was not to be
7931: beheld in all the wide dominions of sleep, that night.
7932: 
7933: She timidly laid her hand on his dear breast, and put up a prayer that
7934: she might ever be as true to him as her love aspired to be, and as his
7935: sorrows deserved. Then, she withdrew her hand, and kissed his lips once
7936: more, and went away. So, the sunrise came, and the shadows of the leaves
7937: of the plane-tree moved upon his face, as softly as her lips had moved
7938: in praying for him.
7939: 
7940: 
7941: 
7942: 
7943: XVIII. Nine Days
7944: 
7945: 
7946: The marriage-day was shining brightly, and they were ready outside the
7947: closed door of the Doctor's room, where he was speaking with Charles
7948: Darnay. They were ready to go to church; the beautiful bride, Mr.
7949: Lorry, and Miss Pross--to whom the event, through a gradual process of
7950: reconcilement to the inevitable, would have been one of absolute bliss,
7951: but for the yet lingering consideration that her brother Solomon should
7952: have been the bridegroom.
7953: 
7954: "And so," said Mr. Lorry, who could not sufficiently admire the bride,
7955: and who had been moving round her to take in every point of her quiet,
7956: pretty dress; "and so it was for this, my sweet Lucie, that I brought
7957: you across the Channel, such a baby! Lord bless me! How little I thought
7958: what I was doing! How lightly I valued the obligation I was conferring
7959: on my friend Mr. Charles!"
7960: 
7961: "You didn't mean it," remarked the matter-of-fact Miss Pross, "and
7962: therefore how could you know it? Nonsense!"
7963: 
7964: "Really? Well; but don't cry," said the gentle Mr. Lorry.
7965: 
7966: "I am not crying," said Miss Pross; "_you_ are."
7967: 
7968: "I, my Pross?" (By this time, Mr. Lorry dared to be pleasant with her,
7969: on occasion.)
7970: 
7971: "You were, just now; I saw you do it, and I don't wonder at it. Such
7972: a present of plate as you have made 'em, is enough to bring tears into
7973: anybody's eyes. There's not a fork or a spoon in the collection," said
7974: Miss Pross, "that I didn't cry over, last night after the box came, till
7975: I couldn't see it."
7976: 
7977: "I am highly gratified," said Mr. Lorry, "though, upon my honour, I
7978: had no intention of rendering those trifling articles of remembrance
7979: invisible to any one. Dear me! This is an occasion that makes a man
7980: speculate on all he has lost. Dear, dear, dear! To think that there
7981: might have been a Mrs. Lorry, any time these fifty years almost!"
7982: 
7983: "Not at all!" From Miss Pross.
7984: 
7985: "You think there never might have been a Mrs. Lorry?" asked the
7986: gentleman of that name.
7987: 
7988: "Pooh!" rejoined Miss Pross; "you were a bachelor in your cradle."
7989: 
7990: "Well!" observed Mr. Lorry, beamingly adjusting his little wig, "that
7991: seems probable, too."
7992: 
7993: "And you were cut out for a bachelor," pursued Miss Pross, "before you
7994: were put in your cradle."
7995: 
7996: "Then, I think," said Mr. Lorry, "that I was very unhandsomely dealt
7997: with, and that I ought to have had a voice in the selection of my
7998: pattern. Enough! Now, my dear Lucie," drawing his arm soothingly round
7999: her waist, "I hear them moving in the next room, and Miss Pross and
8000: I, as two formal folks of business, are anxious not to lose the final
8001: opportunity of saying something to you that you wish to hear. You leave
8002: your good father, my dear, in hands as earnest and as loving as your
8003: own; he shall be taken every conceivable care of; during the next
8004: fortnight, while you are in Warwickshire and thereabouts, even Tellson's
8005: shall go to the wall (comparatively speaking) before him. And when, at
8006: the fortnight's end, he comes to join you and your beloved husband, on
8007: your other fortnight's trip in Wales, you shall say that we have sent
8008: him to you in the best health and in the happiest frame. Now, I hear
8009: Somebody's step coming to the door. Let me kiss my dear girl with an
8010: old-fashioned bachelor blessing, before Somebody comes to claim his
8011: own."
8012: 
8013: For a moment, he held the fair face from him to look at the
8014: well-remembered expression on the forehead, and then laid the bright
8015: golden hair against his little brown wig, with a genuine tenderness and
8016: delicacy which, if such things be old-fashioned, were as old as Adam.
8017: 
8018: The door of the Doctor's room opened, and he came out with Charles
8019: Darnay. He was so deadly pale--which had not been the case when they
8020: went in together--that no vestige of colour was to be seen in his face.
8021: But, in the composure of his manner he was unaltered, except that to the
8022: shrewd glance of Mr. Lorry it disclosed some shadowy indication that the
8023: old air of avoidance and dread had lately passed over him, like a cold
8024: wind.
8025: 
8026: He gave his arm to his daughter, and took her down-stairs to the chariot
8027: which Mr. Lorry had hired in honour of the day. The rest followed in
8028: another carriage, and soon, in a neighbouring church, where no strange
8029: eyes looked on, Charles Darnay and Lucie Manette were happily married.
8030: 
8031: Besides the glancing tears that shone among the smiles of the little
8032: group when it was done, some diamonds, very bright and sparkling,
8033: glanced on the bride's hand, which were newly released from the
8034: dark obscurity of one of Mr. Lorry's pockets. They returned home to
8035: breakfast, and all went well, and in due course the golden hair that had
8036: mingled with the poor shoemaker's white locks in the Paris garret, were
8037: mingled with them again in the morning sunlight, on the threshold of the
8038: door at parting.
8039: 
8040: It was a hard parting, though it was not for long. But her father
8041: cheered her, and said at last, gently disengaging himself from her
8042: enfolding arms, "Take her, Charles! She is yours!"
8043: 
8044: And her agitated hand waved to them from a chaise window, and she was
8045: gone.
8046: 
8047: The corner being out of the way of the idle and curious, and the
8048: preparations having been very simple and few, the Doctor, Mr. Lorry,
8049: and Miss Pross, were left quite alone. It was when they turned into
8050: the welcome shade of the cool old hall, that Mr. Lorry observed a great
8051: change to have come over the Doctor; as if the golden arm uplifted
8052: there, had struck him a poisoned blow.
8053: 
8054: He had naturally repressed much, and some revulsion might have been
8055: expected in him when the occasion for repression was gone. But, it was
8056: the old scared lost look that troubled Mr. Lorry; and through his absent
8057: manner of clasping his head and drearily wandering away into his own
8058: room when they got up-stairs, Mr. Lorry was reminded of Defarge the
8059: wine-shop keeper, and the starlight ride.
8060: 
8061: "I think," he whispered to Miss Pross, after anxious consideration, "I
8062: think we had best not speak to him just now, or at all disturb him.
8063: I must look in at Tellson's; so I will go there at once and come back
8064: presently. Then, we will take him a ride into the country, and dine
8065: there, and all will be well."
8066: 
8067: It was easier for Mr. Lorry to look in at Tellson's, than to look out of
8068: Tellson's. He was detained two hours. When he came back, he ascended the
8069: old staircase alone, having asked no question of the servant; going thus
8070: into the Doctor's rooms, he was stopped by a low sound of knocking.
8071: 
8072: "Good God!" he said, with a start. "What's that?"
8073: 
8074: Miss Pross, with a terrified face, was at his ear. "O me, O me! All is
8075: lost!" cried she, wringing her hands. "What is to be told to Ladybird?
8076: He doesn't know me, and is making shoes!"
8077: 
8078: Mr. Lorry said what he could to calm her, and went himself into the
8079: Doctor's room. The bench was turned towards the light, as it had been
8080: when he had seen the shoemaker at his work before, and his head was bent
8081: down, and he was very busy.
8082: 
8083: "Doctor Manette. My dear friend, Doctor Manette!"
8084: 
8085: The Doctor looked at him for a moment--half inquiringly, half as if he
8086: were angry at being spoken to--and bent over his work again.
8087: 
8088: He had laid aside his coat and waistcoat; his shirt was open at the
8089: throat, as it used to be when he did that work; and even the old
8090: haggard, faded surface of face had come back to him. He worked
8091: hard--impatiently--as if in some sense of having been interrupted.
8092: 
8093: Mr. Lorry glanced at the work in his hand, and observed that it was a
8094: shoe of the old size and shape. He took up another that was lying by
8095: him, and asked what it was.
8096: 
8097: "A young lady's walking shoe," he muttered, without looking up. "It
8098: ought to have been finished long ago. Let it be."
8099: 
8100: "But, Doctor Manette. Look at me!"
8101: 
8102: He obeyed, in the old mechanically submissive manner, without pausing in
8103: his work.
8104: 
8105: "You know me, my dear friend? Think again. This is not your proper
8106: occupation. Think, dear friend!"
8107: 
8108: Nothing would induce him to speak more. He looked up, for an instant at
8109: a time, when he was requested to do so; but, no persuasion would extract
8110: a word from him. He worked, and worked, and worked, in silence, and
8111: words fell on him as they would have fallen on an echoless wall, or on
8112: the air. The only ray of hope that Mr. Lorry could discover, was, that
8113: he sometimes furtively looked up without being asked. In that, there
8114: seemed a faint expression of curiosity or perplexity--as though he were
8115: trying to reconcile some doubts in his mind.
8116: 
8117: Two things at once impressed themselves on Mr. Lorry, as important above
8118: all others; the first, that this must be kept secret from Lucie;
8119: the second, that it must be kept secret from all who knew him. In
8120: conjunction with Miss Pross, he took immediate steps towards the latter
8121: precaution, by giving out that the Doctor was not well, and required a
8122: few days of complete rest. In aid of the kind deception to be practised
8123: on his daughter, Miss Pross was to write, describing his having been
8124: called away professionally, and referring to an imaginary letter of
8125: two or three hurried lines in his own hand, represented to have been
8126: addressed to her by the same post.
8127: 
8128: These measures, advisable to be taken in any case, Mr. Lorry took in
8129: the hope of his coming to himself. If that should happen soon, he kept
8130: another course in reserve; which was, to have a certain opinion that he
8131: thought the best, on the Doctor's case.
8132: 
8133: In the hope of his recovery, and of resort to this third course
8134: being thereby rendered practicable, Mr. Lorry resolved to watch him
8135: attentively, with as little appearance as possible of doing so. He
8136: therefore made arrangements to absent himself from Tellson's for the
8137: first time in his life, and took his post by the window in the same
8138: room.
8139: 
8140: He was not long in discovering that it was worse than useless to speak
8141: to him, since, on being pressed, he became worried. He abandoned that
8142: attempt on the first day, and resolved merely to keep himself always
8143: before him, as a silent protest against the delusion into which he had
8144: fallen, or was falling. He remained, therefore, in his seat near the
8145: window, reading and writing, and expressing in as many pleasant and
8146: natural ways as he could think of, that it was a free place.
8147: 
8148: Doctor Manette took what was given him to eat and drink, and worked on,
8149: that first day, until it was too dark to see--worked on, half an hour
8150: after Mr. Lorry could not have seen, for his life, to read or write.
8151: When he put his tools aside as useless, until morning, Mr. Lorry rose
8152: and said to him:
8153: 
8154: "Will you go out?"
8155: 
8156: He looked down at the floor on either side of him in the old manner,
8157: looked up in the old manner, and repeated in the old low voice:
8158: 
8159: "Out?"
8160: 
8161: "Yes; for a walk with me. Why not?"
8162: 
8163: He made no effort to say why not, and said not a word more. But, Mr.
8164: Lorry thought he saw, as he leaned forward on his bench in the dusk,
8165: with his elbows on his knees and his head in his hands, that he was in
8166: some misty way asking himself, "Why not?" The sagacity of the man of
8167: business perceived an advantage here, and determined to hold it.
8168: 
8169: Miss Pross and he divided the night into two watches, and observed him
8170: at intervals from the adjoining room. He paced up and down for a long
8171: time before he lay down; but, when he did finally lay himself down, he
8172: fell asleep. In the morning, he was up betimes, and went straight to his
8173: bench and to work.
8174: 
8175: On this second day, Mr. Lorry saluted him cheerfully by his name,
8176: and spoke to him on topics that had been of late familiar to them. He
8177: returned no reply, but it was evident that he heard what was said, and
8178: that he thought about it, however confusedly. This encouraged Mr. Lorry
8179: to have Miss Pross in with her work, several times during the day;
8180: at those times, they quietly spoke of Lucie, and of her father then
8181: present, precisely in the usual manner, and as if there were nothing
8182: amiss. This was done without any demonstrative accompaniment, not long
8183: enough, or often enough to harass him; and it lightened Mr. Lorry's
8184: friendly heart to believe that he looked up oftener, and that he
8185: appeared to be stirred by some perception of inconsistencies surrounding
8186: him.
8187: 
8188: When it fell dark again, Mr. Lorry asked him as before:
8189: 
8190: "Dear Doctor, will you go out?"
8191: 
8192: As before, he repeated, "Out?"
8193: 
8194: "Yes; for a walk with me. Why not?"
8195: 
8196: This time, Mr. Lorry feigned to go out when he could extract no answer
8197: from him, and, after remaining absent for an hour, returned. In the
8198: meanwhile, the Doctor had removed to the seat in the window, and had
8199: sat there looking down at the plane-tree; but, on Mr. Lorry's return, he
8200: slipped away to his bench.
8201: 
8202: The time went very slowly on, and Mr. Lorry's hope darkened, and his
8203: heart grew heavier again, and grew yet heavier and heavier every day.
8204: The third day came and went, the fourth, the fifth. Five days, six days,
8205: seven days, eight days, nine days.
8206: 
8207: With a hope ever darkening, and with a heart always growing heavier and
8208: heavier, Mr. Lorry passed through this anxious time. The secret was
8209: well kept, and Lucie was unconscious and happy; but he could not fail to
8210: observe that the shoemaker, whose hand had been a little out at first,
8211: was growing dreadfully skilful, and that he had never been so intent on
8212: his work, and that his hands had never been so nimble and expert, as in
8213: the dusk of the ninth evening.
8214: 
8215: 
8216: 
8217: 
8218: XIX. An Opinion
8219: 
8220: 
8221: Worn out by anxious watching, Mr. Lorry fell asleep at his post. On the
8222: tenth morning of his suspense, he was startled by the shining of the sun
8223: into the room where a heavy slumber had overtaken him when it was dark
8224: night.
8225: 
8226: He rubbed his eyes and roused himself; but he doubted, when he had
8227: done so, whether he was not still asleep. For, going to the door of the
8228: Doctor's room and looking in, he perceived that the shoemaker's bench
8229: and tools were put aside again, and that the Doctor himself sat reading
8230: at the window. He was in his usual morning dress, and his face (which
8231: Mr. Lorry could distinctly see), though still very pale, was calmly
8232: studious and attentive.
8233: 
8234: Even when he had satisfied himself that he was awake, Mr. Lorry felt
8235: giddily uncertain for some few moments whether the late shoemaking might
8236: not be a disturbed dream of his own; for, did not his eyes show him his
8237: friend before him in his accustomed clothing and aspect, and employed
8238: as usual; and was there any sign within their range, that the change of
8239: which he had so strong an impression had actually happened?
8240: 
8241: It was but the inquiry of his first confusion and astonishment, the
8242: answer being obvious. If the impression were not produced by a real
8243: corresponding and sufficient cause, how came he, Jarvis Lorry, there?
8244: How came he to have fallen asleep, in his clothes, on the sofa in Doctor
8245: Manette's consulting-room, and to be debating these points outside the
8246: Doctor's bedroom door in the early morning?
8247: 
8248: Within a few minutes, Miss Pross stood whispering at his side. If he
8249: had had any particle of doubt left, her talk would of necessity have
8250: resolved it; but he was by that time clear-headed, and had none.
8251: He advised that they should let the time go by until the regular
8252: breakfast-hour, and should then meet the Doctor as if nothing unusual
8253: had occurred. If he appeared to be in his customary state of mind, Mr.
8254: Lorry would then cautiously proceed to seek direction and guidance from
8255: the opinion he had been, in his anxiety, so anxious to obtain.
8256: 
8257: Miss Pross, submitting herself to his judgment, the scheme was worked
8258: out with care. Having abundance of time for his usual methodical
8259: toilette, Mr. Lorry presented himself at the breakfast-hour in his usual
8260: white linen, and with his usual neat leg. The Doctor was summoned in the
8261: usual way, and came to breakfast.
8262: 
8263: So far as it was possible to comprehend him without overstepping those
8264: delicate and gradual approaches which Mr. Lorry felt to be the only safe
8265: advance, he at first supposed that his daughter's marriage had taken
8266: place yesterday. An incidental allusion, purposely thrown out, to
8267: the day of the week, and the day of the month, set him thinking and
8268: counting, and evidently made him uneasy. In all other respects, however,
8269: he was so composedly himself, that Mr. Lorry determined to have the aid
8270: he sought. And that aid was his own.
8271: 
8272: Therefore, when the breakfast was done and cleared away, and he and the
8273: Doctor were left together, Mr. Lorry said, feelingly:
8274: 
8275: "My dear Manette, I am anxious to have your opinion, in confidence, on a
8276: very curious case in which I am deeply interested; that is to say, it is
8277: very curious to me; perhaps, to your better information it may be less
8278: so."
8279: 
8280: Glancing at his hands, which were discoloured by his late work, the
8281: Doctor looked troubled, and listened attentively. He had already glanced
8282: at his hands more than once.
8283: 
8284: "Doctor Manette," said Mr. Lorry, touching him affectionately on the
8285: arm, "the case is the case of a particularly dear friend of mine. Pray
8286: give your mind to it, and advise me well for his sake--and above all,
8287: for his daughter's--his daughter's, my dear Manette."
8288: 
8289: "If I understand," said the Doctor, in a subdued tone, "some mental
8290: shock--?"
8291: 
8292: "Yes!"
8293: 
8294: "Be explicit," said the Doctor. "Spare no detail."
8295: 
8296: Mr. Lorry saw that they understood one another, and proceeded.
8297: 
8298: "My dear Manette, it is the case of an old and a prolonged shock,
8299: of great acuteness and severity to the affections, the feelings,
8300: the--the--as you express it--the mind. The mind. It is the case of a
8301: shock under which the sufferer was borne down, one cannot say for how
8302: long, because I believe he cannot calculate the time himself, and there
8303: are no other means of getting at it. It is the case of a shock from
8304: which the sufferer recovered, by a process that he cannot trace
8305: himself--as I once heard him publicly relate in a striking manner. It is
8306: the case of a shock from which he has recovered, so completely, as to
8307: be a highly intelligent man, capable of close application of mind, and
8308: great exertion of body, and of constantly making fresh additions to his
8309: stock of knowledge, which was already very large. But, unfortunately,
8310: there has been," he paused and took a deep breath--"a slight relapse."
8311: 
8312: The Doctor, in a low voice, asked, "Of how long duration?"
8313: 
8314: "Nine days and nights."
8315: 
8316: "How did it show itself? I infer," glancing at his hands again, "in the
8317: resumption of some old pursuit connected with the shock?"
8318: 
8319: "That is the fact."
8320: 
8321: "Now, did you ever see him," asked the Doctor, distinctly and
8322: collectedly, though in the same low voice, "engaged in that pursuit
8323: originally?"
8324: 
8325: "Once."
8326: 
8327: "And when the relapse fell on him, was he in most respects--or in all
8328: respects--as he was then?"
8329: 
8330: "I think in all respects."
8331: 
8332: "You spoke of his daughter. Does his daughter know of the relapse?"
8333: 
8334: "No. It has been kept from her, and I hope will always be kept from her.
8335: It is known only to myself, and to one other who may be trusted."
8336: 
8337: The Doctor grasped his hand, and murmured, "That was very kind. That was
8338: very thoughtful!" Mr. Lorry grasped his hand in return, and neither of
8339: the two spoke for a little while.
8340: 
8341: "Now, my dear Manette," said Mr. Lorry, at length, in his most
8342: considerate and most affectionate way, "I am a mere man of business,
8343: and unfit to cope with such intricate and difficult matters. I do not
8344: possess the kind of information necessary; I do not possess the kind of
8345: intelligence; I want guiding. There is no man in this world on whom
8346: I could so rely for right guidance, as on you. Tell me, how does this
8347: relapse come about? Is there danger of another? Could a repetition of it
8348: be prevented? How should a repetition of it be treated? How does it come
8349: about at all? What can I do for my friend? No man ever can have been
8350: more desirous in his heart to serve a friend, than I am to serve mine,
8351: if I knew how.
8352: 
8353: "But I don't know how to originate, in such a case. If your sagacity,
8354: knowledge, and experience, could put me on the right track, I might be
8355: able to do so much; unenlightened and undirected, I can do so little.
8356: Pray discuss it with me; pray enable me to see it a little more clearly,
8357: and teach me how to be a little more useful."
8358: 
8359: Doctor Manette sat meditating after these earnest words were spoken, and
8360: Mr. Lorry did not press him.
8361: 
8362: "I think it probable," said the Doctor, breaking silence with an effort,
8363: "that the relapse you have described, my dear friend, was not quite
8364: unforeseen by its subject."
8365: 
8366: "Was it dreaded by him?" Mr. Lorry ventured to ask.
8367: 
8368: "Very much." He said it with an involuntary shudder.
8369: 
8370: "You have no idea how such an apprehension weighs on the sufferer's
8371: mind, and how difficult--how almost impossible--it is, for him to force
8372: himself to utter a word upon the topic that oppresses him."
8373: 
8374: "Would he," asked Mr. Lorry, "be sensibly relieved if he could prevail
8375: upon himself to impart that secret brooding to any one, when it is on
8376: him?"
8377: 
8378: "I think so. But it is, as I have told you, next to impossible. I even
8379: believe it--in some cases--to be quite impossible."
8380: 
8381: "Now," said Mr. Lorry, gently laying his hand on the Doctor's arm again,
8382: after a short silence on both sides, "to what would you refer this
8383: attack?"
8384: 
8385: "I believe," returned Doctor Manette, "that there had been a strong and
8386: extraordinary revival of the train of thought and remembrance that
8387: was the first cause of the malady. Some intense associations of a most
8388: distressing nature were vividly recalled, I think. It is probable that
8389: there had long been a dread lurking in his mind, that those associations
8390: would be recalled--say, under certain circumstances--say, on a
8391: particular occasion. He tried to prepare himself in vain; perhaps the
8392: effort to prepare himself made him less able to bear it."
8393: 
8394: "Would he remember what took place in the relapse?" asked Mr. Lorry,
8395: with natural hesitation.
8396: 
8397: The Doctor looked desolately round the room, shook his head, and
8398: answered, in a low voice, "Not at all."
8399: 
8400: "Now, as to the future," hinted Mr. Lorry.
8401: 
8402: "As to the future," said the Doctor, recovering firmness, "I should have
8403: great hope. As it pleased Heaven in its mercy to restore him so soon, I
8404: should have great hope. He, yielding under the pressure of a complicated
8405: something, long dreaded and long vaguely foreseen and contended against,
8406: and recovering after the cloud had burst and passed, I should hope that
8407: the worst was over."
8408: 
8409: "Well, well! That's good comfort. I am thankful!" said Mr. Lorry.
8410: 
8411: "I am thankful!" repeated the Doctor, bending his head with reverence.
8412: 
8413: "There are two other points," said Mr. Lorry, "on which I am anxious to
8414: be instructed. I may go on?"
8415: 
8416: "You cannot do your friend a better service." The Doctor gave him his
8417: hand.
8418: 
8419: "To the first, then. He is of a studious habit, and unusually energetic;
8420: he applies himself with great ardour to the acquisition of professional
8421: knowledge, to the conducting of experiments, to many things. Now, does
8422: he do too much?"
8423: 
8424: "I think not. It may be the character of his mind, to be always in
8425: singular need of occupation. That may be, in part, natural to it; in
8426: part, the result of affliction. The less it was occupied with healthy
8427: things, the more it would be in danger of turning in the unhealthy
8428: direction. He may have observed himself, and made the discovery."
8429: 
8430: "You are sure that he is not under too great a strain?"
8431: 
8432: "I think I am quite sure of it."
8433: 
8434: "My dear Manette, if he were overworked now--"
8435: 
8436: "My dear Lorry, I doubt if that could easily be. There has been a
8437: violent stress in one direction, and it needs a counterweight."
8438: 
8439: "Excuse me, as a persistent man of business. Assuming for a moment,
8440: that he _was_ overworked; it would show itself in some renewal of this
8441: disorder?"
8442: 
8443: "I do not think so. I do not think," said Doctor Manette with the
8444: firmness of self-conviction, "that anything but the one train of
8445: association would renew it. I think that, henceforth, nothing but some
8446: extraordinary jarring of that chord could renew it. After what has
8447: happened, and after his recovery, I find it difficult to imagine any
8448: such violent sounding of that string again. I trust, and I almost
8449: believe, that the circumstances likely to renew it are exhausted."
8450: 
8451: He spoke with the diffidence of a man who knew how slight a thing
8452: would overset the delicate organisation of the mind, and yet with the
8453: confidence of a man who had slowly won his assurance out of personal
8454: endurance and distress. It was not for his friend to abate that
8455: confidence. He professed himself more relieved and encouraged than he
8456: really was, and approached his second and last point. He felt it to
8457: be the most difficult of all; but, remembering his old Sunday morning
8458: conversation with Miss Pross, and remembering what he had seen in the
8459: last nine days, he knew that he must face it.
8460: 
8461: "The occupation resumed under the influence of this passing affliction
8462: so happily recovered from," said Mr. Lorry, clearing his throat, "we
8463: will call--Blacksmith's work, Blacksmith's work. We will say, to put a
8464: case and for the sake of illustration, that he had been used, in his bad
8465: time, to work at a little forge. We will say that he was unexpectedly
8466: found at his forge again. Is it not a pity that he should keep it by
8467: him?"
8468: 
8469: The Doctor shaded his forehead with his hand, and beat his foot
8470: nervously on the ground.
8471: 
8472: "He has always kept it by him," said Mr. Lorry, with an anxious look at
8473: his friend. "Now, would it not be better that he should let it go?"
8474: 
8475: Still, the Doctor, with shaded forehead, beat his foot nervously on the
8476: ground.
8477: 
8478: "You do not find it easy to advise me?" said Mr. Lorry. "I quite
8479: understand it to be a nice question. And yet I think--" And there he
8480: shook his head, and stopped.
8481: 
8482: "You see," said Doctor Manette, turning to him after an uneasy pause,
8483: "it is very hard to explain, consistently, the innermost workings
8484: of this poor man's mind. He once yearned so frightfully for that
8485: occupation, and it was so welcome when it came; no doubt it relieved
8486: his pain so much, by substituting the perplexity of the fingers for
8487: the perplexity of the brain, and by substituting, as he became more
8488: practised, the ingenuity of the hands, for the ingenuity of the mental
8489: torture; that he has never been able to bear the thought of putting it
8490: quite out of his reach. Even now, when I believe he is more hopeful of
8491: himself than he has ever been, and even speaks of himself with a kind
8492: of confidence, the idea that he might need that old employment, and not
8493: find it, gives him a sudden sense of terror, like that which one may
8494: fancy strikes to the heart of a lost child."
8495: 
8496: He looked like his illustration, as he raised his eyes to Mr. Lorry's
8497: face.
8498: 
8499: "But may not--mind! I ask for information, as a plodding man of business
8500: who only deals with such material objects as guineas, shillings, and
8501: bank-notes--may not the retention of the thing involve the retention of
8502: the idea? If the thing were gone, my dear Manette, might not the fear go
8503: with it? In short, is it not a concession to the misgiving, to keep the
8504: forge?"
8505: 
8506: There was another silence.
8507: 
8508: "You see, too," said the Doctor, tremulously, "it is such an old
8509: companion."
8510: 
8511: "I would not keep it," said Mr. Lorry, shaking his head; for he gained
8512: in firmness as he saw the Doctor disquieted. "I would recommend him to
8513: sacrifice it. I only want your authority. I am sure it does no good.
8514: Come! Give me your authority, like a dear good man. For his daughter's
8515: sake, my dear Manette!"
8516: 
8517: Very strange to see what a struggle there was within him!
8518: 
8519: "In her name, then, let it be done; I sanction it. But, I would not take
8520: it away while he was present. Let it be removed when he is not there;
8521: let him miss his old companion after an absence."
8522: 
8523: Mr. Lorry readily engaged for that, and the conference was ended. They
8524: passed the day in the country, and the Doctor was quite restored. On the
8525: three following days he remained perfectly well, and on the fourteenth
8526: day he went away to join Lucie and her husband. The precaution that
8527: had been taken to account for his silence, Mr. Lorry had previously
8528: explained to him, and he had written to Lucie in accordance with it, and
8529: she had no suspicions.
8530: 
8531: On the night of the day on which he left the house, Mr. Lorry went into
8532: his room with a chopper, saw, chisel, and hammer, attended by Miss Pross
8533: carrying a light. There, with closed doors, and in a mysterious and
8534: guilty manner, Mr. Lorry hacked the shoemaker's bench to pieces, while
8535: Miss Pross held the candle as if she were assisting at a murder--for
8536: which, indeed, in her grimness, she was no unsuitable figure. The
8537: burning of the body (previously reduced to pieces convenient for the
8538: purpose) was commenced without delay in the kitchen fire; and the tools,
8539: shoes, and leather, were buried in the garden. So wicked do destruction
8540: and secrecy appear to honest minds, that Mr. Lorry and Miss Pross,
8541: while engaged in the commission of their deed and in the removal of its
8542: traces, almost felt, and almost looked, like accomplices in a horrible
8543: crime.
8544: 
8545: 
8546: 
8547: 
8548: XX. A Plea
8549: 
8550: 
8551: When the newly-married pair came home, the first person who appeared, to
8553: many hours, when he presented himself. He was not improved in habits, or
8554: in looks, or in manner; but there was a certain rugged air of fidelity
8555: about him, which was new to the observation of Charles Darnay.
8556: 
8557: He watched his opportunity of taking Darnay aside into a window, and of
8558: speaking to him when no one overheard.
8559: 
8560: "Mr. Darnay," said Carton, "I wish we might be friends."
8561: 
8562: "We are already friends, I hope."
8563: 
8564: "You are good enough to say so, as a fashion of speech; but, I don't
8565: mean any fashion of speech. Indeed, when I say I wish we might be
8566: friends, I scarcely mean quite that, either."
8567: 
8568: Charles Darnay--as was natural--asked him, in all good-humour and
8569: good-fellowship, what he did mean?
8570: 
8571: "Upon my life," said Carton, smiling, "I find that easier to comprehend
8572: in my own mind, than to convey to yours. However, let me try. You
8573: remember a certain famous occasion when I was more drunk than--than
8574: usual?"
8575: 
8576: "I remember a certain famous occasion when you forced me to confess that
8577: you had been drinking."
8578: 
8579: "I remember it too. The curse of those occasions is heavy upon me, for I
8580: always remember them. I hope it may be taken into account one day,
8581: when all days are at an end for me! Don't be alarmed; I am not going to
8582: preach."
8583: 
8584: "I am not at all alarmed. Earnestness in you, is anything but alarming
8585: to me."
8586: 
8587: "Ah!" said Carton, with a careless wave of his hand, as if he waved that
8588: away. "On the drunken occasion in question (one of a large number, as
8589: you know), I was insufferable about liking you, and not liking you. I
8590: wish you would forget it."
8591: 
8592: "I forgot it long ago."
8593: 
8594: "Fashion of speech again! But, Mr. Darnay, oblivion is not so easy to
8595: me, as you represent it to be to you. I have by no means forgotten it,
8596: and a light answer does not help me to forget it."
8597: 
8598: "If it was a light answer," returned Darnay, "I beg your forgiveness
8599: for it. I had no other object than to turn a slight thing, which, to my
8600: surprise, seems to trouble you too much, aside. I declare to you, on the
8601: faith of a gentleman, that I have long dismissed it from my mind. Good
8602: Heaven, what was there to dismiss! Have I had nothing more important to
8603: remember, in the great service you rendered me that day?"
8604: 
8605: "As to the great service," said Carton, "I am bound to avow to you, when
8606: you speak of it in that way, that it was mere professional claptrap, I
8607: don't know that I cared what became of you, when I rendered it.--Mind! I
8608: say when I rendered it; I am speaking of the past."
8609: 
8610: "You make light of the obligation," returned Darnay, "but I will not
8611: quarrel with _your_ light answer."
8612: 
8613: "Genuine truth, Mr. Darnay, trust me! I have gone aside from my purpose;
8614: I was speaking about our being friends. Now, you know me; you know I am
8615: incapable of all the higher and better flights of men. If you doubt it,
8616: ask Stryver, and he'll tell you so."
8617: 
8618: "I prefer to form my own opinion, without the aid of his."
8619: 
8620: "Well! At any rate you know me as a dissolute dog, who has never done
8621: any good, and never will."
8622: 
8623: "I don't know that you 'never will.'"
8624: 
8625: "But I do, and you must take my word for it. Well! If you could endure
8626: to have such a worthless fellow, and a fellow of such indifferent
8627: reputation, coming and going at odd times, I should ask that I might be
8628: permitted to come and go as a privileged person here; that I might
8629: be regarded as an useless (and I would add, if it were not for the
8630: resemblance I detected between you and me, an unornamental) piece of
8631: furniture, tolerated for its old service, and taken no notice of. I
8632: doubt if I should abuse the permission. It is a hundred to one if I
8633: should avail myself of it four times in a year. It would satisfy me, I
8634: dare say, to know that I had it."
8635: 
8636: "Will you try?"
8637: 
8638: "That is another way of saying that I am placed on the footing I have
8639: indicated. I thank you, Darnay. I may use that freedom with your name?"
8640: 
8641: "I think so, Carton, by this time."
8642: 
8643: They shook hands upon it, and Sydney turned away. Within a minute
8644: afterwards, he was, to all outward appearance, as unsubstantial as ever.
8645: 
8646: When he was gone, and in the course of an evening passed with Miss
8647: Pross, the Doctor, and Mr. Lorry, Charles Darnay made some mention of
8649: problem of carelessness and recklessness. He spoke of him, in short, not
8650: bitterly or meaning to bear hard upon him, but as anybody might who saw
8651: him as he showed himself.
8652: 
8653: He had no idea that this could dwell in the thoughts of his fair young
8654: wife; but, when he afterwards joined her in their own rooms, he found
8655: her waiting for him with the old pretty lifting of the forehead strongly
8656: marked.
8657: 
8658: "We are thoughtful to-night!" said Darnay, drawing his arm about her.
8659: 
8660: "Yes, dearest Charles," with her hands on his breast, and the inquiring
8661: and attentive expression fixed upon him; "we are rather thoughtful
8662: to-night, for we have something on our mind to-night."
8663: 
8664: "What is it, my Lucie?"
8665: 
8666: "Will you promise not to press one question on me, if I beg you not to
8667: ask it?"
8668: 
8669: "Will I promise? What will I not promise to my Love?"
8670: 
8671: What, indeed, with his hand putting aside the golden hair from the
8672: cheek, and his other hand against the heart that beat for him!
8673: 
8674: "I think, Charles, poor Mr. Carton deserves more consideration and
8675: respect than you expressed for him to-night."
8676: 
8677: "Indeed, my own? Why so?"
8678: 
8679: "That is what you are not to ask me. But I think--I know--he does."
8680: 
8681: "If you know it, it is enough. What would you have me do, my Life?"
8682: 
8683: "I would ask you, dearest, to be very generous with him always, and very
8684: lenient on his faults when he is not by. I would ask you to believe that
8685: he has a heart he very, very seldom reveals, and that there are deep
8686: wounds in it. My dear, I have seen it bleeding."
8687: 
8688: "It is a painful reflection to me," said Charles Darnay, quite
8689: astounded, "that I should have done him any wrong. I never thought this
8690: of him."
8691: 
8692: "My husband, it is so. I fear he is not to be reclaimed; there is
8693: scarcely a hope that anything in his character or fortunes is reparable
8694: now. But, I am sure that he is capable of good things, gentle things,
8695: even magnanimous things."
8696: 
8697: She looked so beautiful in the purity of her faith in this lost man,
8698: that her husband could have looked at her as she was for hours.
8699: 
8700: "And, O my dearest Love!" she urged, clinging nearer to him, laying her
8701: head upon his breast, and raising her eyes to his, "remember how strong
8702: we are in our happiness, and how weak he is in his misery!"
8703: 
8704: The supplication touched him home. "I will always remember it, dear
8705: Heart! I will remember it as long as I live."
8706: 
8707: He bent over the golden head, and put the rosy lips to his, and folded
8708: her in his arms. If one forlorn wanderer then pacing the dark streets,
8709: could have heard her innocent disclosure, and could have seen the drops
8710: of pity kissed away by her husband from the soft blue eyes so loving of
8711: that husband, he might have cried to the night--and the words would not
8712: have parted from his lips for the first time--
8713: 
8714: "God bless her for her sweet compassion!"
8715: 
8716: 
8717: 
8718: 
8719: XXI. Echoing Footsteps
8720: 
8721: 
8722: A wonderful corner for echoes, it has been remarked, that corner where
8723: the Doctor lived. Ever busily winding the golden thread which bound
8724: her husband, and her father, and herself, and her old directress and
8725: companion, in a life of quiet bliss, Lucie sat in the still house in
8726: the tranquilly resounding corner, listening to the echoing footsteps of
8727: years.
8728: 
8729: At first, there were times, though she was a perfectly happy young wife,
8730: when her work would slowly fall from her hands, and her eyes would be
8731: dimmed. For, there was something coming in the echoes, something light,
8732: afar off, and scarcely audible yet, that stirred her heart too much.
8733: Fluttering hopes and doubts--hopes, of a love as yet unknown to her:
8734: doubts, of her remaining upon earth, to enjoy that new delight--divided
8735: her breast. Among the echoes then, there would arise the sound of
8736: footsteps at her own early grave; and thoughts of the husband who would
8737: be left so desolate, and who would mourn for her so much, swelled to her
8738: eyes, and broke like waves.
8739: 
8740: That time passed, and her little Lucie lay on her bosom. Then, among the
8741: advancing echoes, there was the tread of her tiny feet and the sound of
8742: her prattling words. Let greater echoes resound as they would, the young
8743: mother at the cradle side could always hear those coming. They came, and
8744: the shady house was sunny with a child's laugh, and the Divine friend of
8745: children, to whom in her trouble she had confided hers, seemed to take
8746: her child in his arms, as He took the child of old, and made it a sacred
8747: joy to her.
8748: 
8749: Ever busily winding the golden thread that bound them all together,
8750: weaving the service of her happy influence through the tissue of all
8751: their lives, and making it predominate nowhere, Lucie heard in the
8752: echoes of years none but friendly and soothing sounds. Her husband's
8753: step was strong and prosperous among them; her father's firm and equal.
8754: Lo, Miss Pross, in harness of string, awakening the echoes, as an
8755: unruly charger, whip-corrected, snorting and pawing the earth under the
8756: plane-tree in the garden!
8757: 
8758: Even when there were sounds of sorrow among the rest, they were not
8759: harsh nor cruel. Even when golden hair, like her own, lay in a halo on a
8760: pillow round the worn face of a little boy, and he said, with a radiant
8761: smile, "Dear papa and mamma, I am very sorry to leave you both, and to
8762: leave my pretty sister; but I am called, and I must go!" those were not
8763: tears all of agony that wetted his young mother's cheek, as the spirit
8764: departed from her embrace that had been entrusted to it. Suffer them and
8765: forbid them not. They see my Father's face. O Father, blessed words!
8766: 
8767: Thus, the rustling of an Angel's wings got blended with the other
8768: echoes, and they were not wholly of earth, but had in them that breath
8769: of Heaven. Sighs of the winds that blew over a little garden-tomb were
8770: mingled with them also, and both were audible to Lucie, in a hushed
8771: murmur--like the breathing of a summer sea asleep upon a sandy shore--as
8772: the little Lucie, comically studious at the task of the morning, or
8773: dressing a doll at her mother's footstool, chattered in the tongues of
8774: the Two Cities that were blended in her life.
8775: 
8777: half-dozen times a year, at most, he claimed his privilege of coming in
8778: uninvited, and would sit among them through the evening, as he had once
8779: done often. He never came there heated with wine. And one other thing
8780: regarding him was whispered in the echoes, which has been whispered by
8781: all true echoes for ages and ages.
8782: 
8783: No man ever really loved a woman, lost her, and knew her with a
8784: blameless though an unchanged mind, when she was a wife and a mother,
8785: but her children had a strange sympathy with him--an instinctive
8786: delicacy of pity for him. What fine hidden sensibilities are touched in
8787: such a case, no echoes tell; but it is so, and it was so here. Carton
8788: was the first stranger to whom little Lucie held out her chubby arms,
8789: and he kept his place with her as she grew. The little boy had spoken of
8790: him, almost at the last. "Poor Carton! Kiss him for me!"
8791: 
8792: Mr. Stryver shouldered his way through the law, like some great engine
8793: forcing itself through turbid water, and dragged his useful friend in
8794: his wake, like a boat towed astern. As the boat so favoured is usually
8795: in a rough plight, and mostly under water, so, Sydney had a swamped
8796: life of it. But, easy and strong custom, unhappily so much easier and
8797: stronger in him than any stimulating sense of desert or disgrace, made
8798: it the life he was to lead; and he no more thought of emerging from his
8799: state of lion's jackal, than any real jackal may be supposed to think of
8800: rising to be a lion. Stryver was rich; had married a florid widow with
8801: property and three boys, who had nothing particularly shining about them
8802: but the straight hair of their dumpling heads.
8803: 
8804: These three young gentlemen, Mr. Stryver, exuding patronage of the most
8805: offensive quality from every pore, had walked before him like three
8806: sheep to the quiet corner in Soho, and had offered as pupils to
8807: Lucie's husband: delicately saying "Halloa! here are three lumps of
8808: bread-and-cheese towards your matrimonial picnic, Darnay!" The polite
8809: rejection of the three lumps of bread-and-cheese had quite bloated Mr.
8810: Stryver with indignation, which he afterwards turned to account in the
8811: training of the young gentlemen, by directing them to beware of the
8812: pride of Beggars, like that tutor-fellow. He was also in the habit of
8813: declaiming to Mrs. Stryver, over his full-bodied wine, on the arts
8814: Mrs. Darnay had once put in practice to "catch" him, and on the
8815: diamond-cut-diamond arts in himself, madam, which had rendered him "not
8816: to be caught." Some of his King's Bench familiars, who were occasionally
8817: parties to the full-bodied wine and the lie, excused him for the
8818: latter by saying that he had told it so often, that he believed
8819: it himself--which is surely such an incorrigible aggravation of an
8820: originally bad offence, as to justify any such offender's being carried
8821: off to some suitably retired spot, and there hanged out of the way.
8822: 
8823: These were among the echoes to which Lucie, sometimes pensive, sometimes
8824: amused and laughing, listened in the echoing corner, until her little
8825: daughter was six years old. How near to her heart the echoes of her
8826: child's tread came, and those of her own dear father's, always active
8827: and self-possessed, and those of her dear husband's, need not be told.
8828: Nor, how the lightest echo of their united home, directed by herself
8829: with such a wise and elegant thrift that it was more abundant than any
8830: waste, was music to her. Nor, how there were echoes all about her, sweet
8831: in her ears, of the many times her father had told her that he found her
8832: more devoted to him married (if that could be) than single, and of the
8833: many times her husband had said to her that no cares and duties seemed
8834: to divide her love for him or her help to him, and asked her "What is
8835: the magic secret, my darling, of your being everything to all of us,
8836: as if there were only one of us, yet never seeming to be hurried, or to
8837: have too much to do?"
8838: 
8839: But, there were other echoes, from a distance, that rumbled menacingly
8840: in the corner all through this space of time. And it was now, about
8841: little Lucie's sixth birthday, that they began to have an awful sound,
8842: as of a great storm in France with a dreadful sea rising.
8843: 
8844: On a night in mid-July, one thousand seven hundred and eighty-nine, Mr.
8845: Lorry came in late, from Tellson's, and sat himself down by Lucie and
8846: her husband in the dark window. It was a hot, wild night, and they were
8847: all three reminded of the old Sunday night when they had looked at the
8848: lightning from the same place.
8849: 
8850: "I began to think," said Mr. Lorry, pushing his brown wig back, "that
8851: I should have to pass the night at Tellson's. We have been so full of
8852: business all day, that we have not known what to do first, or which way
8853: to turn. There is such an uneasiness in Paris, that we have actually a
8854: run of confidence upon us! Our customers over there, seem not to be able
8855: to confide their property to us fast enough. There is positively a mania
8856: among some of them for sending it to England."
8857: 
8858: "That has a bad look," said Darnay--
8859: 
8860: "A bad look, you say, my dear Darnay? Yes, but we don't know what reason
8861: there is in it. People are so unreasonable! Some of us at Tellson's are
8862: getting old, and we really can't be troubled out of the ordinary course
8863: without due occasion."
8864: 
8865: "Still," said Darnay, "you know how gloomy and threatening the sky is."
8866: 
8867: "I know that, to be sure," assented Mr. Lorry, trying to persuade
8868: himself that his sweet temper was soured, and that he grumbled, "but I
8869: am determined to be peevish after my long day's botheration. Where is
8870: Manette?"
8871: 
8872: "Here he is," said the Doctor, entering the dark room at the moment.
8873: 
8874: "I am quite glad you are at home; for these hurries and forebodings by
8875: which I have been surrounded all day long, have made me nervous without
8876: reason. You are not going out, I hope?"
8877: 
8878: "No; I am going to play backgammon with you, if you like," said the
8879: Doctor.
8880: 
8881: "I don't think I do like, if I may speak my mind. I am not fit to be
8882: pitted against you to-night. Is the teaboard still there, Lucie? I can't
8883: see."
8884: 
8885: "Of course, it has been kept for you."
8886: 
8887: "Thank ye, my dear. The precious child is safe in bed?"
8888: 
8889: "And sleeping soundly."
8890: 
8891: "That's right; all safe and well! I don't know why anything should be
8892: otherwise than safe and well here, thank God; but I have been so put out
8893: all day, and I am not as young as I was! My tea, my dear! Thank ye. Now,
8894: come and take your place in the circle, and let us sit quiet, and hear
8895: the echoes about which you have your theory."
8896: 
8897: "Not a theory; it was a fancy."
8898: 
8899: "A fancy, then, my wise pet," said Mr. Lorry, patting her hand. "They
8900: are very numerous and very loud, though, are they not? Only hear them!"
8901: 
8902: Headlong, mad, and dangerous footsteps to force their way into anybody's
8903: life, footsteps not easily made clean again if once stained red, the
8904: footsteps raging in Saint Antoine afar off, as the little circle sat in
8905: the dark London window.
8906: 
8907: Saint Antoine had been, that morning, a vast dusky mass of scarecrows
8908: heaving to and fro, with frequent gleams of light above the billowy
8909: heads, where steel blades and bayonets shone in the sun. A tremendous
8910: roar arose from the throat of Saint Antoine, and a forest of naked arms
8911: struggled in the air like shrivelled branches of trees in a winter wind:
8912: all the fingers convulsively clutching at every weapon or semblance of a
8913: weapon that was thrown up from the depths below, no matter how far off.
8914: 
8915: Who gave them out, whence they last came, where they began, through what
8916: agency they crookedly quivered and jerked, scores at a time, over the
8917: heads of the crowd, like a kind of lightning, no eye in the throng could
8918: have told; but, muskets were being distributed--so were cartridges,
8919: powder, and ball, bars of iron and wood, knives, axes, pikes, every
8920: weapon that distracted ingenuity could discover or devise. People who
8921: could lay hold of nothing else, set themselves with bleeding hands to
8922: force stones and bricks out of their places in walls. Every pulse and
8923: heart in Saint Antoine was on high-fever strain and at high-fever heat.
8924: Every living creature there held life as of no account, and was demented
8925: with a passionate readiness to sacrifice it.
8926: 
8927: As a whirlpool of boiling waters has a centre point, so, all this raging
8928: circled round Defarge's wine-shop, and every human drop in the caldron
8929: had a tendency to be sucked towards the vortex where Defarge himself,
8930: already begrimed with gunpowder and sweat, issued orders, issued arms,
8931: thrust this man back, dragged this man forward, disarmed one to arm
8932: another, laboured and strove in the thickest of the uproar.
8933: 
8934: "Keep near to me, Jacques Three," cried Defarge; "and do you, Jacques
8935: One and Two, separate and put yourselves at the head of as many of these
8936: patriots as you can. Where is my wife?"
8937: 
8938: "Eh, well! Here you see me!" said madame, composed as ever, but not
8939: knitting to-day. Madame's resolute right hand was occupied with an axe,
8940: in place of the usual softer implements, and in her girdle were a pistol
8941: and a cruel knife.
8942: 
8943: "Where do you go, my wife?"
8944: 
8945: "I go," said madame, "with you at present. You shall see me at the head
8946: of women, by-and-bye."
8947: 
8948: "Come, then!" cried Defarge, in a resounding voice. "Patriots and
8949: friends, we are ready! The Bastille!"
8950: 
8951: With a roar that sounded as if all the breath in France had been shaped
8952: into the detested word, the living sea rose, wave on wave, depth on
8953: depth, and overflowed the city to that point. Alarm-bells ringing, drums
8954: beating, the sea raging and thundering on its new beach, the attack
8955: began.
8956: 
8957: Deep ditches, double drawbridge, massive stone walls, eight great
8958: towers, cannon, muskets, fire and smoke. Through the fire and through
8959: the smoke--in the fire and in the smoke, for the sea cast him up against
8960: a cannon, and on the instant he became a cannonier--Defarge of the
