10378: 
10379: The drunken patriot had them in his cap, and produced them. Casting his
10380: eyes over Gabelle's letter, the same personage in authority showed some
10381: disorder and surprise, and looked at Darnay with a close attention.
10382: 
10383: He left escort and escorted without saying a word, however, and went
10384: into the guard-room; meanwhile, they sat upon their horses outside the
10385: gate. Looking about him while in this state of suspense, Charles
10386: Darnay observed that the gate was held by a mixed guard of soldiers and
10387: patriots, the latter far outnumbering the former; and that while ingress
10388: into the city for peasants' carts bringing in supplies, and for similar
10389: traffic and traffickers, was easy enough, egress, even for the homeliest
10390: people, was very difficult. A numerous medley of men and women, not
10391: to mention beasts and vehicles of various sorts, was waiting to issue
10392: forth; but, the previous identification was so strict, that they
10393: filtered through the barrier very slowly. Some of these people knew
10394: their turn for examination to be so far off, that they lay down on the
10395: ground to sleep or smoke, while others talked together, or loitered
10396: about. The red cap and tri-colour cockade were universal, both among men
10397: and women.
10398: 
10399: When he had sat in his saddle some half-hour, taking note of these
10400: things, Darnay found himself confronted by the same man in authority,
10401: who directed the guard to open the barrier. Then he delivered to the
10402: escort, drunk and sober, a receipt for the escorted, and requested him
10403: to dismount. He did so, and the two patriots, leading his tired horse,
10404: turned and rode away without entering the city.
10405: 
10406: He accompanied his conductor into a guard-room, smelling of common wine
10407: and tobacco, where certain soldiers and patriots, asleep and awake,
10408: drunk and sober, and in various neutral states between sleeping and
10409: waking, drunkenness and sobriety, were standing and lying about. The
10410: light in the guard-house, half derived from the waning oil-lamps of
10411: the night, and half from the overcast day, was in a correspondingly
10412: uncertain condition. Some registers were lying open on a desk, and an
10413: officer of a coarse, dark aspect, presided over these.
10414: 
10415: "Citizen Defarge," said he to Darnay's conductor, as he took a slip of
10416: paper to write on. "Is this the emigrant Evremonde?"
10417: 
10418: "This is the man."
10419: 
10420: "Your age, Evremonde?"
10421: 
10422: "Thirty-seven."
10423: 
10424: "Married, Evremonde?"
10425: 
10426: "Yes."
10427: 
10428: "Where married?"
10429: 
10430: "In England."
10431: 
10432: "Without doubt. Where is your wife, Evremonde?"
10433: 
10434: "In England."
10435: 
10436: "Without doubt. You are consigned, Evremonde, to the prison of La
10437: Force."
10438: 
10439: "Just Heaven!" exclaimed Darnay. "Under what law, and for what offence?"
10440: 
10441: The officer looked up from his slip of paper for a moment.
10442: 
10443: "We have new laws, Evremonde, and new offences, since you were here." He
10444: said it with a hard smile, and went on writing.
10445: 
10446: "I entreat you to observe that I have come here voluntarily, in response
10447: to that written appeal of a fellow-countryman which lies before you. I
10448: demand no more than the opportunity to do so without delay. Is not that
10449: my right?"
10450: 
10451: "Emigrants have no rights, Evremonde," was the stolid reply. The officer
10452: wrote until he had finished, read over to himself what he had written,
10453: sanded it, and handed it to Defarge, with the words "In secret."
10454: 
10455: Defarge motioned with the paper to the prisoner that he must accompany
10456: him. The prisoner obeyed, and a guard of two armed patriots attended
10457: them.
10458: 
10459: "Is it you," said Defarge, in a low voice, as they went down the
10460: guardhouse steps and turned into Paris, "who married the daughter of
10461: Doctor Manette, once a prisoner in the Bastille that is no more?"
10462: 
10463: "Yes," replied Darnay, looking at him with surprise.
10464: 
10465: "My name is Defarge, and I keep a wine-shop in the Quarter Saint
10466: Antoine. Possibly you have heard of me."
10467: 
10468: "My wife came to your house to reclaim her father? Yes!"
10469: 
10470: The word "wife" seemed to serve as a gloomy reminder to Defarge, to say
10471: with sudden impatience, "In the name of that sharp female newly-born,
10472: and called La Guillotine, why did you come to France?"
10473: 
10474: "You heard me say why, a minute ago. Do you not believe it is the
10475: truth?"
10476: 
10477: "A bad truth for you," said Defarge, speaking with knitted brows, and
10478: looking straight before him.
10479: 
10480: "Indeed I am lost here. All here is so unprecedented, so changed, so
10481: sudden and unfair, that I am absolutely lost. Will you render me a
10482: little help?"
10483: 
10484: "None." Defarge spoke, always looking straight before him.
10485: 
10486: "Will you answer me a single question?"
10487: 
10488: "Perhaps. According to its nature. You can say what it is."
10489: 
10490: "In this prison that I am going to so unjustly, shall I have some free
10491: communication with the world outside?"
10492: 
10493: "You will see."
10494: 
10495: "I am not to be buried there, prejudged, and without any means of
10496: presenting my case?"
10497: 
10498: "You will see. But, what then? Other people have been similarly buried
10499: in worse prisons, before now."
10500: 
10501: "But never by me, Citizen Defarge."
10502: 
10503: Defarge glanced darkly at him for answer, and walked on in a steady
10504: and set silence. The deeper he sank into this silence, the fainter hope
10505: there was--or so Darnay thought--of his softening in any slight degree.
10506: He, therefore, made haste to say:
10507: 
10508: "It is of the utmost importance to me (you know, Citizen, even better
10509: than I, of how much importance), that I should be able to communicate to
10510: Mr. Lorry of Tellson's Bank, an English gentleman who is now in Paris,
10511: the simple fact, without comment, that I have been thrown into the
10512: prison of La Force. Will you cause that to be done for me?"
10513: 
10514: "I will do," Defarge doggedly rejoined, "nothing for you. My duty is to
10515: my country and the People. I am the sworn servant of both, against you.
10516: I will do nothing for you."
10517: 
10518: Charles Darnay felt it hopeless to entreat him further, and his pride
10519: was touched besides. As they walked on in silence, he could not but see
10520: how used the people were to the spectacle of prisoners passing along the
10521: streets. The very children scarcely noticed him. A few passers turned
10522: their heads, and a few shook their fingers at him as an aristocrat;
10523: otherwise, that a man in good clothes should be going to prison, was no
10524: more remarkable than that a labourer in working clothes should be
10525: going to work. In one narrow, dark, and dirty street through which they
10526: passed, an excited orator, mounted on a stool, was addressing an excited
10527: audience on the crimes against the people, of the king and the royal
10528: family. The few words that he caught from this man's lips, first made
10529: it known to Charles Darnay that the king was in prison, and that the
10530: foreign ambassadors had one and all left Paris. On the road (except at
10531: Beauvais) he had heard absolutely nothing. The escort and the universal
10532: watchfulness had completely isolated him.
10533: 
10534: That he had fallen among far greater dangers than those which had
10535: developed themselves when he left England, he of course knew now. That
10536: perils had thickened about him fast, and might thicken faster and faster
10537: yet, he of course knew now. He could not but admit to himself that he
10538: might not have made this journey, if he could have foreseen the events
10539: of a few days. And yet his misgivings were not so dark as, imagined by
10540: the light of this later time, they would appear. Troubled as the future
10541: was, it was the unknown future, and in its obscurity there was ignorant
10542: hope. The horrible massacre, days and nights long, which, within a few
10543: rounds of the clock, was to set a great mark of blood upon the blessed
10544: garnering time of harvest, was as far out of his knowledge as if it had
10545: been a hundred thousand years away. The "sharp female newly-born, and
10546: called La Guillotine," was hardly known to him, or to the generality
10547: of people, by name. The frightful deeds that were to be soon done, were
10548: probably unimagined at that time in the brains of the doers. How could
10549: they have a place in the shadowy conceptions of a gentle mind?
10550: 
10551: Of unjust treatment in detention and hardship, and in cruel separation
10552: from his wife and child, he foreshadowed the likelihood, or the
10553: certainty; but, beyond this, he dreaded nothing distinctly. With this on
10554: his mind, which was enough to carry into a dreary prison courtyard, he
10555: arrived at the prison of La Force.
10556: 
10557: A man with a bloated face opened the strong wicket, to whom Defarge
10558: presented "The Emigrant Evremonde."
10559: 
10560: "What the Devil! How many more of them!" exclaimed the man with the
10561: bloated face.
10562: 
10563: Defarge took his receipt without noticing the exclamation, and withdrew,
10564: with his two fellow-patriots.
10565: 
10566: "What the Devil, I say again!" exclaimed the gaoler, left with his wife.
10567: "How many more!"
10568: 
10569: The gaoler's wife, being provided with no answer to the question, merely
10570: replied, "One must have patience, my dear!" Three turnkeys who entered
10571: responsive to a bell she rang, echoed the sentiment, and one added, "For
10572: the love of Liberty;" which sounded in that place like an inappropriate
10573: conclusion.
10574: 
10575: The prison of La Force was a gloomy prison, dark and filthy, and with a
10576: horrible smell of foul sleep in it. Extraordinary how soon the noisome
10577: flavour of imprisoned sleep, becomes manifest in all such places that
10578: are ill cared for!
10579: 
10580: "In secret, too," grumbled the gaoler, looking at the written paper. "As
10581: if I was not already full to bursting!"
10582: 
10583: He stuck the paper on a file, in an ill-humour, and Charles Darnay
10584: awaited his further pleasure for half an hour: sometimes, pacing to and
10585: fro in the strong arched room: sometimes, resting on a stone seat: in
10586: either case detained to be imprinted on the memory of the chief and his
10587: subordinates.
10588: 
10589: "Come!" said the chief, at length taking up his keys, "come with me,
10590: emigrant."
10591: 
10592: Through the dismal prison twilight, his new charge accompanied him by
10593: corridor and staircase, many doors clanging and locking behind them,
10594: until they came into a large, low, vaulted chamber, crowded with
10595: prisoners of both sexes. The women were seated at a long table, reading
10596: and writing, knitting, sewing, and embroidering; the men were for the
10597: most part standing behind their chairs, or lingering up and down the
10598: room.
10599: 
10600: In the instinctive association of prisoners with shameful crime and
10601: disgrace, the new-comer recoiled from this company. But the crowning
10602: unreality of his long unreal ride, was, their all at once rising to
10603: receive him, with every refinement of manner known to the time, and with
10604: all the engaging graces and courtesies of life.
10605: 
10606: So strangely clouded were these refinements by the prison manners and
10607: gloom, so spectral did they become in the inappropriate squalor and
10608: misery through which they were seen, that Charles Darnay seemed to stand
10609: in a company of the dead. Ghosts all! The ghost of beauty, the ghost
10610: of stateliness, the ghost of elegance, the ghost of pride, the ghost of
10611: frivolity, the ghost of wit, the ghost of youth, the ghost of age, all
10612: waiting their dismissal from the desolate shore, all turning on him eyes
10613: that were changed by the death they had died in coming there.
10614: 
10615: It struck him motionless. The gaoler standing at his side, and the other
10616: gaolers moving about, who would have been well enough as to appearance
10617: in the ordinary exercise of their functions, looked so extravagantly
10618: coarse contrasted with sorrowing mothers and blooming daughters who were
10619: there--with the apparitions of the coquette, the young beauty, and the
10620: mature woman delicately bred--that the inversion of all experience and
10621: likelihood which the scene of shadows presented, was heightened to its
10622: utmost. Surely, ghosts all. Surely, the long unreal ride some progress
10623: of disease that had brought him to these gloomy shades!
10624: 
10625: "In the name of the assembled companions in misfortune," said a
10626: gentleman of courtly appearance and address, coming forward, "I have the
10627: honour of giving you welcome to La Force, and of condoling with you
10628: on the calamity that has brought you among us. May it soon terminate
10629: happily! It would be an impertinence elsewhere, but it is not so here,
10630: to ask your name and condition?"
10631: 
10632: Charles Darnay roused himself, and gave the required information, in
10633: words as suitable as he could find.
10634: 
10635: "But I hope," said the gentleman, following the chief gaoler with his
10636: eyes, who moved across the room, "that you are not in secret?"
10637: 
10638: "I do not understand the meaning of the term, but I have heard them say
10639: so."
10640: 
10641: "Ah, what a pity! We so much regret it! But take courage; several
10642: members of our society have been in secret, at first, and it has lasted
10643: but a short time." Then he added, raising his voice, "I grieve to inform
10644: the society--in secret."
10645: 
10646: There was a murmur of commiseration as Charles Darnay crossed the room
10647: to a grated door where the gaoler awaited him, and many voices--among
10648: which, the soft and compassionate voices of women were conspicuous--gave
10649: him good wishes and encouragement. He turned at the grated door, to
10650: render the thanks of his heart; it closed under the gaoler's hand; and
10651: the apparitions vanished from his sight forever.
10652: 
10653: The wicket opened on a stone staircase, leading upward. When they had
10654: ascended forty steps (the prisoner of half an hour already counted
10655: them), the gaoler opened a low black door, and they passed into a
10656: solitary cell. It struck cold and damp, but was not dark.
10657: 
10658: "Yours," said the gaoler.
10659: 
10660: "Why am I confined alone?"
10661: 
10662: "How do I know!"
10663: 
10664: "I can buy pen, ink, and paper?"
10665: 
10666: "Such are not my orders. You will be visited, and can ask then. At
10667: present, you may buy your food, and nothing more."
10668: 
10669: There were in the cell, a chair, a table, and a straw mattress. As
10670: the gaoler made a general inspection of these objects, and of the four
10671: walls, before going out, a wandering fancy wandered through the mind of
10672: the prisoner leaning against the wall opposite to him, that this gaoler
10673: was so unwholesomely bloated, both in face and person, as to look like
10674: a man who had been drowned and filled with water. When the gaoler was
10675: gone, he thought in the same wandering way, "Now am I left, as if I were
10676: dead." Stopping then, to look down at the mattress, he turned from it
10677: with a sick feeling, and thought, "And here in these crawling creatures
10678: is the first condition of the body after death."
10679: 
10680: "Five paces by four and a half, five paces by four and a half, five
10681: paces by four and a half." The prisoner walked to and fro in his cell,
10682: counting its measurement, and the roar of the city arose like muffled
10683: drums with a wild swell of voices added to them. "He made shoes, he made
10684: shoes, he made shoes." The prisoner counted the measurement again, and
10685: paced faster, to draw his mind with him from that latter repetition.
10686: "The ghosts that vanished when the wicket closed. There was one among
10687: them, the appearance of a lady dressed in black, who was leaning in the
10688: embrasure of a window, and she had a light shining upon her golden
10689: hair, and she looked like * * * * Let us ride on again, for God's sake,
10690: through the illuminated villages with the people all awake! * * * * He
10691: made shoes, he made shoes, he made shoes. * * * * Five paces by four and
10692: a half." With such scraps tossing and rolling upward from the depths of
10693: his mind, the prisoner walked faster and faster, obstinately counting
10694: and counting; and the roar of the city changed to this extent--that it
10695: still rolled in like muffled drums, but with the wail of voices that he
10696: knew, in the swell that rose above them.
10697: 
10698: 
10699: 
10700: 
10701: II. The Grindstone
10702: 
10703: 
10704: Tellson's Bank, established in the Saint Germain Quarter of Paris, was
10705: in a wing of a large house, approached by a courtyard and shut off from
10706: the street by a high wall and a strong gate. The house belonged to
10707: a great nobleman who had lived in it until he made a flight from the
10708: troubles, in his own cook's dress, and got across the borders. A
10709: mere beast of the chase flying from hunters, he was still in his
10710: metempsychosis no other than the same Monseigneur, the preparation
10711: of whose chocolate for whose lips had once occupied three strong men
10712: besides the cook in question.
10713: 
10714: Monseigneur gone, and the three strong men absolving themselves from the
10715: sin of having drawn his high wages, by being more than ready and
10716: willing to cut his throat on the altar of the dawning Republic one and
10717: indivisible of Liberty, Equality, Fraternity, or Death, Monseigneur's
10718: house had been first sequestrated, and then confiscated. For, all
10719: things moved so fast, and decree followed decree with that fierce
10720: precipitation, that now upon the third night of the autumn month
10721: of September, patriot emissaries of the law were in possession of
10722: Monseigneur's house, and had marked it with the tri-colour, and were
10723: drinking brandy in its state apartments.
10724: 
10725: A place of business in London like Tellson's place of business in Paris,
10726: would soon have driven the House out of its mind and into the Gazette.
10727: For, what would staid British responsibility and respectability have
10728: said to orange-trees in boxes in a Bank courtyard, and even to a Cupid
10729: over the counter? Yet such things were. Tellson's had whitewashed the
10730: Cupid, but he was still to be seen on the ceiling, in the coolest
10731: linen, aiming (as he very often does) at money from morning to
10732: night. Bankruptcy must inevitably have come of this young Pagan, in
10733: Lombard-street, London, and also of a curtained alcove in the rear of
10734: the immortal boy, and also of a looking-glass let into the wall, and
10735: also of clerks not at all old, who danced in public on the slightest
10736: provocation. Yet, a French Tellson's could get on with these things
10737: exceedingly well, and, as long as the times held together, no man had
10738: taken fright at them, and drawn out his money.
10739: 
10740: What money would be drawn out of Tellson's henceforth, and what would
10741: lie there, lost and forgotten; what plate and jewels would tarnish in
10742: Tellson's hiding-places, while the depositors rusted in prisons,
10743: and when they should have violently perished; how many accounts with
10744: Tellson's never to be balanced in this world, must be carried over into
10745: the next; no man could have said, that night, any more than Mr. Jarvis
10746: Lorry could, though he thought heavily of these questions. He sat by
10747: a newly-lighted wood fire (the blighted and unfruitful year was
10748: prematurely cold), and on his honest and courageous face there was a
10749: deeper shade than the pendent lamp could throw, or any object in the
10750: room distortedly reflect--a shade of horror.
10751: 
10752: He occupied rooms in the Bank, in his fidelity to the House of which
10753: he had grown to be a part, like strong root-ivy. It chanced that they
10754: derived a kind of security from the patriotic occupation of the main
10755: building, but the true-hearted old gentleman never calculated about
10756: that. All such circumstances were indifferent to him, so that he did
10757: his duty. On the opposite side of the courtyard, under a colonnade,
10758: was extensive standing--for carriages--where, indeed, some carriages
10759: of Monseigneur yet stood. Against two of the pillars were fastened two
10760: great flaring flambeaux, and in the light of these, standing out in the
10761: open air, was a large grindstone: a roughly mounted thing which appeared
10762: to have hurriedly been brought there from some neighbouring smithy,
10763: or other workshop. Rising and looking out of window at these harmless
10764: objects, Mr. Lorry shivered, and retired to his seat by the fire. He had
10765: opened, not only the glass window, but the lattice blind outside it, and
10766: he had closed both again, and he shivered through his frame.
10767: 
10768: From the streets beyond the high wall and the strong gate, there came
10769: the usual night hum of the city, with now and then an indescribable ring
10770: in it, weird and unearthly, as if some unwonted sounds of a terrible
10771: nature were going up to Heaven.
10772: 
10773: "Thank God," said Mr. Lorry, clasping his hands, "that no one near and
10774: dear to me is in this dreadful town to-night. May He have mercy on all
10775: who are in danger!"
10776: 
10777: Soon afterwards, the bell at the great gate sounded, and he thought,
10778: "They have come back!" and sat listening. But, there was no loud
10779: irruption into the courtyard, as he had expected, and he heard the gate
10780: clash again, and all was quiet.
10781: 
10782: The nervousness and dread that were upon him inspired that vague
10783: uneasiness respecting the Bank, which a great change would naturally
10784: awaken, with such feelings roused. It was well guarded, and he got up to
10785: go among the trusty people who were watching it, when his door suddenly
10786: opened, and two figures rushed in, at sight of which he fell back in
10787: amazement.
10788: 
10789: Lucie and her father! Lucie with her arms stretched out to him, and with
10790: that old look of earnestness so concentrated and intensified, that it
10791: seemed as though it had been stamped upon her face expressly to give
10792: force and power to it in this one passage of her life.
10793: 
10794: "What is this?" cried Mr. Lorry, breathless and confused. "What is the
10795: matter? Lucie! Manette! What has happened? What has brought you here?
10796: What is it?"
10797: 
10798: With the look fixed upon him, in her paleness and wildness, she panted
10799: out in his arms, imploringly, "O my dear friend! My husband!"
10800: 
10801: "Your husband, Lucie?"
10802: 
10803: "Charles."
10804: 
10805: "What of Charles?"
10806: 
10807: "Here.
10808: 
10809: "Here, in Paris?"
10810: 
10811: "Has been here some days--three or four--I don't know how many--I can't
10812: collect my thoughts. An errand of generosity brought him here unknown to
10813: us; he was stopped at the barrier, and sent to prison."
10814: 
10815: The old man uttered an irrepressible cry. Almost at the same moment, the
10816: bell of the great gate rang again, and a loud noise of feet and voices
10817: came pouring into the courtyard.
10818: 
10819: "What is that noise?" said the Doctor, turning towards the window.
10820: 
10821: "Don't look!" cried Mr. Lorry. "Don't look out! Manette, for your life,
10822: don't touch the blind!"
10823: 
10824: The Doctor turned, with his hand upon the fastening of the window, and
10825: said, with a cool, bold smile:
10826: 
10827: "My dear friend, I have a charmed life in this city. I have been
10828: a Bastille prisoner. There is no patriot in Paris--in Paris? In
10829: France--who, knowing me to have been a prisoner in the Bastille, would
10830: touch me, except to overwhelm me with embraces, or carry me in triumph.
10831: My old pain has given me a power that has brought us through the
10832: barrier, and gained us news of Charles there, and brought us here. I
10833: knew it would be so; I knew I could help Charles out of all danger; I
10834: told Lucie so.--What is that noise?" His hand was again upon the window.
10835: 
10836: "Don't look!" cried Mr. Lorry, absolutely desperate. "No, Lucie, my
10837: dear, nor you!" He got his arm round her, and held her. "Don't be so
10838: terrified, my love. I solemnly swear to you that I know of no harm
10839: having happened to Charles; that I had no suspicion even of his being in
10840: this fatal place. What prison is he in?"
10841: 
10842: "La Force!"
10843: 
10844: "La Force! Lucie, my child, if ever you were brave and serviceable in
10845: your life--and you were always both--you will compose yourself now, to
10846: do exactly as I bid you; for more depends upon it than you can think, or
10847: I can say. There is no help for you in any action on your part to-night;
10848: you cannot possibly stir out. I say this, because what I must bid you
10849: to do for Charles's sake, is the hardest thing to do of all. You must
10850: instantly be obedient, still, and quiet. You must let me put you in a
10851: room at the back here. You must leave your father and me alone for
10852: two minutes, and as there are Life and Death in the world you must not
10853: delay."
10854: 
10855: "I will be submissive to you. I see in your face that you know I can do
10856: nothing else than this. I know you are true."
10857: 
10858: The old man kissed her, and hurried her into his room, and turned the
10859: key; then, came hurrying back to the Doctor, and opened the window and
10860: partly opened the blind, and put his hand upon the Doctor's arm, and
10861: looked out with him into the courtyard.
10862: 
10863: Looked out upon a throng of men and women: not enough in number, or near
10864: enough, to fill the courtyard: not more than forty or fifty in all. The
10865: people in possession of the house had let them in at the gate, and they
10866: had rushed in to work at the grindstone; it had evidently been set up
10867: there for their purpose, as in a convenient and retired spot.
10868: 
10869: But, such awful workers, and such awful work!
10870: 
10871: The grindstone had a double handle, and, turning at it madly were two
10872: men, whose faces, as their long hair flapped back when the whirlings of
10873: the grindstone brought their faces up, were more horrible and cruel than
10874: the visages of the wildest savages in their most barbarous disguise.
10875: False eyebrows and false moustaches were stuck upon them, and their
10876: hideous countenances were all bloody and sweaty, and all awry with
10877: howling, and all staring and glaring with beastly excitement and want of
10878: sleep. As these ruffians turned and turned, their matted locks now flung
10879: forward over their eyes, now flung backward over their necks, some women
10880: held wine to their mouths that they might drink; and what with dropping
10881: blood, and what with dropping wine, and what with the stream of sparks
10882: struck out of the stone, all their wicked atmosphere seemed gore and
10883: fire. The eye could not detect one creature in the group free from
10884: the smear of blood. Shouldering one another to get next at the
10885: sharpening-stone, were men stripped to the waist, with the stain all
10886: over their limbs and bodies; men in all sorts of rags, with the stain
10887: upon those rags; men devilishly set off with spoils of women's lace
10888: and silk and ribbon, with the stain dyeing those trifles through
10889: and through. Hatchets, knives, bayonets, swords, all brought to be
10890: sharpened, were all red with it. Some of the hacked swords were tied to
10891: the wrists of those who carried them, with strips of linen and fragments
10892: of dress: ligatures various in kind, but all deep of the one colour. And
10893: as the frantic wielders of these weapons snatched them from the stream
10894: of sparks and tore away into the streets, the same red hue was red in
10895: their frenzied eyes;--eyes which any unbrutalised beholder would have
10896: given twenty years of life, to petrify with a well-directed gun.
10897: 
10898: All this was seen in a moment, as the vision of a drowning man, or of
10899: any human creature at any very great pass, could see a world if it
10900: were there. They drew back from the window, and the Doctor looked for
10901: explanation in his friend's ashy face.
10902: 
10903: "They are," Mr. Lorry whispered the words, glancing fearfully round at
10904: the locked room, "murdering the prisoners. If you are sure of what you
10905: say; if you really have the power you think you have--as I believe you
10906: have--make yourself known to these devils, and get taken to La Force. It
10907: may be too late, I don't know, but let it not be a minute later!"
10908: 
10909: Doctor Manette pressed his hand, hastened bareheaded out of the room,
10910: and was in the courtyard when Mr. Lorry regained the blind.
10911: 
10912: His streaming white hair, his remarkable face, and the impetuous
10913: confidence of his manner, as he put the weapons aside like water,
10914: carried him in an instant to the heart of the concourse at the stone.
10915: For a few moments there was a pause, and a hurry, and a murmur, and
10916: the unintelligible sound of his voice; and then Mr. Lorry saw him,
10917: surrounded by all, and in the midst of a line of twenty men long, all
10918: linked shoulder to shoulder, and hand to shoulder, hurried out with
10919: cries of--"Live the Bastille prisoner! Help for the Bastille prisoner's
10920: kindred in La Force! Room for the Bastille prisoner in front there! Save
10921: the prisoner Evremonde at La Force!" and a thousand answering shouts.
10922: 
10923: He closed the lattice again with a fluttering heart, closed the window
10924: and the curtain, hastened to Lucie, and told her that her father was
10925: assisted by the people, and gone in search of her husband. He found
10926: her child and Miss Pross with her; but, it never occurred to him to be
10927: surprised by their appearance until a long time afterwards, when he sat
10928: watching them in such quiet as the night knew.
10929: 
10930: Lucie had, by that time, fallen into a stupor on the floor at his feet,
10931: clinging to his hand. Miss Pross had laid the child down on his own
10932: bed, and her head had gradually fallen on the pillow beside her pretty
10933: charge. O the long, long night, with the moans of the poor wife! And O
10934: the long, long night, with no return of her father and no tidings!
10935: 
10936: Twice more in the darkness the bell at the great gate sounded, and the
10937: irruption was repeated, and the grindstone whirled and spluttered.
10938: "What is it?" cried Lucie, affrighted. "Hush! The soldiers' swords are
10939: sharpened there," said Mr. Lorry. "The place is national property now,
10940: and used as a kind of armoury, my love."
10941: 
10942: Twice more in all; but, the last spell of work was feeble and fitful.
10943: Soon afterwards the day began to dawn, and he softly detached himself
10944: from the clasping hand, and cautiously looked out again. A man, so
10945: besmeared that he might have been a sorely wounded soldier creeping back
10946: to consciousness on a field of slain, was rising from the pavement by
10947: the side of the grindstone, and looking about him with a vacant air.
10948: Shortly, this worn-out murderer descried in the imperfect light one of
10949: the carriages of Monseigneur, and, staggering to that gorgeous vehicle,
10950: climbed in at the door, and shut himself up to take his rest on its
10951: dainty cushions.
10952: 
10953: The great grindstone, Earth, had turned when Mr. Lorry looked out again,
10954: and the sun was red on the courtyard. But, the lesser grindstone stood
10955: alone there in the calm morning air, with a red upon it that the sun had
10956: never given, and would never take away.
10957: 
10958: 
10959: 
10960: 
10961: III. The Shadow
10962: 
10963: 
10964: One of the first considerations which arose in the business mind of Mr.
10965: Lorry when business hours came round, was this:--that he had no right to
10966: imperil Tellson's by sheltering the wife of an emigrant prisoner under
10967: the Bank roof. His own possessions, safety, life, he would have hazarded
10968: for Lucie and her child, without a moment's demur; but the great trust
10969: he held was not his own, and as to that business charge he was a strict
10970: man of business.
10971: 
10972: At first, his mind reverted to Defarge, and he thought of finding out
10973: the wine-shop again and taking counsel with its master in reference to
10974: the safest dwelling-place in the distracted state of the city. But, the
10975: same consideration that suggested him, repudiated him; he lived in the
10976: most violent Quarter, and doubtless was influential there, and deep in
10977: its dangerous workings.
10978: 
10979: Noon coming, and the Doctor not returning, and every minute's delay
10980: tending to compromise Tellson's, Mr. Lorry advised with Lucie. She said
10981: that her father had spoken of hiring a lodging for a short term, in that
10982: Quarter, near the Banking-house. As there was no business objection to
10983: this, and as he foresaw that even if it were all well with Charles, and
10984: he were to be released, he could not hope to leave the city, Mr. Lorry
10985: went out in quest of such a lodging, and found a suitable one, high up
10986: in a removed by-street where the closed blinds in all the other windows
10987: of a high melancholy square of buildings marked deserted homes.
10988: 
10989: To this lodging he at once removed Lucie and her child, and Miss Pross:
10990: giving them what comfort he could, and much more than he had himself.
10991: He left Jerry with them, as a figure to fill a doorway that would bear
10992: considerable knocking on the head, and returned to his own occupations.
10993: A disturbed and doleful mind he brought to bear upon them, and slowly
10994: and heavily the day lagged on with him.
10995: 
10996: It wore itself out, and wore him out with it, until the Bank closed. He
10997: was again alone in his room of the previous night, considering what to
10998: do next, when he heard a foot upon the stair. In a few moments, a
10999: man stood in his presence, who, with a keenly observant look at him,
11000: addressed him by his name.
11001: 
11002: "Your servant," said Mr. Lorry. "Do you know me?"
11003: 
11004: He was a strongly made man with dark curling hair, from forty-five
11005: to fifty years of age. For answer he repeated, without any change of
11006: emphasis, the words:
11007: 
11008: "Do you know me?"
11009: 
11010: "I have seen you somewhere."
11011: 
11012: "Perhaps at my wine-shop?"
11013: 
11014: Much interested and agitated, Mr. Lorry said: "You come from Doctor
11015: Manette?"
11016: 
11017: "Yes. I come from Doctor Manette."
11018: 
11019: "And what says he? What does he send me?"
11020: 
11021: Defarge gave into his anxious hand, an open scrap of paper. It bore the
11022: words in the Doctor's writing:
11023: 
11024:     "Charles is safe, but I cannot safely leave this place yet.
11025:      I have obtained the favour that the bearer has a short note
11026:      from Charles to his wife.  Let the bearer see his wife."
11027: 
11028: It was dated from La Force, within an hour.
11029: 
11030: "Will you accompany me," said Mr. Lorry, joyfully relieved after reading
11031: this note aloud, "to where his wife resides?"
11032: 
11033: "Yes," returned Defarge.
11034: 
11035: Scarcely noticing as yet, in what a curiously reserved and mechanical
11036: way Defarge spoke, Mr. Lorry put on his hat and they went down into the
11037: courtyard. There, they found two women; one, knitting.
11038: 
11039: "Madame Defarge, surely!" said Mr. Lorry, who had left her in exactly
11040: the same attitude some seventeen years ago.
11041: 
11042: "It is she," observed her husband.
11043: 
11044: "Does Madame go with us?" inquired Mr. Lorry, seeing that she moved as
11045: they moved.
11046: 
11047: "Yes. That she may be able to recognise the faces and know the persons.
11048: It is for their safety."
11049: 
11050: Beginning to be struck by Defarge's manner, Mr. Lorry looked dubiously
11051: at him, and led the way. Both the women followed; the second woman being
11052: The Vengeance.
11053: 
11054: They passed through the intervening streets as quickly as they might,
11055: ascended the staircase of the new domicile, were admitted by Jerry,
11056: and found Lucie weeping, alone. She was thrown into a transport by the
11057: tidings Mr. Lorry gave her of her husband, and clasped the hand that
11058: delivered his note--little thinking what it had been doing near him in
11059: the night, and might, but for a chance, have done to him.
11060: 
11061:      "DEAREST,--Take courage.  I am well, and your father has
11062:       influence around me.  You cannot answer this.
11063:       Kiss our child for me."
11064: 
11065: That was all the writing. It was so much, however, to her who received
11066: it, that she turned from Defarge to his wife, and kissed one of the
11067: hands that knitted. It was a passionate, loving, thankful, womanly
11068: action, but the hand made no response--dropped cold and heavy, and took
11069: to its knitting again.
11070: 
11071: There was something in its touch that gave Lucie a check. She stopped in
11072: the act of putting the note in her bosom, and, with her hands yet at her
11073: neck, looked terrified at Madame Defarge. Madame Defarge met the lifted
11074: eyebrows and forehead with a cold, impassive stare.
11075: 
11076: "My dear," said Mr. Lorry, striking in to explain; "there are frequent
11077: risings in the streets; and, although it is not likely they will ever
11078: trouble you, Madame Defarge wishes to see those whom she has the power
11079: to protect at such times, to the end that she may know them--that she
11080: may identify them. I believe," said Mr. Lorry, rather halting in his
11081: reassuring words, as the stony manner of all the three impressed itself
11082: upon him more and more, "I state the case, Citizen Defarge?"
11083: 
11084: Defarge looked gloomily at his wife, and gave no other answer than a
11085: gruff sound of acquiescence.
11086: 
11087: "You had better, Lucie," said Mr. Lorry, doing all he could to
11088: propitiate, by tone and manner, "have the dear child here, and our
11089: good Pross. Our good Pross, Defarge, is an English lady, and knows no
11090: French."
11091: 
11092: The lady in question, whose rooted conviction that she was more than a
11093: match for any foreigner, was not to be shaken by distress and, danger,
11094: appeared with folded arms, and observed in English to The Vengeance,
11095: whom her eyes first encountered, "Well, I am sure, Boldface! I hope
11096: _you_ are pretty well!" She also bestowed a British cough on Madame
11097: Defarge; but, neither of the two took much heed of her.
11098: 
11099: "Is that his child?" said Madame Defarge, stopping in her work for the
11100: first time, and pointing her knitting-needle at little Lucie as if it
11101: were the finger of Fate.
11102: 
11103: "Yes, madame," answered Mr. Lorry; "this is our poor prisoner's darling
11104: daughter, and only child."
11105: 
11106: The shadow attendant on Madame Defarge and her party seemed to fall so
11107: threatening and dark on the child, that her mother instinctively
11108: kneeled on the ground beside her, and held her to her breast. The
11109: shadow attendant on Madame Defarge and her party seemed then to fall,
11110: threatening and dark, on both the mother and the child.
11111: 
11112: "It is enough, my husband," said Madame Defarge. "I have seen them. We
11113: may go."
11114: 
11115: But, the suppressed manner had enough of menace in it--not visible and
11116: presented, but indistinct and withheld--to alarm Lucie into saying, as
11117: she laid her appealing hand on Madame Defarge's dress:
11118: 
11119: "You will be good to my poor husband. You will do him no harm. You will
11120: help me to see him if you can?"
11121: 
11122: "Your husband is not my business here," returned Madame Defarge, looking
11123: down at her with perfect composure. "It is the daughter of your father
11124: who is my business here."
11125: 
11126: "For my sake, then, be merciful to my husband. For my child's sake! She
11127: will put her hands together and pray you to be merciful. We are more
11128: afraid of you than of these others."
11129: 
11130: Madame Defarge received it as a compliment, and looked at her husband.
11131: Defarge, who had been uneasily biting his thumb-nail and looking at her,
11132: collected his face into a sterner expression.
11133: 
11134: "What is it that your husband says in that little letter?" asked Madame
11135: Defarge, with a lowering smile. "Influence; he says something touching
11136: influence?"
11137: 
11138: "That my father," said Lucie, hurriedly taking the paper from her
11139: breast, but with her alarmed eyes on her questioner and not on it, "has
11140: much influence around him."
11141: 
11142: "Surely it will release him!" said Madame Defarge. "Let it do so."
11143: 
11144: "As a wife and mother," cried Lucie, most earnestly, "I implore you to
11145: have pity on me and not to exercise any power that you possess, against
11146: my innocent husband, but to use it in his behalf. O sister-woman, think
11147: of me. As a wife and mother!"
11148: 
11149: Madame Defarge looked, coldly as ever, at the suppliant, and said,
11150: turning to her friend The Vengeance:
11151: 
11152: "The wives and mothers we have been used to see, since we were as little
11153: as this child, and much less, have not been greatly considered? We have
11154: known _their_ husbands and fathers laid in prison and kept from them,
11155: often enough? All our lives, we have seen our sister-women suffer, in
11156: themselves and in their children, poverty, nakedness, hunger, thirst,
11157: sickness, misery, oppression and neglect of all kinds?"
11158: 
11159: "We have seen nothing else," returned The Vengeance.
11160: 
11161: "We have borne this a long time," said Madame Defarge, turning her eyes
11162: again upon Lucie. "Judge you! Is it likely that the trouble of one wife
11163: and mother would be much to us now?"
11164: 
11165: She resumed her knitting and went out. The Vengeance followed. Defarge
11166: went last, and closed the door.
11167: 
11168: "Courage, my dear Lucie," said Mr. Lorry, as he raised her. "Courage,
11169: courage! So far all goes well with us--much, much better than it has of
11170: late gone with many poor souls. Cheer up, and have a thankful heart."
11171: 
11172: "I am not thankless, I hope, but that dreadful woman seems to throw a
11173: shadow on me and on all my hopes."
11174: 
11175: "Tut, tut!" said Mr. Lorry; "what is this despondency in the brave
11176: little breast? A shadow indeed! No substance in it, Lucie."
11177: 
11178: But the shadow of the manner of these Defarges was dark upon himself,
11179: for all that, and in his secret mind it troubled him greatly.
11180: 
11181: 
11182: 
11183: 
11184: IV. Calm in Storm
11185: 
11186: 
11187: Doctor Manette did not return until the morning of the fourth day of his
11188: absence. So much of what had happened in that dreadful time as could be
11189: kept from the knowledge of Lucie was so well concealed from her, that
11190: not until long afterwards, when France and she were far apart, did she
11191: know that eleven hundred defenceless prisoners of both sexes and all
11192: ages had been killed by the populace; that four days and nights had been
11193: darkened by this deed of horror; and that the air around her had been
11194: tainted by the slain. She only knew that there had been an attack upon
11195: the prisons, that all political prisoners had been in danger, and that
11196: some had been dragged out by the crowd and murdered.
11197: 
11198: To Mr. Lorry, the Doctor communicated under an injunction of secrecy on
11199: which he had no need to dwell, that the crowd had taken him through a
11200: scene of carnage to the prison of La Force. That, in the prison he had
11201: found a self-appointed Tribunal sitting, before which the prisoners were
11202: brought singly, and by which they were rapidly ordered to be put forth
11203: to be massacred, or to be released, or (in a few cases) to be sent back
11204: to their cells. That, presented by his conductors to this Tribunal, he
11205: had announced himself by name and profession as having been for eighteen
11206: years a secret and unaccused prisoner in the Bastille; that, one of the
11207: body so sitting in judgment had risen and identified him, and that this
11208: man was Defarge.
11209: 
11210: That, hereupon he had ascertained, through the registers on the table,
11211: that his son-in-law was among the living prisoners, and had pleaded hard
11212: to the Tribunal--of whom some members were asleep and some awake, some
11213: dirty with murder and some clean, some sober and some not--for his life
11214: and liberty. That, in the first frantic greetings lavished on himself as
11215: a notable sufferer under the overthrown system, it had been accorded
11216: to him to have Charles Darnay brought before the lawless Court, and
11217: examined. That, he seemed on the point of being at once released, when
11218: the tide in his favour met with some unexplained check (not intelligible
11219: to the Doctor), which led to a few words of secret conference. That,
11220: the man sitting as President had then informed Doctor Manette that
11221: the prisoner must remain in custody, but should, for his sake, be held
11222: inviolate in safe custody. That, immediately, on a signal, the prisoner
11223: was removed to the interior of the prison again; but, that he, the
11224: Doctor, had then so strongly pleaded for permission to remain and
11225: assure himself that his son-in-law was, through no malice or mischance,
11226: delivered to the concourse whose murderous yells outside the gate had
11227: often drowned the proceedings, that he had obtained the permission, and
11228: had remained in that Hall of Blood until the danger was over.
11229: 
11230: The sights he had seen there, with brief snatches of food and sleep by
11231: intervals, shall remain untold. The mad joy over the prisoners who were
11232: saved, had astounded him scarcely less than the mad ferocity against
11233: those who were cut to pieces. One prisoner there was, he said, who had
11234: been discharged into the street free, but at whom a mistaken savage had
11235: thrust a pike as he passed out. Being besought to go to him and dress
11236: the wound, the Doctor had passed out at the same gate, and had found him
11237: in the arms of a company of Samaritans, who were seated on the bodies
11238: of their victims. With an inconsistency as monstrous as anything in this
11239: awful nightmare, they had helped the healer, and tended the wounded man
11240: with the gentlest solicitude--had made a litter for him and escorted him
11241: carefully from the spot--had then caught up their weapons and plunged
11242: anew into a butchery so dreadful, that the Doctor had covered his eyes
11243: with his hands, and swooned away in the midst of it.
11244: 
11245: As Mr. Lorry received these confidences, and as he watched the face of
11246: his friend now sixty-two years of age, a misgiving arose within him that
11247: such dread experiences would revive the old danger.
11248: 
11249: But, he had never seen his friend in his present aspect: he had never
11250: at all known him in his present character. For the first time the Doctor
11251: felt, now, that his suffering was strength and power. For the first time
11252: he felt that in that sharp fire, he had slowly forged the iron which
11253: could break the prison door of his daughter's husband, and deliver him.
11254: "It all tended to a good end, my friend; it was not mere waste and ruin.
11255: As my beloved child was helpful in restoring me to myself, I will be
11256: helpful now in restoring the dearest part of herself to her; by the aid
11257: of Heaven I will do it!" Thus, Doctor Manette. And when Jarvis Lorry saw
11258: the kindled eyes, the resolute face, the calm strong look and bearing
11259: of the man whose life always seemed to him to have been stopped, like a
11260: clock, for so many years, and then set going again with an energy which
11261: had lain dormant during the cessation of its usefulness, he believed.
11262: 
11263: Greater things than the Doctor had at that time to contend with, would
11264: have yielded before his persevering purpose. While he kept himself
11265: in his place, as a physician, whose business was with all degrees
11266: of mankind, bond and free, rich and poor, bad and good, he used his
11267: personal influence so wisely, that he was soon the inspecting physician
11268: of three prisons, and among them of La Force. He could now assure Lucie
11269: that her husband was no longer confined alone, but was mixed with the
11270: general body of prisoners; he saw her husband weekly, and brought sweet
11271: messages to her, straight from his lips; sometimes her husband himself
11272: sent a letter to her (though never by the Doctor's hand), but she was
11273: not permitted to write to him: for, among the many wild suspicions of
11274: plots in the prisons, the wildest of all pointed at emigrants who were
11275: known to have made friends or permanent connections abroad.
11276: 
11277: This new life of the Doctor's was an anxious life, no doubt; still, the
11278: sagacious Mr. Lorry saw that there was a new sustaining pride in it.
11279: Nothing unbecoming tinged the pride; it was a natural and worthy one;
11280: but he observed it as a curiosity. The Doctor knew, that up to that
11281: time, his imprisonment had been associated in the minds of his daughter
11282: and his friend, with his personal affliction, deprivation, and weakness.
11283: Now that this was changed, and he knew himself to be invested through
11284: that old trial with forces to which they both looked for Charles's
11285: ultimate safety and deliverance, he became so far exalted by the change,
11286: that he took the lead and direction, and required them as the weak, to
11287: trust to him as the strong. The preceding relative positions of himself
11288: and Lucie were reversed, yet only as the liveliest gratitude and
11289: affection could reverse them, for he could have had no pride but in
11290: rendering some service to her who had rendered so much to him. "All
11291: curious to see," thought Mr. Lorry, in his amiably shrewd way, "but all
11292: natural and right; so, take the lead, my dear friend, and keep it; it
11293: couldn't be in better hands."
11294: 
11295: But, though the Doctor tried hard, and never ceased trying, to get
11296: Charles Darnay set at liberty, or at least to get him brought to trial,
11297: the public current of the time set too strong and fast for him. The new
11298: era began; the king was tried, doomed, and beheaded; the Republic of
11299: Liberty, Equality, Fraternity, or Death, declared for victory or death
11300: against the world in arms; the black flag waved night and day from the
11301: great towers of Notre Dame; three hundred thousand men, summoned to rise
11302: against the tyrants of the earth, rose from all the varying soils
11303: of France, as if the dragon's teeth had been sown broadcast, and
11304: had yielded fruit equally on hill and plain, on rock, in gravel, and
11305: alluvial mud, under the bright sky of the South and under the clouds of
11306: the North, in fell and forest, in the vineyards and the olive-grounds
11307: and among the cropped grass and the stubble of the corn, along the
11308: fruitful banks of the broad rivers, and in the sand of the sea-shore.
11309: What private solicitude could rear itself against the deluge of the Year
11310: One of Liberty--the deluge rising from below, not falling from above,
11311: and with the windows of Heaven shut, not opened!
11312: 
11313: There was no pause, no pity, no peace, no interval of relenting rest, no
11314: measurement of time. Though days and nights circled as regularly as when
11315: time was young, and the evening and morning were the first day, other
11316: count of time there was none. Hold of it was lost in the raging fever
11317: of a nation, as it is in the fever of one patient. Now, breaking the
11318: unnatural silence of a whole city, the executioner showed the people the
11319: head of the king--and now, it seemed almost in the same breath, the
11320: head of his fair wife which had had eight weary months of imprisoned
11321: widowhood and misery, to turn it grey.
11322: 
11323: And yet, observing the strange law of contradiction which obtains in
11324: all such cases, the time was long, while it flamed by so fast. A
11325: revolutionary tribunal in the capital, and forty or fifty thousand
11326: revolutionary committees all over the land; a law of the Suspected,
11327: which struck away all security for liberty or life, and delivered over
11328: any good and innocent person to any bad and guilty one; prisons gorged
11329: with people who had committed no offence, and could obtain no hearing;
11330: these things became the established order and nature of appointed
11331: things, and seemed to be ancient usage before they were many weeks old.
11332: Above all, one hideous figure grew as familiar as if it had been before
11333: the general gaze from the foundations of the world--the figure of the
11334: sharp female called La Guillotine.
11335: 
11336: It was the popular theme for jests; it was the best cure for headache,
11337: it infallibly prevented the hair from turning grey, it imparted a
11338: peculiar delicacy to the complexion, it was the National Razor which
11339: shaved close: who kissed La Guillotine, looked through the little window
11340: and sneezed into the sack. It was the sign of the regeneration of the
11341: human race. It superseded the Cross. Models of it were worn on breasts
11342: from which the Cross was discarded, and it was bowed down to and
11343: believed in where the Cross was denied.
11344: 
11345: It sheared off heads so many, that it, and the ground it most polluted,
11346: were a rotten red. It was taken to pieces, like a toy-puzzle for a young
11347: Devil, and was put together again when the occasion wanted it. It hushed
11348: the eloquent, struck down the powerful, abolished the beautiful and
11349: good. Twenty-two friends of high public mark, twenty-one living and one
11350: dead, it had lopped the heads off, in one morning, in as many minutes.
11351: The name of the strong man of Old Scripture had descended to the chief
11352: functionary who worked it; but, so armed, he was stronger than his
11353: namesake, and blinder, and tore away the gates of God's own Temple every
11354: day.
11355: 
11356: Among these terrors, and the brood belonging to them, the Doctor walked
11357: with a steady head: confident in his power, cautiously persistent in his
11358: end, never doubting that he would save Lucie's husband at last. Yet the
11359: current of the time swept by, so strong and deep, and carried the time
11360: away so fiercely, that Charles had lain in prison one year and three
11361: months when the Doctor was thus steady and confident. So much more
11362: wicked and distracted had the Revolution grown in that December month,
11363: that the rivers of the South were encumbered with the bodies of the
11364: violently drowned by night, and prisoners were shot in lines and squares
11365: under the southern wintry sun. Still, the Doctor walked among the
11366: terrors with a steady head. No man better known than he, in Paris at
11367: that day; no man in a stranger situation. Silent, humane, indispensable
11368: in hospital and prison, using his art equally among assassins and
11369: victims, he was a man apart. In the exercise of his skill, the
11370: appearance and the story of the Bastille Captive removed him from all
11371: other men. He was not suspected or brought in question, any more than if
11372: he had indeed been recalled to life some eighteen years before, or were
11373: a Spirit moving among mortals.
11374: 
11375: 
11376: 
11377: 
11378: V. The Wood-Sawyer
11379: 
11380: 
11381: One year and three months. During all that time Lucie was never
11382: sure, from hour to hour, but that the Guillotine would strike off her
11383: husband's head next day. Every day, through the stony streets, the
11384: tumbrils now jolted heavily, filled with Condemned. Lovely girls; bright
11385: women, brown-haired, black-haired, and grey; youths; stalwart men and
11386: old; gentle born and peasant born; all red wine for La Guillotine, all
11387: daily brought into light from the dark cellars of the loathsome prisons,
11388: and carried to her through the streets to slake her devouring thirst.
11389: Liberty, equality, fraternity, or death;--the last, much the easiest to
11390: bestow, O Guillotine!
11391: 
11392: If the suddenness of her calamity, and the whirling wheels of the time,
11393: had stunned the Doctor's daughter into awaiting the result in idle
11394: despair, it would but have been with her as it was with many. But, from
11395: the hour when she had taken the white head to her fresh young bosom in
11396: the garret of Saint Antoine, she had been true to her duties. She was
11397: truest to them in the season of trial, as all the quietly loyal and good
11398: will always be.
11399: 
11400: As soon as they were established in their new residence, and her father
11401: had entered on the routine of his avocations, she arranged the little
11402: household as exactly as if her husband had been there. Everything had
11403: its appointed place and its appointed time. Little Lucie she taught,
11404: as regularly, as if they had all been united in their English home. The
11405: slight devices with which she cheated herself into the show of a belief
11406: that they would soon be reunited--the little preparations for his speedy
11407: return, the setting aside of his chair and his books--these, and the
11408: solemn prayer at night for one dear prisoner especially, among the many
11409: unhappy souls in prison and the shadow of death--were almost the only
11410: outspoken reliefs of her heavy mind.
11411: 
11412: She did not greatly alter in appearance. The plain dark dresses, akin to
11413: mourning dresses, which she and her child wore, were as neat and as well
11414: attended to as the brighter clothes of happy days. She lost her colour,
11415: and the old and intent expression was a constant, not an occasional,
11416: thing; otherwise, she remained very pretty and comely. Sometimes, at
11417: night on kissing her father, she would burst into the grief she had
11418: repressed all day, and would say that her sole reliance, under Heaven,
11419: was on him. He always resolutely answered: "Nothing can happen to him
11420: without my knowledge, and I know that I can save him, Lucie."
11421: 
11422: They had not made the round of their changed life many weeks, when her
11423: father said to her, on coming home one evening:
11424: 
11425: "My dear, there is an upper window in the prison, to which Charles can
11426: sometimes gain access at three in the afternoon. When he can get to
11427: it--which depends on many uncertainties and incidents--he might see you
11428: in the street, he thinks, if you stood in a certain place that I can
11429: show you. But you will not be able to see him, my poor child, and even
11430: if you could, it would be unsafe for you to make a sign of recognition."
11431: 
11432: "O show me the place, my father, and I will go there every day."
11433: 
11434: From that time, in all weathers, she waited there two hours. As the
11435: clock struck two, she was there, and at four she turned resignedly away.
11436: When it was not too wet or inclement for her child to be with her, they
11437: went together; at other times she was alone; but, she never missed a
11438: single day.
11439: 
11440: It was the dark and dirty corner of a small winding street. The hovel
11441: of a cutter of wood into lengths for burning, was the only house at that
11442: end; all else was wall. On the third day of her being there, he noticed
11443: her.
11444: 
11445: "Good day, citizeness."
11446: 
11447: "Good day, citizen."
11448: 
11449: This mode of address was now prescribed by decree. It had been
11450: established voluntarily some time ago, among the more thorough patriots;
11451: but, was now law for everybody.
11452: 
11453: "Walking here again, citizeness?"
11454: 
11455: "You see me, citizen!"
11456: 
11457: The wood-sawyer, who was a little man with a redundancy of gesture (he
11458: had once been a mender of roads), cast a glance at the prison, pointed
11459: at the prison, and putting his ten fingers before his face to represent
11460: bars, peeped through them jocosely.
11461: 
11462: "But it's not my business," said he. And went on sawing his wood.
11463: 
11464: Next day he was looking out for her, and accosted her the moment she
11465: appeared.
11466: 
11467: "What? Walking here again, citizeness?"
11468: 
11469: "Yes, citizen."
11470: 
11471: "Ah! A child too! Your mother, is it not, my little citizeness?"
11472: 
11473: "Do I say yes, mamma?" whispered little Lucie, drawing close to her.
11474: 
11475: "Yes, dearest."
11476: 
11477: "Yes, citizen."
11478: 
11479: "Ah! But it's not my business. My work is my business. See my saw! I
11480: call it my Little Guillotine. La, la, la; La, la, la! And off his head
11481: comes!"
11482: 
11483: The billet fell as he spoke, and he threw it into a basket.
11484: 
11485: "I call myself the Samson of the firewood guillotine. See here again!
11486: Loo, loo, loo; Loo, loo, loo! And off _her_ head comes! Now, a child.
11487: Tickle, tickle; Pickle, pickle! And off _its_ head comes. All the
11488: family!"
11489: 
11490: Lucie shuddered as he threw two more billets into his basket, but it was
11491: impossible to be there while the wood-sawyer was at work, and not be in
11492: his sight. Thenceforth, to secure his good will, she always spoke to him
11493: first, and often gave him drink-money, which he readily received.
11494: 
11495: He was an inquisitive fellow, and sometimes when she had quite forgotten
11496: him in gazing at the prison roof and grates, and in lifting her heart
11497: up to her husband, she would come to herself to find him looking at her,
11498: with his knee on his bench and his saw stopped in its work. "But it's
11499: not my business!" he would generally say at those times, and would
11500: briskly fall to his sawing again.
11501: 
11502: In all weathers, in the snow and frost of winter, in the bitter winds of
11503: spring, in the hot sunshine of summer, in the rains of autumn, and again
11504: in the snow and frost of winter, Lucie passed two hours of every day at
11505: this place; and every day on leaving it, she kissed the prison wall.
11506: Her husband saw her (so she learned from her father) it might be once in
11507: five or six times: it might be twice or thrice running: it might be, not
11508: for a week or a fortnight together. It was enough that he could and did
11509: see her when the chances served, and on that possibility she would have
11510: waited out the day, seven days a week.
11511: 
11512: These occupations brought her round to the December month, wherein her
11513: father walked among the terrors with a steady head. On a lightly-snowing
11514: afternoon she arrived at the usual corner. It was a day of some wild
11515: rejoicing, and a festival. She had seen the houses, as she came along,
11516: decorated with little pikes, and with little red caps stuck upon them;
11517: also, with tricoloured ribbons; also, with the standard inscription
11518: (tricoloured letters were the favourite), Republic One and Indivisible.
11519: Liberty, Equality, Fraternity, or Death!
11520: 
11521: The miserable shop of the wood-sawyer was so small, that its whole
11522: surface furnished very indifferent space for this legend. He had got
11523: somebody to scrawl it up for him, however, who had squeezed Death in
11524: with most inappropriate difficulty. On his house-top, he displayed pike
11525: and cap, as a good citizen must, and in a window he had stationed his
11526: saw inscribed as his "Little Sainte Guillotine"--for the great sharp
11527: female was by that time popularly canonised. His shop was shut and he
11528: was not there, which was a relief to Lucie, and left her quite alone.
11529: 
11530: But, he was not far off, for presently she heard a troubled movement
11531: and a shouting coming along, which filled her with fear. A moment
11532: afterwards, and a throng of people came pouring round the corner by the
11533: prison wall, in the midst of whom was the wood-sawyer hand in hand with
11534: The Vengeance. There could not be fewer than five hundred people, and
11535: they were dancing like five thousand demons. There was no other music
11536: than their own singing. They danced to the popular Revolution song,
11537: keeping a ferocious time that was like a gnashing of teeth in unison.
11538: Men and women danced together, women danced together, men danced
11539: together, as hazard had brought them together. At first, they were a
11540: mere storm of coarse red caps and coarse woollen rags; but, as they
11541: filled the place, and stopped to dance about Lucie, some ghastly
11542: apparition of a dance-figure gone raving mad arose among them. They
11543: advanced, retreated, struck at one another's hands, clutched at one
11544: another's heads, spun round alone, caught one another and spun round
11545: in pairs, until many of them dropped. While those were down, the rest
11546: linked hand in hand, and all spun round together: then the ring broke,
11547: and in separate rings of two and four they turned and turned until they
11548: all stopped at once, began again, struck, clutched, and tore, and then
11549: reversed the spin, and all spun round another way. Suddenly they stopped
11550: again, paused, struck out the time afresh, formed into lines the width
11551: of the public way, and, with their heads low down and their hands high
11552: up, swooped screaming off. No fight could have been half so terrible
11553: as this dance. It was so emphatically a fallen sport--a something, once
11554: innocent, delivered over to all devilry--a healthy pastime changed into
11555: a means of angering the blood, bewildering the senses, and steeling the
11556: heart. Such grace as was visible in it, made it the uglier, showing how
11557: warped and perverted all things good by nature were become. The maidenly
11558: bosom bared to this, the pretty almost-child's head thus distracted, the
11559: delicate foot mincing in this slough of blood and dirt, were types of
11560: the disjointed time.
11561: 
11562: This was the Carmagnole. As it passed, leaving Lucie frightened and
11563: bewildered in the doorway of the wood-sawyer's house, the feathery snow
11564: fell as quietly and lay as white and soft, as if it had never been.
11565: 
11566: "O my father!" for he stood before her when she lifted up the eyes she
11567: had momentarily darkened with her hand; "such a cruel, bad sight."
11568: 
11569: "I know, my dear, I know. I have seen it many times. Don't be
11570: frightened! Not one of them would harm you."
11571: 
11572: "I am not frightened for myself, my father. But when I think of my
11573: husband, and the mercies of these people--"
11574: 
11575: "We will set him above their mercies very soon. I left him climbing to
11576: the window, and I came to tell you. There is no one here to see. You may
11577: kiss your hand towards that highest shelving roof."
11578: 
11579: "I do so, father, and I send him my Soul with it!"
11580: 
11581: "You cannot see him, my poor dear?"
11582: 
11583: "No, father," said Lucie, yearning and weeping as she kissed her hand,
11584: "no."
11585: 
11586: A footstep in the snow. Madame Defarge. "I salute you, citizeness,"
11587: from the Doctor. "I salute you, citizen." This in passing. Nothing more.
11588: Madame Defarge gone, like a shadow over the white road.
11589: 
11590: "Give me your arm, my love. Pass from here with an air of cheerfulness
11591: and courage, for his sake. That was well done;" they had left the spot;
11592: "it shall not be in vain. Charles is summoned for to-morrow."
11593: 
11594: "For to-morrow!"
11595: 
11596: "There is no time to lose. I am well prepared, but there are precautions
11597: to be taken, that could not be taken until he was actually summoned
11598: before the Tribunal. He has not received the notice yet, but I know
11599: that he will presently be summoned for to-morrow, and removed to the
11600: Conciergerie; I have timely information. You are not afraid?"
11601: 
11602: She could scarcely answer, "I trust in you."
11603: 
11604: "Do so, implicitly. Your suspense is nearly ended, my darling; he shall
11605: be restored to you within a few hours; I have encompassed him with every
11606: protection. I must see Lorry."
11607: 
11608: He stopped. There was a heavy lumbering of wheels within hearing. They
11609: both knew too well what it meant. One. Two. Three. Three tumbrils faring
11610: away with their dread loads over the hushing snow.
11611: 
11612: "I must see Lorry," the Doctor repeated, turning her another way.
11613: 
11614: The staunch old gentleman was still in his trust; had never left it. He
11615: and his books were in frequent requisition as to property confiscated
11616: and made national. What he could save for the owners, he saved. No
11617: better man living to hold fast by what Tellson's had in keeping, and to
11618: hold his peace.
11619: 
11620: A murky red and yellow sky, and a rising mist from the Seine, denoted
11621: the approach of darkness. It was almost dark when they arrived at the
11622: Bank. The stately residence of Monseigneur was altogether blighted and
11623: deserted. Above a heap of dust and ashes in the court, ran the letters:
11624: National Property. Republic One and Indivisible. Liberty, Equality,
11625: Fraternity, or Death!
11626: 
11627: Who could that be with Mr. Lorry--the owner of the riding-coat upon the
11628: chair--who must not be seen? From whom newly arrived, did he come out,
11629: agitated and surprised, to take his favourite in his arms? To whom did
11630: he appear to repeat her faltering words, when, raising his voice and
11631: turning his head towards the door of the room from which he had issued,
11632: he said: "Removed to the Conciergerie, and summoned for to-morrow?"
11633: 
11634: 
11635: 
11636: 
11637: VI. Triumph
11638: 
11639: 
11640: The dread tribunal of five Judges, Public Prosecutor, and determined
11641: Jury, sat every day. Their lists went forth every evening, and were
11642: read out by the gaolers of the various prisons to their prisoners. The
11643: standard gaoler-joke was, "Come out and listen to the Evening Paper, you
11644: inside there!"
11645: 
11646: "Charles Evremonde, called Darnay!"
11647: 
11648: So at last began the Evening Paper at La Force.
11649: 
11650: When a name was called, its owner stepped apart into a spot reserved
11651: for those who were announced as being thus fatally recorded. Charles
11652: Evremonde, called Darnay, had reason to know the usage; he had seen
11653: hundreds pass away so.
11654: 
11655: His bloated gaoler, who wore spectacles to read with, glanced over them
11656: to assure himself that he had taken his place, and went through the
11657: list, making a similar short pause at each name. There were twenty-three
11658: names, but only twenty were responded to; for one of the prisoners so
11659: summoned had died in gaol and been forgotten, and two had already been
11660: guillotined and forgotten. The list was read, in the vaulted chamber
11661: where Darnay had seen the associated prisoners on the night of his
11662: arrival. Every one of those had perished in the massacre; every human
11663: creature he had since cared for and parted with, had died on the
11664: scaffold.
11665: 
11666: There were hurried words of farewell and kindness, but the parting was
11667: soon over. It was the incident of every day, and the society of La Force
11668: were engaged in the preparation of some games of forfeits and a little
11669: concert, for that evening. They crowded to the grates and shed tears
11670: there; but, twenty places in the projected entertainments had to be
11671: refilled, and the time was, at best, short to the lock-up hour, when the
11672: common rooms and corridors would be delivered over to the great dogs
11673: who kept watch there through the night. The prisoners were far from
11674: insensible or unfeeling; their ways arose out of the condition of the
11675: time. Similarly, though with a subtle difference, a species of fervour
11676: or intoxication, known, without doubt, to have led some persons to
11677: brave the guillotine unnecessarily, and to die by it, was not mere
11678: boastfulness, but a wild infection of the wildly shaken public mind. In
11679: seasons of pestilence, some of us will have a secret attraction to the
11680: disease--a terrible passing inclination to die of it. And all of us have
11681: like wonders hidden in our breasts, only needing circumstances to evoke
11682: them.
11683: 
11684: The passage to the Conciergerie was short and dark; the night in its
11685: vermin-haunted cells was long and cold. Next day, fifteen prisoners were
11686: put to the bar before Charles Darnay's name was called. All the fifteen
11687: were condemned, and the trials of the whole occupied an hour and a half.
11688: 
11689: "Charles Evremonde, called Darnay," was at length arraigned.
11690: 
11691: His judges sat upon the Bench in feathered hats; but the rough red cap
11692: and tricoloured cockade was the head-dress otherwise prevailing. Looking
11693: at the Jury and the turbulent audience, he might have thought that the
11694: usual order of things was reversed, and that the felons were trying the
11695: honest men. The lowest, cruelest, and worst populace of a city, never
11696: without its quantity of low, cruel, and bad, were the directing
11697: spirits of the scene: noisily commenting, applauding, disapproving,
11698: anticipating, and precipitating the result, without a check. Of the men,
11699: the greater part were armed in various ways; of the women, some wore
11700: knives, some daggers, some ate and drank as they looked on, many
11701: knitted. Among these last, was one, with a spare piece of knitting under
11702: her arm as she worked. She was in a front row, by the side of a man whom
11703: he had never seen since his arrival at the Barrier, but whom he directly
11704: remembered as Defarge. He noticed that she once or twice whispered in
11705: his ear, and that she seemed to be his wife; but, what he most noticed
11706: in the two figures was, that although they were posted as close to
11707: himself as they could be, they never looked towards him. They seemed to
11708: be waiting for something with a dogged determination, and they looked at
11709: the Jury, but at nothing else. Under the President sat Doctor Manette,
11710: in his usual quiet dress. As well as the prisoner could see, he and Mr.
11711: Lorry were the only men there, unconnected with the Tribunal, who
11712: wore their usual clothes, and had not assumed the coarse garb of the
11713: Carmagnole.
11714: 
11715: Charles Evremonde, called Darnay, was accused by the public prosecutor
11716: as an emigrant, whose life was forfeit to the Republic, under the decree
11717: which banished all emigrants on pain of Death. It was nothing that the
11718: decree bore date since his return to France. There he was, and there was
11719: the decree; he had been taken in France, and his head was demanded.
11720: 
11721: "Take off his head!" cried the audience. "An enemy to the Republic!"
11722: 
11723: The President rang his bell to silence those cries, and asked the
11724: prisoner whether it was not true that he had lived many years in
11725: England?
11726: 
11727: Undoubtedly it was.
11728: 
11729: Was he not an emigrant then? What did he call himself?
11730: 
11731: Not an emigrant, he hoped, within the sense and spirit of the law.
11732: 
11733: Why not? the President desired to know.
11734: 
11735: Because he had voluntarily relinquished a title that was distasteful
11736: to him, and a station that was distasteful to him, and had left
11737: his country--he submitted before the word emigrant in the present
11738: acceptation by the Tribunal was in use--to live by his own industry in
11739: England, rather than on the industry of the overladen people of France.
11740: 
11741: What proof had he of this?
11742: 
11743: He handed in the names of two witnesses; Theophile Gabelle, and
11744: Alexandre Manette.
11745: 
11746: But he had married in England? the President reminded him.
11747: 
11748: True, but not an English woman.
11749: 
11750: A citizeness of France?
11751: 
11752: Yes. By birth.
11753: 
11754: Her name and family?
11755: 
11756: "Lucie Manette, only daughter of Doctor Manette, the good physician who
11757: sits there."
11758: 
11759: This answer had a happy effect upon the audience. Cries in exaltation
11760: of the well-known good physician rent the hall. So capriciously were
11761: the people moved, that tears immediately rolled down several ferocious
11762: countenances which had been glaring at the prisoner a moment before, as
11763: if with impatience to pluck him out into the streets and kill him.
11764: 
11765: On these few steps of his dangerous way, Charles Darnay had set his foot
11766: according to Doctor Manette's reiterated instructions. The same cautious
11767: counsel directed every step that lay before him, and had prepared every
11768: inch of his road.
11769: 
11770: The President asked, why had he returned to France when he did, and not
11771: sooner?
11772: 
11773: He had not returned sooner, he replied, simply because he had no means
11774: of living in France, save those he had resigned; whereas, in England,
11775: he lived by giving instruction in the French language and literature.
11776: He had returned when he did, on the pressing and written entreaty of
11777: a French citizen, who represented that his life was endangered by his
11778: absence. He had come back, to save a citizen's life, and to bear his
11779: testimony, at whatever personal hazard, to the truth. Was that criminal
