11780: in the eyes of the Republic?
11781: 
11782: The populace cried enthusiastically, "No!" and the President rang his
11783: bell to quiet them. Which it did not, for they continued to cry "No!"
11784: until they left off, of their own will.
11785: 
11786: The President required the name of that citizen. The accused explained
11787: that the citizen was his first witness. He also referred with confidence
11788: to the citizen's letter, which had been taken from him at the Barrier,
11789: but which he did not doubt would be found among the papers then before
11790: the President.
11791: 
11792: The Doctor had taken care that it should be there--had assured him that
11793: it would be there--and at this stage of the proceedings it was produced
11794: and read. Citizen Gabelle was called to confirm it, and did so. Citizen
11795: Gabelle hinted, with infinite delicacy and politeness, that in the
11796: pressure of business imposed on the Tribunal by the multitude of
11797: enemies of the Republic with which it had to deal, he had been slightly
11798: overlooked in his prison of the Abbaye--in fact, had rather passed out
11799: of the Tribunal's patriotic remembrance--until three days ago; when he
11800: had been summoned before it, and had been set at liberty on the Jury's
11801: declaring themselves satisfied that the accusation against him was
11802: answered, as to himself, by the surrender of the citizen Evremonde,
11803: called Darnay.
11804: 
11805: Doctor Manette was next questioned. His high personal popularity,
11806: and the clearness of his answers, made a great impression; but, as he
11807: proceeded, as he showed that the Accused was his first friend on his
11808: release from his long imprisonment; that, the accused had remained in
11809: England, always faithful and devoted to his daughter and himself in
11810: their exile; that, so far from being in favour with the Aristocrat
11811: government there, he had actually been tried for his life by it, as
11812: the foe of England and friend of the United States--as he brought these
11813: circumstances into view, with the greatest discretion and with the
11814: straightforward force of truth and earnestness, the Jury and the
11815: populace became one. At last, when he appealed by name to Monsieur
11816: Lorry, an English gentleman then and there present, who, like himself,
11817: had been a witness on that English trial and could corroborate his
11818: account of it, the Jury declared that they had heard enough, and that
11819: they were ready with their votes if the President were content to
11820: receive them.
11821: 
11822: At every vote (the Jurymen voted aloud and individually), the populace
11823: set up a shout of applause. All the voices were in the prisoner's
11824: favour, and the President declared him free.
11825: 
11826: Then, began one of those extraordinary scenes with which the populace
11827: sometimes gratified their fickleness, or their better impulses towards
11828: generosity and mercy, or which they regarded as some set-off against
11829: their swollen account of cruel rage. No man can decide now to which of
11830: these motives such extraordinary scenes were referable; it is probable,
11831: to a blending of all the three, with the second predominating. No sooner
11832: was the acquittal pronounced, than tears were shed as freely as blood
11833: at another time, and such fraternal embraces were bestowed upon the
11834: prisoner by as many of both sexes as could rush at him, that after
11835: his long and unwholesome confinement he was in danger of fainting from
11836: exhaustion; none the less because he knew very well, that the very same
11837: people, carried by another current, would have rushed at him with
11838: the very same intensity, to rend him to pieces and strew him over the
11839: streets.
11840: 
11841: His removal, to make way for other accused persons who were to be tried,
11842: rescued him from these caresses for the moment. Five were to be tried
11843: together, next, as enemies of the Republic, forasmuch as they had not
11844: assisted it by word or deed. So quick was the Tribunal to compensate
11845: itself and the nation for a chance lost, that these five came down to
11846: him before he left the place, condemned to die within twenty-four
11847: hours. The first of them told him so, with the customary prison sign
11848: of Death--a raised finger--and they all added in words, "Long live the
11849: Republic!"
11850: 
11851: The five had had, it is true, no audience to lengthen their proceedings,
11852: for when he and Doctor Manette emerged from the gate, there was a great
11853: crowd about it, in which there seemed to be every face he had seen in
11854: Court--except two, for which he looked in vain. On his coming out, the
11855: concourse made at him anew, weeping, embracing, and shouting, all by
11856: turns and all together, until the very tide of the river on the bank of
11857: which the mad scene was acted, seemed to run mad, like the people on the
11858: shore.
11859: 
11860: They put him into a great chair they had among them, and which they had
11861: taken either out of the Court itself, or one of its rooms or passages.
11862: Over the chair they had thrown a red flag, and to the back of it they
11863: had bound a pike with a red cap on its top. In this car of triumph, not
11864: even the Doctor's entreaties could prevent his being carried to his home
11865: on men's shoulders, with a confused sea of red caps heaving about him,
11866: and casting up to sight from the stormy deep such wrecks of faces, that
11867: he more than once misdoubted his mind being in confusion, and that he
11868: was in the tumbril on his way to the Guillotine.
11869: 
11870: In wild dreamlike procession, embracing whom they met and pointing
11871: him out, they carried him on. Reddening the snowy streets with the
11872: prevailing Republican colour, in winding and tramping through them, as
11873: they had reddened them below the snow with a deeper dye, they carried
11874: him thus into the courtyard of the building where he lived. Her father
11875: had gone on before, to prepare her, and when her husband stood upon his
11876: feet, she dropped insensible in his arms.
11877: 
11878: As he held her to his heart and turned her beautiful head between his
11879: face and the brawling crowd, so that his tears and her lips might come
11880: together unseen, a few of the people fell to dancing. Instantly, all the
11881: rest fell to dancing, and the courtyard overflowed with the Carmagnole.
11882: Then, they elevated into the vacant chair a young woman from the
11883: crowd to be carried as the Goddess of Liberty, and then swelling and
11884: overflowing out into the adjacent streets, and along the river's bank,
11885: and over the bridge, the Carmagnole absorbed them every one and whirled
11886: them away.
11887: 
11888: After grasping the Doctor's hand, as he stood victorious and proud
11889: before him; after grasping the hand of Mr. Lorry, who came panting in
11890: breathless from his struggle against the waterspout of the Carmagnole;
11891: after kissing little Lucie, who was lifted up to clasp her arms round
11892: his neck; and after embracing the ever zealous and faithful Pross who
11893: lifted her; he took his wife in his arms, and carried her up to their
11894: rooms.
11895: 
11896: "Lucie! My own! I am safe."
11897: 
11898: "O dearest Charles, let me thank God for this on my knees as I have
11899: prayed to Him."
11900: 
11901: They all reverently bowed their heads and hearts. When she was again in
11902: his arms, he said to her:
11903: 
11904: "And now speak to your father, dearest. No other man in all this France
11905: could have done what he has done for me."
11906: 
11907: She laid her head upon her father's breast, as she had laid his poor
11908: head on her own breast, long, long ago. He was happy in the return he
11909: had made her, he was recompensed for his suffering, he was proud of his
11910: strength. "You must not be weak, my darling," he remonstrated; "don't
11911: tremble so. I have saved him."
11912: 
11913: 
11914: 
11915: 
11916: VII. A Knock at the Door
11917: 
11918: 
11919: "I have saved him." It was not another of the dreams in which he had
11920: often come back; he was really here. And yet his wife trembled, and a
11921: vague but heavy fear was upon her.
11922: 
11923: All the air round was so thick and dark, the people were so passionately
11924: revengeful and fitful, the innocent were so constantly put to death on
11925: vague suspicion and black malice, it was so impossible to forget that
11926: many as blameless as her husband and as dear to others as he was to
11927: her, every day shared the fate from which he had been clutched, that her
11928: heart could not be as lightened of its load as she felt it ought to be.
11929: The shadows of the wintry afternoon were beginning to fall, and even now
11930: the dreadful carts were rolling through the streets. Her mind pursued
11931: them, looking for him among the Condemned; and then she clung closer to
11932: his real presence and trembled more.
11933: 
11934: Her father, cheering her, showed a compassionate superiority to this
11935: woman's weakness, which was wonderful to see. No garret, no shoemaking,
11936: no One Hundred and Five, North Tower, now! He had accomplished the task
11937: he had set himself, his promise was redeemed, he had saved Charles. Let
11938: them all lean upon him.
11939: 
11940: Their housekeeping was of a very frugal kind: not only because that was
11941: the safest way of life, involving the least offence to the people, but
11942: because they were not rich, and Charles, throughout his imprisonment,
11943: had had to pay heavily for his bad food, and for his guard, and towards
11944: the living of the poorer prisoners. Partly on this account, and
11945: partly to avoid a domestic spy, they kept no servant; the citizen and
11946: citizeness who acted as porters at the courtyard gate, rendered them
11947: occasional service; and Jerry (almost wholly transferred to them by
11948: Mr. Lorry) had become their daily retainer, and had his bed there every
11949: night.
11950: 
11951: It was an ordinance of the Republic One and Indivisible of Liberty,
11952: Equality, Fraternity, or Death, that on the door or doorpost of every
11953: house, the name of every inmate must be legibly inscribed in letters
11954: of a certain size, at a certain convenient height from the ground. Mr.
11955: Jerry Cruncher's name, therefore, duly embellished the doorpost down
11956: below; and, as the afternoon shadows deepened, the owner of that name
11957: himself appeared, from overlooking a painter whom Doctor Manette had
11958: employed to add to the list the name of Charles Evremonde, called
11959: Darnay.
11960: 
11961: In the universal fear and distrust that darkened the time, all the usual
11962: harmless ways of life were changed. In the Doctor's little household, as
11963: in very many others, the articles of daily consumption that were wanted
11964: were purchased every evening, in small quantities and at various small
11965: shops. To avoid attracting notice, and to give as little occasion as
11966: possible for talk and envy, was the general desire.
11967: 
11968: For some months past, Miss Pross and Mr. Cruncher had discharged the
11969: office of purveyors; the former carrying the money; the latter, the
11970: basket. Every afternoon at about the time when the public lamps were
11971: lighted, they fared forth on this duty, and made and brought home
11972: such purchases as were needful. Although Miss Pross, through her long
11973: association with a French family, might have known as much of their
11974: language as of her own, if she had had a mind, she had no mind in that
11975: direction; consequently she knew no more of that "nonsense" (as she was
11976: pleased to call it) than Mr. Cruncher did. So her manner of marketing
11977: was to plump a noun-substantive at the head of a shopkeeper without any
11978: introduction in the nature of an article, and, if it happened not to be
11979: the name of the thing she wanted, to look round for that thing, lay hold
11980: of it, and hold on by it until the bargain was concluded. She always
11981: made a bargain for it, by holding up, as a statement of its just price,
11982: one finger less than the merchant held up, whatever his number might be.
11983: 
11984: "Now, Mr. Cruncher," said Miss Pross, whose eyes were red with felicity;
11985: "if you are ready, I am."
11986: 
11987: Jerry hoarsely professed himself at Miss Pross's service. He had worn
11988: all his rust off long ago, but nothing would file his spiky head down.
11989: 
11990: "There's all manner of things wanted," said Miss Pross, "and we shall
11991: have a precious time of it. We want wine, among the rest. Nice toasts
11992: these Redheads will be drinking, wherever we buy it."
11993: 
11994: "It will be much the same to your knowledge, miss, I should think,"
11995: retorted Jerry, "whether they drink your health or the Old Un's."
11996: 
11997: "Who's he?" said Miss Pross.
11998: 
11999: Mr. Cruncher, with some diffidence, explained himself as meaning "Old
12000: Nick's."
12001: 
12002: "Ha!" said Miss Pross, "it doesn't need an interpreter to explain the
12003: meaning of these creatures. They have but one, and it's Midnight Murder,
12004: and Mischief."
12005: 
12006: "Hush, dear! Pray, pray, be cautious!" cried Lucie.
12007: 
12008: "Yes, yes, yes, I'll be cautious," said Miss Pross; "but I may say
12009: among ourselves, that I do hope there will be no oniony and tobaccoey
12010: smotherings in the form of embracings all round, going on in the
12011: streets. Now, Ladybird, never you stir from that fire till I come back!
12012: Take care of the dear husband you have recovered, and don't move your
12013: pretty head from his shoulder as you have it now, till you see me again!
12014: May I ask a question, Doctor Manette, before I go?"
12015: 
12016: "I think you may take that liberty," the Doctor answered, smiling.
12017: 
12018: "For gracious sake, don't talk about Liberty; we have quite enough of
12019: that," said Miss Pross.
12020: 
12021: "Hush, dear! Again?" Lucie remonstrated.
12022: 
12023: "Well, my sweet," said Miss Pross, nodding her head emphatically, "the
12024: short and the long of it is, that I am a subject of His Most Gracious
12025: Majesty King George the Third;" Miss Pross curtseyed at the name; "and
12026: as such, my maxim is, Confound their politics, Frustrate their knavish
12027: tricks, On him our hopes we fix, God save the King!"
12028: 
12029: Mr. Cruncher, in an access of loyalty, growlingly repeated the words
12030: after Miss Pross, like somebody at church.
12031: 
12032: "I am glad you have so much of the Englishman in you, though I wish you
12033: had never taken that cold in your voice," said Miss Pross, approvingly.
12034: "But the question, Doctor Manette. Is there"--it was the good creature's
12035: way to affect to make light of anything that was a great anxiety
12036: with them all, and to come at it in this chance manner--"is there any
12037: prospect yet, of our getting out of this place?"
12038: 
12039: "I fear not yet. It would be dangerous for Charles yet."
12040: 
12041: "Heigh-ho-hum!" said Miss Pross, cheerfully repressing a sigh as she
12042: glanced at her darling's golden hair in the light of the fire, "then we
12043: must have patience and wait: that's all. We must hold up our heads and
12044: fight low, as my brother Solomon used to say. Now, Mr. Cruncher!--Don't
12045: you move, Ladybird!"
12046: 
12047: They went out, leaving Lucie, and her husband, her father, and the
12048: child, by a bright fire. Mr. Lorry was expected back presently from the
12049: Banking House. Miss Pross had lighted the lamp, but had put it aside in
12050: a corner, that they might enjoy the fire-light undisturbed. Little Lucie
12051: sat by her grandfather with her hands clasped through his arm: and he,
12052: in a tone not rising much above a whisper, began to tell her a story of
12053: a great and powerful Fairy who had opened a prison-wall and let out
12054: a captive who had once done the Fairy a service. All was subdued and
12055: quiet, and Lucie was more at ease than she had been.
12056: 
12057: "What is that?" she cried, all at once.
12058: 
12059: "My dear!" said her father, stopping in his story, and laying his hand
12060: on hers, "command yourself. What a disordered state you are in! The
12061: least thing--nothing--startles you! _You_, your father's daughter!"
12062: 
12063: "I thought, my father," said Lucie, excusing herself, with a pale face
12064: and in a faltering voice, "that I heard strange feet upon the stairs."
12065: 
12066: "My love, the staircase is as still as Death."
12067: 
12068: As he said the word, a blow was struck upon the door.
12069: 
12070: "Oh father, father. What can this be! Hide Charles. Save him!"
12071: 
12072: "My child," said the Doctor, rising, and laying his hand upon her
12073: shoulder, "I _have_ saved him. What weakness is this, my dear! Let me go
12074: to the door."
12075: 
12076: He took the lamp in his hand, crossed the two intervening outer rooms,
12077: and opened it. A rude clattering of feet over the floor, and four rough
12078: men in red caps, armed with sabres and pistols, entered the room.
12079: 
12080: "The Citizen Evremonde, called Darnay," said the first.
12081: 
12082: "Who seeks him?" answered Darnay.
12083: 
12084: "I seek him. We seek him. I know you, Evremonde; I saw you before the
12085: Tribunal to-day. You are again the prisoner of the Republic."
12086: 
12087: The four surrounded him, where he stood with his wife and child clinging
12088: to him.
12089: 
12090: "Tell me how and why am I again a prisoner?"
12091: 
12092: "It is enough that you return straight to the Conciergerie, and will
12093: know to-morrow. You are summoned for to-morrow."
12094: 
12095: Doctor Manette, whom this visitation had so turned into stone, that he
12096: stood with the lamp in his hand, as if he were a statue made to hold it,
12097: moved after these words were spoken, put the lamp down, and confronting
12098: the speaker, and taking him, not ungently, by the loose front of his red
12099: woollen shirt, said:
12100: 
12101: "You know him, you have said. Do you know me?"
12102: 
12103: "Yes, I know you, Citizen Doctor."
12104: 
12105: "We all know you, Citizen Doctor," said the other three.
12106: 
12107: He looked abstractedly from one to another, and said, in a lower voice,
12108: after a pause:
12109: 
12110: "Will you answer his question to me then? How does this happen?"
12111: 
12112: "Citizen Doctor," said the first, reluctantly, "he has been denounced to
12113: the Section of Saint Antoine. This citizen," pointing out the second who
12114: had entered, "is from Saint Antoine."
12115: 
12116: The citizen here indicated nodded his head, and added:
12117: 
12118: "He is accused by Saint Antoine."
12119: 
12120: "Of what?" asked the Doctor.
12121: 
12122: "Citizen Doctor," said the first, with his former reluctance, "ask no
12123: more. If the Republic demands sacrifices from you, without doubt you as
12124: a good patriot will be happy to make them. The Republic goes before all.
12125: The People is supreme. Evremonde, we are pressed."
12126: 
12127: "One word," the Doctor entreated. "Will you tell me who denounced him?"
12128: 
12129: "It is against rule," answered the first; "but you can ask Him of Saint
12130: Antoine here."
12131: 
12132: The Doctor turned his eyes upon that man. Who moved uneasily on his
12133: feet, rubbed his beard a little, and at length said:
12134: 
12135: "Well! Truly it is against rule. But he is denounced--and gravely--by
12136: the Citizen and Citizeness Defarge. And by one other."
12137: 
12138: "What other?"
12139: 
12140: "Do _you_ ask, Citizen Doctor?"
12141: 
12142: "Yes."
12143: 
12144: "Then," said he of Saint Antoine, with a strange look, "you will be
12145: answered to-morrow. Now, I am dumb!"
12146: 
12147: 
12148: 
12149: 
12150: VIII. A Hand at Cards
12151: 
12152: 
12153: Happily unconscious of the new calamity at home, Miss Pross threaded her
12154: way along the narrow streets and crossed the river by the bridge of the
12155: Pont-Neuf, reckoning in her mind the number of indispensable purchases
12156: she had to make. Mr. Cruncher, with the basket, walked at her side. They
12157: both looked to the right and to the left into most of the shops they
12158: passed, had a wary eye for all gregarious assemblages of people, and
12159: turned out of their road to avoid any very excited group of talkers. It
12160: was a raw evening, and the misty river, blurred to the eye with blazing
12161: lights and to the ear with harsh noises, showed where the barges were
12162: stationed in which the smiths worked, making guns for the Army of the
12163: Republic. Woe to the man who played tricks with _that_ Army, or got
12164: undeserved promotion in it! Better for him that his beard had never
12165: grown, for the National Razor shaved him close.
12166: 
12167: Having purchased a few small articles of grocery, and a measure of oil
12168: for the lamp, Miss Pross bethought herself of the wine they wanted.
12169: After peeping into several wine-shops, she stopped at the sign of the
12170: Good Republican Brutus of Antiquity, not far from the National Palace,
12171: once (and twice) the Tuileries, where the aspect of things rather
12172: took her fancy. It had a quieter look than any other place of the same
12173: description they had passed, and, though red with patriotic caps, was
12174: not so red as the rest. Sounding Mr. Cruncher, and finding him of her
12175: opinion, Miss Pross resorted to the Good Republican Brutus of Antiquity,
12176: attended by her cavalier.
12177: 
12178: Slightly observant of the smoky lights; of the people, pipe in mouth,
12179: playing with limp cards and yellow dominoes; of the one bare-breasted,
12180: bare-armed, soot-begrimed workman reading a journal aloud, and of
12181: the others listening to him; of the weapons worn, or laid aside to be
12182: resumed; of the two or three customers fallen forward asleep, who in the
12183: popular high-shouldered shaggy black spencer looked, in that attitude,
12184: like slumbering bears or dogs; the two outlandish customers approached
12185: the counter, and showed what they wanted.
12186: 
12187: As their wine was measuring out, a man parted from another man in a
12188: corner, and rose to depart. In going, he had to face Miss Pross. No
12189: sooner did he face her, than Miss Pross uttered a scream, and clapped
12190: her hands.
12191: 
12192: In a moment, the whole company were on their feet. That somebody was
12193: assassinated by somebody vindicating a difference of opinion was the
12194: likeliest occurrence. Everybody looked to see somebody fall, but only
12195: saw a man and a woman standing staring at each other; the man with all
12196: the outward aspect of a Frenchman and a thorough Republican; the woman,
12197: evidently English.
12198: 
12199: What was said in this disappointing anti-climax, by the disciples of the
12200: Good Republican Brutus of Antiquity, except that it was something very
12201: voluble and loud, would have been as so much Hebrew or Chaldean to Miss
12202: Pross and her protector, though they had been all ears. But, they had no
12203: ears for anything in their surprise. For, it must be recorded, that
12204: not only was Miss Pross lost in amazement and agitation, but,
12205: Mr. Cruncher--though it seemed on his own separate and individual
12206: account--was in a state of the greatest wonder.
12207: 
12208: "What is the matter?" said the man who had caused Miss Pross to scream;
12209: speaking in a vexed, abrupt voice (though in a low tone), and in
12210: English.
12211: 
12212: "Oh, Solomon, dear Solomon!" cried Miss Pross, clapping her hands again.
12213: "After not setting eyes upon you or hearing of you for so long a time,
12214: do I find you here!"
12215: 
12216: "Don't call me Solomon. Do you want to be the death of me?" asked the
12217: man, in a furtive, frightened way.
12218: 
12219: "Brother, brother!" cried Miss Pross, bursting into tears. "Have I ever
12220: been so hard with you that you ask me such a cruel question?"
12221: 
12222: "Then hold your meddlesome tongue," said Solomon, "and come out, if you
12223: want to speak to me. Pay for your wine, and come out. Who's this man?"
12224: 
12225: Miss Pross, shaking her loving and dejected head at her by no means
12226: affectionate brother, said through her tears, "Mr. Cruncher."
12227: 
12228: "Let him come out too," said Solomon. "Does he think me a ghost?"
12229: 
12230: Apparently, Mr. Cruncher did, to judge from his looks. He said not a
12231: word, however, and Miss Pross, exploring the depths of her reticule
12232: through her tears with great difficulty paid for her wine. As she did
12233: so, Solomon turned to the followers of the Good Republican Brutus
12234: of Antiquity, and offered a few words of explanation in the French
12235: language, which caused them all to relapse into their former places and
12236: pursuits.
12237: 
12238: "Now," said Solomon, stopping at the dark street corner, "what do you
12239: want?"
12240: 
12241: "How dreadfully unkind in a brother nothing has ever turned my love away
12242: from!" cried Miss Pross, "to give me such a greeting, and show me no
12243: affection."
12244: 
12245: "There. Confound it! There," said Solomon, making a dab at Miss Pross's
12246: lips with his own. "Now are you content?"
12247: 
12248: Miss Pross only shook her head and wept in silence.
12249: 
12250: "If you expect me to be surprised," said her brother Solomon, "I am not
12251: surprised; I knew you were here; I know of most people who are here. If
12252: you really don't want to endanger my existence--which I half believe you
12253: do--go your ways as soon as possible, and let me go mine. I am busy. I
12254: am an official."
12255: 
12256: "My English brother Solomon," mourned Miss Pross, casting up her
12257: tear-fraught eyes, "that had the makings in him of one of the best and
12258: greatest of men in his native country, an official among foreigners, and
12259: such foreigners! I would almost sooner have seen the dear boy lying in
12260: his--"
12261: 
12262: "I said so!" cried her brother, interrupting. "I knew it. You want to be
12263: the death of me. I shall be rendered Suspected, by my own sister. Just
12264: as I am getting on!"
12265: 
12266: "The gracious and merciful Heavens forbid!" cried Miss Pross. "Far
12267: rather would I never see you again, dear Solomon, though I have ever
12268: loved you truly, and ever shall. Say but one affectionate word to me,
12269: and tell me there is nothing angry or estranged between us, and I will
12270: detain you no longer."
12271: 
12272: Good Miss Pross! As if the estrangement between them had come of any
12273: culpability of hers. As if Mr. Lorry had not known it for a fact, years
12274: ago, in the quiet corner in Soho, that this precious brother had spent
12275: her money and left her!
12276: 
12277: He was saying the affectionate word, however, with a far more grudging
12278: condescension and patronage than he could have shown if their relative
12279: merits and positions had been reversed (which is invariably the case,
12280: all the world over), when Mr. Cruncher, touching him on the shoulder,
12281: hoarsely and unexpectedly interposed with the following singular
12282: question:
12283: 
12284: "I say! Might I ask the favour? As to whether your name is John Solomon,
12285: or Solomon John?"
12286: 
12287: The official turned towards him with sudden distrust. He had not
12288: previously uttered a word.
12289: 
12290: "Come!" said Mr. Cruncher. "Speak out, you know." (Which, by the way,
12291: was more than he could do himself.) "John Solomon, or Solomon John? She
12292: calls you Solomon, and she must know, being your sister. And _I_ know
12293: you're John, you know. Which of the two goes first? And regarding that
12294: name of Pross, likewise. That warn't your name over the water."
12295: 
12296: "What do you mean?"
12297: 
12298: "Well, I don't know all I mean, for I can't call to mind what your name
12299: was, over the water."
12300: 
12301: "No?"
12302: 
12303: "No. But I'll swear it was a name of two syllables."
12304: 
12305: "Indeed?"
12306: 
12307: "Yes. T'other one's was one syllable. I know you. You was a spy--witness
12308: at the Bailey. What, in the name of the Father of Lies, own father to
12309: yourself, was you called at that time?"
12310: 
12311: "Barsad," said another voice, striking in.
12312: 
12313: "That's the name for a thousand pound!" cried Jerry.
12314: 
12316: him under the skirts of his riding-coat, and he stood at Mr. Cruncher's
12317: elbow as negligently as he might have stood at the Old Bailey itself.
12318: 
12319: "Don't be alarmed, my dear Miss Pross. I arrived at Mr. Lorry's, to his
12320: surprise, yesterday evening; we agreed that I would not present myself
12321: elsewhere until all was well, or unless I could be useful; I present
12322: myself here, to beg a little talk with your brother. I wish you had a
12323: better employed brother than Mr. Barsad. I wish for your sake Mr. Barsad
12324: was not a Sheep of the Prisons."
12325: 
12326: Sheep was a cant word of the time for a spy, under the gaolers. The spy,
12327: who was pale, turned paler, and asked him how he dared--
12328: 
12329: "I'll tell you," said Sydney. "I lighted on you, Mr. Barsad, coming out
12330: of the prison of the Conciergerie while I was contemplating the walls,
12331: an hour or more ago. You have a face to be remembered, and I remember
12332: faces well. Made curious by seeing you in that connection, and having
12333: a reason, to which you are no stranger, for associating you with
12334: the misfortunes of a friend now very unfortunate, I walked in your
12335: direction. I walked into the wine-shop here, close after you, and
12336: sat near you. I had no difficulty in deducing from your unreserved
12337: conversation, and the rumour openly going about among your admirers, the
12338: nature of your calling. And gradually, what I had done at random, seemed
12339: to shape itself into a purpose, Mr. Barsad."
12340: 
12341: "What purpose?" the spy asked.
12342: 
12343: "It would be troublesome, and might be dangerous, to explain in the
12344: street. Could you favour me, in confidence, with some minutes of your
12345: company--at the office of Tellson's Bank, for instance?"
12346: 
12347: "Under a threat?"
12348: 
12349: "Oh! Did I say that?"
12350: 
12351: "Then, why should I go there?"
12352: 
12353: "Really, Mr. Barsad, I can't say, if you can't."
12354: 
12355: "Do you mean that you won't say, sir?" the spy irresolutely asked.
12356: 
12357: "You apprehend me very clearly, Mr. Barsad. I won't."
12358: 
12359: Carton's negligent recklessness of manner came powerfully in aid of his
12360: quickness and skill, in such a business as he had in his secret mind,
12361: and with such a man as he had to do with. His practised eye saw it, and
12362: made the most of it.
12363: 
12364: "Now, I told you so," said the spy, casting a reproachful look at his
12365: sister; "if any trouble comes of this, it's your doing."
12366: 
12367: "Come, come, Mr. Barsad!" exclaimed Sydney. "Don't be ungrateful.
12368: But for my great respect for your sister, I might not have led up so
12369: pleasantly to a little proposal that I wish to make for our mutual
12370: satisfaction. Do you go with me to the Bank?"
12371: 
12372: "I'll hear what you have got to say. Yes, I'll go with you."
12373: 
12374: "I propose that we first conduct your sister safely to the corner of her
12375: own street. Let me take your arm, Miss Pross. This is not a good city,
12376: at this time, for you to be out in, unprotected; and as your escort
12377: knows Mr. Barsad, I will invite him to Mr. Lorry's with us. Are we
12378: ready? Come then!"
12379: 
12380: Miss Pross recalled soon afterwards, and to the end of her life
12381: remembered, that as she pressed her hands on Sydney's arm and looked up
12382: in his face, imploring him to do no hurt to Solomon, there was a braced
12383: purpose in the arm and a kind of inspiration in the eyes, which not only
12384: contradicted his light manner, but changed and raised the man. She was
12385: too much occupied then with fears for the brother who so little deserved
12386: her affection, and with Sydney's friendly reassurances, adequately to
12387: heed what she observed.
12388: 
12389: They left her at the corner of the street, and Carton led the way to Mr.
12390: Lorry's, which was within a few minutes' walk. John Barsad, or Solomon
12391: Pross, walked at his side.
12392: 
12393: Mr. Lorry had just finished his dinner, and was sitting before a cheery
12394: little log or two of fire--perhaps looking into their blaze for the
12395: picture of that younger elderly gentleman from Tellson's, who had looked
12396: into the red coals at the Royal George at Dover, now a good many years
12397: ago. He turned his head as they entered, and showed the surprise with
12398: which he saw a stranger.
12399: 
12400: "Miss Pross's brother, sir," said Sydney. "Mr. Barsad."
12401: 
12402: "Barsad?" repeated the old gentleman, "Barsad? I have an association
12403: with the name--and with the face."
12404: 
12405: "I told you you had a remarkable face, Mr. Barsad," observed Carton,
12406: coolly. "Pray sit down."
12407: 
12408: As he took a chair himself, he supplied the link that Mr. Lorry wanted,
12409: by saying to him with a frown, "Witness at that trial." Mr. Lorry
12410: immediately remembered, and regarded his new visitor with an undisguised
12411: look of abhorrence.
12412: 
12413: "Mr. Barsad has been recognised by Miss Pross as the affectionate
12414: brother you have heard of," said Sydney, "and has acknowledged the
12415: relationship. I pass to worse news. Darnay has been arrested again."
12416: 
12417: Struck with consternation, the old gentleman exclaimed, "What do you
12418: tell me! I left him safe and free within these two hours, and am about
12419: to return to him!"
12420: 
12421: "Arrested for all that. When was it done, Mr. Barsad?"
12422: 
12423: "Just now, if at all."
12424: 
12425: "Mr. Barsad is the best authority possible, sir," said Sydney, "and I
12426: have it from Mr. Barsad's communication to a friend and brother Sheep
12427: over a bottle of wine, that the arrest has taken place. He left the
12428: messengers at the gate, and saw them admitted by the porter. There is no
12429: earthly doubt that he is retaken."
12430: 
12431: Mr. Lorry's business eye read in the speaker's face that it was loss
12432: of time to dwell upon the point. Confused, but sensible that something
12433: might depend on his presence of mind, he commanded himself, and was
12434: silently attentive.
12435: 
12436: "Now, I trust," said Sydney to him, "that the name and influence of
12437: Doctor Manette may stand him in as good stead to-morrow--you said he
12438: would be before the Tribunal again to-morrow, Mr. Barsad?--"
12439: 
12440: "Yes; I believe so."
12441: 
12442: "--In as good stead to-morrow as to-day. But it may not be so. I own
12443: to you, I am shaken, Mr. Lorry, by Doctor Manette's not having had the
12444: power to prevent this arrest."
12445: 
12446: "He may not have known of it beforehand," said Mr. Lorry.
12447: 
12448: "But that very circumstance would be alarming, when we remember how
12449: identified he is with his son-in-law."
12450: 
12451: "That's true," Mr. Lorry acknowledged, with his troubled hand at his
12452: chin, and his troubled eyes on Carton.
12453: 
12454: "In short," said Sydney, "this is a desperate time, when desperate games
12455: are played for desperate stakes. Let the Doctor play the winning game; I
12456: will play the losing one. No man's life here is worth purchase. Any one
12457: carried home by the people to-day, may be condemned tomorrow. Now, the
12458: stake I have resolved to play for, in case of the worst, is a friend
12459: in the Conciergerie. And the friend I purpose to myself to win, is Mr.
12460: Barsad."
12461: 
12462: "You need have good cards, sir," said the spy.
12463: 
12464: "I'll run them over. I'll see what I hold,--Mr. Lorry, you know what a
12465: brute I am; I wish you'd give me a little brandy."
12466: 
12467: It was put before him, and he drank off a glassful--drank off another
12468: glassful--pushed the bottle thoughtfully away.
12469: 
12470: "Mr. Barsad," he went on, in the tone of one who really was looking
12471: over a hand at cards: "Sheep of the prisons, emissary of Republican
12472: committees, now turnkey, now prisoner, always spy and secret informer,
12473: so much the more valuable here for being English that an Englishman
12474: is less open to suspicion of subornation in those characters than a
12475: Frenchman, represents himself to his employers under a false name.
12476: That's a very good card. Mr. Barsad, now in the employ of the republican
12477: French government, was formerly in the employ of the aristocratic
12478: English government, the enemy of France and freedom. That's an excellent
12479: card. Inference clear as day in this region of suspicion, that Mr.
12480: Barsad, still in the pay of the aristocratic English government, is the
12481: spy of Pitt, the treacherous foe of the Republic crouching in its bosom,
12482: the English traitor and agent of all mischief so much spoken of and so
12483: difficult to find. That's a card not to be beaten. Have you followed my
12484: hand, Mr. Barsad?"
12485: 
12486: "Not to understand your play," returned the spy, somewhat uneasily.
12487: 
12488: "I play my Ace, Denunciation of Mr. Barsad to the nearest Section
12489: Committee. Look over your hand, Mr. Barsad, and see what you have. Don't
12490: hurry."
12491: 
12492: He drew the bottle near, poured out another glassful of brandy, and
12493: drank it off. He saw that the spy was fearful of his drinking himself
12494: into a fit state for the immediate denunciation of him. Seeing it, he
12495: poured out and drank another glassful.
12496: 
12497: "Look over your hand carefully, Mr. Barsad. Take time."
12498: 
12499: It was a poorer hand than he suspected. Mr. Barsad saw losing cards
12501: employment in England, through too much unsuccessful hard swearing
12502: there--not because he was not wanted there; our English reasons for
12503: vaunting our superiority to secrecy and spies are of very modern
12504: date--he knew that he had crossed the Channel, and accepted service in
12505: France: first, as a tempter and an eavesdropper among his own countrymen
12506: there: gradually, as a tempter and an eavesdropper among the natives. He
12507: knew that under the overthrown government he had been a spy upon Saint
12508: Antoine and Defarge's wine-shop; had received from the watchful police
12509: such heads of information concerning Doctor Manette's imprisonment,
12510: release, and history, as should serve him for an introduction to
12511: familiar conversation with the Defarges; and tried them on Madame
12512: Defarge, and had broken down with them signally. He always remembered
12513: with fear and trembling, that that terrible woman had knitted when he
12514: talked with her, and had looked ominously at him as her fingers moved.
12515: He had since seen her, in the Section of Saint Antoine, over and over
12516: again produce her knitted registers, and denounce people whose lives the
12517: guillotine then surely swallowed up. He knew, as every one employed as
12518: he was did, that he was never safe; that flight was impossible; that
12519: he was tied fast under the shadow of the axe; and that in spite of
12520: his utmost tergiversation and treachery in furtherance of the reigning
12521: terror, a word might bring it down upon him. Once denounced, and on such
12522: grave grounds as had just now been suggested to his mind, he foresaw
12523: that the dreadful woman of whose unrelenting character he had seen many
12524: proofs, would produce against him that fatal register, and would quash
12525: his last chance of life. Besides that all secret men are men soon
12526: terrified, here were surely cards enough of one black suit, to justify
12527: the holder in growing rather livid as he turned them over.
12528: 
12529: "You scarcely seem to like your hand," said Sydney, with the greatest
12530: composure. "Do you play?"
12531: 
12532: "I think, sir," said the spy, in the meanest manner, as he turned to Mr.
12533: Lorry, "I may appeal to a gentleman of your years and benevolence, to
12534: put it to this other gentleman, so much your junior, whether he can
12535: under any circumstances reconcile it to his station to play that Ace
12536: of which he has spoken. I admit that _I_ am a spy, and that it is
12537: considered a discreditable station--though it must be filled by
12538: somebody; but this gentleman is no spy, and why should he so demean
12539: himself as to make himself one?"
12540: 
12541: "I play my Ace, Mr. Barsad," said Carton, taking the answer on himself,
12542: and looking at his watch, "without any scruple, in a very few minutes."
12543: 
12544: "I should have hoped, gentlemen both," said the spy, always striving to
12545: hook Mr. Lorry into the discussion, "that your respect for my sister--"
12546: 
12547: "I could not better testify my respect for your sister than by finally
12549: 
12550: "You think not, sir?"
12551: 
12552: "I have thoroughly made up my mind about it."
12553: 
12554: The smooth manner of the spy, curiously in dissonance with his
12555: ostentatiously rough dress, and probably with his usual demeanour,
12556: received such a check from the inscrutability of Carton,--who was a
12557: mystery to wiser and honester men than he,--that it faltered here and
12558: failed him. While he was at a loss, Carton said, resuming his former air
12559: of contemplating cards:
12560: 
12561: "And indeed, now I think again, I have a strong impression that I
12562: have another good card here, not yet enumerated. That friend and
12563: fellow-Sheep, who spoke of himself as pasturing in the country prisons;
12564: who was he?"
12565: 
12566: "French. You don't know him," said the spy, quickly.
12567: 
12568: "French, eh?" repeated Carton, musing, and not appearing to notice him
12569: at all, though he echoed his word. "Well; he may be."
12570: 
12571: "Is, I assure you," said the spy; "though it's not important."
12572: 
12573: "Though it's not important," repeated Carton, in the same mechanical
12574: way--"though it's not important--No, it's not important. No. Yet I know
12575: the face."
12576: 
12577: "I think not. I am sure not. It can't be," said the spy.
12578: 
12580: glass (which fortunately was a small one) again. "Can't-be. Spoke good
12581: French. Yet like a foreigner, I thought?"
12582: 
12583: "Provincial," said the spy.
12584: 
12585: "No. Foreign!" cried Carton, striking his open hand on the table, as a
12586: light broke clearly on his mind. "Cly! Disguised, but the same man. We
12587: had that man before us at the Old Bailey."
12588: 
12589: "Now, there you are hasty, sir," said Barsad, with a smile that gave his
12590: aquiline nose an extra inclination to one side; "there you really give
12591: me an advantage over you. Cly (who I will unreservedly admit, at this
12592: distance of time, was a partner of mine) has been dead several years. I
12593: attended him in his last illness. He was buried in London, at the church
12594: of Saint Pancras-in-the-Fields. His unpopularity with the blackguard
12595: multitude at the moment prevented my following his remains, but I helped
12596: to lay him in his coffin."
12597: 
12598: Here, Mr. Lorry became aware, from where he sat, of a most remarkable
12599: goblin shadow on the wall. Tracing it to its source, he discovered it
12600: to be caused by a sudden extraordinary rising and stiffening of all the
12601: risen and stiff hair on Mr. Cruncher's head.
12602: 
12603: "Let us be reasonable," said the spy, "and let us be fair. To show you
12604: how mistaken you are, and what an unfounded assumption yours is, I will
12605: lay before you a certificate of Cly's burial, which I happened to have
12606: carried in my pocket-book," with a hurried hand he produced and opened
12607: it, "ever since. There it is. Oh, look at it, look at it! You may take
12608: it in your hand; it's no forgery."
12609: 
12610: Here, Mr. Lorry perceived the reflection on the wall to elongate, and
12611: Mr. Cruncher rose and stepped forward. His hair could not have been more
12612: violently on end, if it had been that moment dressed by the Cow with the
12613: crumpled horn in the house that Jack built.
12614: 
12615: Unseen by the spy, Mr. Cruncher stood at his side, and touched him on
12616: the shoulder like a ghostly bailiff.
12617: 
12618: "That there Roger Cly, master," said Mr. Cruncher, with a taciturn and
12619: iron-bound visage. "So _you_ put him in his coffin?"
12620: 
12621: "I did."
12622: 
12623: "Who took him out of it?"
12624: 
12625: Barsad leaned back in his chair, and stammered, "What do you mean?"
12626: 
12627: "I mean," said Mr. Cruncher, "that he warn't never in it. No! Not he!
12628: I'll have my head took off, if he was ever in it."
12629: 
12630: The spy looked round at the two gentlemen; they both looked in
12631: unspeakable astonishment at Jerry.
12632: 
12633: "I tell you," said Jerry, "that you buried paving-stones and earth in
12634: that there coffin. Don't go and tell me that you buried Cly. It was a
12635: take in. Me and two more knows it."
12636: 
12637: "How do you know it?"
12638: 
12639: "What's that to you? Ecod!" growled Mr. Cruncher, "it's you I have got a
12640: old grudge again, is it, with your shameful impositions upon tradesmen!
12641: I'd catch hold of your throat and choke you for half a guinea."
12642: 
12643: Sydney Carton, who, with Mr. Lorry, had been lost in amazement at
12644: this turn of the business, here requested Mr. Cruncher to moderate and
12645: explain himself.
12646: 
12647: "At another time, sir," he returned, evasively, "the present time is
12648: ill-conwenient for explainin'. What I stand to, is, that he knows well
12649: wot that there Cly was never in that there coffin. Let him say he was,
12650: in so much as a word of one syllable, and I'll either catch hold of his
12651: throat and choke him for half a guinea;" Mr. Cruncher dwelt upon this as
12652: quite a liberal offer; "or I'll out and announce him."
12653: 
12654: "Humph! I see one thing," said Carton. "I hold another card, Mr. Barsad.
12655: Impossible, here in raging Paris, with Suspicion filling the air, for
12656: you to outlive denunciation, when you are in communication with another
12657: aristocratic spy of the same antecedents as yourself, who, moreover, has
12658: the mystery about him of having feigned death and come to life again!
12659: A plot in the prisons, of the foreigner against the Republic. A strong
12660: card--a certain Guillotine card! Do you play?"
12661: 
12662: "No!" returned the spy. "I throw up. I confess that we were so unpopular
12663: with the outrageous mob, that I only got away from England at the risk
12664: of being ducked to death, and that Cly was so ferreted up and down, that
12665: he never would have got away at all but for that sham. Though how this
12666: man knows it was a sham, is a wonder of wonders to me."
12667: 
12668: "Never you trouble your head about this man," retorted the contentious
12669: Mr. Cruncher; "you'll have trouble enough with giving your attention to
12670: that gentleman. And look here! Once more!"--Mr. Cruncher could not
12671: be restrained from making rather an ostentatious parade of his
12672: liberality--"I'd catch hold of your throat and choke you for half a
12673: guinea."
12674: 
12676: with more decision, "It has come to a point. I go on duty soon, and
12677: can't overstay my time. You told me you had a proposal; what is it?
12678: Now, it is of no use asking too much of me. Ask me to do anything in my
12679: office, putting my head in great extra danger, and I had better trust my
12680: life to the chances of a refusal than the chances of consent. In short,
12681: I should make that choice. You talk of desperation. We are all desperate
12682: here. Remember! I may denounce you if I think proper, and I can swear my
12683: way through stone walls, and so can others. Now, what do you want with
12684: me?"
12685: 
12686: "Not very much. You are a turnkey at the Conciergerie?"
12687: 
12688: "I tell you once for all, there is no such thing as an escape possible,"
12689: said the spy, firmly.
12690: 
12691: "Why need you tell me what I have not asked? You are a turnkey at the
12692: Conciergerie?"
12693: 
12694: "I am sometimes."
12695: 
12696: "You can be when you choose?"
12697: 
12698: "I can pass in and out when I choose."
12699: 
12700: Sydney Carton filled another glass with brandy, poured it slowly out
12701: upon the hearth, and watched it as it dropped. It being all spent, he
12702: said, rising:
12703: 
12704: "So far, we have spoken before these two, because it was as well that
12705: the merits of the cards should not rest solely between you and me. Come
12706: into the dark room here, and let us have one final word alone."
12707: 
12708: 
12709: 
12710: 
12711: IX. The Game Made
12712: 
12713: 
12715: dark room, speaking so low that not a sound was heard, Mr. Lorry looked
12716: at Jerry in considerable doubt and mistrust. That honest tradesman's
12717: manner of receiving the look, did not inspire confidence; he changed the
12718: leg on which he rested, as often as if he had fifty of those limbs,
12719: and were trying them all; he examined his finger-nails with a very
12720: questionable closeness of attention; and whenever Mr. Lorry's eye caught
12721: his, he was taken with that peculiar kind of short cough requiring the
12722: hollow of a hand before it, which is seldom, if ever, known to be an
12723: infirmity attendant on perfect openness of character.
12724: 
12725: "Jerry," said Mr. Lorry. "Come here."
12726: 
12727: Mr. Cruncher came forward sideways, with one of his shoulders in advance
12728: of him.
12729: 
12730: "What have you been, besides a messenger?"
12731: 
12732: After some cogitation, accompanied with an intent look at his patron,
12733: Mr. Cruncher conceived the luminous idea of replying, "Agicultooral
12734: character."
12735: 
12736: "My mind misgives me much," said Mr. Lorry, angrily shaking a forefinger
12737: at him, "that you have used the respectable and great house of Tellson's
12738: as a blind, and that you have had an unlawful occupation of an infamous
12739: description. If you have, don't expect me to befriend you when you
12740: get back to England. If you have, don't expect me to keep your secret.
12741: Tellson's shall not be imposed upon."
12742: 
12743: "I hope, sir," pleaded the abashed Mr. Cruncher, "that a gentleman like
12744: yourself wot I've had the honour of odd jobbing till I'm grey at it,
12745: would think twice about harming of me, even if it wos so--I don't say it
12746: is, but even if it wos. And which it is to be took into account that if
12747: it wos, it wouldn't, even then, be all o' one side. There'd be two sides
12748: to it. There might be medical doctors at the present hour, a picking
12749: up their guineas where a honest tradesman don't pick up his
12750: fardens--fardens! no, nor yet his half fardens--half fardens! no, nor
12751: yet his quarter--a banking away like smoke at Tellson's, and a cocking
12752: their medical eyes at that tradesman on the sly, a going in and going
12753: out to their own carriages--ah! equally like smoke, if not more so.
12754: Well, that 'ud be imposing, too, on Tellson's. For you cannot sarse the
12755: goose and not the gander. And here's Mrs. Cruncher, or leastways wos
12756: in the Old England times, and would be to-morrow, if cause given,
12757: a floppin' again the business to that degree as is ruinating--stark
12758: ruinating! Whereas them medical doctors' wives don't flop--catch 'em at
12759: it! Or, if they flop, their floppings goes in favour of more patients,
12760: and how can you rightly have one without t'other? Then, wot with
12761: undertakers, and wot with parish clerks, and wot with sextons, and wot
12762: with private watchmen (all awaricious and all in it), a man wouldn't get
12763: much by it, even if it wos so. And wot little a man did get, would never
12764: prosper with him, Mr. Lorry. He'd never have no good of it; he'd want
12765: all along to be out of the line, if he, could see his way out, being
12766: once in--even if it wos so."
12767: 
12768: "Ugh!" cried Mr. Lorry, rather relenting, nevertheless, "I am shocked at
12769: the sight of you."
12770: 
12771: "Now, what I would humbly offer to you, sir," pursued Mr. Cruncher,
12772: "even if it wos so, which I don't say it is--"
12773: 
12774: "Don't prevaricate," said Mr. Lorry.
12775: 
12776: "No, I will _not_, sir," returned Mr. Crunches as if nothing were
12777: further from his thoughts or practice--"which I don't say it is--wot I
12778: would humbly offer to you, sir, would be this. Upon that there stool, at
12779: that there Bar, sets that there boy of mine, brought up and growed up to
12780: be a man, wot will errand you, message you, general-light-job you, till
12781: your heels is where your head is, if such should be your wishes. If it
12782: wos so, which I still don't say it is (for I will not prewaricate to
12783: you, sir), let that there boy keep his father's place, and take care of
12784: his mother; don't blow upon that boy's father--do not do it, sir--and
12785: let that father go into the line of the reg'lar diggin', and make amends
12786: for what he would have undug--if it wos so--by diggin' of 'em in with
12787: a will, and with conwictions respectin' the futur' keepin' of 'em safe.
12788: That, Mr. Lorry," said Mr. Cruncher, wiping his forehead with his
12789: arm, as an announcement that he had arrived at the peroration of his
12790: discourse, "is wot I would respectfully offer to you, sir. A man don't
12791: see all this here a goin' on dreadful round him, in the way of Subjects
12792: without heads, dear me, plentiful enough fur to bring the price down
12793: to porterage and hardly that, without havin' his serious thoughts of
12794: things. And these here would be mine, if it wos so, entreatin' of you
12795: fur to bear in mind that wot I said just now, I up and said in the good
12796: cause when I might have kep' it back."
12797: 
12798: "That at least is true," said Mr. Lorry. "Say no more now. It may be
12799: that I shall yet stand your friend, if you deserve it, and repent in
12800: action--not in words. I want no more words."
12801: 
12803: returned from the dark room. "Adieu, Mr. Barsad," said the former; "our
12804: arrangement thus made, you have nothing to fear from me."
12805: 
12806: He sat down in a chair on the hearth, over against Mr. Lorry. When they
12807: were alone, Mr. Lorry asked him what he had done?
12808: 
12809: "Not much. If it should go ill with the prisoner, I have ensured access
12810: to him, once."
12811: 
12812: Mr. Lorry's countenance fell.
12813: 
12814: "It is all I could do," said Carton. "To propose too much, would be
12815: to put this man's head under the axe, and, as he himself said, nothing
12816: worse could happen to him if he were denounced. It was obviously the
12817: weakness of the position. There is no help for it."
12818: 
12819: "But access to him," said Mr. Lorry, "if it should go ill before the
12820: Tribunal, will not save him."
12821: 
12822: "I never said it would."
12823: 
12824: Mr. Lorry's eyes gradually sought the fire; his sympathy with his
12825: darling, and the heavy disappointment of his second arrest, gradually
12826: weakened them; he was an old man now, overborne with anxiety of late,
12827: and his tears fell.
12828: 
12829: "You are a good man and a true friend," said Carton, in an altered
12830: voice. "Forgive me if I notice that you are affected. I could not see my
12831: father weep, and sit by, careless. And I could not respect your
12832: sorrow more, if you were my father. You are free from that misfortune,
12833: however."
12834: 
12835: Though he said the last words, with a slip into his usual manner, there
12836: was a true feeling and respect both in his tone and in his touch,
12837: that Mr. Lorry, who had never seen the better side of him, was wholly
12838: unprepared for. He gave him his hand, and Carton gently pressed it.
12839: 
12840: "To return to poor Darnay," said Carton. "Don't tell Her of this
12841: interview, or this arrangement. It would not enable Her to go to see
12842: him. She might think it was contrived, in case of the worse, to convey
12843: to him the means of anticipating the sentence."
12844: 
12845: Mr. Lorry had not thought of that, and he looked quickly at Carton to
12846: see if it were in his mind. It seemed to be; he returned the look, and
12847: evidently understood it.
12848: 
12849: "She might think a thousand things," Carton said, "and any of them would
12850: only add to her trouble. Don't speak of me to her. As I said to you when
12851: I first came, I had better not see her. I can put my hand out, to do any
12852: little helpful work for her that my hand can find to do, without that.
12853: You are going to her, I hope? She must be very desolate to-night."
12854: 
12855: "I am going now, directly."
12856: 
12857: "I am glad of that. She has such a strong attachment to you and reliance
12858: on you. How does she look?"
12859: 
12860: "Anxious and unhappy, but very beautiful."
12861: 
12862: "Ah!"
12863: 
12864: It was a long, grieving sound, like a sigh--almost like a sob. It
12865: attracted Mr. Lorry's eyes to Carton's face, which was turned to the
12866: fire. A light, or a shade (the old gentleman could not have said which),
12867: passed from it as swiftly as a change will sweep over a hill-side on a
12868: wild bright day, and he lifted his foot to put back one of the little
12869: flaming logs, which was tumbling forward. He wore the white riding-coat
12870: and top-boots, then in vogue, and the light of the fire touching their
12871: light surfaces made him look very pale, with his long brown hair,
12872: all untrimmed, hanging loose about him. His indifference to fire was
12873: sufficiently remarkable to elicit a word of remonstrance from Mr. Lorry;
12874: his boot was still upon the hot embers of the flaming log, when it had
12875: broken under the weight of his foot.
12876: 
12877: "I forgot it," he said.
12878: 
12879: Mr. Lorry's eyes were again attracted to his face. Taking note of the
12880: wasted air which clouded the naturally handsome features, and having
12881: the expression of prisoners' faces fresh in his mind, he was strongly
12882: reminded of that expression.
12883: 
12884: "And your duties here have drawn to an end, sir?" said Carton, turning
12885: to him.
12886: 
12887: "Yes. As I was telling you last night when Lucie came in so
12888: unexpectedly, I have at length done all that I can do here. I hoped to
12889: have left them in perfect safety, and then to have quitted Paris. I have
12890: my Leave to Pass. I was ready to go."
12891: 
12892: They were both silent.
12893: 
12894: "Yours is a long life to look back upon, sir?" said Carton, wistfully.
12895: 
12896: "I am in my seventy-eighth year."
12897: 
12898: "You have been useful all your life; steadily and constantly occupied;
12899: trusted, respected, and looked up to?"
12900: 
12901: "I have been a man of business, ever since I have been a man. Indeed, I
12902: may say that I was a man of business when a boy."
12903: 
12904: "See what a place you fill at seventy-eight. How many people will miss
12905: you when you leave it empty!"
12906: 
12907: "A solitary old bachelor," answered Mr. Lorry, shaking his head. "There
12908: is nobody to weep for me."
12909: 
12910: "How can you say that? Wouldn't She weep for you? Wouldn't her child?"
12911: 
12912: "Yes, yes, thank God. I didn't quite mean what I said."
12913: 
12914: "It _is_ a thing to thank God for; is it not?"
12915: 
12916: "Surely, surely."
12917: 
12918: "If you could say, with truth, to your own solitary heart, to-night,
12919: 'I have secured to myself the love and attachment, the gratitude or
12920: respect, of no human creature; I have won myself a tender place in no
12921: regard; I have done nothing good or serviceable to be remembered by!'
12922: your seventy-eight years would be seventy-eight heavy curses; would they
12923: not?"
12924: 
12925: "You say truly, Mr. Carton; I think they would be."
12926: 
12927: Sydney turned his eyes again upon the fire, and, after a silence of a
12928: few moments, said:
12929: 
12930: "I should like to ask you:--Does your childhood seem far off? Do the
12931: days when you sat at your mother's knee, seem days of very long ago?"
12932: 
12933: Responding to his softened manner, Mr. Lorry answered:
12934: 
12935: "Twenty years back, yes; at this time of my life, no. For, as I draw
12936: closer and closer to the end, I travel in the circle, nearer and
12937: nearer to the beginning. It seems to be one of the kind smoothings and
12938: preparings of the way. My heart is touched now, by many remembrances
12939: that had long fallen asleep, of my pretty young mother (and I so old!),
12940: and by many associations of the days when what we call the World was not
12941: so real with me, and my faults were not confirmed in me."
12942: 
12943: "I understand the feeling!" exclaimed Carton, with a bright flush. "And
12944: you are the better for it?"
12945: 
12946: "I hope so."
12947: 
12948: Carton terminated the conversation here, by rising to help him on with
12949: his outer coat; "But you," said Mr. Lorry, reverting to the theme, "you
12950: are young."
12951: 
12952: "Yes," said Carton. "I am not old, but my young way was never the way to
12953: age. Enough of me."
12954: 
12955: "And of me, I am sure," said Mr. Lorry. "Are you going out?"
12956: 
12957: "I'll walk with you to her gate. You know my vagabond and restless
12958: habits. If I should prowl about the streets a long time, don't be
12959: uneasy; I shall reappear in the morning. You go to the Court to-morrow?"
12960: 
12961: "Yes, unhappily."
12962: 
12963: "I shall be there, but only as one of the crowd. My Spy will find a
12964: place for me. Take my arm, sir."
12965: 
12966: Mr. Lorry did so, and they went down-stairs and out in the streets. A
12967: few minutes brought them to Mr. Lorry's destination. Carton left him
12968: there; but lingered at a little distance, and turned back to the gate
12969: again when it was shut, and touched it. He had heard of her going to
12970: the prison every day. "She came out here," he said, looking about him,
12971: "turned this way, must have trod on these stones often. Let me follow in
12972: her steps."
12973: 
12974: It was ten o'clock at night when he stood before the prison of La Force,
12975: where she had stood hundreds of times. A little wood-sawyer, having
12976: closed his shop, was smoking his pipe at his shop-door.
12977: 
12979: man eyed him inquisitively.
12980: 
12981: "Good night, citizen."
12982: 
12983: "How goes the Republic?"
12984: 
12985: "You mean the Guillotine. Not ill. Sixty-three to-day. We shall mount
12986: to a hundred soon. Samson and his men complain sometimes, of being
12987: exhausted. Ha, ha, ha! He is so droll, that Samson. Such a Barber!"
12988: 
12989: "Do you often go to see him--"
12990: 
12991: "Shave? Always. Every day. What a barber! You have seen him at work?"
12992: 
12993: "Never."
12994: 
12995: "Go and see him when he has a good batch. Figure this to yourself,
12996: citizen; he shaved the sixty-three to-day, in less than two pipes! Less
12997: than two pipes. Word of honour!"
12998: 
12999: As the grinning little man held out the pipe he was smoking, to explain
13000: how he timed the executioner, Carton was so sensible of a rising desire
13001: to strike the life out of him, that he turned away.
13002: 
13003: "But you are not English," said the wood-sawyer, "though you wear
13004: English dress?"
13005: 
13006: "Yes," said Carton, pausing again, and answering over his shoulder.
13007: 
13008: "You speak like a Frenchman."
13009: 
13010: "I am an old student here."
13011: 
13012: "Aha, a perfect Frenchman! Good night, Englishman."
13013: 
13014: "Good night, citizen."
13015: 
13016: "But go and see that droll dog," the little man persisted, calling after
13017: him. "And take a pipe with you!"
13018: 
13019: Sydney had not gone far out of sight, when he stopped in the middle of
13020: the street under a glimmering lamp, and wrote with his pencil on a scrap
13021: of paper. Then, traversing with the decided step of one who remembered
13022: the way well, several dark and dirty streets--much dirtier than usual,
13023: for the best public thoroughfares remained uncleansed in those times of
13024: terror--he stopped at a chemist's shop, which the owner was closing with
13025: his own hands. A small, dim, crooked shop, kept in a tortuous, up-hill
13026: thoroughfare, by a small, dim, crooked man.
13027: 
13028: Giving this citizen, too, good night, as he confronted him at his
13029: counter, he laid the scrap of paper before him. "Whew!" the chemist
13030: whistled softly, as he read it. "Hi! hi! hi!"
13031: 
13032: Sydney Carton took no heed, and the chemist said:
13033: 
13034: "For you, citizen?"
13035: 
13036: "For me."
13037: 
13038: "You will be careful to keep them separate, citizen? You know the
13039: consequences of mixing them?"
13040: 
13041: "Perfectly."
13042: 
13043: Certain small packets were made and given to him. He put them, one by
13044: one, in the breast of his inner coat, counted out the money for them,
13045: and deliberately left the shop. "There is nothing more to do," said he,
13046: glancing upward at the moon, "until to-morrow. I can't sleep."
13047: 
13048: It was not a reckless manner, the manner in which he said these words
13049: aloud under the fast-sailing clouds, nor was it more expressive of
13050: negligence than defiance. It was the settled manner of a tired man, who
13051: had wandered and struggled and got lost, but who at length struck into
13052: his road and saw its end.
13053: 
13054: Long ago, when he had been famous among his earliest competitors as a
13055: youth of great promise, he had followed his father to the grave. His
13056: mother had died, years before. These solemn words, which had been
13057: read at his father's grave, arose in his mind as he went down the dark
13058: streets, among the heavy shadows, with the moon and the clouds sailing
13059: on high above him. "I am the resurrection and the life, saith the Lord:
13060: he that believeth in me, though he were dead, yet shall he live: and
13061: whosoever liveth and believeth in me, shall never die."
13062: 
13063: In a city dominated by the axe, alone at night, with natural sorrow
13064: rising in him for the sixty-three who had been that day put to death,
13065: and for to-morrow's victims then awaiting their doom in the prisons,
13066: and still of to-morrow's and to-morrow's, the chain of association that
13067: brought the words home, like a rusty old ship's anchor from the deep,
13068: might have been easily found. He did not seek it, but repeated them and
13069: went on.
13070: 
13071: With a solemn interest in the lighted windows where the people were
13072: going to rest, forgetful through a few calm hours of the horrors
13073: surrounding them; in the towers of the churches, where no prayers
13074: were said, for the popular revulsion had even travelled that length
13075: of self-destruction from years of priestly impostors, plunderers, and
13076: profligates; in the distant burial-places, reserved, as they wrote upon
13077: the gates, for Eternal Sleep; in the abounding gaols; and in the streets
13078: along which the sixties rolled to a death which had become so common and
13079: material, that no sorrowful story of a haunting Spirit ever arose among
13080: the people out of all the working of the Guillotine; with a solemn
13081: interest in the whole life and death of the city settling down to its
13083: the lighter streets.
13084: 
13085: Few coaches were abroad, for riders in coaches were liable to be
13086: suspected, and gentility hid its head in red nightcaps, and put on heavy
13087: shoes, and trudged. But, the theatres were all well filled, and the
13088: people poured cheerfully out as he passed, and went chatting home. At
13089: one of the theatre doors, there was a little girl with a mother, looking
13090: for a way across the street through the mud. He carried the child over,
13091: and before the timid arm was loosed from his neck asked her for a kiss.
13092: 
13093: "I am the resurrection and the life, saith the Lord: he that believeth
13094: in me, though he were dead, yet shall he live: and whosoever liveth and
13095: believeth in me, shall never die."
13096: 
13097: Now, that the streets were quiet, and the night wore on, the words
13098: were in the echoes of his feet, and were in the air. Perfectly calm
13099: and steady, he sometimes repeated them to himself as he walked; but, he
13100: heard them always.
13101: 
13102: The night wore out, and, as he stood upon the bridge listening to the
13103: water as it splashed the river-walls of the Island of Paris, where the
13104: picturesque confusion of houses and cathedral shone bright in the light
13105: of the moon, the day came coldly, looking like a dead face out of the
13106: sky. Then, the night, with the moon and the stars, turned pale and died,
13107: and for a little while it seemed as if Creation were delivered over to
13108: Death's dominion.
13109: 
13110: But, the glorious sun, rising, seemed to strike those words, that burden
13111: of the night, straight and warm to his heart in its long bright rays.
13112: And looking along them, with reverently shaded eyes, a bridge of light
13113: appeared to span the air between him and the sun, while the river
13114: sparkled under it.
13115: 
13116: The strong tide, so swift, so deep, and certain, was like a congenial
13117: friend, in the morning stillness. He walked by the stream, far from the
13118: houses, and in the light and warmth of the sun fell asleep on the
13119: bank. When he awoke and was afoot again, he lingered there yet a little
13120: longer, watching an eddy that turned and turned purposeless, until the
13121: stream absorbed it, and carried it on to the sea.--"Like me."
13122: 
13123: A trading-boat, with a sail of the softened colour of a dead leaf, then
13124: glided into his view, floated by him, and died away. As its silent track
13125: in the water disappeared, the prayer that had broken up out of his heart
13126: for a merciful consideration of all his poor blindnesses and errors,
13127: ended in the words, "I am the resurrection and the life."
13128: 
13129: Mr. Lorry was already out when he got back, and it was easy to surmise
13131: little coffee, ate some bread, and, having washed and changed to refresh
13132: himself, went out to the place of trial.
13133: 
13134: The court was all astir and a-buzz, when the black sheep--whom many fell
13135: away from in dread--pressed him into an obscure corner among the crowd.
13136: Mr. Lorry was there, and Doctor Manette was there. She was there,
13137: sitting beside her father.
13138: 
13139: When her husband was brought in, she turned a look upon him, so
13140: sustaining, so encouraging, so full of admiring love and pitying
13141: tenderness, yet so courageous for his sake, that it called the healthy
13142: blood into his face, brightened his glance, and animated his heart. If
13143: there had been any eyes to notice the influence of her look, on Sydney
13144: Carton, it would have been seen to be the same influence exactly.
13145: 
13146: Before that unjust Tribunal, there was little or no order of procedure,
13147: ensuring to any accused person any reasonable hearing. There could have
13148: been no such Revolution, if all laws, forms, and ceremonies, had not
13149: first been so monstrously abused, that the suicidal vengeance of the
13150: Revolution was to scatter them all to the winds.
13151: 
13152: Every eye was turned to the jury. The same determined patriots and good
13153: republicans as yesterday and the day before, and to-morrow and the day
13154: after. Eager and prominent among them, one man with a craving face, and
13155: his fingers perpetually hovering about his lips, whose appearance
13156: gave great satisfaction to the spectators. A life-thirsting,
13157: cannibal-looking, bloody-minded juryman, the Jacques Three of St.
13158: Antoine. The whole jury, as a jury of dogs empannelled to try the deer.
13159: 
13160: Every eye then turned to the five judges and the public prosecutor.
13161: No favourable leaning in that quarter to-day. A fell, uncompromising,
13162: murderous business-meaning there. Every eye then sought some other eye
13163: in the crowd, and gleamed at it approvingly; and heads nodded at one
13164: another, before bending forward with a strained attention.
13165: 
13166: Charles Evremonde, called Darnay. Released yesterday. Reaccused and
13167: retaken yesterday. Indictment delivered to him last night. Suspected and
13168: Denounced enemy of the Republic, Aristocrat, one of a family of tyrants,
13169: one of a race proscribed, for that they had used their abolished
13170: privileges to the infamous oppression of the people. Charles Evremonde,
13171: called Darnay, in right of such proscription, absolutely Dead in Law.
13172: 
13173: To this effect, in as few or fewer words, the Public Prosecutor.
13174: 
13175: The President asked, was the Accused openly denounced or secretly?
13176: 
13177: "Openly, President."
13178: 
13179: "By whom?"
13180: 
13181: "Three voices. Ernest Defarge, wine-vendor of St. Antoine."
13182: 
13183: "Good."
13184: 
13185: "Therese Defarge, his wife."
13186: 
13187: "Good."
13188: 
13189: "Alexandre Manette, physician."
13190: 
13191: A great uproar took place in the court, and in the midst of it, Doctor
13192: Manette was seen, pale and trembling, standing where he had been seated.
13193: 
13194: "President, I indignantly protest to you that this is a forgery and
13195: a fraud. You know the accused to be the husband of my daughter. My
13196: daughter, and those dear to her, are far dearer to me than my life. Who
13197: and where is the false conspirator who says that I denounce the husband
13198: of my child!"
13199: 
13200: "Citizen Manette, be tranquil. To fail in submission to the authority of
13201: the Tribunal would be to put yourself out of Law. As to what is dearer
13202: to you than life, nothing can be so dear to a good citizen as the
13203: Republic."
13204: 
13205: Loud acclamations hailed this rebuke. The President rang his bell, and
13206: with warmth resumed.
13207: 
13208: "If the Republic should demand of you the sacrifice of your child
13209: herself, you would have no duty but to sacrifice her. Listen to what is
13210: to follow. In the meanwhile, be silent!"
13211: 
13212: Frantic acclamations were again raised. Doctor Manette sat down, with
13213: his eyes looking around, and his lips trembling; his daughter drew
13214: closer to him. The craving man on the jury rubbed his hands together,
13215: and restored the usual hand to his mouth.
13216: 
13217: Defarge was produced, when the court was quiet enough to admit of his
13218: being heard, and rapidly expounded the story of the imprisonment, and of
13219: his having been a mere boy in the Doctor's service, and of the release,
13220: and of the state of the prisoner when released and delivered to him.
13221: This short examination followed, for the court was quick with its work.
13222: 
13223: "You did good service at the taking of the Bastille, citizen?"
13224: 
13225: "I believe so."
13226: 
13227: Here, an excited woman screeched from the crowd: "You were one of the
13228: best patriots there. Why not say so? You were a cannonier that day
13229: there, and you were among the first to enter the accursed fortress when
13230: it fell. Patriots, I speak the truth!"
13231: 
13232: It was The Vengeance who, amidst the warm commendations of the audience,
13233: thus assisted the proceedings. The President rang his bell; but, The
13234: Vengeance, warming with encouragement, shrieked, "I defy that bell!"
13235: wherein she was likewise much commended.
13236: 
13237: "Inform the Tribunal of what you did that day within the Bastille,
13238: citizen."
13239: 
13240: "I knew," said Defarge, looking down at his wife, who stood at the
13241: bottom of the steps on which he was raised, looking steadily up at him;
13242: "I knew that this prisoner, of whom I speak, had been confined in a cell
13243: known as One Hundred and Five, North Tower. I knew it from himself. He
13244: knew himself by no other name than One Hundred and Five, North Tower,
13245: when he made shoes under my care. As I serve my gun that day, I resolve,
13246: when the place shall fall, to examine that cell. It falls. I mount to
13247: the cell, with a fellow-citizen who is one of the Jury, directed by a
13248: gaoler. I examine it, very closely. In a hole in the chimney, where a
13249: stone has been worked out and replaced, I find a written paper. This is
13250: that written paper. I have made it my business to examine some specimens
13251: of the writing of Doctor Manette. This is the writing of Doctor Manette.
13252: I confide this paper, in the writing of Doctor Manette, to the hands of
13253: the President."
13254: 
13255: "Let it be read."
13256: 
13257: In a dead silence and stillness--the prisoner under trial looking
13258: lovingly at his wife, his wife only looking from him to look with
13259: solicitude at her father, Doctor Manette keeping his eyes fixed on the
13260: reader, Madame Defarge never taking hers from the prisoner, Defarge
13261: never taking his from his feasting wife, and all the other eyes there
13262: intent upon the Doctor, who saw none of them--the paper was read, as
13263: follows.
13264: 
13265: 
13266: 
13267: 
13268: X. The Substance of the Shadow
13269: 
13270: 
13271: "I, Alexandre Manette, unfortunate physician, native of Beauvais, and
13272: afterwards resident in Paris, write this melancholy paper in my doleful
13273: cell in the Bastille, during the last month of the year, 1767. I write
13274: it at stolen intervals, under every difficulty. I design to secrete it
13275: in the wall of the chimney, where I have slowly and laboriously made a
13276: place of concealment for it. Some pitying hand may find it there, when I
13277: and my sorrows are dust.
13278: 
13279: "These words are formed by the rusty iron point with which I write with
13280: difficulty in scrapings of soot and charcoal from the chimney, mixed
13281: with blood, in the last month of the tenth year of my captivity. Hope
13282: has quite departed from my breast. I know from terrible warnings I have
13283: noted in myself that my reason will not long remain unimpaired, but I
13284: solemnly declare that I am at this time in the possession of my right
