13285: mind--that my memory is exact and circumstantial--and that I write the
13286: truth as I shall answer for these my last recorded words, whether they
13287: be ever read by men or not, at the Eternal Judgment-seat.
13288: 
13289: "One cloudy moonlight night, in the third week of December (I think the
13290: twenty-second of the month) in the year 1757, I was walking on a retired
13291: part of the quay by the Seine for the refreshment of the frosty air,
13292: at an hour's distance from my place of residence in the Street of the
13293: School of Medicine, when a carriage came along behind me, driven very
13294: fast. As I stood aside to let that carriage pass, apprehensive that it
13295: might otherwise run me down, a head was put out at the window, and a
13296: voice called to the driver to stop.
13297: 
13298: "The carriage stopped as soon as the driver could rein in his horses,
13299: and the same voice called to me by my name. I answered. The carriage
13300: was then so far in advance of me that two gentlemen had time to open the
13301: door and alight before I came up with it.
13302: 
13303: "I observed that they were both wrapped in cloaks, and appeared to
13304: conceal themselves. As they stood side by side near the carriage door,
13305: I also observed that they both looked of about my own age, or rather
13306: younger, and that they were greatly alike, in stature, manner, voice,
13307: and (as far as I could see) face too.
13308: 
13309: "'You are Doctor Manette?' said one.
13310: 
13311: "I am."
13312: 
13313: "'Doctor Manette, formerly of Beauvais,' said the other; 'the young
13314: physician, originally an expert surgeon, who within the last year or two
13315: has made a rising reputation in Paris?'
13316: 
13317: "'Gentlemen,' I returned, 'I am that Doctor Manette of whom you speak so
13318: graciously.'
13319: 
13320: "'We have been to your residence,' said the first, 'and not being
13321: so fortunate as to find you there, and being informed that you were
13322: probably walking in this direction, we followed, in the hope of
13323: overtaking you. Will you please to enter the carriage?'
13324: 
13325: "The manner of both was imperious, and they both moved, as these words
13326: were spoken, so as to place me between themselves and the carriage door.
13327: They were armed. I was not.
13328: 
13329: "'Gentlemen,' said I, 'pardon me; but I usually inquire who does me
13330: the honour to seek my assistance, and what is the nature of the case to
13331: which I am summoned.'
13332: 
13333: "The reply to this was made by him who had spoken second. 'Doctor,
13334: your clients are people of condition. As to the nature of the case,
13335: our confidence in your skill assures us that you will ascertain it for
13336: yourself better than we can describe it. Enough. Will you please to
13337: enter the carriage?'
13338: 
13339: "I could do nothing but comply, and I entered it in silence. They both
13340: entered after me--the last springing in, after putting up the steps. The
13341: carriage turned about, and drove on at its former speed.
13342: 
13343: "I repeat this conversation exactly as it occurred. I have no doubt that
13344: it is, word for word, the same. I describe everything exactly as it took
13345: place, constraining my mind not to wander from the task. Where I make
13346: the broken marks that follow here, I leave off for the time, and put my
13347: paper in its hiding-place.
13348: 
13349:         *****
13350: 
13351: "The carriage left the streets behind, passed the North Barrier, and
13352: emerged upon the country road. At two-thirds of a league from the
13353: Barrier--I did not estimate the distance at that time, but afterwards
13354: when I traversed it--it struck out of the main avenue, and presently
13355: stopped at a solitary house, We all three alighted, and walked, by
13356: a damp soft footpath in a garden where a neglected fountain had
13357: overflowed, to the door of the house. It was not opened immediately, in
13358: answer to the ringing of the bell, and one of my two conductors struck
13359: the man who opened it, with his heavy riding glove, across the face.
13360: 
13361: "There was nothing in this action to attract my particular attention,
13362: for I had seen common people struck more commonly than dogs. But, the
13363: other of the two, being angry likewise, struck the man in like manner
13364: with his arm; the look and bearing of the brothers were then so exactly
13365: alike, that I then first perceived them to be twin brothers.
13366: 
13367: "From the time of our alighting at the outer gate (which we found
13368: locked, and which one of the brothers had opened to admit us, and had
13369: relocked), I had heard cries proceeding from an upper chamber. I was
13370: conducted to this chamber straight, the cries growing louder as we
13371: ascended the stairs, and I found a patient in a high fever of the brain,
13372: lying on a bed.
13373: 
13374: "The patient was a woman of great beauty, and young; assuredly not much
13375: past twenty. Her hair was torn and ragged, and her arms were bound to
13376: her sides with sashes and handkerchiefs. I noticed that these bonds were
13377: all portions of a gentleman's dress. On one of them, which was a fringed
13378: scarf for a dress of ceremony, I saw the armorial bearings of a Noble,
13379: and the letter E.
13380: 
13381: "I saw this, within the first minute of my contemplation of the patient;
13382: for, in her restless strivings she had turned over on her face on the
13383: edge of the bed, had drawn the end of the scarf into her mouth, and was
13384: in danger of suffocation. My first act was to put out my hand to relieve
13385: her breathing; and in moving the scarf aside, the embroidery in the
13386: corner caught my sight.
13387: 
13388: "I turned her gently over, placed my hands upon her breast to calm her
13389: and keep her down, and looked into her face. Her eyes were dilated and
13390: wild, and she constantly uttered piercing shrieks, and repeated the
13391: words, 'My husband, my father, and my brother!' and then counted up to
13392: twelve, and said, 'Hush!' For an instant, and no more, she would pause
13393: to listen, and then the piercing shrieks would begin again, and she
13394: would repeat the cry, 'My husband, my father, and my brother!' and
13395: would count up to twelve, and say, 'Hush!' There was no variation in the
13396: order, or the manner. There was no cessation, but the regular moment's
13397: pause, in the utterance of these sounds.
13398: 
13399: "'How long,' I asked, 'has this lasted?'
13400: 
13401: "To distinguish the brothers, I will call them the elder and the
13402: younger; by the elder, I mean him who exercised the most authority. It
13403: was the elder who replied, 'Since about this hour last night.'
13404: 
13405: "'She has a husband, a father, and a brother?'
13406: 
13407: "'A brother.'
13408: 
13409: "'I do not address her brother?'
13410: 
13411: "He answered with great contempt, 'No.'
13412: 
13413: "'She has some recent association with the number twelve?'
13414: 
13415: "The younger brother impatiently rejoined, 'With twelve o'clock?'
13416: 
13417: "'See, gentlemen,' said I, still keeping my hands upon her breast, 'how
13418: useless I am, as you have brought me! If I had known what I was coming
13419: to see, I could have come provided. As it is, time must be lost. There
13420: are no medicines to be obtained in this lonely place.'
13421: 
13422: "The elder brother looked to the younger, who said haughtily, 'There is
13423: a case of medicines here;' and brought it from a closet, and put it on
13424: the table.
13425: 
13426:         *****
13427: 
13428: "I opened some of the bottles, smelt them, and put the stoppers to my
13429: lips. If I had wanted to use anything save narcotic medicines that were
13430: poisons in themselves, I would not have administered any of those.
13431: 
13432: "'Do you doubt them?' asked the younger brother.
13433: 
13434: "'You see, monsieur, I am going to use them,' I replied, and said no
13435: more.
13436: 
13437: "I made the patient swallow, with great difficulty, and after many
13438: efforts, the dose that I desired to give. As I intended to repeat it
13439: after a while, and as it was necessary to watch its influence, I then
13440: sat down by the side of the bed. There was a timid and suppressed woman
13441: in attendance (wife of the man down-stairs), who had retreated into
13442: a corner. The house was damp and decayed, indifferently
13443: furnished--evidently, recently occupied and temporarily used. Some thick
13444: old hangings had been nailed up before the windows, to deaden the
13445: sound of the shrieks. They continued to be uttered in their regular
13446: succession, with the cry, 'My husband, my father, and my brother!' the
13447: counting up to twelve, and 'Hush!' The frenzy was so violent, that I had
13448: not unfastened the bandages restraining the arms; but, I had looked to
13449: them, to see that they were not painful. The only spark of encouragement
13450: in the case, was, that my hand upon the sufferer's breast had this much
13451: soothing influence, that for minutes at a time it tranquillised the
13452: figure. It had no effect upon the cries; no pendulum could be more
13453: regular.
13454: 
13455: "For the reason that my hand had this effect (I assume), I had sat by
13456: the side of the bed for half an hour, with the two brothers looking on,
13457: before the elder said:
13458: 
13459: "'There is another patient.'
13460: 
13461: "I was startled, and asked, 'Is it a pressing case?'
13462: 
13463: "'You had better see,' he carelessly answered; and took up a light.
13464: 
13465:         *****
13466: 
13467: "The other patient lay in a back room across a second staircase, which
13468: was a species of loft over a stable. There was a low plastered ceiling
13469: to a part of it; the rest was open, to the ridge of the tiled roof, and
13470: there were beams across. Hay and straw were stored in that portion of
13471: the place, fagots for firing, and a heap of apples in sand. I had to
13472: pass through that part, to get at the other. My memory is circumstantial
13473: and unshaken. I try it with these details, and I see them all, in
13474: this my cell in the Bastille, near the close of the tenth year of my
13475: captivity, as I saw them all that night.
13476: 
13477: "On some hay on the ground, with a cushion thrown under his head, lay a
13478: handsome peasant boy--a boy of not more than seventeen at the most.
13479: He lay on his back, with his teeth set, his right hand clenched on his
13480: breast, and his glaring eyes looking straight upward. I could not see
13481: where his wound was, as I kneeled on one knee over him; but, I could see
13482: that he was dying of a wound from a sharp point.
13483: 
13484: "'I am a doctor, my poor fellow,' said I. 'Let me examine it.'
13485: 
13486: "'I do not want it examined,' he answered; 'let it be.'
13487: 
13488: "It was under his hand, and I soothed him to let me move his hand away.
13489: The wound was a sword-thrust, received from twenty to twenty-four hours
13490: before, but no skill could have saved him if it had been looked to
13491: without delay. He was then dying fast. As I turned my eyes to the elder
13492: brother, I saw him looking down at this handsome boy whose life was
13493: ebbing out, as if he were a wounded bird, or hare, or rabbit; not at all
13494: as if he were a fellow-creature.
13495: 
13496: "'How has this been done, monsieur?' said I.
13497: 
13498: "'A crazed young common dog! A serf! Forced my brother to draw upon him,
13499: and has fallen by my brother's sword--like a gentleman.'
13500: 
13501: "There was no touch of pity, sorrow, or kindred humanity, in this
13502: answer. The speaker seemed to acknowledge that it was inconvenient to
13503: have that different order of creature dying there, and that it would
13504: have been better if he had died in the usual obscure routine of his
13505: vermin kind. He was quite incapable of any compassionate feeling about
13506: the boy, or about his fate.
13507: 
13508: "The boy's eyes had slowly moved to him as he had spoken, and they now
13509: slowly moved to me.
13510: 
13511: "'Doctor, they are very proud, these Nobles; but we common dogs are
13512: proud too, sometimes. They plunder us, outrage us, beat us, kill us; but
13513: we have a little pride left, sometimes. She--have you seen her, Doctor?'
13514: 
13515: "The shrieks and the cries were audible there, though subdued by the
13516: distance. He referred to them, as if she were lying in our presence.
13517: 
13518: "I said, 'I have seen her.'
13519: 
13520: "'She is my sister, Doctor. They have had their shameful rights, these
13521: Nobles, in the modesty and virtue of our sisters, many years, but we
13522: have had good girls among us. I know it, and have heard my father say
13523: so. She was a good girl. She was betrothed to a good young man, too: a
13524: tenant of his. We were all tenants of his--that man's who stands there.
13525: The other is his brother, the worst of a bad race.'
13526: 
13527: "It was with the greatest difficulty that the boy gathered bodily force
13528: to speak; but, his spirit spoke with a dreadful emphasis.
13529: 
13530: "'We were so robbed by that man who stands there, as all we common dogs
13531: are by those superior Beings--taxed by him without mercy, obliged to
13532: work for him without pay, obliged to grind our corn at his mill, obliged
13533: to feed scores of his tame birds on our wretched crops, and forbidden
13534: for our lives to keep a single tame bird of our own, pillaged and
13535: plundered to that degree that when we chanced to have a bit of meat, we
13536: ate it in fear, with the door barred and the shutters closed, that his
13537: people should not see it and take it from us--I say, we were so robbed,
13538: and hunted, and were made so poor, that our father told us it was a
13539: dreadful thing to bring a child into the world, and that what we should
13540: most pray for, was, that our women might be barren and our miserable
13541: race die out!'
13542: 
13543: "I had never before seen the sense of being oppressed, bursting forth
13544: like a fire. I had supposed that it must be latent in the people
13545: somewhere; but, I had never seen it break out, until I saw it in the
13546: dying boy.
13547: 
13548: "'Nevertheless, Doctor, my sister married. He was ailing at that time,
13549: poor fellow, and she married her lover, that she might tend and comfort
13550: him in our cottage--our dog-hut, as that man would call it. She had not
13551: been married many weeks, when that man's brother saw her and admired
13552: her, and asked that man to lend her to him--for what are husbands among
13553: us! He was willing enough, but my sister was good and virtuous, and
13554: hated his brother with a hatred as strong as mine. What did the two
13555: then, to persuade her husband to use his influence with her, to make her
13556: willing?'
13557: 
13558: "The boy's eyes, which had been fixed on mine, slowly turned to the
13559: looker-on, and I saw in the two faces that all he said was true. The two
13560: opposing kinds of pride confronting one another, I can see, even in this
13561: Bastille; the gentleman's, all negligent indifference; the peasant's, all
13562: trodden-down sentiment, and passionate revenge.
13563: 
13564: "'You know, Doctor, that it is among the Rights of these Nobles to
13565: harness us common dogs to carts, and drive us. They so harnessed him and
13566: drove him. You know that it is among their Rights to keep us in their
13567: grounds all night, quieting the frogs, in order that their noble sleep
13568: may not be disturbed. They kept him out in the unwholesome mists at
13569: night, and ordered him back into his harness in the day. But he was
13570: not persuaded. No! Taken out of harness one day at noon, to feed--if he
13571: could find food--he sobbed twelve times, once for every stroke of the
13572: bell, and died on her bosom.'
13573: 
13574: "Nothing human could have held life in the boy but his determination to
13575: tell all his wrong. He forced back the gathering shadows of death, as
13576: he forced his clenched right hand to remain clenched, and to cover his
13577: wound.
13578: 
13579: "'Then, with that man's permission and even with his aid, his
13580: brother took her away; in spite of what I know she must have told his
13581: brother--and what that is, will not be long unknown to you, Doctor, if
13582: it is now--his brother took her away--for his pleasure and diversion,
13583: for a little while. I saw her pass me on the road. When I took the
13584: tidings home, our father's heart burst; he never spoke one of the words
13585: that filled it. I took my young sister (for I have another) to a place
13586: beyond the reach of this man, and where, at least, she will never be
13587: _his_ vassal. Then, I tracked the brother here, and last night climbed
13588: in--a common dog, but sword in hand.--Where is the loft window? It was
13589: somewhere here?'
13590: 
13591: "The room was darkening to his sight; the world was narrowing around
13592: him. I glanced about me, and saw that the hay and straw were trampled
13593: over the floor, as if there had been a struggle.
13594: 
13595: "'She heard me, and ran in. I told her not to come near us till he was
13596: dead. He came in and first tossed me some pieces of money; then struck
13597: at me with a whip. But I, though a common dog, so struck at him as to
13598: make him draw. Let him break into as many pieces as he will, the sword
13599: that he stained with my common blood; he drew to defend himself--thrust
13600: at me with all his skill for his life.'
13601: 
13602: "My glance had fallen, but a few moments before, on the fragments of
13603: a broken sword, lying among the hay. That weapon was a gentleman's. In
13604: another place, lay an old sword that seemed to have been a soldier's.
13605: 
13606: "'Now, lift me up, Doctor; lift me up. Where is he?'
13607: 
13608: "'He is not here,' I said, supporting the boy, and thinking that he
13609: referred to the brother.
13610: 
13611: "'He! Proud as these nobles are, he is afraid to see me. Where is the
13612: man who was here? Turn my face to him.'
13613: 
13614: "I did so, raising the boy's head against my knee. But, invested for the
13615: moment with extraordinary power, he raised himself completely: obliging
13616: me to rise too, or I could not have still supported him.
13617: 
13618: "'Marquis,' said the boy, turned to him with his eyes opened wide, and
13619: his right hand raised, 'in the days when all these things are to be
13620: answered for, I summon you and yours, to the last of your bad race, to
13621: answer for them. I mark this cross of blood upon you, as a sign that
13622: I do it. In the days when all these things are to be answered for,
13623: I summon your brother, the worst of the bad race, to answer for them
13624: separately. I mark this cross of blood upon him, as a sign that I do
13625: it.'
13626: 
13627: "Twice, he put his hand to the wound in his breast, and with his
13628: forefinger drew a cross in the air. He stood for an instant with the
13629: finger yet raised, and as it dropped, he dropped with it, and I laid him
13630: down dead.
13631: 
13632:         *****
13633: 
13634: "When I returned to the bedside of the young woman, I found her raving
13635: in precisely the same order of continuity. I knew that this might last
13636: for many hours, and that it would probably end in the silence of the
13637: grave.
13638: 
13639: "I repeated the medicines I had given her, and I sat at the side of
13640: the bed until the night was far advanced. She never abated the piercing
13641: quality of her shrieks, never stumbled in the distinctness or the order
13642: of her words. They were always 'My husband, my father, and my brother!
13643: One, two, three, four, five, six, seven, eight, nine, ten, eleven,
13644: twelve. Hush!'
13645: 
13646: "This lasted twenty-six hours from the time when I first saw her. I had
13647: come and gone twice, and was again sitting by her, when she began to
13648: falter. I did what little could be done to assist that opportunity, and
13649: by-and-bye she sank into a lethargy, and lay like the dead.
13650: 
13651: "It was as if the wind and rain had lulled at last, after a long and
13652: fearful storm. I released her arms, and called the woman to assist me to
13653: compose her figure and the dress she had torn. It was then that I knew
13654: her condition to be that of one in whom the first expectations of being
13655: a mother have arisen; and it was then that I lost the little hope I had
13656: had of her.
13657: 
13658: "'Is she dead?' asked the Marquis, whom I will still describe as the
13659: elder brother, coming booted into the room from his horse.
13660: 
13661: "'Not dead,' said I; 'but like to die.'
13662: 
13663: "'What strength there is in these common bodies!' he said, looking down
13664: at her with some curiosity.
13665: 
13666: "'There is prodigious strength,' I answered him, 'in sorrow and
13667: despair.'
13668: 
13669: "He first laughed at my words, and then frowned at them. He moved a
13670: chair with his foot near to mine, ordered the woman away, and said in a
13671: subdued voice,
13672: 
13673: "'Doctor, finding my brother in this difficulty with these hinds, I
13674: recommended that your aid should be invited. Your reputation is high,
13675: and, as a young man with your fortune to make, you are probably mindful
13676: of your interest. The things that you see here, are things to be seen,
13677: and not spoken of.'
13678: 
13679: "I listened to the patient's breathing, and avoided answering.
13680: 
13681: "'Do you honour me with your attention, Doctor?'
13682: 
13683: "'Monsieur,' said I, 'in my profession, the communications of patients
13684: are always received in confidence.' I was guarded in my answer, for I
13685: was troubled in my mind with what I had heard and seen.
13686: 
13687: "Her breathing was so difficult to trace, that I carefully tried the
13688: pulse and the heart. There was life, and no more. Looking round as I
13689: resumed my seat, I found both the brothers intent upon me.
13690: 
13691:         *****
13692: 
13693: "I write with so much difficulty, the cold is so severe, I am so
13694: fearful of being detected and consigned to an underground cell and total
13695: darkness, that I must abridge this narrative. There is no confusion or
13696: failure in my memory; it can recall, and could detail, every word that
13697: was ever spoken between me and those brothers.
13698: 
13699: "She lingered for a week. Towards the last, I could understand some few
13700: syllables that she said to me, by placing my ear close to her lips. She
13701: asked me where she was, and I told her; who I was, and I told her. It
13702: was in vain that I asked her for her family name. She faintly shook her
13703: head upon the pillow, and kept her secret, as the boy had done.
13704: 
13705: "I had no opportunity of asking her any question, until I had told the
13706: brothers she was sinking fast, and could not live another day. Until
13707: then, though no one was ever presented to her consciousness save the
13708: woman and myself, one or other of them had always jealously sat behind
13709: the curtain at the head of the bed when I was there. But when it came to
13710: that, they seemed careless what communication I might hold with her; as
13711: if--the thought passed through my mind--I were dying too.
13712: 
13713: "I always observed that their pride bitterly resented the younger
13714: brother's (as I call him) having crossed swords with a peasant, and that
13715: peasant a boy. The only consideration that appeared to affect the mind
13716: of either of them was the consideration that this was highly degrading
13717: to the family, and was ridiculous. As often as I caught the younger
13718: brother's eyes, their expression reminded me that he disliked me deeply,
13719: for knowing what I knew from the boy. He was smoother and more polite to
13720: me than the elder; but I saw this. I also saw that I was an incumbrance
13721: in the mind of the elder, too.
13722: 
13723: "My patient died, two hours before midnight--at a time, by my watch,
13724: answering almost to the minute when I had first seen her. I was alone
13725: with her, when her forlorn young head drooped gently on one side, and
13726: all her earthly wrongs and sorrows ended.
13727: 
13728: "The brothers were waiting in a room down-stairs, impatient to ride
13729: away. I had heard them, alone at the bedside, striking their boots with
13730: their riding-whips, and loitering up and down.
13731: 
13732: "'At last she is dead?' said the elder, when I went in.
13733: 
13734: "'She is dead,' said I.
13735: 
13736: "'I congratulate you, my brother,' were his words as he turned round.
13737: 
13738: "He had before offered me money, which I had postponed taking. He now
13739: gave me a rouleau of gold. I took it from his hand, but laid it on
13740: the table. I had considered the question, and had resolved to accept
13741: nothing.
13742: 
13743: "'Pray excuse me,' said I. 'Under the circumstances, no.'
13744: 
13745: "They exchanged looks, but bent their heads to me as I bent mine to
13746: them, and we parted without another word on either side.
13747: 
13748:         *****
13749: 
13750: "I am weary, weary, weary--worn down by misery. I cannot read what I
13751: have written with this gaunt hand.
13752: 
13753: "Early in the morning, the rouleau of gold was left at my door in a
13754: little box, with my name on the outside. From the first, I had anxiously
13755: considered what I ought to do. I decided, that day, to write privately
13756: to the Minister, stating the nature of the two cases to which I had been
13757: summoned, and the place to which I had gone: in effect, stating all the
13758: circumstances. I knew what Court influence was, and what the immunities
13759: of the Nobles were, and I expected that the matter would never be
13760: heard of; but, I wished to relieve my own mind. I had kept the matter a
13761: profound secret, even from my wife; and this, too, I resolved to state
13762: in my letter. I had no apprehension whatever of my real danger; but
13763: I was conscious that there might be danger for others, if others were
13764: compromised by possessing the knowledge that I possessed.
13765: 
13766: "I was much engaged that day, and could not complete my letter that
13767: night. I rose long before my usual time next morning to finish it.
13768: It was the last day of the year. The letter was lying before me just
13769: completed, when I was told that a lady waited, who wished to see me.
13770: 
13771:         *****
13772: 
13773: "I am growing more and more unequal to the task I have set myself. It is
13774: so cold, so dark, my senses are so benumbed, and the gloom upon me is so
13775: dreadful.
13776: 
13777: "The lady was young, engaging, and handsome, but not marked for long
13778: life. She was in great agitation. She presented herself to me as the
13779: wife of the Marquis St. Evremonde. I connected the title by which the
13780: boy had addressed the elder brother, with the initial letter embroidered
13781: on the scarf, and had no difficulty in arriving at the conclusion that I
13782: had seen that nobleman very lately.
13783: 
13784: "My memory is still accurate, but I cannot write the words of our
13785: conversation. I suspect that I am watched more closely than I was, and I
13786: know not at what times I may be watched. She had in part suspected, and
13787: in part discovered, the main facts of the cruel story, of her husband's
13788: share in it, and my being resorted to. She did not know that the girl
13789: was dead. Her hope had been, she said in great distress, to show her,
13790: in secret, a woman's sympathy. Her hope had been to avert the wrath of
13791: Heaven from a House that had long been hateful to the suffering many.
13792: 
13793: "She had reasons for believing that there was a young sister living, and
13794: her greatest desire was, to help that sister. I could tell her nothing
13795: but that there was such a sister; beyond that, I knew nothing. Her
13796: inducement to come to me, relying on my confidence, had been the hope
13797: that I could tell her the name and place of abode. Whereas, to this
13798: wretched hour I am ignorant of both.
13799: 
13800:         *****
13801: 
13802: "These scraps of paper fail me. One was taken from me, with a warning,
13803: yesterday. I must finish my record to-day.
13804: 
13805: "She was a good, compassionate lady, and not happy in her marriage. How
13806: could she be! The brother distrusted and disliked her, and his influence
13807: was all opposed to her; she stood in dread of him, and in dread of her
13808: husband too. When I handed her down to the door, there was a child, a
13809: pretty boy from two to three years old, in her carriage.
13810: 
13811: "'For his sake, Doctor,' she said, pointing to him in tears, 'I would do
13812: all I can to make what poor amends I can. He will never prosper in his
13813: inheritance otherwise. I have a presentiment that if no other innocent
13814: atonement is made for this, it will one day be required of him. What
13815: I have left to call my own--it is little beyond the worth of a few
13816: jewels--I will make it the first charge of his life to bestow, with the
13817: compassion and lamenting of his dead mother, on this injured family, if
13818: the sister can be discovered.'
13819: 
13820: "She kissed the boy, and said, caressing him, 'It is for thine own dear
13821: sake. Thou wilt be faithful, little Charles?' The child answered her
13822: bravely, 'Yes!' I kissed her hand, and she took him in her arms, and
13823: went away caressing him. I never saw her more.
13824: 
13825: "As she had mentioned her husband's name in the faith that I knew it,
13826: I added no mention of it to my letter. I sealed my letter, and, not
13827: trusting it out of my own hands, delivered it myself that day.
13828: 
13829: "That night, the last night of the year, towards nine o'clock, a man in
13830: a black dress rang at my gate, demanded to see me, and softly followed
13831: my servant, Ernest Defarge, a youth, up-stairs. When my servant came
13832: into the room where I sat with my wife--O my wife, beloved of my heart!
13833: My fair young English wife!--we saw the man, who was supposed to be at
13834: the gate, standing silent behind him.
13835: 
13836: "An urgent case in the Rue St. Honore, he said. It would not detain me,
13837: he had a coach in waiting.
13838: 
13839: "It brought me here, it brought me to my grave. When I was clear of the
13840: house, a black muffler was drawn tightly over my mouth from behind, and
13841: my arms were pinioned. The two brothers crossed the road from a dark
13842: corner, and identified me with a single gesture. The Marquis took from
13843: his pocket the letter I had written, showed it me, burnt it in the light
13844: of a lantern that was held, and extinguished the ashes with his foot.
13845: Not a word was spoken. I was brought here, I was brought to my living
13846: grave.
13847: 
13848: "If it had pleased _God_ to put it in the hard heart of either of the
13849: brothers, in all these frightful years, to grant me any tidings of
13850: my dearest wife--so much as to let me know by a word whether alive or
13851: dead--I might have thought that He had not quite abandoned them. But,
13852: now I believe that the mark of the red cross is fatal to them, and that
13853: they have no part in His mercies. And them and their descendants, to the
13854: last of their race, I, Alexandre Manette, unhappy prisoner, do this last
13855: night of the year 1767, in my unbearable agony, denounce to the times
13856: when all these things shall be answered for. I denounce them to Heaven
13857: and to earth."
13858: 
13859: A terrible sound arose when the reading of this document was done. A
13860: sound of craving and eagerness that had nothing articulate in it but
13861: blood. The narrative called up the most revengeful passions of the time,
13862: and there was not a head in the nation but must have dropped before it.
13863: 
13864: Little need, in presence of that tribunal and that auditory, to show
13865: how the Defarges had not made the paper public, with the other captured
13866: Bastille memorials borne in procession, and had kept it, biding their
13867: time. Little need to show that this detested family name had long been
13868: anathematised by Saint Antoine, and was wrought into the fatal register.
13869: The man never trod ground whose virtues and services would have
13870: sustained him in that place that day, against such denunciation.
13871: 
13872: And all the worse for the doomed man, that the denouncer was a
13873: well-known citizen, his own attached friend, the father of his wife. One
13874: of the frenzied aspirations of the populace was, for imitations of
13875: the questionable public virtues of antiquity, and for sacrifices and
13876: self-immolations on the people's altar. Therefore when the President
13877: said (else had his own head quivered on his shoulders), that the good
13878: physician of the Republic would deserve better still of the Republic by
13879: rooting out an obnoxious family of Aristocrats, and would doubtless feel
13880: a sacred glow and joy in making his daughter a widow and her child an
13881: orphan, there was wild excitement, patriotic fervour, not a touch of
13882: human sympathy.
13883: 
13884: "Much influence around him, has that Doctor?" murmured Madame Defarge,
13885: smiling to The Vengeance. "Save him now, my Doctor, save him!"
13886: 
13887: At every juryman's vote, there was a roar. Another and another. Roar and
13888: roar.
13889: 
13890: Unanimously voted. At heart and by descent an Aristocrat, an enemy
13891: of the Republic, a notorious oppressor of the People. Back to the
13892: Conciergerie, and Death within four-and-twenty hours!
13893: 
13894: 
13895: 
13896: 
13897: XI. Dusk
13898: 
13899: 
13900: The wretched wife of the innocent man thus doomed to die, fell under
13901: the sentence, as if she had been mortally stricken. But, she uttered no
13902: sound; and so strong was the voice within her, representing that it was
13903: she of all the world who must uphold him in his misery and not augment
13904: it, that it quickly raised her, even from that shock.
13905: 
13906: The Judges having to take part in a public demonstration out of doors,
13907: the Tribunal adjourned. The quick noise and movement of the court's
13908: emptying itself by many passages had not ceased, when Lucie stood
13909: stretching out her arms towards her husband, with nothing in her face
13910: but love and consolation.
13911: 
13912: "If I might touch him! If I might embrace him once! O, good citizens, if
13913: you would have so much compassion for us!"
13914: 
13915: There was but a gaoler left, along with two of the four men who had
13916: taken him last night, and Barsad. The people had all poured out to the
13917: show in the streets. Barsad proposed to the rest, "Let her embrace
13918: him then; it is but a moment." It was silently acquiesced in, and they
13919: passed her over the seats in the hall to a raised place, where he, by
13920: leaning over the dock, could fold her in his arms.
13921: 
13922: "Farewell, dear darling of my soul. My parting blessing on my love. We
13923: shall meet again, where the weary are at rest!"
13924: 
13925: They were her husband's words, as he held her to his bosom.
13926: 
13927: "I can bear it, dear Charles. I am supported from above: don't suffer
13928: for me. A parting blessing for our child."
13929: 
13930: "I send it to her by you. I kiss her by you. I say farewell to her by
13931: you."
13932: 
13933: "My husband. No! A moment!" He was tearing himself apart from her.
13934: "We shall not be separated long. I feel that this will break my heart
13935: by-and-bye; but I will do my duty while I can, and when I leave her, God
13936: will raise up friends for her, as He did for me."
13937: 
13938: Her father had followed her, and would have fallen on his knees to both
13939: of them, but that Darnay put out a hand and seized him, crying:
13940: 
13941: "No, no! What have you done, what have you done, that you should kneel
13942: to us! We know now, what a struggle you made of old. We know, now what
13943: you underwent when you suspected my descent, and when you knew it. We
13944: know now, the natural antipathy you strove against, and conquered, for
13945: her dear sake. We thank you with all our hearts, and all our love and
13946: duty. Heaven be with you!"
13947: 
13948: Her father's only answer was to draw his hands through his white hair,
13949: and wring them with a shriek of anguish.
13950: 
13951: "It could not be otherwise," said the prisoner. "All things have worked
13952: together as they have fallen out. It was the always-vain endeavour to
13953: discharge my poor mother's trust that first brought my fatal presence
13954: near you. Good could never come of such evil, a happier end was not in
13955: nature to so unhappy a beginning. Be comforted, and forgive me. Heaven
13956: bless you!"
13957: 
13958: As he was drawn away, his wife released him, and stood looking after him
13959: with her hands touching one another in the attitude of prayer, and
13960: with a radiant look upon her face, in which there was even a comforting
13961: smile. As he went out at the prisoners' door, she turned, laid her head
13962: lovingly on her father's breast, tried to speak to him, and fell at his
13963: feet.
13964: 
13965: Then, issuing from the obscure corner from which he had never moved,
13966: Sydney Carton came and took her up. Only her father and Mr. Lorry were
13967: with her. His arm trembled as it raised her, and supported her head.
13968: Yet, there was an air about him that was not all of pity--that had a
13969: flush of pride in it.
13970: 
13971: "Shall I take her to a coach? I shall never feel her weight."
13972: 
13973: He carried her lightly to the door, and laid her tenderly down in a
13974: coach. Her father and their old friend got into it, and he took his seat
13975: beside the driver.
13976: 
13977: When they arrived at the gateway where he had paused in the dark not
13978: many hours before, to picture to himself on which of the rough stones of
13979: the street her feet had trodden, he lifted her again, and carried her up
13980: the staircase to their rooms. There, he laid her down on a couch, where
13981: her child and Miss Pross wept over her.
13982: 
13983: "Don't recall her to herself," he said, softly, to the latter, "she is
13984: better so. Don't revive her to consciousness, while she only faints."
13985: 
13986: "Oh, Carton, Carton, dear Carton!" cried little Lucie, springing up and
13987: throwing her arms passionately round him, in a burst of grief. "Now that
13988: you have come, I think you will do something to help mamma, something to
13989: save papa! O, look at her, dear Carton! Can you, of all the people who
13990: love her, bear to see her so?"
13991: 
13992: He bent over the child, and laid her blooming cheek against his face. He
13993: put her gently from him, and looked at her unconscious mother.
13994: 
13995: "Before I go," he said, and paused--"I may kiss her?"
13996: 
13997: It was remembered afterwards that when he bent down and touched her face
13998: with his lips, he murmured some words. The child, who was nearest to
13999: him, told them afterwards, and told her grandchildren when she was a
14000: handsome old lady, that she heard him say, "A life you love."
14001: 
14002: When he had gone out into the next room, he turned suddenly on Mr. Lorry
14003: and her father, who were following, and said to the latter:
14004: 
14005: "You had great influence but yesterday, Doctor Manette; let it at least
14006: be tried. These judges, and all the men in power, are very friendly to
14007: you, and very recognisant of your services; are they not?"
14008: 
14009: "Nothing connected with Charles was concealed from me. I had the
14010: strongest assurances that I should save him; and I did." He returned the
14011: answer in great trouble, and very slowly.
14012: 
14013: "Try them again. The hours between this and to-morrow afternoon are few
14014: and short, but try."
14015: 
14016: "I intend to try. I will not rest a moment."
14017: 
14018: "That's well. I have known such energy as yours do great things before
14019: now--though never," he added, with a smile and a sigh together, "such
14020: great things as this. But try! Of little worth as life is when we misuse
14021: it, it is worth that effort. It would cost nothing to lay down if it
14022: were not."
14023: 
14024: "I will go," said Doctor Manette, "to the Prosecutor and the President
14025: straight, and I will go to others whom it is better not to name. I will
14026: write too, and--But stay! There is a Celebration in the streets, and no
14027: one will be accessible until dark."
14028: 
14029: "That's true. Well! It is a forlorn hope at the best, and not much the
14030: forlorner for being delayed till dark. I should like to know how you
14031: speed; though, mind! I expect nothing! When are you likely to have seen
14032: these dread powers, Doctor Manette?"
14033: 
14034: "Immediately after dark, I should hope. Within an hour or two from
14035: this."
14036: 
14037: "It will be dark soon after four. Let us stretch the hour or two. If I
14038: go to Mr. Lorry's at nine, shall I hear what you have done, either from
14039: our friend or from yourself?"
14040: 
14041: "Yes."
14042: 
14043: "May you prosper!"
14044: 
14045: Mr. Lorry followed Sydney to the outer door, and, touching him on the
14046: shoulder as he was going away, caused him to turn.
14047: 
14048: "I have no hope," said Mr. Lorry, in a low and sorrowful whisper.
14049: 
14050: "Nor have I."
14051: 
14052: "If any one of these men, or all of these men, were disposed to spare
14053: him--which is a large supposition; for what is his life, or any man's
14054: to them!--I doubt if they durst spare him after the demonstration in the
14055: court."
14056: 
14057: "And so do I. I heard the fall of the axe in that sound."
14058: 
14059: Mr. Lorry leaned his arm upon the door-post, and bowed his face upon it.
14060: 
14061: "Don't despond," said Carton, very gently; "don't grieve. I encouraged
14062: Doctor Manette in this idea, because I felt that it might one day be
14063: consolatory to her. Otherwise, she might think 'his life was wantonly
14064: thrown away or wasted,' and that might trouble her."
14065: 
14066: "Yes, yes, yes," returned Mr. Lorry, drying his eyes, "you are right.
14067: But he will perish; there is no real hope."
14068: 
14069: "Yes. He will perish: there is no real hope," echoed Carton.
14070: 
14071: And walked with a settled step, down-stairs.
14072: 
14073: 
14074: 
14075: 
14076: XII. Darkness
14077: 
14078: 
14079: Sydney Carton paused in the street, not quite decided where to go. "At
14080: Tellson's banking-house at nine," he said, with a musing face. "Shall I
14081: do well, in the mean time, to show myself? I think so. It is best that
14082: these people should know there is such a man as I here; it is a sound
14083: precaution, and may be a necessary preparation. But care, care, care!
14084: Let me think it out!"
14085: 
14086: Checking his steps which had begun to tend towards an object, he took a
14087: turn or two in the already darkening street, and traced the thought
14088: in his mind to its possible consequences. His first impression was
14089: confirmed. "It is best," he said, finally resolved, "that these people
14090: should know there is such a man as I here." And he turned his face
14091: towards Saint Antoine.
14092: 
14093: Defarge had described himself, that day, as the keeper of a wine-shop in
14094: the Saint Antoine suburb. It was not difficult for one who knew the city
14095: well, to find his house without asking any question. Having ascertained
14096: its situation, Carton came out of those closer streets again, and dined
14097: at a place of refreshment and fell sound asleep after dinner. For the
14098: first time in many years, he had no strong drink. Since last night he
14099: had taken nothing but a little light thin wine, and last night he had
14100: dropped the brandy slowly down on Mr. Lorry's hearth like a man who had
14101: done with it.
14102: 
14103: It was as late as seven o'clock when he awoke refreshed, and went out
14104: into the streets again. As he passed along towards Saint Antoine, he
14105: stopped at a shop-window where there was a mirror, and slightly altered
14106: the disordered arrangement of his loose cravat, and his coat-collar, and
14107: his wild hair. This done, he went on direct to Defarge's, and went in.
14108: 
14109: There happened to be no customer in the shop but Jacques Three, of the
14110: restless fingers and the croaking voice. This man, whom he had seen upon
14111: the Jury, stood drinking at the little counter, in conversation with the
14112: Defarges, man and wife. The Vengeance assisted in the conversation, like
14113: a regular member of the establishment.
14114: 
14115: As Carton walked in, took his seat and asked (in very indifferent
14116: French) for a small measure of wine, Madame Defarge cast a careless
14117: glance at him, and then a keener, and then a keener, and then advanced
14118: to him herself, and asked him what it was he had ordered.
14119: 
14120: He repeated what he had already said.
14121: 
14122: "English?" asked Madame Defarge, inquisitively raising her dark
14123: eyebrows.
14124: 
14125: After looking at her, as if the sound of even a single French word were
14126: slow to express itself to him, he answered, in his former strong foreign
14127: accent. "Yes, madame, yes. I am English!"
14128: 
14129: Madame Defarge returned to her counter to get the wine, and, as he
14130: took up a Jacobin journal and feigned to pore over it puzzling out its
14131: meaning, he heard her say, "I swear to you, like Evremonde!"
14132: 
14133: Defarge brought him the wine, and gave him Good Evening.
14134: 
14135: "How?"
14136: 
14137: "Good evening."
14138: 
14139: "Oh! Good evening, citizen," filling his glass. "Ah! and good wine. I
14140: drink to the Republic."
14141: 
14142: Defarge went back to the counter, and said, "Certainly, a little like."
14143: Madame sternly retorted, "I tell you a good deal like." Jacques Three
14144: pacifically remarked, "He is so much in your mind, see you, madame."
14145: The amiable Vengeance added, with a laugh, "Yes, my faith! And you
14146: are looking forward with so much pleasure to seeing him once more
14147: to-morrow!"
14148: 
14149: Carton followed the lines and words of his paper, with a slow
14150: forefinger, and with a studious and absorbed face. They were all leaning
14151: their arms on the counter close together, speaking low. After a silence
14152: of a few moments, during which they all looked towards him without
14153: disturbing his outward attention from the Jacobin editor, they resumed
14154: their conversation.
14155: 
14156: "It is true what madame says," observed Jacques Three. "Why stop? There
14157: is great force in that. Why stop?"
14158: 
14159: "Well, well," reasoned Defarge, "but one must stop somewhere. After all,
14160: the question is still where?"
14161: 
14162: "At extermination," said madame.
14163: 
14164: "Magnificent!" croaked Jacques Three. The Vengeance, also, highly
14165: approved.
14166: 
14167: "Extermination is good doctrine, my wife," said Defarge, rather
14168: troubled; "in general, I say nothing against it. But this Doctor has
14169: suffered much; you have seen him to-day; you have observed his face when
14170: the paper was read."
14171: 
14172: "I have observed his face!" repeated madame, contemptuously and angrily.
14173: "Yes. I have observed his face. I have observed his face to be not the
14174: face of a true friend of the Republic. Let him take care of his face!"
14175: 
14176: "And you have observed, my wife," said Defarge, in a deprecatory manner,
14177: "the anguish of his daughter, which must be a dreadful anguish to him!"
14178: 
14179: "I have observed his daughter," repeated madame; "yes, I have observed
14180: his daughter, more times than one. I have observed her to-day, and I
14181: have observed her other days. I have observed her in the court, and
14182: I have observed her in the street by the prison. Let me but lift my
14183: finger--!" She seemed to raise it (the listener's eyes were always on
14184: his paper), and to let it fall with a rattle on the ledge before her, as
14185: if the axe had dropped.
14186: 
14187: "The citizeness is superb!" croaked the Juryman.
14188: 
14189: "She is an Angel!" said The Vengeance, and embraced her.
14190: 
14191: "As to thee," pursued madame, implacably, addressing her husband, "if it
14192: depended on thee--which, happily, it does not--thou wouldst rescue this
14193: man even now."
14194: 
14195: "No!" protested Defarge. "Not if to lift this glass would do it! But I
14196: would leave the matter there. I say, stop there."
14197: 
14198: "See you then, Jacques," said Madame Defarge, wrathfully; "and see you,
14199: too, my little Vengeance; see you both! Listen! For other crimes as
14200: tyrants and oppressors, I have this race a long time on my register,
14201: doomed to destruction and extermination. Ask my husband, is that so."
14202: 
14203: "It is so," assented Defarge, without being asked.
14204: 
14205: "In the beginning of the great days, when the Bastille falls, he finds
14206: this paper of to-day, and he brings it home, and in the middle of the
14207: night when this place is clear and shut, we read it, here on this spot,
14208: by the light of this lamp. Ask him, is that so."
14209: 
14210: "It is so," assented Defarge.
14211: 
14212: "That night, I tell him, when the paper is read through, and the lamp is
14213: burnt out, and the day is gleaming in above those shutters and between
14214: those iron bars, that I have now a secret to communicate. Ask him, is
14215: that so."
14216: 
14217: "It is so," assented Defarge again.
14218: 
14219: "I communicate to him that secret. I smite this bosom with these two
14220: hands as I smite it now, and I tell him, 'Defarge, I was brought up
14221: among the fishermen of the sea-shore, and that peasant family so injured
14222: by the two Evremonde brothers, as that Bastille paper describes, is my
14223: family. Defarge, that sister of the mortally wounded boy upon the ground
14224: was my sister, that husband was my sister's husband, that unborn child
14225: was their child, that brother was my brother, that father was my father,
14226: those dead are my dead, and that summons to answer for those things
14227: descends to me!' Ask him, is that so."
14228: 
14229: "It is so," assented Defarge once more.
14230: 
14231: "Then tell Wind and Fire where to stop," returned madame; "but don't
14232: tell me."
14233: 
14234: Both her hearers derived a horrible enjoyment from the deadly nature
14235: of her wrath--the listener could feel how white she was, without seeing
14236: her--and both highly commended it. Defarge, a weak minority, interposed
14237: a few words for the memory of the compassionate wife of the Marquis; but
14238: only elicited from his own wife a repetition of her last reply. "Tell
14239: the Wind and the Fire where to stop; not me!"
14240: 
14241: Customers entered, and the group was broken up. The English customer
14242: paid for what he had had, perplexedly counted his change, and asked, as
14243: a stranger, to be directed towards the National Palace. Madame Defarge
14244: took him to the door, and put her arm on his, in pointing out the road.
14245: The English customer was not without his reflections then, that it might
14246: be a good deed to seize that arm, lift it, and strike under it sharp and
14247: deep.
14248: 
14249: But, he went his way, and was soon swallowed up in the shadow of the
14250: prison wall. At the appointed hour, he emerged from it to present
14251: himself in Mr. Lorry's room again, where he found the old gentleman
14252: walking to and fro in restless anxiety. He said he had been with Lucie
14253: until just now, and had only left her for a few minutes, to come and
14254: keep his appointment. Her father had not been seen, since he quitted the
14255: banking-house towards four o'clock. She had some faint hopes that his
14256: mediation might save Charles, but they were very slight. He had been
14257: more than five hours gone: where could he be?
14258: 
14259: Mr. Lorry waited until ten; but, Doctor Manette not returning, and
14260: he being unwilling to leave Lucie any longer, it was arranged that he
14261: should go back to her, and come to the banking-house again at midnight.
14262: In the meanwhile, Carton would wait alone by the fire for the Doctor.
14263: 
14264: He waited and waited, and the clock struck twelve; but Doctor Manette
14265: did not come back. Mr. Lorry returned, and found no tidings of him, and
14266: brought none. Where could he be?
14267: 
14268: They were discussing this question, and were almost building up some
14269: weak structure of hope on his prolonged absence, when they heard him on
14270: the stairs. The instant he entered the room, it was plain that all was
14271: lost.
14272: 
14273: Whether he had really been to any one, or whether he had been all that
14274: time traversing the streets, was never known. As he stood staring at
14275: them, they asked him no question, for his face told them everything.
14276: 
14277: "I cannot find it," said he, "and I must have it. Where is it?"
14278: 
14279: His head and throat were bare, and, as he spoke with a helpless look
14280: straying all around, he took his coat off, and let it drop on the floor.
14281: 
14282: "Where is my bench? I have been looking everywhere for my bench, and I
14283: can't find it. What have they done with my work? Time presses: I must
14284: finish those shoes."
14285: 
14286: They looked at one another, and their hearts died within them.
14287: 
14288: "Come, come!" said he, in a whimpering miserable way; "let me get to
14289: work. Give me my work."
14290: 
14291: Receiving no answer, he tore his hair, and beat his feet upon the
14292: ground, like a distracted child.
14293: 
14294: "Don't torture a poor forlorn wretch," he implored them, with a dreadful
14295: cry; "but give me my work! What is to become of us, if those shoes are
14296: not done to-night?"
14297: 
14298: Lost, utterly lost!
14299: 
14300: It was so clearly beyond hope to reason with him, or try to restore him,
14301: that--as if by agreement--they each put a hand upon his shoulder, and
14302: soothed him to sit down before the fire, with a promise that he should
14303: have his work presently. He sank into the chair, and brooded over the
14304: embers, and shed tears. As if all that had happened since the garret
14305: time were a momentary fancy, or a dream, Mr. Lorry saw him shrink into
14306: the exact figure that Defarge had had in keeping.
14307: 
14308: Affected, and impressed with terror as they both were, by this spectacle
14309: of ruin, it was not a time to yield to such emotions. His lonely
14310: daughter, bereft of her final hope and reliance, appealed to them both
14311: too strongly. Again, as if by agreement, they looked at one another with
14312: one meaning in their faces. Carton was the first to speak:
14313: 
14314: "The last chance is gone: it was not much. Yes; he had better be taken
14315: to her. But, before you go, will you, for a moment, steadily attend to
14316: me? Don't ask me why I make the stipulations I am going to make, and
14317: exact the promise I am going to exact; I have a reason--a good one."
14318: 
14319: "I do not doubt it," answered Mr. Lorry. "Say on."
14320: 
14321: The figure in the chair between them, was all the time monotonously
14322: rocking itself to and fro, and moaning. They spoke in such a tone as
14323: they would have used if they had been watching by a sick-bed in the
14324: night.
14325: 
14326: Carton stooped to pick up the coat, which lay almost entangling his
14327: feet. As he did so, a small case in which the Doctor was accustomed to
14328: carry the lists of his day's duties, fell lightly on the floor. Carton
14329: took it up, and there was a folded paper in it. "We should look
14330: at this!" he said. Mr. Lorry nodded his consent. He opened it, and
14331: exclaimed, "Thank _God!_"
14332: 
14333: "What is it?" asked Mr. Lorry, eagerly.
14334: 
14335: "A moment! Let me speak of it in its place. First," he put his hand in
14336: his coat, and took another paper from it, "that is the certificate which
14338: an Englishman?"
14339: 
14340: Mr. Lorry held it open in his hand, gazing in his earnest face.
14341: 
14342: "Keep it for me until to-morrow. I shall see him to-morrow, you
14343: remember, and I had better not take it into the prison."
14344: 
14345: "Why not?"
14346: 
14347: "I don't know; I prefer not to do so. Now, take this paper that Doctor
14348: Manette has carried about him. It is a similar certificate, enabling him
14349: and his daughter and her child, at any time, to pass the barrier and the
14350: frontier! You see?"
14351: 
14352: "Yes!"
14353: 
14354: "Perhaps he obtained it as his last and utmost precaution against evil,
14355: yesterday. When is it dated? But no matter; don't stay to look; put it
14356: up carefully with mine and your own. Now, observe! I never doubted until
14357: within this hour or two, that he had, or could have such a paper. It is
14358: good, until recalled. But it may be soon recalled, and, I have reason to
14359: think, will be."
14360: 
14361: "They are not in danger?"
14362: 
14363: "They are in great danger. They are in danger of denunciation by Madame
14364: Defarge. I know it from her own lips. I have overheard words of that
14365: woman's, to-night, which have presented their danger to me in strong
14366: colours. I have lost no time, and since then, I have seen the spy. He
14367: confirms me. He knows that a wood-sawyer, living by the prison wall,
14368: is under the control of the Defarges, and has been rehearsed by
14369: Madame Defarge as to his having seen Her"--he never mentioned Lucie's
14370: name--"making signs and signals to prisoners. It is easy to foresee that
14371: the pretence will be the common one, a prison plot, and that it will
14372: involve her life--and perhaps her child's--and perhaps her father's--for
14373: both have been seen with her at that place. Don't look so horrified. You
14374: will save them all."
14375: 
14376: "Heaven grant I may, Carton! But how?"
14377: 
14378: "I am going to tell you how. It will depend on you, and it could depend
14379: on no better man. This new denunciation will certainly not take place
14380: until after to-morrow; probably not until two or three days afterwards;
14381: more probably a week afterwards. You know it is a capital crime, to
14382: mourn for, or sympathise with, a victim of the Guillotine. She and her
14383: father would unquestionably be guilty of this crime, and this woman (the
14384: inveteracy of whose pursuit cannot be described) would wait to add that
14385: strength to her case, and make herself doubly sure. You follow me?"
14386: 
14387: "So attentively, and with so much confidence in what you say, that for
14388: the moment I lose sight," touching the back of the Doctor's chair, "even
14389: of this distress."
14390: 
14391: "You have money, and can buy the means of travelling to the seacoast
14392: as quickly as the journey can be made. Your preparations have been
14393: completed for some days, to return to England. Early to-morrow have your
14394: horses ready, so that they may be in starting trim at two o'clock in the
14395: afternoon."
14396: 
14397: "It shall be done!"
14398: 
14399: His manner was so fervent and inspiring, that Mr. Lorry caught the
14400: flame, and was as quick as youth.
14401: 
14402: "You are a noble heart. Did I say we could depend upon no better man?
14403: Tell her, to-night, what you know of her danger as involving her child
14404: and her father. Dwell upon that, for she would lay her own fair head
14405: beside her husband's cheerfully." He faltered for an instant; then went
14406: on as before. "For the sake of her child and her father, press upon her
14407: the necessity of leaving Paris, with them and you, at that hour. Tell
14408: her that it was her husband's last arrangement. Tell her that more
14409: depends upon it than she dare believe, or hope. You think that her
14410: father, even in this sad state, will submit himself to her; do you not?"
14411: 
14412: "I am sure of it."
14413: 
14414: "I thought so. Quietly and steadily have all these arrangements made in
14415: the courtyard here, even to the taking of your own seat in the carriage.
14416: The moment I come to you, take me in, and drive away."
14417: 
14418: "I understand that I wait for you under all circumstances?"
14419: 
14420: "You have my certificate in your hand with the rest, you know, and will
14421: reserve my place. Wait for nothing but to have my place occupied, and
14422: then for England!"
14423: 
14424: "Why, then," said Mr. Lorry, grasping his eager but so firm and steady
14425: hand, "it does not all depend on one old man, but I shall have a young
14426: and ardent man at my side."
14427: 
14428: "By the help of Heaven you shall! Promise me solemnly that nothing will
14429: influence you to alter the course on which we now stand pledged to one
14430: another."
14431: 
14432: "Nothing, Carton."
14433: 
14434: "Remember these words to-morrow: change the course, or delay in it--for
14435: any reason--and no life can possibly be saved, and many lives must
14436: inevitably be sacrificed."
14437: 
14438: "I will remember them. I hope to do my part faithfully."
14439: 
14440: "And I hope to do mine. Now, good bye!"
14441: 
14442: Though he said it with a grave smile of earnestness, and though he even
14443: put the old man's hand to his lips, he did not part from him then. He
14444: helped him so far to arouse the rocking figure before the dying embers,
14445: as to get a cloak and hat put upon it, and to tempt it forth to find
14446: where the bench and work were hidden that it still moaningly besought
14447: to have. He walked on the other side of it and protected it to the
14448: courtyard of the house where the afflicted heart--so happy in
14449: the memorable time when he had revealed his own desolate heart to
14450: it--outwatched the awful night. He entered the courtyard and remained
14451: there for a few moments alone, looking up at the light in the window of
14452: her room. Before he went away, he breathed a blessing towards it, and a
14453: Farewell.
14454: 
14455: 
14456: 
14457: 
14458: XIII. Fifty-two
14459: 
14460: 
14461: In the black prison of the Conciergerie, the doomed of the day awaited
14462: their fate. They were in number as the weeks of the year. Fifty-two were
14463: to roll that afternoon on the life-tide of the city to the boundless
14464: everlasting sea. Before their cells were quit of them, new occupants
14465: were appointed; before their blood ran into the blood spilled yesterday,
14466: the blood that was to mingle with theirs to-morrow was already set
14467: apart.
14468: 
14469: Two score and twelve were told off. From the farmer-general of seventy,
14470: whose riches could not buy his life, to the seamstress of twenty, whose
14471: poverty and obscurity could not save her. Physical diseases, engendered
14472: in the vices and neglects of men, will seize on victims of all degrees;
14473: and the frightful moral disorder, born of unspeakable suffering,
14474: intolerable oppression, and heartless indifference, smote equally
14475: without distinction.
14476: 
14477: Charles Darnay, alone in a cell, had sustained himself with no
14478: flattering delusion since he came to it from the Tribunal. In every line
14479: of the narrative he had heard, he had heard his condemnation. He had
14480: fully comprehended that no personal influence could possibly save him,
14481: that he was virtually sentenced by the millions, and that units could
14482: avail him nothing.
14483: 
14484: Nevertheless, it was not easy, with the face of his beloved wife fresh
14485: before him, to compose his mind to what it must bear. His hold on life
14486: was strong, and it was very, very hard, to loosen; by gradual efforts
14487: and degrees unclosed a little here, it clenched the tighter there; and
14488: when he brought his strength to bear on that hand and it yielded,
14489: this was closed again. There was a hurry, too, in all his thoughts,
14490: a turbulent and heated working of his heart, that contended against
14491: resignation. If, for a moment, he did feel resigned, then his wife and
14492: child who had to live after him, seemed to protest and to make it a
14493: selfish thing.
14494: 
14495: But, all this was at first. Before long, the consideration that there
14496: was no disgrace in the fate he must meet, and that numbers went the same
14497: road wrongfully, and trod it firmly every day, sprang up to stimulate
14498: him. Next followed the thought that much of the future peace of mind
14499: enjoyable by the dear ones, depended on his quiet fortitude. So,
14500: by degrees he calmed into the better state, when he could raise his
14501: thoughts much higher, and draw comfort down.
14502: 
14503: Before it had set in dark on the night of his condemnation, he had
14504: travelled thus far on his last way. Being allowed to purchase the means
14505: of writing, and a light, he sat down to write until such time as the
14506: prison lamps should be extinguished.
14507: 
14508: He wrote a long letter to Lucie, showing her that he had known nothing
14509: of her father's imprisonment, until he had heard of it from herself,
14510: and that he had been as ignorant as she of his father's and uncle's
14511: responsibility for that misery, until the paper had been read. He had
14512: already explained to her that his concealment from herself of the name
14513: he had relinquished, was the one condition--fully intelligible now--that
14514: her father had attached to their betrothal, and was the one promise he
14515: had still exacted on the morning of their marriage. He entreated her,
14516: for her father's sake, never to seek to know whether her father had
14517: become oblivious of the existence of the paper, or had had it recalled
14518: to him (for the moment, or for good), by the story of the Tower, on
14519: that old Sunday under the dear old plane-tree in the garden. If he had
14520: preserved any definite remembrance of it, there could be no doubt that
14521: he had supposed it destroyed with the Bastille, when he had found no
14522: mention of it among the relics of prisoners which the populace had
14523: discovered there, and which had been described to all the world. He
14524: besought her--though he added that he knew it was needless--to console
14525: her father, by impressing him through every tender means she could think
14526: of, with the truth that he had done nothing for which he could justly
14527: reproach himself, but had uniformly forgotten himself for their joint
14528: sakes. Next to her preservation of his own last grateful love and
14529: blessing, and her overcoming of her sorrow, to devote herself to their
14530: dear child, he adjured her, as they would meet in Heaven, to comfort her
14531: father.
14532: 
14533: To her father himself, he wrote in the same strain; but, he told her
14534: father that he expressly confided his wife and child to his care. And
14535: he told him this, very strongly, with the hope of rousing him from any
14536: despondency or dangerous retrospect towards which he foresaw he might be
14537: tending.
14538: 
14539: To Mr. Lorry, he commended them all, and explained his worldly affairs.
14540: That done, with many added sentences of grateful friendship and warm
14541: attachment, all was done. He never thought of Carton. His mind was so
14542: full of the others, that he never once thought of him.
14543: 
14544: He had time to finish these letters before the lights were put out. When
14545: he lay down on his straw bed, he thought he had done with this world.
14546: 
14547: But, it beckoned him back in his sleep, and showed itself in shining
14548: forms. Free and happy, back in the old house in Soho (though it had
14549: nothing in it like the real house), unaccountably released and light of
14550: heart, he was with Lucie again, and she told him it was all a dream, and
14551: he had never gone away. A pause of forgetfulness, and then he had even
14552: suffered, and had come back to her, dead and at peace, and yet there
14553: was no difference in him. Another pause of oblivion, and he awoke in the
14554: sombre morning, unconscious where he was or what had happened, until it
14555: flashed upon his mind, "this is the day of my death!"
14556: 
14557: Thus, had he come through the hours, to the day when the fifty-two heads
14558: were to fall. And now, while he was composed, and hoped that he could
14559: meet the end with quiet heroism, a new action began in his waking
14560: thoughts, which was very difficult to master.
14561: 
14562: He had never seen the instrument that was to terminate his life. How
14563: high it was from the ground, how many steps it had, where he would be
14564: stood, how he would be touched, whether the touching hands would be dyed
14565: red, which way his face would be turned, whether he would be the first,
14566: or might be the last: these and many similar questions, in nowise
14567: directed by his will, obtruded themselves over and over again, countless
14568: times. Neither were they connected with fear: he was conscious of no
14569: fear. Rather, they originated in a strange besetting desire to know what
14570: to do when the time came; a desire gigantically disproportionate to the
14571: few swift moments to which it referred; a wondering that was more like
14572: the wondering of some other spirit within his, than his own.
14573: 
14574: The hours went on as he walked to and fro, and the clocks struck the
14575: numbers he would never hear again. Nine gone for ever, ten gone for
14576: ever, eleven gone for ever, twelve coming on to pass away. After a hard
14577: contest with that eccentric action of thought which had last perplexed
14578: him, he had got the better of it. He walked up and down, softly
14579: repeating their names to himself. The worst of the strife was over.
14580: He could walk up and down, free from distracting fancies, praying for
14581: himself and for them.
14582: 
14583: Twelve gone for ever.
14584: 
14585: He had been apprised that the final hour was Three, and he knew he would
14586: be summoned some time earlier, inasmuch as the tumbrils jolted heavily
14587: and slowly through the streets. Therefore, he resolved to keep Two
14588: before his mind, as the hour, and so to strengthen himself in the
14589: interval that he might be able, after that time, to strengthen others.
14590: 
14591: Walking regularly to and fro with his arms folded on his breast, a very
14592: different man from the prisoner, who had walked to and fro at La Force,
14593: he heard One struck away from him, without surprise. The hour had
14594: measured like most other hours. Devoutly thankful to Heaven for his
14595: recovered self-possession, he thought, "There is but another now," and
14596: turned to walk again.
14597: 
14598: Footsteps in the stone passage outside the door. He stopped.
14599: 
14600: The key was put in the lock, and turned. Before the door was opened, or
14601: as it opened, a man said in a low voice, in English: "He has never seen
14602: me here; I have kept out of his way. Go you in alone; I wait near. Lose
14603: no time!"
14604: 
14605: The door was quickly opened and closed, and there stood before him
14606: face to face, quiet, intent upon him, with the light of a smile on his
14608: 
14609: There was something so bright and remarkable in his look, that, for the
14610: first moment, the prisoner misdoubted him to be an apparition of his own
14611: imagining. But, he spoke, and it was his voice; he took the prisoner's
14612: hand, and it was his real grasp.
14613: 
14614: "Of all the people upon earth, you least expected to see me?" he said.
14615: 
14616: "I could not believe it to be you. I can scarcely believe it now. You
14617: are not"--the apprehension came suddenly into his mind--"a prisoner?"
14618: 
14619: "No. I am accidentally possessed of a power over one of the keepers
14620: here, and in virtue of it I stand before you. I come from her--your
14621: wife, dear Darnay."
14622: 
14623: The prisoner wrung his hand.
14624: 
14625: "I bring you a request from her."
14626: 
14627: "What is it?"
14628: 
14629: "A most earnest, pressing, and emphatic entreaty, addressed to you
14630: in the most pathetic tones of the voice so dear to you, that you well
14631: remember."
14632: 
14633: The prisoner turned his face partly aside.
14634: 
14635: "You have no time to ask me why I bring it, or what it means; I have
14636: no time to tell you. You must comply with it--take off those boots you
14637: wear, and draw on these of mine."
14638: 
14639: There was a chair against the wall of the cell, behind the prisoner.
14640: Carton, pressing forward, had already, with the speed of lightning, got
14641: him down into it, and stood over him, barefoot.
14642: 
14643: "Draw on these boots of mine. Put your hands to them; put your will to
14644: them. Quick!"
14645: 
14646: "Carton, there is no escaping from this place; it never can be done. You
14647: will only die with me. It is madness."
14648: 
14649: "It would be madness if I asked you to escape; but do I? When I ask you
14650: to pass out at that door, tell me it is madness and remain here. Change
14651: that cravat for this of mine, that coat for this of mine. While you do
14652: it, let me take this ribbon from your hair, and shake out your hair like
14653: this of mine!"
14654: 
14655: With wonderful quickness, and with a strength both of will and action,
14656: that appeared quite supernatural, he forced all these changes upon him.
14657: The prisoner was like a young child in his hands.
14658: 
14659: "Carton! Dear Carton! It is madness. It cannot be accomplished, it never
14660: can be done, it has been attempted, and has always failed. I implore you
14661: not to add your death to the bitterness of mine."
14662: 
14663: "Do I ask you, my dear Darnay, to pass the door? When I ask that,
14664: refuse. There are pen and ink and paper on this table. Is your hand
14665: steady enough to write?"
14666: 
14667: "It was when you came in."
14668: 
14669: "Steady it again, and write what I shall dictate. Quick, friend, quick!"
14670: 
14671: Pressing his hand to his bewildered head, Darnay sat down at the table.
14672: Carton, with his right hand in his breast, stood close beside him.
14673: 
14674: "Write exactly as I speak."
14675: 
14676: "To whom do I address it?"
14677: 
14678: "To no one." Carton still had his hand in his breast.
14679: 
14680: "Do I date it?"
14681: 
14682: "No."
14683: 
14684: The prisoner looked up, at each question. Carton, standing over him with
14685: his hand in his breast, looked down.
14686: 
14687: "'If you remember,'" said Carton, dictating, "'the words that passed
14688: between us, long ago, you will readily comprehend this when you see it.
14689: You do remember them, I know. It is not in your nature to forget them.'"
14690: 
14691: He was drawing his hand from his breast; the prisoner chancing to look
14692: up in his hurried wonder as he wrote, the hand stopped, closing upon
14693: something.
14694: 
14695: "Have you written 'forget them'?" Carton asked.
14696: 
14697: "I have. Is that a weapon in your hand?"
14698: 
14699: "No; I am not armed."
14700: 
14701: "What is it in your hand?"
14702: 
14703: "You shall know directly. Write on; there are but a few words more." He
14704: dictated again. "'I am thankful that the time has come, when I can prove
14705: them. That I do so is no subject for regret or grief.'" As he said these
14706: words with his eyes fixed on the writer, his hand slowly and softly
14707: moved down close to the writer's face.
14708: 
14709: The pen dropped from Darnay's fingers on the table, and he looked about
14710: him vacantly.
14711: 
14712: "What vapour is that?" he asked.
14713: 
14714: "Vapour?"
14715: 
14716: "Something that crossed me?"
14717: 
14718: "I am conscious of nothing; there can be nothing here. Take up the pen
14719: and finish. Hurry, hurry!"
14720: 
14721: As if his memory were impaired, or his faculties disordered, the
14722: prisoner made an effort to rally his attention. As he looked at Carton
14723: with clouded eyes and with an altered manner of breathing, Carton--his
14724: hand again in his breast--looked steadily at him.
14725: 
14726: "Hurry, hurry!"
14727: 
14728: The prisoner bent over the paper, once more.
14729: 
14730: "'If it had been otherwise;'" Carton's hand was again watchfully and
14731: softly stealing down; "'I never should have used the longer opportunity.
14732: If it had been otherwise;'" the hand was at the prisoner's face; "'I
14733: should but have had so much the more to answer for. If it had been
14734: otherwise--'" Carton looked at the pen and saw it was trailing off into
14735: unintelligible signs.
14736: 
14737: Carton's hand moved back to his breast no more. The prisoner sprang up
14738: with a reproachful look, but Carton's hand was close and firm at his
14739: nostrils, and Carton's left arm caught him round the waist. For a few
14740: seconds he faintly struggled with the man who had come to lay down his
14741: life for him; but, within a minute or so, he was stretched insensible on
14742: the ground.
14743: 
14744: Quickly, but with hands as true to the purpose as his heart was, Carton
14745: dressed himself in the clothes the prisoner had laid aside, combed back
14746: his hair, and tied it with the ribbon the prisoner had worn. Then, he
14747: softly called, "Enter there! Come in!" and the Spy presented himself.
14748: 
14749: "You see?" said Carton, looking up, as he kneeled on one knee beside the
14750: insensible figure, putting the paper in the breast: "is your hazard very
14751: great?"
14752: 
14753: "Mr. Carton," the Spy answered, with a timid snap of his fingers, "my
14754: hazard is not _that_, in the thick of business here, if you are true to
14755: the whole of your bargain."
14756: 
14757: "Don't fear me. I will be true to the death."
14758: 
14759: "You must be, Mr. Carton, if the tale of fifty-two is to be right. Being
14760: made right by you in that dress, I shall have no fear."
14761: 
14762: "Have no fear! I shall soon be out of the way of harming you, and the
14763: rest will soon be far from here, please God! Now, get assistance and
14764: take me to the coach."
14765: 
14766: "You?" said the Spy nervously.
14767: 
14768: "Him, man, with whom I have exchanged. You go out at the gate by which
14769: you brought me in?"
14770:
