14771: "Of course."
14772: 
14773: "I was weak and faint when you brought me in, and I am fainter now you
14774: take me out. The parting interview has overpowered me. Such a thing has
14775: happened here, often, and too often. Your life is in your own hands.
14776: Quick! Call assistance!"
14777: 
14778: "You swear not to betray me?" said the trembling Spy, as he paused for a
14779: last moment.
14780: 
14781: "Man, man!" returned Carton, stamping his foot; "have I sworn by no
14782: solemn vow already, to go through with this, that you waste the precious
14783: moments now? Take him yourself to the courtyard you know of, place
14784: him yourself in the carriage, show him yourself to Mr. Lorry, tell him
14785: yourself to give him no restorative but air, and to remember my words of
14786: last night, and his promise of last night, and drive away!"
14787: 
14788: The Spy withdrew, and Carton seated himself at the table, resting his
14789: forehead on his hands. The Spy returned immediately, with two men.
14790: 
14791: "How, then?" said one of them, contemplating the fallen figure. "So
14792: afflicted to find that his friend has drawn a prize in the lottery of
14793: Sainte Guillotine?"
14794: 
14795: "A good patriot," said the other, "could hardly have been more afflicted
14796: if the Aristocrat had drawn a blank."
14797: 
14798: They raised the unconscious figure, placed it on a litter they had
14799: brought to the door, and bent to carry it away.
14800: 
14801: "The time is short, Evremonde," said the Spy, in a warning voice.
14802: 
14803: "I know it well," answered Carton. "Be careful of my friend, I entreat
14804: you, and leave me."
14805: 
14806: "Come, then, my children," said Barsad. "Lift him, and come away!"
14807: 
14808: The door closed, and Carton was left alone. Straining his powers of
14809: listening to the utmost, he listened for any sound that might denote
14810: suspicion or alarm. There was none. Keys turned, doors clashed,
14811: footsteps passed along distant passages: no cry was raised, or hurry
14812: made, that seemed unusual. Breathing more freely in a little while, he
14813: sat down at the table, and listened again until the clock struck Two.
14814: 
14815: Sounds that he was not afraid of, for he divined their meaning, then
14816: began to be audible. Several doors were opened in succession, and
14817: finally his own. A gaoler, with a list in his hand, looked in, merely
14818: saying, "Follow me, Evremonde!" and he followed into a large dark room,
14819: at a distance. It was a dark winter day, and what with the shadows
14820: within, and what with the shadows without, he could but dimly discern
14821: the others who were brought there to have their arms bound. Some were
14822: standing; some seated. Some were lamenting, and in restless motion;
14823: but, these were few. The great majority were silent and still, looking
14824: fixedly at the ground.
14825: 
14826: As he stood by the wall in a dim corner, while some of the fifty-two
14827: were brought in after him, one man stopped in passing, to embrace him,
14828: as having a knowledge of him. It thrilled him with a great dread of
14829: discovery; but the man went on. A very few moments after that, a young
14830: woman, with a slight girlish form, a sweet spare face in which there was
14831: no vestige of colour, and large widely opened patient eyes, rose from
14832: the seat where he had observed her sitting, and came to speak to him.
14833: 
14834: "Citizen Evremonde," she said, touching him with her cold hand. "I am a
14835: poor little seamstress, who was with you in La Force."
14836: 
14837: He murmured for answer: "True. I forget what you were accused of?"
14838: 
14839: "Plots. Though the just Heaven knows that I am innocent of any. Is it
14840: likely? Who would think of plotting with a poor little weak creature
14841: like me?"
14842: 
14843: The forlorn smile with which she said it, so touched him, that tears
14844: started from his eyes.
14845: 
14846: "I am not afraid to die, Citizen Evremonde, but I have done nothing. I
14847: am not unwilling to die, if the Republic which is to do so much good
14848: to us poor, will profit by my death; but I do not know how that can be,
14849: Citizen Evremonde. Such a poor weak little creature!"
14850: 
14851: As the last thing on earth that his heart was to warm and soften to, it
14852: warmed and softened to this pitiable girl.
14853: 
14854: "I heard you were released, Citizen Evremonde. I hoped it was true?"
14855: 
14856: "It was. But, I was again taken and condemned."
14857: 
14858: "If I may ride with you, Citizen Evremonde, will you let me hold your
14859: hand? I am not afraid, but I am little and weak, and it will give me
14860: more courage."
14861: 
14862: As the patient eyes were lifted to his face, he saw a sudden doubt in
14863: them, and then astonishment. He pressed the work-worn, hunger-worn young
14864: fingers, and touched his lips.
14865: 
14866: "Are you dying for him?" she whispered.
14867: 
14868: "And his wife and child. Hush! Yes."
14869: 
14870: "O you will let me hold your brave hand, stranger?"
14871: 
14872: "Hush! Yes, my poor sister; to the last."
14873: 
14874:         *****
14875: 
14876: The same shadows that are falling on the prison, are falling, in that
14877: same hour of the early afternoon, on the Barrier with the crowd about
14878: it, when a coach going out of Paris drives up to be examined.
14879: 
14880: "Who goes here? Whom have we within? Papers!"
14881: 
14882: The papers are handed out, and read.
14883: 
14884: "Alexandre Manette. Physician. French. Which is he?"
14885: 
14886: This is he; this helpless, inarticulately murmuring, wandering old man
14887: pointed out.
14888: 
14889: "Apparently the Citizen-Doctor is not in his right mind? The
14890: Revolution-fever will have been too much for him?"
14891: 
14892: Greatly too much for him.
14893: 
14894: "Hah! Many suffer with it. Lucie. His daughter. French. Which is she?"
14895: 
14896: This is she.
14897: 
14898: "Apparently it must be. Lucie, the wife of Evremonde; is it not?"
14899: 
14900: It is.
14901: 
14902: "Hah! Evremonde has an assignation elsewhere. Lucie, her child. English.
14903: This is she?"
14904: 
14905: She and no other.
14906: 
14907: "Kiss me, child of Evremonde. Now, thou hast kissed a good Republican;
14909: English. Which is he?"
14910: 
14911: He lies here, in this corner of the carriage. He, too, is pointed out.
14912: 
14913: "Apparently the English advocate is in a swoon?"
14914: 
14915: It is hoped he will recover in the fresher air. It is represented that
14916: he is not in strong health, and has separated sadly from a friend who is
14917: under the displeasure of the Republic.
14918: 
14919: "Is that all? It is not a great deal, that! Many are under the
14920: displeasure of the Republic, and must look out at the little window.
14921: Jarvis Lorry. Banker. English. Which is he?"
14922: 
14923: "I am he. Necessarily, being the last."
14924: 
14925: It is Jarvis Lorry who has replied to all the previous questions. It
14926: is Jarvis Lorry who has alighted and stands with his hand on the coach
14927: door, replying to a group of officials. They leisurely walk round the
14928: carriage and leisurely mount the box, to look at what little luggage it
14929: carries on the roof; the country-people hanging about, press nearer to
14930: the coach doors and greedily stare in; a little child, carried by its
14931: mother, has its short arm held out for it, that it may touch the wife of
14932: an aristocrat who has gone to the Guillotine.
14933: 
14934: "Behold your papers, Jarvis Lorry, countersigned."
14935: 
14936: "One can depart, citizen?"
14937: 
14938: "One can depart. Forward, my postilions! A good journey!"
14939: 
14940: "I salute you, citizens.--And the first danger passed!"
14941: 
14942: These are again the words of Jarvis Lorry, as he clasps his hands, and
14943: looks upward. There is terror in the carriage, there is weeping, there
14944: is the heavy breathing of the insensible traveller.
14945: 
14946: "Are we not going too slowly? Can they not be induced to go faster?"
14947: asks Lucie, clinging to the old man.
14948: 
14949: "It would seem like flight, my darling. I must not urge them too much;
14950: it would rouse suspicion."
14951: 
14952: "Look back, look back, and see if we are pursued!"
14953: 
14954: "The road is clear, my dearest. So far, we are not pursued."
14955: 
14956: Houses in twos and threes pass by us, solitary farms, ruinous buildings,
14957: dye-works, tanneries, and the like, open country, avenues of leafless
14958: trees. The hard uneven pavement is under us, the soft deep mud is on
14959: either side. Sometimes, we strike into the skirting mud, to avoid the
14960: stones that clatter us and shake us; sometimes, we stick in ruts and
14961: sloughs there. The agony of our impatience is then so great, that in our
14962: wild alarm and hurry we are for getting out and running--hiding--doing
14963: anything but stopping.
14964: 
14965: Out of the open country, in again among ruinous buildings, solitary
14966: farms, dye-works, tanneries, and the like, cottages in twos and threes,
14967: avenues of leafless trees. Have these men deceived us, and taken us back
14968: by another road? Is not this the same place twice over? Thank Heaven,
14969: no. A village. Look back, look back, and see if we are pursued! Hush!
14970: the posting-house.
14971: 
14972: Leisurely, our four horses are taken out; leisurely, the coach stands in
14973: the little street, bereft of horses, and with no likelihood upon it
14974: of ever moving again; leisurely, the new horses come into visible
14975: existence, one by one; leisurely, the new postilions follow, sucking and
14976: plaiting the lashes of their whips; leisurely, the old postilions count
14977: their money, make wrong additions, and arrive at dissatisfied results.
14978: All the time, our overfraught hearts are beating at a rate that would
14979: far outstrip the fastest gallop of the fastest horses ever foaled.
14980: 
14981: At length the new postilions are in their saddles, and the old are left
14982: behind. We are through the village, up the hill, and down the hill, and
14983: on the low watery grounds. Suddenly, the postilions exchange speech with
14984: animated gesticulation, and the horses are pulled up, almost on their
14985: haunches. We are pursued?
14986: 
14987: "Ho! Within the carriage there. Speak then!"
14988: 
14989: "What is it?" asks Mr. Lorry, looking out at window.
14990: 
14991: "How many did they say?"
14992: 
14993: "I do not understand you."
14994: 
14995: "--At the last post. How many to the Guillotine to-day?"
14996: 
14997: "Fifty-two."
14998: 
14999: "I said so! A brave number! My fellow-citizen here would have it
15000: forty-two; ten more heads are worth having. The Guillotine goes
15001: handsomely. I love it. Hi forward. Whoop!"
15002: 
15003: The night comes on dark. He moves more; he is beginning to revive, and
15004: to speak intelligibly; he thinks they are still together; he asks him,
15005: by his name, what he has in his hand. O pity us, kind Heaven, and help
15006: us! Look out, look out, and see if we are pursued.
15007: 
15008: The wind is rushing after us, and the clouds are flying after us, and
15009: the moon is plunging after us, and the whole wild night is in pursuit of
15010: us; but, so far, we are pursued by nothing else.
15011: 
15012: 
15013: 
15014: 
15015: XIV. The Knitting Done
15016: 
15017: 
15018: In that same juncture of time when the Fifty-Two awaited their fate
15019: Madame Defarge held darkly ominous council with The Vengeance and
15020: Jacques Three of the Revolutionary Jury. Not in the wine-shop did Madame
15021: Defarge confer with these ministers, but in the shed of the wood-sawyer,
15022: erst a mender of roads. The sawyer himself did not participate in the
15023: conference, but abided at a little distance, like an outer satellite who
15024: was not to speak until required, or to offer an opinion until invited.
15025: 
15026: "But our Defarge," said Jacques Three, "is undoubtedly a good
15027: Republican? Eh?"
15028: 
15029: "There is no better," the voluble Vengeance protested in her shrill
15030: notes, "in France."
15031: 
15032: "Peace, little Vengeance," said Madame Defarge, laying her hand with
15033: a slight frown on her lieutenant's lips, "hear me speak. My husband,
15034: fellow-citizen, is a good Republican and a bold man; he has deserved
15035: well of the Republic, and possesses its confidence. But my husband has
15036: his weaknesses, and he is so weak as to relent towards this Doctor."
15037: 
15038: "It is a great pity," croaked Jacques Three, dubiously shaking his head,
15039: with his cruel fingers at his hungry mouth; "it is not quite like a good
15040: citizen; it is a thing to regret."
15041: 
15042: "See you," said madame, "I care nothing for this Doctor, I. He may wear
15043: his head or lose it, for any interest I have in him; it is all one to
15044: me. But, the Evremonde people are to be exterminated, and the wife and
15045: child must follow the husband and father."
15046: 
15047: "She has a fine head for it," croaked Jacques Three. "I have seen blue
15048: eyes and golden hair there, and they looked charming when Samson held
15049: them up." Ogre that he was, he spoke like an epicure.
15050: 
15051: Madame Defarge cast down her eyes, and reflected a little.
15052: 
15053: "The child also," observed Jacques Three, with a meditative enjoyment
15054: of his words, "has golden hair and blue eyes. And we seldom have a child
15055: there. It is a pretty sight!"
15056: 
15057: "In a word," said Madame Defarge, coming out of her short abstraction,
15058: "I cannot trust my husband in this matter. Not only do I feel, since
15059: last night, that I dare not confide to him the details of my projects;
15060: but also I feel that if I delay, there is danger of his giving warning,
15061: and then they might escape."
15062: 
15063: "That must never be," croaked Jacques Three; "no one must escape. We
15064: have not half enough as it is. We ought to have six score a day."
15065: 
15066: "In a word," Madame Defarge went on, "my husband has not my reason for
15067: pursuing this family to annihilation, and I have not his reason for
15068: regarding this Doctor with any sensibility. I must act for myself,
15069: therefore. Come hither, little citizen."
15070: 
15071: The wood-sawyer, who held her in the respect, and himself in the
15072: submission, of mortal fear, advanced with his hand to his red cap.
15073: 
15074: "Touching those signals, little citizen," said Madame Defarge, sternly,
15075: "that she made to the prisoners; you are ready to bear witness to them
15076: this very day?"
15077: 
15078: "Ay, ay, why not!" cried the sawyer. "Every day, in all weathers, from
15079: two to four, always signalling, sometimes with the little one, sometimes
15080: without. I know what I know. I have seen with my eyes."
15081: 
15082: He made all manner of gestures while he spoke, as if in incidental
15083: imitation of some few of the great diversity of signals that he had
15084: never seen.
15085: 
15086: "Clearly plots," said Jacques Three. "Transparently!"
15087: 
15088: "There is no doubt of the Jury?" inquired Madame Defarge, letting her
15089: eyes turn to him with a gloomy smile.
15090: 
15091: "Rely upon the patriotic Jury, dear citizeness. I answer for my
15092: fellow-Jurymen."
15093: 
15094: "Now, let me see," said Madame Defarge, pondering again. "Yet once more!
15095: Can I spare this Doctor to my husband? I have no feeling either way. Can
15096: I spare him?"
15097: 
15098: "He would count as one head," observed Jacques Three, in a low voice.
15099: "We really have not heads enough; it would be a pity, I think."
15100: 
15101: "He was signalling with her when I saw her," argued Madame Defarge; "I
15102: cannot speak of one without the other; and I must not be silent, and
15103: trust the case wholly to him, this little citizen here. For, I am not a
15104: bad witness."
15105: 
15106: The Vengeance and Jacques Three vied with each other in their fervent
15107: protestations that she was the most admirable and marvellous of
15108: witnesses. The little citizen, not to be outdone, declared her to be a
15109: celestial witness.
15110: 
15111: "He must take his chance," said Madame Defarge. "No, I cannot spare
15112: him! You are engaged at three o'clock; you are going to see the batch of
15113: to-day executed.--You?"
15114: 
15115: The question was addressed to the wood-sawyer, who hurriedly replied in
15116: the affirmative: seizing the occasion to add that he was the most ardent
15117: of Republicans, and that he would be in effect the most desolate of
15118: Republicans, if anything prevented him from enjoying the pleasure of
15119: smoking his afternoon pipe in the contemplation of the droll national
15120: barber. He was so very demonstrative herein, that he might have been
15121: suspected (perhaps was, by the dark eyes that looked contemptuously at
15122: him out of Madame Defarge's head) of having his small individual fears
15123: for his own personal safety, every hour in the day.
15124: 
15125: "I," said madame, "am equally engaged at the same place. After it is
15126: over--say at eight to-night--come you to me, in Saint Antoine, and we
15127: will give information against these people at my Section."
15128: 
15129: The wood-sawyer said he would be proud and flattered to attend the
15130: citizeness. The citizeness looking at him, he became embarrassed, evaded
15131: her glance as a small dog would have done, retreated among his wood, and
15132: hid his confusion over the handle of his saw.
15133: 
15134: Madame Defarge beckoned the Juryman and The Vengeance a little nearer to
15135: the door, and there expounded her further views to them thus:
15136: 
15137: "She will now be at home, awaiting the moment of his death. She will
15138: be mourning and grieving. She will be in a state of mind to impeach the
15139: justice of the Republic. She will be full of sympathy with its enemies.
15140: I will go to her."
15141: 
15142: "What an admirable woman; what an adorable woman!" exclaimed Jacques
15143: Three, rapturously. "Ah, my cherished!" cried The Vengeance; and
15144: embraced her.
15145: 
15146: "Take you my knitting," said Madame Defarge, placing it in her
15147: lieutenant's hands, "and have it ready for me in my usual seat. Keep
15148: me my usual chair. Go you there, straight, for there will probably be a
15149: greater concourse than usual, to-day."
15150: 
15151: "I willingly obey the orders of my Chief," said The Vengeance with
15152: alacrity, and kissing her cheek. "You will not be late?"
15153: 
15154: "I shall be there before the commencement."
15155: 
15156: "And before the tumbrils arrive. Be sure you are there, my soul," said
15157: The Vengeance, calling after her, for she had already turned into the
15158: street, "before the tumbrils arrive!"
15159: 
15160: Madame Defarge slightly waved her hand, to imply that she heard, and
15161: might be relied upon to arrive in good time, and so went through the
15162: mud, and round the corner of the prison wall. The Vengeance and the
15163: Juryman, looking after her as she walked away, were highly appreciative
15164: of her fine figure, and her superb moral endowments.
15165: 
15166: There were many women at that time, upon whom the time laid a dreadfully
15167: disfiguring hand; but, there was not one among them more to be dreaded
15168: than this ruthless woman, now taking her way along the streets. Of a
15169: strong and fearless character, of shrewd sense and readiness, of great
15170: determination, of that kind of beauty which not only seems to impart
15171: to its possessor firmness and animosity, but to strike into others an
15172: instinctive recognition of those qualities; the troubled time would have
15173: heaved her up, under any circumstances. But, imbued from her childhood
15174: with a brooding sense of wrong, and an inveterate hatred of a class,
15175: opportunity had developed her into a tigress. She was absolutely without
15176: pity. If she had ever had the virtue in her, it had quite gone out of
15177: her.
15178: 
15179: It was nothing to her, that an innocent man was to die for the sins of
15180: his forefathers; she saw, not him, but them. It was nothing to her, that
15181: his wife was to be made a widow and his daughter an orphan; that was
15182: insufficient punishment, because they were her natural enemies and
15183: her prey, and as such had no right to live. To appeal to her, was made
15184: hopeless by her having no sense of pity, even for herself. If she had
15185: been laid low in the streets, in any of the many encounters in which
15186: she had been engaged, she would not have pitied herself; nor, if she had
15187: been ordered to the axe to-morrow, would she have gone to it with any
15188: softer feeling than a fierce desire to change places with the man who
15189: sent her there.
15190: 
15191: Such a heart Madame Defarge carried under her rough robe. Carelessly
15192: worn, it was a becoming robe enough, in a certain weird way, and her
15193: dark hair looked rich under her coarse red cap. Lying hidden in her
15194: bosom, was a loaded pistol. Lying hidden at her waist, was a sharpened
15195: dagger. Thus accoutred, and walking with the confident tread of such
15196: a character, and with the supple freedom of a woman who had habitually
15197: walked in her girlhood, bare-foot and bare-legged, on the brown
15198: sea-sand, Madame Defarge took her way along the streets.
15199: 
15200: Now, when the journey of the travelling coach, at that very moment
15201: waiting for the completion of its load, had been planned out last night,
15202: the difficulty of taking Miss Pross in it had much engaged Mr. Lorry's
15203: attention. It was not merely desirable to avoid overloading the coach,
15204: but it was of the highest importance that the time occupied in examining
15205: it and its passengers, should be reduced to the utmost; since their
15206: escape might depend on the saving of only a few seconds here and there.
15207: Finally, he had proposed, after anxious consideration, that Miss Pross
15208: and Jerry, who were at liberty to leave the city, should leave it at
15209: three o'clock in the lightest-wheeled conveyance known to that period.
15210: Unencumbered with luggage, they would soon overtake the coach, and,
15211: passing it and preceding it on the road, would order its horses in
15212: advance, and greatly facilitate its progress during the precious hours
15213: of the night, when delay was the most to be dreaded.
15214: 
15215: Seeing in this arrangement the hope of rendering real service in that
15216: pressing emergency, Miss Pross hailed it with joy. She and Jerry had
15217: beheld the coach start, had known who it was that Solomon brought, had
15218: passed some ten minutes in tortures of suspense, and were now concluding
15219: their arrangements to follow the coach, even as Madame Defarge,
15220: taking her way through the streets, now drew nearer and nearer to the
15221: else-deserted lodging in which they held their consultation.
15222: 
15223: "Now what do you think, Mr. Cruncher," said Miss Pross, whose agitation
15224: was so great that she could hardly speak, or stand, or move, or live:
15225: "what do you think of our not starting from this courtyard? Another
15226: carriage having already gone from here to-day, it might awaken
15227: suspicion."
15228: 
15229: "My opinion, miss," returned Mr. Cruncher, "is as you're right. Likewise
15230: wot I'll stand by you, right or wrong."
15231: 
15232: "I am so distracted with fear and hope for our precious creatures," said
15233: Miss Pross, wildly crying, "that I am incapable of forming any plan. Are
15234: _you_ capable of forming any plan, my dear good Mr. Cruncher?"
15235: 
15236: "Respectin' a future spear o' life, miss," returned Mr. Cruncher, "I
15237: hope so. Respectin' any present use o' this here blessed old head o'
15238: mine, I think not. Would you do me the favour, miss, to take notice o'
15239: two promises and wows wot it is my wishes fur to record in this here
15240: crisis?"
15241: 
15242: "Oh, for gracious sake!" cried Miss Pross, still wildly crying, "record
15243: them at once, and get them out of the way, like an excellent man."
15244: 
15245: "First," said Mr. Cruncher, who was all in a tremble, and who spoke with
15246: an ashy and solemn visage, "them poor things well out o' this, never no
15247: more will I do it, never no more!"
15248: 
15249: "I am quite sure, Mr. Cruncher," returned Miss Pross, "that you
15250: never will do it again, whatever it is, and I beg you not to think it
15251: necessary to mention more particularly what it is."
15252: 
15253: "No, miss," returned Jerry, "it shall not be named to you. Second: them
15254: poor things well out o' this, and never no more will I interfere with
15255: Mrs. Cruncher's flopping, never no more!"
15256: 
15257: "Whatever housekeeping arrangement that may be," said Miss Pross,
15258: striving to dry her eyes and compose herself, "I have no doubt it
15259: is best that Mrs. Cruncher should have it entirely under her own
15260: superintendence.--O my poor darlings!"
15261: 
15262: "I go so far as to say, miss, moreover," proceeded Mr. Cruncher, with a
15263: most alarming tendency to hold forth as from a pulpit--"and let my words
15264: be took down and took to Mrs. Cruncher through yourself--that wot my
15265: opinions respectin' flopping has undergone a change, and that wot I only
15266: hope with all my heart as Mrs. Cruncher may be a flopping at the present
15267: time."
15268: 
15269: "There, there, there! I hope she is, my dear man," cried the distracted
15270: Miss Pross, "and I hope she finds it answering her expectations."
15271: 
15272: "Forbid it," proceeded Mr. Cruncher, with additional solemnity,
15273: additional slowness, and additional tendency to hold forth and hold
15274: out, "as anything wot I have ever said or done should be wisited on my
15275: earnest wishes for them poor creeturs now! Forbid it as we shouldn't all
15276: flop (if it was anyways conwenient) to get 'em out o' this here dismal
15277: risk! Forbid it, miss! Wot I say, for-_bid_ it!" This was Mr. Cruncher's
15278: conclusion after a protracted but vain endeavour to find a better one.
15279: 
15280: And still Madame Defarge, pursuing her way along the streets, came
15281: nearer and nearer.
15282: 
15283: "If we ever get back to our native land," said Miss Pross, "you may rely
15284: upon my telling Mrs. Cruncher as much as I may be able to remember and
15285: understand of what you have so impressively said; and at all events
15286: you may be sure that I shall bear witness to your being thoroughly in
15287: earnest at this dreadful time. Now, pray let us think! My esteemed Mr.
15288: Cruncher, let us think!"
15289: 
15290: Still, Madame Defarge, pursuing her way along the streets, came nearer
15291: and nearer.
15292: 
15293: "If you were to go before," said Miss Pross, "and stop the vehicle and
15294: horses from coming here, and were to wait somewhere for me; wouldn't
15295: that be best?"
15296: 
15297: Mr. Cruncher thought it might be best.
15298: 
15299: "Where could you wait for me?" asked Miss Pross.
15300: 
15301: Mr. Cruncher was so bewildered that he could think of no locality but
15302: Temple Bar. Alas! Temple Bar was hundreds of miles away, and Madame
15303: Defarge was drawing very near indeed.
15304: 
15305: "By the cathedral door," said Miss Pross. "Would it be much out of
15306: the way, to take me in, near the great cathedral door between the two
15307: towers?"
15308: 
15309: "No, miss," answered Mr. Cruncher.
15310: 
15311: "Then, like the best of men," said Miss Pross, "go to the posting-house
15312: straight, and make that change."
15313: 
15314: "I am doubtful," said Mr. Cruncher, hesitating and shaking his head,
15315: "about leaving of you, you see. We don't know what may happen."
15316: 
15317: "Heaven knows we don't," returned Miss Pross, "but have no fear for me.
15318: Take me in at the cathedral, at Three o'Clock, or as near it as you can,
15319: and I am sure it will be better than our going from here. I feel certain
15320: of it. There! Bless you, Mr. Cruncher! Think-not of me, but of the lives
15321: that may depend on both of us!"
15322: 
15323: This exordium, and Miss Pross's two hands in quite agonised entreaty
15324: clasping his, decided Mr. Cruncher. With an encouraging nod or two, he
15325: immediately went out to alter the arrangements, and left her by herself
15326: to follow as she had proposed.
15327: 
15328: The having originated a precaution which was already in course of
15329: execution, was a great relief to Miss Pross. The necessity of composing
15330: her appearance so that it should attract no special notice in the
15331: streets, was another relief. She looked at her watch, and it was twenty
15332: minutes past two. She had no time to lose, but must get ready at once.
15333: 
15334: Afraid, in her extreme perturbation, of the loneliness of the deserted
15335: rooms, and of half-imagined faces peeping from behind every open door
15336: in them, Miss Pross got a basin of cold water and began laving her eyes,
15337: which were swollen and red. Haunted by her feverish apprehensions, she
15338: could not bear to have her sight obscured for a minute at a time by the
15339: dripping water, but constantly paused and looked round to see that there
15340: was no one watching her. In one of those pauses she recoiled and cried
15341: out, for she saw a figure standing in the room.
15342: 
15343: The basin fell to the ground broken, and the water flowed to the feet of
15344: Madame Defarge. By strange stern ways, and through much staining blood,
15345: those feet had come to meet that water.
15346: 
15347: Madame Defarge looked coldly at her, and said, "The wife of Evremonde;
15348: where is she?"
15349: 
15350: It flashed upon Miss Pross's mind that the doors were all standing open,
15351: and would suggest the flight. Her first act was to shut them. There were
15352: four in the room, and she shut them all. She then placed herself before
15353: the door of the chamber which Lucie had occupied.
15354: 
15355: Madame Defarge's dark eyes followed her through this rapid movement,
15356: and rested on her when it was finished. Miss Pross had nothing beautiful
15357: about her; years had not tamed the wildness, or softened the grimness,
15358: of her appearance; but, she too was a determined woman in her different
15359: way, and she measured Madame Defarge with her eyes, every inch.
15360: 
15361: "You might, from your appearance, be the wife of Lucifer," said Miss
15362: Pross, in her breathing. "Nevertheless, you shall not get the better of
15363: me. I am an Englishwoman."
15364: 
15365: Madame Defarge looked at her scornfully, but still with something of
15366: Miss Pross's own perception that they two were at bay. She saw a tight,
15367: hard, wiry woman before her, as Mr. Lorry had seen in the same figure a
15368: woman with a strong hand, in the years gone by. She knew full well that
15369: Miss Pross was the family's devoted friend; Miss Pross knew full well
15370: that Madame Defarge was the family's malevolent enemy.
15371: 
15372: "On my way yonder," said Madame Defarge, with a slight movement of
15373: her hand towards the fatal spot, "where they reserve my chair and my
15374: knitting for me, I am come to make my compliments to her in passing. I
15375: wish to see her."
15376: 
15377: "I know that your intentions are evil," said Miss Pross, "and you may
15378: depend upon it, I'll hold my own against them."
15379: 
15380: Each spoke in her own language; neither understood the other's words;
15381: both were very watchful, and intent to deduce from look and manner, what
15382: the unintelligible words meant.
15383: 
15384: "It will do her no good to keep herself concealed from me at this
15385: moment," said Madame Defarge. "Good patriots will know what that means.
15386: Let me see her. Go tell her that I wish to see her. Do you hear?"
15387: 
15388: "If those eyes of yours were bed-winches," returned Miss Pross, "and I
15389: was an English four-poster, they shouldn't loose a splinter of me. No,
15390: you wicked foreign woman; I am your match."
15391: 
15392: Madame Defarge was not likely to follow these idiomatic remarks in
15393: detail; but, she so far understood them as to perceive that she was set
15394: at naught.
15395: 
15396: "Woman imbecile and pig-like!" said Madame Defarge, frowning. "I take no
15397: answer from you. I demand to see her. Either tell her that I demand
15398: to see her, or stand out of the way of the door and let me go to her!"
15399: This, with an angry explanatory wave of her right arm.
15400: 
15401: "I little thought," said Miss Pross, "that I should ever want to
15402: understand your nonsensical language; but I would give all I have,
15403: except the clothes I wear, to know whether you suspect the truth, or any
15404: part of it."
15405: 
15406: Neither of them for a single moment released the other's eyes. Madame
15407: Defarge had not moved from the spot where she stood when Miss Pross
15408: first became aware of her; but, she now advanced one step.
15409: 
15410: "I am a Briton," said Miss Pross, "I am desperate. I don't care an
15411: English Twopence for myself. I know that the longer I keep you here, the
15412: greater hope there is for my Ladybird. I'll not leave a handful of that
15413: dark hair upon your head, if you lay a finger on me!"
15414: 
15415: Thus Miss Pross, with a shake of her head and a flash of her eyes
15416: between every rapid sentence, and every rapid sentence a whole breath.
15417: Thus Miss Pross, who had never struck a blow in her life.
15418: 
15419: But, her courage was of that emotional nature that it brought the
15420: irrepressible tears into her eyes. This was a courage that Madame
15421: Defarge so little comprehended as to mistake for weakness. "Ha, ha!" she
15422: laughed, "you poor wretch! What are you worth! I address myself to that
15423: Doctor." Then she raised her voice and called out, "Citizen Doctor! Wife
15424: of Evremonde! Child of Evremonde! Any person but this miserable fool,
15425: answer the Citizeness Defarge!"
15426: 
15427: Perhaps the following silence, perhaps some latent disclosure in the
15428: expression of Miss Pross's face, perhaps a sudden misgiving apart from
15429: either suggestion, whispered to Madame Defarge that they were gone.
15430: Three of the doors she opened swiftly, and looked in.
15431: 
15432: "Those rooms are all in disorder, there has been hurried packing, there
15433: are odds and ends upon the ground. There is no one in that room behind
15434: you! Let me look."
15435: 
15436: "Never!" said Miss Pross, who understood the request as perfectly as
15437: Madame Defarge understood the answer.
15438: 
15439: "If they are not in that room, they are gone, and can be pursued and
15440: brought back," said Madame Defarge to herself.
15441: 
15442: "As long as you don't know whether they are in that room or not, you are
15443: uncertain what to do," said Miss Pross to herself; "and you shall not
15444: know that, if I can prevent your knowing it; and know that, or not know
15445: that, you shall not leave here while I can hold you."
15446: 
15447: "I have been in the streets from the first, nothing has stopped me,
15448: I will tear you to pieces, but I will have you from that door," said
15449: Madame Defarge.
15450: 
15451: "We are alone at the top of a high house in a solitary courtyard, we are
15452: not likely to be heard, and I pray for bodily strength to keep you here,
15453: while every minute you are here is worth a hundred thousand guineas to
15454: my darling," said Miss Pross.
15455: 
15456: Madame Defarge made at the door. Miss Pross, on the instinct of the
15457: moment, seized her round the waist in both her arms, and held her tight.
15458: It was in vain for Madame Defarge to struggle and to strike; Miss Pross,
15459: with the vigorous tenacity of love, always so much stronger than hate,
15460: clasped her tight, and even lifted her from the floor in the struggle
15461: that they had. The two hands of Madame Defarge buffeted and tore her
15462: face; but, Miss Pross, with her head down, held her round the waist, and
15463: clung to her with more than the hold of a drowning woman.
15464: 
15465: Soon, Madame Defarge's hands ceased to strike, and felt at her encircled
15466: waist. "It is under my arm," said Miss Pross, in smothered tones, "you
15467: shall not draw it. I am stronger than you, I bless Heaven for it. I hold
15468: you till one or other of us faints or dies!"
15469: 
15470: Madame Defarge's hands were at her bosom. Miss Pross looked up, saw
15471: what it was, struck at it, struck out a flash and a crash, and stood
15472: alone--blinded with smoke.
15473: 
15474: All this was in a second. As the smoke cleared, leaving an awful
15475: stillness, it passed out on the air, like the soul of the furious woman
15476: whose body lay lifeless on the ground.
15477: 
15478: In the first fright and horror of her situation, Miss Pross passed the
15479: body as far from it as she could, and ran down the stairs to call for
15480: fruitless help. Happily, she bethought herself of the consequences of
15481: what she did, in time to check herself and go back. It was dreadful to
15482: go in at the door again; but, she did go in, and even went near it, to
15483: get the bonnet and other things that she must wear. These she put on,
15484: out on the staircase, first shutting and locking the door and taking
15485: away the key. She then sat down on the stairs a few moments to breathe
15486: and to cry, and then got up and hurried away.
15487: 
15488: By good fortune she had a veil on her bonnet, or she could hardly have
15489: gone along the streets without being stopped. By good fortune, too, she
15490: was naturally so peculiar in appearance as not to show disfigurement
15491: like any other woman. She needed both advantages, for the marks of
15492: gripping fingers were deep in her face, and her hair was torn, and her
15493: dress (hastily composed with unsteady hands) was clutched and dragged a
15494: hundred ways.
15495: 
15496: In crossing the bridge, she dropped the door key in the river. Arriving
15497: at the cathedral some few minutes before her escort, and waiting there,
15498: she thought, what if the key were already taken in a net, what if
15499: it were identified, what if the door were opened and the remains
15500: discovered, what if she were stopped at the gate, sent to prison, and
15501: charged with murder! In the midst of these fluttering thoughts, the
15502: escort appeared, took her in, and took her away.
15503: 
15504: "Is there any noise in the streets?" she asked him.
15505: 
15506: "The usual noises," Mr. Cruncher replied; and looked surprised by the
15507: question and by her aspect.
15508: 
15509: "I don't hear you," said Miss Pross. "What do you say?"
15510: 
15511: It was in vain for Mr. Cruncher to repeat what he said; Miss Pross could
15512: not hear him. "So I'll nod my head," thought Mr. Cruncher, amazed, "at
15513: all events she'll see that." And she did.
15514: 
15515: "Is there any noise in the streets now?" asked Miss Pross again,
15516: presently.
15517: 
15518: Again Mr. Cruncher nodded his head.
15519: 
15520: "I don't hear it."
15521: 
15522: "Gone deaf in an hour?" said Mr. Cruncher, ruminating, with his mind
15523: much disturbed; "wot's come to her?"
15524: 
15525: "I feel," said Miss Pross, "as if there had been a flash and a crash,
15526: and that crash was the last thing I should ever hear in this life."
15527: 
15528: "Blest if she ain't in a queer condition!" said Mr. Cruncher, more and
15529: more disturbed. "Wot can she have been a takin', to keep her courage up?
15530: Hark! There's the roll of them dreadful carts! You can hear that, miss?"
15531: 
15532: "I can hear," said Miss Pross, seeing that he spoke to her, "nothing. O,
15533: my good man, there was first a great crash, and then a great stillness,
15534: and that stillness seems to be fixed and unchangeable, never to be
15535: broken any more as long as my life lasts."
15536: 
15537: "If she don't hear the roll of those dreadful carts, now very nigh their
15538: journey's end," said Mr. Cruncher, glancing over his shoulder, "it's my
15539: opinion that indeed she never will hear anything else in this world."
15540: 
15541: And indeed she never did.
15542: 
15543: 
15544: 
15545: 
15546: XV. The Footsteps Die Out For Ever
15547: 
15548: 
15549: Along the Paris streets, the death-carts rumble, hollow and harsh. Six
15550: tumbrils carry the day's wine to La Guillotine. All the devouring and
15551: insatiate Monsters imagined since imagination could record itself,
15552: are fused in the one realisation, Guillotine. And yet there is not in
15553: France, with its rich variety of soil and climate, a blade, a leaf,
15554: a root, a sprig, a peppercorn, which will grow to maturity under
15555: conditions more certain than those that have produced this horror. Crush
15556: humanity out of shape once more, under similar hammers, and it will
15557: twist itself into the same tortured forms. Sow the same seed of
15558: rapacious license and oppression over again, and it will surely yield
15559: the same fruit according to its kind.
15560: 
15561: Six tumbrils roll along the streets. Change these back again to what
15562: they were, thou powerful enchanter, Time, and they shall be seen to be
15563: the carriages of absolute monarchs, the equipages of feudal nobles, the
15564: toilettes of flaring Jezebels, the churches that are not my father's
15565: house but dens of thieves, the huts of millions of starving peasants!
15566: No; the great magician who majestically works out the appointed order
15567: of the Creator, never reverses his transformations. "If thou be changed
15568: into this shape by the will of God," say the seers to the enchanted, in
15569: the wise Arabian stories, "then remain so! But, if thou wear this
15570: form through mere passing conjuration, then resume thy former aspect!"
15571: Changeless and hopeless, the tumbrils roll along.
15572: 
15573: As the sombre wheels of the six carts go round, they seem to plough up
15574: a long crooked furrow among the populace in the streets. Ridges of faces
15575: are thrown to this side and to that, and the ploughs go steadily onward.
15576: So used are the regular inhabitants of the houses to the spectacle, that
15577: in many windows there are no people, and in some the occupation of the
15578: hands is not so much as suspended, while the eyes survey the faces in
15579: the tumbrils. Here and there, the inmate has visitors to see the sight;
15580: then he points his finger, with something of the complacency of a
15581: curator or authorised exponent, to this cart and to this, and seems to
15582: tell who sat here yesterday, and who there the day before.
15583: 
15584: Of the riders in the tumbrils, some observe these things, and all
15585: things on their last roadside, with an impassive stare; others, with
15586: a lingering interest in the ways of life and men. Some, seated with
15587: drooping heads, are sunk in silent despair; again, there are some so
15588: heedful of their looks that they cast upon the multitude such glances as
15589: they have seen in theatres, and in pictures. Several close their eyes,
15590: and think, or try to get their straying thoughts together. Only one, and
15591: he a miserable creature, of a crazed aspect, is so shattered and made
15592: drunk by horror, that he sings, and tries to dance. Not one of the whole
15593: number appeals by look or gesture, to the pity of the people.
15594: 
15595: There is a guard of sundry horsemen riding abreast of the tumbrils,
15596: and faces are often turned up to some of them, and they are asked some
15597: question. It would seem to be always the same question, for, it is
15598: always followed by a press of people towards the third cart. The
15599: horsemen abreast of that cart, frequently point out one man in it with
15600: their swords. The leading curiosity is, to know which is he; he stands
15601: at the back of the tumbril with his head bent down, to converse with a
15602: mere girl who sits on the side of the cart, and holds his hand. He has
15603: no curiosity or care for the scene about him, and always speaks to the
15604: girl. Here and there in the long street of St. Honore, cries are raised
15605: against him. If they move him at all, it is only to a quiet smile, as he
15606: shakes his hair a little more loosely about his face. He cannot easily
15607: touch his face, his arms being bound.
15608: 
15609: On the steps of a church, awaiting the coming-up of the tumbrils, stands
15610: the Spy and prison-sheep. He looks into the first of them: not there.
15611: He looks into the second: not there. He already asks himself, "Has he
15612: sacrificed me?" when his face clears, as he looks into the third.
15613: 
15614: "Which is Evremonde?" says a man behind him.
15615: 
15616: "That. At the back there."
15617: 
15618: "With his hand in the girl's?"
15619: 
15620: "Yes."
15621: 
15622: The man cries, "Down, Evremonde! To the Guillotine all aristocrats!
15623: Down, Evremonde!"
15624: 
15625: "Hush, hush!" the Spy entreats him, timidly.
15626: 
15627: "And why not, citizen?"
15628: 
15629: "He is going to pay the forfeit: it will be paid in five minutes more.
15630: Let him be at peace."
15631: 
15632: But the man continuing to exclaim, "Down, Evremonde!" the face of
15633: Evremonde is for a moment turned towards him. Evremonde then sees the
15634: Spy, and looks attentively at him, and goes his way.
15635: 
15636: The clocks are on the stroke of three, and the furrow ploughed among the
15637: populace is turning round, to come on into the place of execution, and
15638: end. The ridges thrown to this side and to that, now crumble in and
15639: close behind the last plough as it passes on, for all are following
15640: to the Guillotine. In front of it, seated in chairs, as in a garden of
15641: public diversion, are a number of women, busily knitting. On one of the
15642: fore-most chairs, stands The Vengeance, looking about for her friend.
15643: 
15644: "Therese!" she cries, in her shrill tones. "Who has seen her? Therese
15645: Defarge!"
15646: 
15647: "She never missed before," says a knitting-woman of the sisterhood.
15648: 
15649: "No; nor will she miss now," cries The Vengeance, petulantly. "Therese."
15650: 
15651: "Louder," the woman recommends.
15652: 
15653: Ay! Louder, Vengeance, much louder, and still she will scarcely hear
15654: thee. Louder yet, Vengeance, with a little oath or so added, and yet
15655: it will hardly bring her. Send other women up and down to seek her,
15656: lingering somewhere; and yet, although the messengers have done dread
15657: deeds, it is questionable whether of their own wills they will go far
15658: enough to find her!
15659: 
15660: "Bad Fortune!" cries The Vengeance, stamping her foot in the chair, "and
15661: here are the tumbrils! And Evremonde will be despatched in a wink, and
15662: she not here! See her knitting in my hand, and her empty chair ready for
15663: her. I cry with vexation and disappointment!"
15664: 
15665: As The Vengeance descends from her elevation to do it, the tumbrils
15666: begin to discharge their loads. The ministers of Sainte Guillotine are
15667: robed and ready. Crash!--A head is held up, and the knitting-women who
15668: scarcely lifted their eyes to look at it a moment ago when it could
15669: think and speak, count One.
15670: 
15671: The second tumbril empties and moves on; the third comes up. Crash!--And
15672: the knitting-women, never faltering or pausing in their Work, count Two.
15673: 
15674: The supposed Evremonde descends, and the seamstress is lifted out next
15675: after him. He has not relinquished her patient hand in getting out, but
15676: still holds it as he promised. He gently places her with her back to the
15677: crashing engine that constantly whirrs up and falls, and she looks into
15678: his face and thanks him.
15679: 
15680: "But for you, dear stranger, I should not be so composed, for I am
15681: naturally a poor little thing, faint of heart; nor should I have been
15682: able to raise my thoughts to Him who was put to death, that we might
15683: have hope and comfort here to-day. I think you were sent to me by
15684: Heaven."
15685: 
15687: and mind no other object."
15688: 
15689: "I mind nothing while I hold your hand. I shall mind nothing when I let
15690: it go, if they are rapid."
15691: 
15692: "They will be rapid. Fear not!"
15693: 
15694: The two stand in the fast-thinning throng of victims, but they speak as
15695: if they were alone. Eye to eye, voice to voice, hand to hand, heart to
15696: heart, these two children of the Universal Mother, else so wide apart
15697: and differing, have come together on the dark highway, to repair home
15698: together, and to rest in her bosom.
15699: 
15700: "Brave and generous friend, will you let me ask you one last question? I
15701: am very ignorant, and it troubles me--just a little."
15702: 
15703: "Tell me what it is."
15704: 
15705: "I have a cousin, an only relative and an orphan, like myself, whom I
15706: love very dearly. She is five years younger than I, and she lives in a
15707: farmer's house in the south country. Poverty parted us, and she knows
15708: nothing of my fate--for I cannot write--and if I could, how should I
15709: tell her! It is better as it is."
15710: 
15711: "Yes, yes: better as it is."
15712: 
15713: "What I have been thinking as we came along, and what I am still
15714: thinking now, as I look into your kind strong face which gives me so
15715: much support, is this:--If the Republic really does good to the poor,
15716: and they come to be less hungry, and in all ways to suffer less, she may
15717: live a long time: she may even live to be old."
15718: 
15719: "What then, my gentle sister?"
15720: 
15721: "Do you think:" the uncomplaining eyes in which there is so much
15722: endurance, fill with tears, and the lips part a little more and tremble:
15723: "that it will seem long to me, while I wait for her in the better land
15724: where I trust both you and I will be mercifully sheltered?"
15725: 
15726: "It cannot be, my child; there is no Time there, and no trouble there."
15727: 
15728: "You comfort me so much! I am so ignorant. Am I to kiss you now? Is the
15729: moment come?"
15730: 
15731: "Yes."
15732: 
15733: She kisses his lips; he kisses hers; they solemnly bless each other.
15734: The spare hand does not tremble as he releases it; nothing worse than
15735: a sweet, bright constancy is in the patient face. She goes next before
15736: him--is gone; the knitting-women count Twenty-Two.
15737: 
15738: "I am the Resurrection and the Life, saith the Lord: he that believeth
15739: in me, though he were dead, yet shall he live: and whosoever liveth and
15740: believeth in me shall never die."
15741: 
15742: The murmuring of many voices, the upturning of many faces, the pressing
15743: on of many footsteps in the outskirts of the crowd, so that it swells
15744: forward in a mass, like one great heave of water, all flashes away.
15745: Twenty-Three.
15746: 
15747:         *****
15748: 
15749: They said of him, about the city that night, that it was the
15750: peacefullest man's face ever beheld there. Many added that he looked
15751: sublime and prophetic.
15752: 
15753: One of the most remarkable sufferers by the same axe--a woman--had asked
15754: at the foot of the same scaffold, not long before, to be allowed to
15755: write down the thoughts that were inspiring her. If he had given any
15756: utterance to his, and they were prophetic, they would have been these:
15757: 
15758: "I see Barsad, and Cly, Defarge, The Vengeance, the Juryman, the Judge,
15759: long ranks of the new oppressors who have risen on the destruction of
15760: the old, perishing by this retributive instrument, before it shall cease
15761: out of its present use. I see a beautiful city and a brilliant people
15762: rising from this abyss, and, in their struggles to be truly free, in
15763: their triumphs and defeats, through long years to come, I see the evil
15764: of this time and of the previous time of which this is the natural
15765: birth, gradually making expiation for itself and wearing out.
15766: 
15767: "I see the lives for which I lay down my life, peaceful, useful,
15768: prosperous and happy, in that England which I shall see no more. I see
15769: Her with a child upon her bosom, who bears my name. I see her father,
15770: aged and bent, but otherwise restored, and faithful to all men in his
15771: healing office, and at peace. I see the good old man, so long their
15772: friend, in ten years' time enriching them with all he has, and passing
15773: tranquilly to his reward.
15774: 
15775: "I see that I hold a sanctuary in their hearts, and in the hearts of
15776: their descendants, generations hence. I see her, an old woman, weeping
15777: for me on the anniversary of this day. I see her and her husband, their
15778: course done, lying side by side in their last earthly bed, and I know
15779: that each was not more honoured and held sacred in the other's soul,
15780: than I was in the souls of both.
15781: 
15782: "I see that child who lay upon her bosom and who bore my name, a man
15783: winning his way up in that path of life which once was mine. I see him
15784: winning it so well, that my name is made illustrious there by the
15785: light of his. I see the blots I threw upon it, faded away. I see him,
15786: fore-most of just judges and honoured men, bringing a boy of my name,
15787: with a forehead that I know and golden hair, to this place--then fair to
15788: look upon, with not a trace of this day's disfigurement--and I hear him
15789: tell the child my story, with a tender and a faltering voice.
15790: 
15791: "It is a far, far better thing that I do, than I have ever done; it is a
15792: far, far better rest that I go to than I have ever known."
